<story>
Halfway down the Rue Saint-Denis, near Rue du Petit-Lion, a weathered house seemed to recreate old Paris. Dilapidated walls exposed timber and cracked plaster; a warped triangular roof overhung the street, its overlapping planks stressing fragility.
On a rainy March morning a young man, cloaked like an antiquary, stood opposite studying its peculiarities. He ignored the boarded first-floor windows and studied the third floor’s modest sash windows, clouded with green glass, while glancing at the shop below that amused him.
A large wooden beam, propped on four pillars and layered with paint, bore a whimsical cat with an oversized racket and absurd ball. A faded sign—“Guillaume, Successor to Master Chevrel”—evoked the shop’s old charm.
Dressed for a ball in a stylish cloak, white silk stockings and gloves, his disheveled hair hinted at revelry. Market gardeners hurried past; his pale face and bright cravat drew attention. Apprentices in the dormer laughed and sprinkled him with shaving cream; a Raphael-like girl peered then fled. The “Cat and Racket” opened: a manservant hung the cloth sign and, behind iron bars, many brown-linen packages appeared. Monsieur Guillaume was old-fashioned but famed for a well-stocked, honest shop, though rivals grumbled at his notary’s heavy discounts.
Monsieur Guillaume, a traditional merchant, watched a stranger; an apprentice hailed a cab, preventing a prank on Augustine. He lamented lost customs and enforced strict routine, reproving them, demanding harder labor than at newer shops. Madame Guillaume’s frugality excluded apprentices from dessert; family attended Mass. Joseph Lebas earned modest wages; Guillaume favored him for Virginie, though Joseph loved Augustine. Virginie was devout and severe; Augustine, delicate and stifled. Amid the shop’s monotony feelings grew and Guillaume feared for his daughters’ prospects.
The old merchant, grieving his eldest daughter’s decline, recalled marrying Mademoiselle Chevrel under circumstances like those of Joseph Lebas and Virginie; marrying off his daughter would repay a sacred debt to an orphan benefactor. Joseph Lebas, thirty-three, aware of the fifteen-year gap with Augustine and Monsieur Guillaume’s rule that the elder sister marry first, suffered in silence—warm-hearted but reserved.
Earlier a young painter paused outside the dark Cat and Racket, transfixed by a scene any artist would envy. The cave-like shop opened on a dining room lit by a hanging lamp that recalled Dutch interiors. The family—Augustine among them—formed a modest tableau; Augustine, pensive and barely touching her food, seemed almost angelic in the lamp’s glow. Overwhelmed, the painter left, then returned in disguise to study her. For eight months he lived in love and art, neglecting friends and routine.
One morning his friend Girodet found two masterpieces; sensing deep feeling, he said love had made the work great. Despite warnings, the paintings were exhibited and created a sensation—launching a new genre and prompting offers, many of which were declined.
Augustine, living a monastic life in Rue Saint-Denis, learned of the exhibition from Madame Guillaume and, with a cousin’s help, obtained permission to see the Louvre. Trembling before the portrait, she recognized herself and heard the artist whisper, “You see how love has inspired me.” Fleeing the crowd, she returned, wept, exchanged a brief nod with the painter, and felt both guilt and exhilaration. Feigning a headache, she longed for the elegance she had admired and to be a genius’s wife. The next day her parents returned disappointed: the painter had removed the two pictures, and Madame Guillaume had lost her shawl. The episode awakened in Augustine a woman’s instinctive sensitivity.
After the ball Theodore de Sommervieux, who had won Augustine’s heart, hesitated to declare his love because her household constrained them. Augustine sat in the counting-house between Mademoiselle Virginie and Madame Guillaume under her mother's watch, while Theodore, fearing rivals among the assistants, bribed a maid for discreet exchanges; they agreed to meet at Mass at Saint-Leu.
At the Cat and Racket the family concentrated on stock-taking under Monsieur Guillaume, tallying inventory and accounts; afterward they relaxed and attended a performance of Cendrillon. The next morning Guillaume summoned his assistant Joseph Lebas, offered him partnership, and was startled when Joseph blurted that he loved Augustine—Guillaume at first misheard and thought he meant Virginie. Guillaume said he would consider Joseph’s feelings and suggested he accompany Augustine to Mass. At breakfast tension rose; Madame reprimanded Augustine for inattentiveness in church, prompting a heated discussion in which Augustine confessed her love for Theodore and defended his respectability. Monsieur, wary of artists, protested until Madame Roguin arrived with news of Theodore’s honors and fortune. Her report swayed the parents; by evening Guillaume informed Joseph, the household celebrated, Theodore presented a painting of the Cat and Racket, and Monsieur consented to the match.
A few months after that memorable Sunday, Saint-Leu’s high altar witnessed two contrasting weddings. Augustine and Theodore radiated happiness while Virginie, simply dressed, followed her sister. Monsieur Guillaume had wanted Virginie married first, but the priests attended the more elegant bride. Neighbors praised Virginie’s sensible match and envied Augustine for marrying an artist of rank; an old fan-maker warned such extravagance could bring ruin, and Monsieur Guillaume felt vindicated. After a splendid ball and supper the Guillaumes returned home and the Lebas family resumed their modest life. The ecstatic artist carried Augustine to their richly adorned apartment.
Their first year was blissful: Theodore lavished affection and Augustine, lost in love, believed her devotion would always enchant him. Naïve at times, she remained happily unaware of many things. As pregnancy neared, Theodore felt the pull of work, reconnected with friends, and began frequenting the Duchesse de Carigliano’s salons, enjoying the prestige of being seen with a beautiful wife.
Augustine liked society but soon felt out of place among Theodore’s artistic circle. Her admiration was sincere but superficial; an intellectual disconnect grew. Studio life did not draw her in, and at gatherings her innocence clashed with freer manners. Gradually Theodore’s warmth cooled. Three years in, Augustine sank into sorrow as his intimacy with the Duchesse stoked fears of being supplanted. Attempts to study poetry and art only revealed her inadequacy. A brief return to her childhood home offered little comfort, and she went back to her husband’s house burdened by uncertainty and longing, trying every remedy to heal a fractured marriage.
The elderly couple welcomed their daughter, interrupting years of monotonous reminiscence about past business troubles—most notably the Lecocq bankruptcy. Monsieur Guillaume fussed over his shop, the Cat and Racket, and chatted with former competitors; Madame Guillaume, appointed to the Army’s clothing consulting board through her son-in-law, filled their home with extravagant, tasteless decor. Their nostalgic stagnation contrasted sharply with Augustine’s turbulent life, leaving them both proud and appalled.
Augustine displayed her achievements and aired marital grievances, which her parents received avidly. Madame Guillaume was especially curious about Augustine’s husband, Theodore, a painter. When Augustine described his late nights and eccentric habits, her mother disapproved, sparking a debate about genius versus marital responsibility. The mention of divorce shocked them; Monsieur offered practical help. Overwhelmed and unable to convey the depth of her suffering, Augustine felt an isolating loneliness only fellow sufferers could know.
Determined to change course, she sought the Duchess de Carigliano—not to force Theodore back but to learn how to captivate him. The Duchess counseled strength, self-control, cultivated mystery, and withholding a full measure of affection, and lent a portrait Theodore had painted to rekindle him. Augustine tried to become that image; Theodore’s fury erupted into a violent quarrel. The next morning her mother found Augustine dead among the wreckage. She died young, her tragic end underscoring the plight of women seeking love and fulfillment amid the complexities of artistic genius; her grave’s inscription bears that sorrow.

The Comte de Fontaine, a loyal royalist from Poitou who had fought for the Bourbons in La Vendée, was ruined when his estate was confiscated. He married Mademoiselle de Kergarouet—noble but penniless—and took his large family to Paris seeking relief. An unexpected promotion and royal decorations revived his hopes, and during the 1815 upheavals he reentered service. His fidelity won Louis XVIII’s confidence; he became a trusted adviser and the family prospered: his sons obtained important posts and his daughters advantageous matches, usually with lesser nobility. Emilie, the youngest, became the spoiled center of Parisian society—beautiful, exacting, and indifferent to most suitors, whom she often snubbed for trivial reasons. As the King’s health declined and political uncertainty grew, the Comte pressed harder to secure Emilie’s marriage to protect the family’s standing, but her disdain through the winter left their future uncertain.
Nature had given Emilie every quality to play Célimène. Tall and slender, she could shift from dignity to coquettishness; her long neck and arched brows expressed scorn and impertinence, her fine black hair and melodious voice charmed or silenced. Her alabaster complexion rarely flushed; many rejected suitors accused her of acting, while she maintained she merely inspired desire in order to govern it.
The Comte recognized too late that home adoration had spoiled her. Constant flattery had inflated her pride; his gentle counsels slid off her like water from marble. Her affection for her parents was often patronizing—a child’s demand for attention between bouts of play. Though she could be tender, she more often withdrew, jealous of the love shared with others. She blamed fate for her solitude, failing to see that contentment is inward. Determined to resist her sisters’ matches, she nevertheless envied their happiness; her unpredictability led her mother to suspect a touch of madness.
This proud, rebellious temperament described a common pattern among beautiful girls of affluent families: feeling misunderstood by mothers, suspecting maternal envy, nursing secret tears, and building elaborate ideals of the perfect partner, only to learn that reality rarely matches fantasy.
Emilie’s standards were precise. Her husband must be a peer of France and a soldier bearing an Order so he could be publicly respected; he must be slim—she despised corpulence and considered slenderness essential to romance. Her disdain for unsuitable suitors was plain, and her father feared her eccentricities would invite ridicule and that delay in the marriage market might have consequences.
That winter the Comte redoubled efforts, hosting gatherings despite his controversial reputation. After a season of introductions he hoped Emilie would choose, but her whims held firm. One morning he made a dignified appeal; she interrupted with chilly levity, mocked his criteria, and reminded him of their agreement. He declared he had done his duty and that the decision now rested with her, warning that marital happiness depends more on mutual respect than rank. Touched, she feigned affection but remained proud.
At a family gathering she refused to consider marriage, even when an uncle jokingly suggested she wait for the Duc de Bordeaux. That summer at the country house she begged to attend the Bal de Sceaux, drawn by the anonymity of the crowd. There she noticed a striking young man apart from the throng—his elegance and dignity sparked immediate attraction. Aloof, he watched another dancer, a pale girl named Clara; curious and jealous, Emilie followed them. Days later, learning he was not the noble she’d hoped, she pressed her uncle for an introduction. The promise intensified her interest; her infatuation grew, nourished by imagination and youthful ideals, still blind to the complexities of love and marriage.
The next morning, before Emilie woke, her uncle hurried to Chevreuse. Recognizing the young man he’d insulted, he recovered the civility of an old courtier. “I’m a vice‑admiral,” he said, apologized for having drunk too much, and proposed they shake hands to avoid family trouble. The young man accepted; they rode into the woods where the Count, mock‑dueling, fired a pistol into a birch and encouraged the fellow to shoot near his mark. The man obliged and won the admiral’s approval.
On the ride the Count tested codes of gentlemanly conduct—asked about debts, teased about mistresses, confessed his own past recklessness—and promised to introduce the young man at the Pavillon Planat and invite him to dinner so he could meet the nephew, the Comte de Fontaine. The newcomer, Longueville, seemed odd but affable and accepted.
When the house party gathered and a servant announced “Monsieur de Longueville,” everyone hurried to appraise him. Simply and elegantly dressed, he looked at ease in the Receiver‑General’s luxury; his polished manner and ready knowledge of naval architecture led a lady to suspect École Polytechnique training. He accepted flattery yet declined the Count’s dinner invitation, explaining he was “Hippocrates” to his delicate sister—an excuse that invited ironic speculation about his profession.
Mademoiselle de Fontaine, who had dressed to attract him, felt slighted by his lack of attention. Emilie, usually winning with newcomers, behaved unusually simple and natural, prompting family comment and varied interpretations—some thinking she was withholding to dazzle later. Longueville’s visit pleased everyone and intensified curiosity. The Count called him “a cunning rascal.” A sarcastic dinner remark by Mademoiselle de Fontaine—that universal perfection must hide a defect, “the worst fault is to have none”—kept speculation alive.
Despite efforts at indifference, Emilie’s feelings soon betrayed her. By his third visit it was clear she was affected: accustomed to being the center of attention, she was unsettled by someone who returned her interest. She tried to resist but could not dismiss him. Anxiety grew because his modesty and discretion clashed with her curiosity—he never spoke of himself or his family and deftly evaded hints. If she discussed painting he answered like a connoisseur; if she played music he proved a good pianist. Once they sang a duet, a moment of intimate harmony, yet he preserved the mystery of his social status.
Her uncle and family schemed to learn more, eager to meet Longueville’s sister, Clara, and proposed an informal dance. Emilie welcomed another person to care for, grew gentler and more indulgent, and her family read this as love transforming selfishness. She believed Longueville’s visits were chiefly for her and, though they never spoke openly of passion, she sensed his affection and led him into revealing small details; he, too, studied her and encouraged her improvement.
On the day of the dance Clara and her brother were guests. Emilie took pleasure in Clara’s triumph and lavished her with sincere attention. Clara, more deft in conversation, charmed Emilie and dismissed her class anxieties with graceful ease—“I’ve heard so much about you from Maximilien; isn’t wanting to know you a wish to love you?” they said to each other, and danced warmly.
Believing him well born and seeing his grace surpass her ideal, Emilie felt satisfaction and sparkle. As autumn deepened, the couple surrendered to the sweet tide of feeling: aimless conversations masking deeper affections, admiring sunsets, gathering daisies, singing passionate duets—all small incidents knitting them closer and confirming the mutual love they had carefully concealed.

At twilight a young painter finishing a large canvas falls from his ladder, strikes his head, and loses consciousness. He is roused by a young woman and her mother who rush to his aid. Recovering, he is struck by the girl’s beauty—matching an ideal he had imagined—and by the mother’s marquise-like air. Both are gentle, reserved, and withdraw without pressing acquaintance.
The porter’s wife identifies them as Mademoiselle Leseigneur and her mother. The painter, Hippolyte Schinner, new to the attic studio and beginning to gain recognition, keeps his affairs guarded; he hopes to secure a better life for his own mother. During convalescence he replays the encounter, especially the image of the girl, and soon feels compelled to visit.
In their modest home he finds clear poverty but maintained elegance: traces of past finery kept clean. Conversation, at first awkward, turns to art. Madame Leseigneur reveals a cherished portrait of her late husband, a naval officer, is decaying. Hippolyte offers to paint a new portrait; they accept, and he senses the emotional weight behind the request.
When Adélaïde briefly leaves and returns with candles, a visitor appears—an older gentleman in outmoded dress whose nobility mixes with a libertine air. Their exchange hints at layered history and social distinctions. Throughout the evening Hippolyte is torn between admiration and uncertainty, drawn to her spirit yet aware of the fragile boundary between their worlds.
A remarkable, faded figure stood beside “Louis XIV’s light infantry,” the Bonapartist sarcasm for the ragged remnants of the Monarchy: lean, aged, powdered hair dulled, intellect shadowed. Rivarol’s quip about Champcenetz seemed apt. The silent old man puzzled the painter Chevalier—no one addressed him. His relation to the gallant man—friend, relative, or companion—was unclear; his presence hinted at a past link to the gallant’s deceased wife but offered no answers.
One of the less dilapidated men approached Madame de Rouville, kissed her hand and sat; another bowed and took a seat. Adelaide leaned over the old gentleman’s chair in a posture recalling a famous painting, though her familiarity appeared to irritate him. “Are you sulky with me?” he asked, casting a shrewd glance at the painter Schinner as if measuring him.
Madame de Rouville introduced Hippolyte, the celebrated painter. The old man noticed Hippolyte’s red ribbon and contrasted it with his own hard-won honors. Hippolyte, captivated by Adelaide’s beauty, was briefly blind to the squalor around them; enthralled, he spoke little while the old gentleman lamented his losing streak at cards. Adelaide offered help; he insisted she stay where he could see her. When the game ended and the gentleman complained of losses, the silent figure only echoed the hour, prompting Hippolyte to take his leave, still under the spell of budding affection.
The next day Hippolyte longed to see Adelaide again. He waited until afternoon before visiting Madame de Rouville and awkwardly asked for the Baron’s portrait. Adelaide, alone in the drawing-room, sent him to fetch it, revealing modesty with a charged glance. Hippolyte took the portrait and promised to return it soon.
Their relationship blossomed with laughter and confidences. Schinner strove to learn piquet but often lost; the unspoken bond between Hippolyte and Adelaide deepened into inseparability. Adelaide’s affection grew and Hippolyte’s jealousy flared when the old Count showed familiarity with her. Their love unfolded without grand gestures, founded on mutual trust and subtle exchanges. Within two months Hippolyte visited daily, the old gentleman’s visits faded, and Hippolyte took his place at the card table, sharing equally in the losses.
Hippolyte fretted about Madame de Rouville’s finances after two months on the Baron’s portrait—a work he prized—yet the Baroness said nothing of payment. Adelaide later visited his studio, moved by the painting; Hippolyte declared his desire to marry her, interrupted by the old vice-admiral’s praise and hint of aid.
That evening strange excitement at cards preceded the theft of Hippolyte’s purse. In pain and suspicion, he feared Adelaide and her mother were guilty; rumors unsettled him. Confronting Adelaide, he was struck by her pallor and sorrow and felt ashamed. During a tender card evening she discreetly placed a new purse before him. Overwhelmed, he vowed to marry her.
The vice-admiral soon returned with word of a promotion and offered support to the young couple, praising the portrait and dispelling doubts. The evening closed in renewed unity. The tale follows characters navigating love, trust, and misunderstanding, finding solace through faith, reconciliation, and shared bonds.

In October 1800 Bartolomeo di Piombo, a distraught man in his sixties, stood outside the Tuileries with his wife and daughter, handed his dagger to his wife, and declared he would confront the Bonapartes. A soldier barred him until Lucien Bonaparte recognized him and brought him to Napoleon. Bartolomeo sought asylum, confessing he had avenged his family by killing the Portas—the clan that burned his vineyard and murdered his son—admitting he had slain all seven Portas. Bitter at perceived Bonaparte indifference, he reminded them he had protected their family.
Napoleon, unwilling to condone private vengeance, acknowledged the Corsican vendetta tradition, offered lodging in Paris and financial aid, but warned that the city would not tolerate private justice. Bartolomeo accepted and pledged loyalty. Lucien found they had arrived with nothing, gave them money and promised help, noting their Corsican property could not sustain them in Paris. Fifteen years later, events would make Bartolomeo’s choices significant.
Servin, a respected artist of about forty, was the first to open a studio specifically for young girls who wished to paint. Morally strict and devoted to art, married to a general’s daughter who brought no dowry, he at first had mothers stay with their daughters; reassured by his principles, they soon left. To avoid social disputes and because professional training required rigor, he limited pupils to well-off young ladies and refused those bent on becoming professionals. His exacting method won him a stellar reputation among the aristocracy: Servin became to feminine art what Herbault was to bonnets and Leroy to gowns, and any mother who suspected talent in her daughter recommended him.
The studio—an attic above his apartment reached by a narrow spiral stair and sealed from below—was carefully arranged: a light-filled room with green-shaded panes, dark-gray walls hung with caricatures and plaster models, a blend of disciplined disorder and harmony.
On a bright July day the attic hummed with girls at their easels. Two cliques had formed: daughters of wealthy merchants and aristocrats led by the vain Amelie Thirion, and the rest. Among them sat Ginevra Piombo, modest and anxious. Her talent and beauty provoked the aristocrats’ jealousy; Amelie shoved Ginevra’s easel amid whispers. The Bourbons’ second return had sharpened political divides: Ginevra, a devoted Bonapartist, drew royalist disdain.
A faint sound behind a partition revealed Servin was sheltering a fugitive officer of the Imperial Guard. To protect him, Ginevra chose proximity rather than risk exposure. When Madame Servin’s arrival threatened discovery, Ginevra quietly hummed a Corsican song to mask his breathing while sketching his head with surprising fervor. Servin praised her likeness; the other girls crowded round, inflaming Amelie’s envy. The shared awareness of the fugitive forged a tacit bond: Ginevra kept the secret and aided his escape. Outside, political turmoil loomed; inside, rivalries and friendships negotiated themselves around their art.
When Servin and Ginevra thought themselves alone, Servin admitted a young Imperial officer with his arm in a sling. He at first recoiled from Ginevra until Servin soothed him, introducing her as the daughter of the Emperor’s loyal friend, the Baron di Piombo; recognizing her patriotism, the soldier relaxed.
Ginevra noticed his wound; he dismissed it, but cries from the street—“Condemned to death!”—made him go pale at the name “Labedoyere” and collapse. He lamented that while Labedoyere faced death for their cause he had been hiding; stripped of his protector, he felt helpless and tormented by the thought that an honest life could not atone for traitors’ deeds. When he tried to leave, Ginevra stopped him and asked if he truly believed he could restore the Emperor. Moved by her calm conviction, he confessed he was ready to give his life.
Alarmed and compassionate, Ginevra offered her savings and observed that her father’s influence could excuse her aid; she urged patience, escape, and service elsewhere. Her maternal grace and hopeful insistence touched him—he called her “an angel of goodness,” though his thoughts kept returning to Labedoyere. A tacit understanding formed. When Servin asked if the officer could save Labedoyere, he replied he could only avenge him; Ginevra felt stirred by the thought of vengeance and by his devotion to Napoleon.
As the sun set over his face she felt a near-mystical connection and left smiling, newly hopeful. The next day the officer—now Luigi—recounted the retreats from Moscow and Waterloo; speaking Corsican and sharing memories deepened their intimacy as Ginevra listened with growing admiration.
Days passed. Her absence from gatherings sparked rumors: Mademoiselle Thirion withdrew, attendance dwindled, and Laure, urged away by her mother, left saddened. One evening Luigi signaled but retreated on seeing Laure; later, after others had gone, Ginevra confronted Servin, who dismissed the gossip. Luigi joked he was to blame and Servin playfully suggested marriage; their silent exchange acknowledged mutual love, and Ginevra offered to seek a pardon through her father.
Her parents grew anxious at her late returns; Bartolomeo paced until she came home, embracing her with relief. Amid hardship and scandal, Ginevra and Luigi’s love—forged by shared danger and ideals—became a sustaining strength that united them and embodied their cause’s resilience.
“Here she is, my Ginevra… mia Ginevra bella!” cried the old baron, embracing his daughter. Ginevra laughed and protested; the baroness revived, Piombo smoothed his hands with courtly ease, and the parents seemed renewed.
At dinner Piombo teased Ginevra about her hours in the studio and hinted at jealousy of painting’s hold on her. The mood changed when, eyes downcast, she confessed she loved a young man: not a prince but a poor comrade of Labedoyere, proscribed and hidden by Servin. Piombo, affronted, declared she should love only her father; Ginevra answered that love was not a matter of will.
Piombo insisted on her fidelity; she reminded him she had not reproached his devotion to Napoleon during long absences and said she wanted a husband to protect her. Accusations followed—his possessive pride versus her plea to choose—and she insisted she would not break her promise: she loved this man truly. The baroness tried to soften things, but Piombo could not accept obedience to what he called an unjust order.
The quarrel escalated: Piombo raged that if the man loved Ginevra he would kill him; if not, Piombo himself would. Ginevra maintained one cannot compare filial and romantic love and admitted the latter might be stronger. He thundered he would not allow the marriage; she calmly replied she would marry him. The baroness entreated peace. Shaken, Piombo asked to think. Ginevra implored him to love her for herself and promised never to abandon him. Their tenderness cooled his anger; he forgave, and for a time they played as in earlier days.
The following week Ginevra grew more affectionate, as if thanking her parents for an ungranted consent. She sang and hinted their nocturnes needed a man’s voice. At last the baroness signaled triumph: she had persuaded Piombo to receive the suitor. That same day Ginevra arrived home on Luigi’s arm.
Luigi, an officer who had reappeared after hiding, had been restored to the service through Ginevra’s appeals to the Duc de Feltre. Warned of Piombo’s possible disapproval, Luigi was anxious; his pallor deepened Ginevra’s love. Piombo sat cold and stern. Ginevra presented Luigi as a soldier who had fought beside the Emperor at Mont-Saint-Jean. Piombo noted he wore no decoration; Luigi said he no longer bore the Legion of Honor.
The baroness, seeking to bridge the moment, remarked that Luigi resembled someone they knew in Corsica—Nina Porta. Luigi answered that Nina had been his sister and, when pressed, confirmed he was Luigi Porta.
At that name Piombo reeled. He and the baroness left the room in stunned silence; Ginevra stood pale and speechless. The revelation plunged the household into dread. The baroness recalled that the Portas had killed Ginevra’s brother and burned their house; Piombo had then massacred the Portas in revenge. Luigi, bewildered, asked how he had survived. He explained that, bound to a bed before his house was set alight, he had been taken to Genoa at six and raised by an old man named Colonna, who warned him of enemies and advised he adopt the name Luigi to hide his identity. How he escaped death remained unclear.
Horrified at the vendetta revealed in a single word, Ginevra urged Luigi to stay for the moment—he was safe in her father’s house—but warned that once he left he would be hunted by Corsicans in Piombo’s service. Luigi, shaken, asked if the vendetta existed between them; she smiled sadly and nodded. Their love, she said, must be pure and resolute to endure what lay ahead. He squeezed her hand; they vowed their destiny was sealed. Ginevra escorted him to his modest lodging herself, determined rather than cowed.
Back home the atmosphere chilled. Piombo fumbled for words, rang for a servant, and ordered a fire kindled though his voice trembled. Both father and daughter nursed private torment their household could not bridge.
On Ginevra’s birthday, hoping to reconcile them, the baroness gathered father and daughter in Piombo’s study. At that moment two notaries arrived with witnesses to fulfill the law regarding Ginevra’s marriage to Luigi Porta. Piombo bristled, furious at what he saw as an assault on paternal authority, demanding whether French laws could tear a daughter from her father. When the notary continued calmly, Piombo suddenly seized a dagger from the wall and rushed toward Ginevra. Witnesses lunged to restrain him; seeing him advance, Ginevra knelt before her father and begged mercy. The sight unnerved him; he flung the dagger away and, struck by shame, pushed her aside.
Then Piombo pronounced his cruelest judgment: “Go. The wife of Luigi Porta cannot be a Piombo. I have no daughter. My Ginevra Piombo is buried here.” He pressed his hand to his heart and bade her leave his house. The baroness could not prevent him; he had renounced Ginevra publicly and irreversibly.
Ginevra fled to Luigi’s humble abode. She confessed the loss of parental blessing and their lack of fortune, but Luigi flung aside material concerns. “Then I am richer than kings!” he exclaimed, vowing to love her in her parents’ place. They resolved to face the long ordeal together. Thus remained the hard facts: Ginevra’s candid declaration of love; Piombo’s possessive claim, violent attempt, and final renunciation; Luigi’s return thanks to Ginevra; and the catastrophic discovery that Luigi was a Porta tied to the vendetta that had cost Ginevra’s brother and destroyed her family’s house, leaving her only Luigi as shelter.
The day after Ginevra was expelled from her father’s house she took refuge with Madame Servin until her marriage to Luigi. Madame Servin received her coldly—her husband blamed Ginevra—and the household’s selfishness left her proud but shaken. She rented a room near Luigi, who devoted himself to her and painted a hopeful future that buoyed her spirits.
One morning trunks arrived from her mother with a loving, sorrowful letter pleading that she reconsider. For an instant Ginevra thought of returning, but when Luigi appeared her resolve collapsed; she buried her grief for the sake of their love.
On the wedding day she was essentially alone; Luigi brought ordinary men as witnesses, and passing joyful processions only emphasized their contrast. In the mayor’s office her parents’ absence and protests made the formalities feel oppressive. An old priest’s remark stung, but a passing sergeant’s simple blessing comforted her.
They moved into a modest apartment Luigi called their true wedding, arranged with art supplies. Ginevra copied paintings and gained clients; Luigi did likewise and hired assistants. Evenings were shared, occasional regrets surfaced, but an anniversary portrait she painted symbolized a contented year together.
In early 1819 picture-dealers urged Ginevra to produce originals instead of copies as competition grew. Madame Porta regretted not turning sooner to genre painting; Ginevra tried portraiture but faced stiff rivalry. Despite modest savings, she and Luigi grew anxious.
That winter Luigi worked relentlessly in a crowded, low-paying writing market and could no longer afford help. Ginevra painted commendable pieces, but dealers shunned lesser-known artists and low prices brought few sales.
Their household became dire: love remained, but poverty overshadowed happiness. Each hid anxieties—Ginevra lavished affection while Luigi masked his fears. Finding him working late to avoid burdening her, she began coloring lithographs, laboring through the night while he toiled in his garret. At dawn they pretended to sleep, each shielding the other. Exhausted, Luigi later saw her lamp across the courtyard; confronted, she said she could not idle while he suffered. They watched the dawn together and felt tentative hope.
The struggle deepened when Ginevra gave birth to a son, Bartolomeo. Joy briefly masked hardship, but they sold possessions and jewels; poverty stripped comfort and pride. Months later frail Ginevra tended a sickly child while Luigi, desperate, sold paintings and furniture for a pittance. She often starved herself to feed him.
One evening she offered Luigi her last bread; he left, sold himself as a military substitute for an advance, and returned to find Bartolomeo cold. Ginevra, delirious, died in his arms. Her parents were shattered by the fatal news—another victim of the cycle of suffering.
################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
Some stories require a delicate, almost anatomical touch—portraits need a soul; certain truths emerge only through subtle harmonies of time, circumstance, or conscience.
That sensitivity is demanded here. This simple tale addresses grave, reflective readers who live on tender emotion. If the writer, like a surgeon at a dying friend’s side, reveres the subject, the reader should feel it. It speaks to those who have known private grief: the luxury of tears, the pangs of memory, regret, and smiles at past joys. Others will set the book aside; those who have never buried a beloved will not grasp these pages. The writer insists the tale is true and worth the reader’s feeling.
In contemporary France, speech varies by speaker: Parisians describe the same person in amusingly different ways. Madame Firmiani is sketched by many voices—a Practical man lists facts (house on the rue du Bac, well-furnished salons, an income of a hundred thousand francs, a husband once receiver-general); a Lounger praises her social standing and tea; an Egotist dismisses salons; a Student finds her enchanting; a Fop laments faded beauty yet admits charm. A Connoisseur praises her paintings; a Spiteful woman calls her a siren; an Attaché and old ladies recall ties to former beauty and the Cadignans. A Ninny wrongly says she sang at the Opera; jokes claim her salon is safe because its scoundrels are high-quality. Others allege affairs—Murat’s mistress—or coquetry that ruined a young man’s prospects.
Madame Firmiani thus appears through many lenses, each reflecting the observer as much as the observed and prompting reflection on deeper emotional truths.
In January 1824 opinions about Madame Firmiani were so contradictory—widow or wife, wise or foolish, virtuous or otherwise, rich or poor, beautiful or plain—that no clear portrait emerged; society warped her reputation like lithographic blocks producing different images from slight changes.
That winter Monsieur de Bourbonne, an elderly magnate from Touraine who adored his nephew Octave de Camps, grew uneasy. After Octave sold an estate without consulting him and a relative claimed Octave had squandered his fortune on Madame Firmiani and been reduced to teaching mathematics, the old man refused to believe the worst and went to Paris to investigate.
Adopting the name de Rouxellay and using faubourg Saint‑Germain connections, he arranged to meet Madame Firmiani while Octave was absent and soon confirmed Octave’s distress. De Rouxellay—no naive countryman, once in the King’s guard and versed in society—entered her salon with the Marquise de Listomère, professed pity, yet found himself captivated, revising his judgment.
Madame Firmiani, said to be twenty‑five though nearer twenty‑eight and married at sixteen, combined beauty, wealth, musical talent, intelligence, and wit, admired even by rivals and thus the target of persistent malicious gossip.
The former guardsman lingered after the guests left; Madame Firmiani found him still seated when the clock struck two. He announced himself as Monsieur Octave de Camps’s uncle and bluntly relayed rumors that his nephew had ruined himself for her while she enjoyed wealth. She sat again, moved, and answered calmly that she scorned money, had married without fortune, and welcomed Octave as a friend only if she esteemed him. She refused to be drawn into gossip, claiming a guardian angel kept her from dishonesty.
The old man, trembling, pleaded like a parent and asked one question only: did she love Octave? She would not confess to a third party: to any other man she would have answered with a look, but to him she asked what a woman who answered such a question would be thought of. “To confess love is the lover’s reward,” she said, and left the sentence unfinished, withdrew to her rooms, and declined to speak further. Monsieur de Bourbonne left puzzled, unsure whether she was sly or angelic.
Next morning he found Octave in his garret and scolded him for squandering the advantages of an uncle who meant to leave him an estate. Octave admitted he had “ruined” himself for Madame Firmiani and produced a letter she had given him. It began, in effect, “My beloved,” and explained why she was sad. She argued that a wife should be gay and confiding, and that a woman’s glory is in the man she loves; yet she had been humiliated on learning of Octave’s family. Knowing his father had unjustly deprived others of property made it impossible for her to respect him while he kept such gains. She urged him to consult his conscience: “If you come to me poor, my love shall be redoubled. If not, renounce it. I am your wife, not your mistress.” She did not command restitution; she appealed to his honor and begged him to perceive his faults himself.
Moved to tears, Octave implied more private confidences. The uncle, surprised by the gravity of the letter and that they considered themselves married, asked whether Octave had gone into teaching and whether he still had the uncle’s capital. Octave explained that the letter had sparked a revolution: conscience made every pleasure feel like theft. He had rushed to Madame Firmiani; together they calculated what ought to be paid to the Bourgneufs—the family his father had wronged. With limited means he condemned himself to pay three percent interest and to make restitution as far as he could. Madame Firmiani, prudent from past insecurity, offered her savings and he accepted them.
“Does that adorable woman actually save money?” the uncle cried, half mocking, half admiring. Octave asked him not to laugh. He then told the old man about Madame Firmiani’s past: her husband had gone to Greece in 1820 and died three years later; proof of his death and his will had been lost or stolen during the troubles there, so she had lived frugally to protect herself against possible loss of fortune. Octave had been trying to acquire property of his own—taking pupils rather than relying on others—to provide for her.
The act of restitution, he said, had brought him joys beyond millions. Finding the Bourgneufs in misery—an old agent father, daughters keeping books and house, a sick mother—he felt like Providence in relieving them. Making amends gave him a purity of feeling he had thought he lacked; he called Madame Firmiani “my dear conscience” and hoped to earn an honest fortune by industry. Touched, the old man exclaimed, “Ah, my boy, you have your mother’s soul.”
At that moment a carriage sounded and Madame Firmiani returned, a little annoyed to find Monsieur de Bourbonne still there yet smiling. She had come to entreat her husband’s acceptance of her fortune; the Austrian Embassy had just sent documents proving Monsieur Firmiani’s death and the will, which his valet had kept safe. Octave could accept it all—yet he was richer already by treasures of the heart “to which none but God can add.” She laid her head on his breast in unrestrained happiness.
Monsieur de Bourbonne concluded with an old-man’s reflection: “In my day we made love; in yours, you love. Women are all that is best in humanity; they are not even guilty of their faults, for they come through us.”
Middle of the story — midpoint of the narrative; "MIDDLE OF THE STORY" reiterated again now.
################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
The Rue du Tourniquet-Saint-Jean, a dark, winding lane near the Hôtel de Ville ending at the Rue Martroi, was closed by a turnstile until 1823. Less than six feet wide at its broadest, it lay perpetually muddy with pooled gutter water; sunlight touched it only briefly in summer and dampness lingered. Residents lit lamps at five in June and kept them burning through winter. Approaching the Marais felt like walking through cellars, and the corner house at Rue du Tourniquet and Rue de la Tixeranderie still bore iron rings from chains once used for public safety.
To combat humidity, the house raised its cellars above ground; an entrance reached by three steps formed a closed arch with a keystone carved as a female head. Three high, iron‑barred windows lit a small dwelling where two beds draped in green serge and an old woman tended a brazier, preparing a porter’s‑wife stew. The kitchen‑dining room was spare but kept with evident cleanliness and thrift.
The old woman’s pale, wrinkled face seemed part of the house; she sat by the fireplace with mischief mingled with resignation while her daughter, Caroline, worked at an embroidery frame. Modest and industrious, Caroline retained youthful charm despite poverty. One August day in 1815 the mother noticed a new face: a tall, thin man in black, solemn and suffering. She watched for him, and Caroline, unusually stirred, felt a sympathy that made her hope his arrival might alter her life.
For two months the “Black Gentleman” was erratic, no longer passing the Rue du Tourniquet at his usual hour. Madame Crochard noticed his distraction—often lost in thought and seldom acknowledging her or Caroline. One mid‑September morning Caroline’s liveliness caught his eye; their fleeting glances hinted at a silent understanding. Weeks later he resumed passing twice daily; their brief encounters became a guarded routine of wordless concern: Caroline sensed his troubles, he noticed her worries about work. Both remained cautious, fearing intimacy. Annoyed by her daughter’s indecision, Madame Crochard turned her displeasure toward the Gentleman, who had once won her mild approval. As winter brought harder times he overheard a revealing mother‑and‑daughter talk about finances and, moved, secretly left money. Caroline found it the next day; a silent gratitude passed between them. In early May Caroline proposed a trip to Montmorency, and the Gentleman—more somber than usual—joined. Madame Crochard pretended to sleep, letting them speak freely. The countryside and easy talk lifted his spirits; sharing family hardships and personal stories deepened their bond. The day brimmed with laughter, and as evening fell Caroline voiced her newfound happiness. Roger—by that name now—playfully held her hand before leaving, sealing an unspoken promise.
Their story continues in Paris. Caroline has spent a month in a charming second‑floor flat on Rue Taitbout, English‑styled with gray hangings trimmed in green silk, comfortable chairs, a writing table for love letters, a bronze clock of Love crowning Psyche, and a Gothic carpet. Preparing for Roger’s visit, her hairdresser flatters her. On a chilly September day in 1816, dressed in green grenadine trimmed with chinchilla, she watches from the balcony; after fifteen minutes impatience gives way to joy when he arrives and they embrace passionately. He must attend a meeting with the Attorney‑General, disappointing her; she objects to his theatre visits without her, declares her deep affection, and, perched on his lap, says she wishes to take his name—Caroline de Bellefeuille—and use her earnings to help the poor. She plays a sonata as he drives away, longing for his return.
Five years after settling in her Rue Taitbout home, Mademoiselle Caroline de Bellefeuille, now twenty-four, sits contentedly with her four-year-old son Charles, who plays with a rocking horse. She has embraced motherhood, devoted to raising her children and cultivating their sense of goodness and beauty, indifferent to society’s demands for luxury or status.
Rocking her infant daughter Eugénie, Caroline recalls joyful visits to Bellefeuille with Roger. Their love has deepened in the simplicity of shared life, yet she wonders why Roger insists on secrecy and what their true arrangement is.
On May 6, 1822—the anniversary of a pivotal day—Caroline prepares for dinner, expecting Roger. He brings a document detailing Eugénie’s marriage portion and explains his view that Charles, as a boy, must learn self-sufficiency while Eugénie deserves a fortune. Their evening is warm, ending with a lantern show for Charles, but as night deepens Roger must leave; he promises three days off, yet duty and secrecy leave Caroline with the familiar ache of separation.
Soon after, Caroline rushes to her mother, Madame Crochard, who is gravely ill. The widow’s acquaintances, unaware Caroline is her daughter, have summoned a priest for last rites amid neighbors scheming to secure the widow’s wealth. Caroline tends her mother, who laments their estrangement and reveals the priest forced a confession about Caroline’s benefactor, hinting a web of secrets.
The tale then backtracks to November 1805: a young barrister, Monsieur de Granville, precarious after leaving a High Chancellor’s gathering, receives a letter proposing marriage to Mademoiselle Angélique Bontems to secure his future despite the family’s stigma. In Bayeux he finds a household stifled by the widow’s piety; during a church service Angélique’s beauty revives a childhood affection, setting a reunion that will test love, duty, and social expectation.
For about two weeks after the Vicomte de Granville’s engagement to Mademoiselle Bontems, he frequented her dreary drawing-room and watched Angelique mark her trousseau. She never argued about religion; when he playfully handled her rosary or mocked practices she calmly returned it to its bag. Firm in the Church’s teachings, she insisted a mother must have a religion and asked how one could judge between disbelievers and God. Granville, taken by her conviction, sometimes mistook desire for love and was even tempted to adopt her beliefs. Angelique reconciled feeling with duty, leaving him uncertain about his own emotions.
At the signing of the marriage contract Madame Bontems exacted a promise from Granville to respect Angelique’s practices and allow her freedom of conscience; Angelique’s gentle look convinced him and Abbe Fontanon approved. After their wedding they moved to Paris, where Granville became public prosecutor. Angelique persuaded him to take a ground-floor apartment in the Marais near a church and chapel for the sake of future children. Granville, who preferred a more fashionable quarter, yielded. He installed his study and left furnishing to Angelique, who delighted in arranging the home. He was dismayed by the cold solemnity she created—a reflection of her austere upbringing. He tried to comfort her that happiness came from love and gentleness, not material things, yet grew frustrated by the lack of warmth and charm.
For a time the couple seemed harmonious; Granville stayed absorbed in work and ignored Angelique’s strict observances, passing off her Lenten austerities as singular zeal. He went to church with her on Sundays but did not grasp her fervor until a disruptive event. In November 1808 the Canon of Bayeux, Angelique’s spiritual director, arrived in Paris alarmed at her perceived moral decline and aimed to reclaim her from laxity. Angelique adopted stricter practices and frequently excused herself from social events, especially balls. After a tense evening Granville confronted her; she cited her director’s prohibitions and even wrote to the Pope for guidance.
The Pope’s reply sided with Granville, saying a wife should accompany her husband even at some personal cost. Rather than reconcile them, the decision deepened the divide: Angelique and her director dismissed it. Frustrated, Granville began asserting his preferences more forcibly—ordering meat daily despite her objections—and retreated from social life. Their home grew oppressive; Angelique’s austerity dimmed her beauty and replaced warmth with duty. Granville felt trapped in a marriage that left little room for affection. Their children, raised under strict rules, suffered: he sent his sons away to school early and planned to marry off his daughters as soon as possible. Increasingly isolated, he sought refuge in work and study, while Angelique’s efforts to reconvert him only worsened their estrangement. The household became a cold, rigid place rather than a home of companionship.
From 1806 to 1825 the household seemed calm, but Madame de Granville was miserable, attending Mass while he lodged in the entresol. He declined a provincial presidency; friends suspected infidelity. In Lent 1822 stress and fever ruined her health and piety chilled warmth. In May Abbé Fontanon revealed he lived seven years with a concubine, had two children and squandered their fortune. Shocked, but before she could act a quarrel with Mlle de Bellefeuille, blaming her, made her faint. Recovering, he accused her of valuing piety over his happiness, admitted the affair, refused restitution and declared the marriage over.
Early one December night in 1833 a white‑haired man whose face showed grief more than years paced the Rue Gaillon and stopped before a modest two‑story house with a dim attic light. A young doctor, Horace Bianchon, emerged; recognizing him, he offered his arm and bantered about the late hour and the slippery pavement. The visitor—Comte de Granville—answered with weary pride that at fifty‑five he was still hale and often walked home from the Courts or the club when not visiting in the evening.
Bianchon teased that a man of his station, with fine horses and money, was courting robbery. Granville shrugged off the danger with melancholy, confessing he had been spying on a shadow in the third‑floor garret of Bianchon’s house. He treated his curiosity as a trivial amusement, like watching the Palais‑Royal being finished. When Bianchon offered to tell him who lived there, Granville refused: he would not pay to learn whether the shadow was man or woman, happy or miserable. Nine years earlier he had stopped expecting human affection to endure. “I grew old within a few days,” he said. He had renounced attachment to people, clinging only to “unreasoning animals, or plants, or superficial things”—even the grace of Taglioni affected him more than human feeling. He professed to abhor life and insisted nothing could interest him.
Bianchon pressed him: did he have children? Granville replied bitterly, listing them: an eldest daughter, the Comtesse de Vandenesse; another daughter suitably married through the sister; a viscount son who had been public prosecutor at Limoges and now President of the Court at Orléans; and a younger son a public prosecutor in Paris. Each had their cares; none had devoted themselves to him. If one had tried to fill the void in his breast, that child would have ruined his life by sacrificing itself to him—and why should it? Perhaps they now waited for his money. Granville described his philanthropy as a form of self‑indulgence: he paid for sensations the way he gambled, seeking illusions that might make his heart glow while expecting no gratitude. He said life’s events had flowed over his heart like lava over Herculaneum—the town remained, dead. He declared he would see Bianchon die without blinking and asked Bianchon to feel the same toward him.
Unable to leave him in that mood, Bianchon insisted he could treat Granville’s malady and, reluctantly, described the garret’s occupant. His portrait was stark: a woman of about thirty whose love had become fanaticism. She adored a young man of pleasing appearance but every vice—Solvet—a gambler given to wine and women, guilty of acts the law might punish. This woman had abandoned a comfortable life and an adoring husband, the father of her children, for Solvet. She worked day and night; he had stolen from her even funds saved for the children’s food and clothes. Only three days earlier she had sold her hair—the finest Bianchon had ever seen—and when the lover returned she could not hide the gold quickly enough. For a smile or a kiss she would give away the price of a fortnight’s subsistence. The toil had hollowed her cheeks; the children’s cries had broken her heart; she lay ill on her wretched bed that night. When Bianchon visited, the family had nothing to eat; the children were so weak they could not even cry.
Despite himself Granville slid his hand toward his waistcoat pocket as if to help, but Bianchon begged him to pity the woman and to help cure her passion. Granville recoiled: how could he pity a misery whose joys he might have prized with his whole fortune? Did not thousands of dead men, thousands of sick and old men, long to barter everything for a return of youth and feeling? Bianchon murmured “Poor Caroline!” At the name Granville shuddered, gripped the doctor’s arm, and asked hoarsely whether the woman’s name was Caroline Crochard. When Bianchon confirmed it, Granville’s reaction was violent: he accused Bianchon of rousing the worst pain his heart could endure, an emotion he called a gift from hell. He vowed he knew how to pay such debts.
They had reached the Rue de la Chaussée d’Antin when a shriveled night scavenger stood by the curb. Granville asked if the scavenger ever found thousand‑franc notes and, hearing that sometimes he did, handed him such a note with a grotesque injunction: spend it in the wine shop, get drunk, start fights, beat your wife, blacken your friends’ eyes—give work to the watch, the surgeon, the druggist, perhaps to the police, public prosecutor, judge, and prison wardens. “Do not try to do anything else, or the devil will be revenged on you sooner or later,” Granville said. He pointed out the scavenger’s corrupt face to Bianchon and declared he had thus “squared accounts with hell” and had found a perverse pleasure in the money. As for Caroline Crochard, he pronounced coldly that she might die of hunger and thirst listening to the cries of her starving children, convinced of her lover’s baseness; he would not give a sou to rescue her. Because Bianchon had helped her, he said, he would see him no more.
Granville left Bianchon stunned and walked to the Rue Saint‑Lazare where a carriage waited. A servant informed him that his son, the attorney‑general, had been to see him an hour earlier and was waiting in his bedroom. Granville dismissed the servant, then confronted his son—in a tone at once imperious and unexpectedly tender—about coming after being ordered not to visit unless summoned.
Eugène, the younger man, spoke with tremor: that afternoon a very young man arrested in the house of a friend he had robbed had appealed to Granville, claiming to be his son. “His name?” Granville asked. “Charles Crochard,” Eugène answered. Granville waved him down: that sufficed. He stood a moment, then spoke as a father. “Charles Crochard spoke the truth. I am glad you came to me tonight, my good Eugène.” He handed his son a considerable bundle of banknotes and authorized him to use it as he saw fit in the affair, trusting him implicitly and approving whatever arrangements Eugène might make.
The mood shifted. Granville’s last words of parental counsel and farewell were almost tender. He warned Eugène about the weight of marriage—“that act is the most important society requires of us”—and urged him not to undertake it lightly, to study a woman’s character and to consult him. A lack of union between husband and wife led to terrible misfortunes and social punishment, he warned with an involuntary shudder. He promised to write on the subject from Florence. Then, with a quietness that suggested this might be their last parting, he asked his son to kiss him and announced his plan: tomorrow he would beg dismissal from the King and go to Italy. He wished to preserve his dignity as a father and as a former presiding officer of a supreme court of justice; he would not wish to blush before his son.
Thus the scene presents two images of Granville: in the street a man hardened into cruelty and misanthropy, cursing pity and spending money to foment petty ruin; in his bedroom a father handing cash and last counsels to his son, preparing to depart. Between those moments stands Bianchon’s account of Caroline Crochard—a portrait of sacrifice and degradation—and the Comte’s icy rejection of any duty toward her, even as the name awakens a torment proving he is not wholly dead to feeling. The narrative closes with Granville resolved to quit Paris and to cut off a friend who had awakened his pity, while privately acknowledging familial ties he attempts to leave behind with money and admonition.
################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
In late November 1809, at the height of Napoleon’s empire, Wagram still haunted Austria as peace talks continued. Kings and princes orbited Napoleon, who reveled in power and staged Parisian entertainments around his marriage to an Austrian archduchess. New opulence—diamonds and gold on women and uniforms—replaced the Republic’s earlier austerity; military leaders indulged in wealth drawn from soldiers’ sacrifices.
A Louis XV–style hedonism prevailed and seemed to foreshadow doom. The craze for military men served the Emperor: frequent calls to arms kept peace provisional, and women’s affections shifted so quickly many moved from mistress to widow in weeks. Soldiers attracted precisely because of risk, promising historical legacy and romantic courage; women imagined passions buried on the battlefield. The culture prized bravery and glitter—diamonds, fireworks, jeweled men like Murat.
Comte de Gondreville, once Citizen Malin, delayed a grand entertainment to outshine rivals and flatter Napoleon. His salons filled with ambassadors and princes expecting the Emperor, unaware a quarrel with Josephine kept him away, while Paris’s elite traded daughters and wealth for favor.
These remarks are necessary to explain the incidents surrounding the little imbroglio that is the subject of this study, reflecting the tone prevalent in Paris drawing-rooms at the time.
“Look toward the pedestal supporting that candelabrum—do you see the young lady with her hair à la Chinoise? She’s in the corner to the left, with bluebells in her chestnut curls. She’s so pale you might think she’s ill, delicate-looking, and very small. Now she’s turning her head; her almond-shaped blue eyes seem made for tears. She’s leaning forward to see Madame de Vaudremont below the crowd.”
“I see her now. You only had to mention her lovely complexion; I would have recognized her. She has the whitest skin here, but she’s either a prude or a coquette; her bodice barely hints at her beauty. What shoulders! What lily-whiteness!”
“Who is she?” asked the first speaker.
“I don’t know.”
“An aristocrat! Do you want to keep them all to yourself, Montcornet?”
“You of all people to tease me!” replied Montcornet, smiling. “Do you think you can insult a poor general like me just because you’re a rival of Soulanges? Or is it because I’ve only just arrived in this Promised Land? Come, Monsieur le Maître des Requêtes, let us glean in the field you can only hold precariously. We have a right to live! If you knew the German women, you might help me with the Parisian you love best.”
“Well, General, since you’ve noticed that lady, have you seen her dance?”
“Where have you been? There’s a triple rank of the most undaunted coquettes of Paris between her and the swarm of dancing men. It’s a miracle anyone could spot her in that corner, buried in gloom despite the candles. Between her and us is a sparkle of diamonds and glances, so many floating plumes and fluttering lace that it’s impossible for any dancer to detect her among those stars. How could you think she’s the wife of some sous-préfet from Lippe or Dyle?”
“Oh, he will be!” exclaimed the Master of Appeals.
“I doubt it,” laughed the Colonel of Cuirassiers. “She seems as raw in intrigue as you are in diplomacy. I bet you don’t know how she got there.”
The lawyer looked at the Colonel with a mix of contempt and curiosity.
“Well,” Montcornet continued, “she probably arrived punctually at nine, the first of the company, and embarrassed the Comtesse de Gondreville, who can’t think straight. Repulsed by the mistress of the house and driven into the corner by jealous ladies, she became a victim of their coalition against her. Each of those sweet faces would have conspired to keep the men from taking her out. That’s how those tender-looking women would have formed a coalition against the stranger without saying a word beyond, ‘Do you know that little woman in blue?’ If you care to run the gauntlet of flattering glances and inviting questions, try to get through the rampart defending that Queen of Dyle, Lippe, or Charente. You’ll see how even the dullest woman will invent a wile to keep the plaintive stranger in the shadows. Doesn’t she look like an elegy?”
“Do you think so, Montcornet? Then she must be married?”
“Why not a widow?”
“She would be less passive,” said the lawyer, laughing.
“She might be the widow of a gambler,” replied the handsome Colonel.
“Of course; since the peace, there are many widows of that sort!” said Martial. “But my dear Montcornet, we are simpletons. That face is too innocent; there’s too much youth and freshness for her to be married. What splendid flesh-tints! Nothing has sunk in the modeling of her nose. Her lips and chin are as fresh as a white rosebud, though her expression is veiled by clouds of sadness. Who could make that young creature weep?”
“Women cry for little,” said the Colonel.
“I don’t know,” replied Martial; “but she’s not crying because she’s left without a partner; her grief is not new. It’s clear she beautified herself for this evening with intention. I would wager she’s in love already.”
“Bah! She’s perhaps the daughter of some German princeling; no one talks to her,” said Montcornet.
“Dear! How unhappy a poor child may be! Can there be anything more graceful and refined than our little stranger? Yet not one of those furies around her, who think they can feel, will say a word to her. If she would only speak, we’d see if she has fine teeth.”
“Bless me, you boil over like milk at the least increase of temperature!” cried the Colonel, nettled at finding a rival in his friend.
“What!” exclaimed the lawyer, ignoring the Colonel’s question. “Can nobody here tell us the name of this exotic flower?”
“Some lady companion!” said Montcornet.
“A companion! Wearing sapphires fit for a queen and a dress of Malines lace? Tell that to the marines, General. You wouldn’t shine in diplomacy if you jump from a German princess to a lady companion.”
Montcornet stopped a man by taking his arm—a fat little man with iron-gray hair and clever eyes, mingling unceremoniously with various groups.
“Gondreville, my friend,” said Montcornet, “who is that charming little woman sitting under that huge candelabrum?”
“The candelabrum? Ravrio’s work; Isabey made the design.”
“Oh, I recognized your lavishness and taste; but the lady?”
“Ah! I don’t know. Some friend of my wife’s, no doubt.”
“Or your mistress, you old rascal.”
“No, on my honor. The Comtesse de Gondreville is the only one capable of inviting people no one knows.”
Despite this acerbic comment, the fat little man’s lips retained a smile from the Colonel’s suggestion. Montcornet returned to the lawyer, who had rejoined a neighboring group, intent on asking, but in vain, for information about the fair unknown. He grasped Martial’s arm and said in his ear:
“Mind what you’re about. Madame de Vaudremont has been watching you for some time with ominous attentiveness; she can guess by the movement of your lips what you say to me. Our eyes have already told her too much; she’s thinking even more than we are about the little blue lady.”
“That’s too old a trick in warfare, my dear Montcornet! However, what do I care? Like the Emperor, when I make a conquest, I keep it.”
“Martial, your fatuity cries out for a lesson. You, a civilian, and so lucky as to be the husband-designate of Madame de Vaudremont, a widow of two-and-twenty with four thousand napoleons a year—who slips such a diamond on your finger,” he added, taking the lawyer’s left hand, which the young man complacently allowed; “and you affect the Lovelace, as if you were a colonel obliged to maintain the military reputation! Fie! Just think of all you may lose.”
“At any rate, I shall not lose my liberty,” replied Martial with a forced laugh.
He cast a passionate glance at Madame de Vaudremont, who responded only with a smile of unease, having seen the Colonel examining the lawyer’s ring.
“Listen to me, Martial. If you flutter around my young stranger, I shall set to work to win Madame de Vaudremont.”
“You have my full permission, my dear Cuirassier, but you will not gain this much,” and the young Maître des Requêtes put his polished thumb-nail under an upper tooth with a mocking click.
“Remember that I am unmarried,” said the Colonel; “that my sword is my whole fortune; and that such a challenge is setting Tantalus down to a banquet he cannot devour.”
“Prrr.”
This defiant roll of consonants was the only reply to the Colonel’s declaration, as Martial looked him over before turning away.
The fashion of the time required men to wear white kerseymere breeches and silk stockings at a ball. This costume showcased Montcornet’s fine shape. At thirty-five, he attracted attention with his stalwart height, emphasized by the handsome uniform of the Cuirassiers of the Imperial Guard. A black moustache highlighted his soldierly countenance, with a broad forehead, aquiline nose, and bright red lips. Montcornet’s manner, marked by a certain superiority from his command experience, might please a woman sensible enough not to aim at making a slave of her husband. The Colonel smiled as he looked at the lawyer, one of his favorite college friends, whose small figure made it necessary for Montcornet to look down slightly as he answered his raillery with a friendly glance.
Baron Martial de la Roche-Hugon was a young Provençal favored by Napoleon; his fate might lead to a splendid embassy. He had won the Emperor with his Italian suppleness, a talent for intrigue, and a knowledge of manners, which are good substitutes for the higher qualities of a sterling man. Though young and eager, his face had acquired the rigid brilliancy of tinned iron, a characteristic of diplomatists that allows them to conceal emotions. The heart of a diplomat may be an insoluble problem, as the most illustrious ambassadors of the time were distinguished by enduring hatreds and romantic attachments.
Martial, however, was one of those men capable of reckoning on the future amid their enjoyment; he had learned to judge the world and hid his ambition under the guise of a lady-killer, cloaking his talent under the commonplace of mediocrity as he observed the rapid advancement of those who gave the master little umbrage.
The two friends parted with a cordial handshake. The introductory tune, signaling the ladies to form squares for a fresh quadrille, cleared the men from the space they had filled while talking in the large drawing-room of Gondreville’s mansion. Their whispered conversation had taken place during the usual interval between dances. The chandeliers and flambeaux on the chimney-shelf illuminated their faces, failing to conceal the faint expressions of their feelings from the keen-sighted countess or the artless stranger. This espionage of thoughts is perhaps one of the pleasures idle people find in society, while many disappointed individuals are bored without daring to admit it.
To fully appreciate the interest of this conversation, it’s necessary to relate an incident that would serve as an invisible bond, drawing together the actors in this little drama, currently scattered through the rooms.
At about eleven o’clock, just as the dancers were returning to their seats, the company observed the entrance of the handsomest woman in Paris, the queen of fashion, the only person missing from the brilliant assembly. She made it a rule never to appear until a party reached a pitch of excitement that doesn’t allow women to preserve the freshness of their faces or dresses. This brief hour is the springtime of a ball. An hour later, when pleasure falls flat and fatigue sets in, everything is spoiled. Madame de Vaudremont never committed the blunder of remaining at a party to be seen with drooping flowers, hair out of curl, tumbled frills, and a face courting sleep—not always without success. She took care not to let her beauty be seen drowsy, as her rivals did; she cleverly maintained her reputation for smartness by always leaving a ballroom in brilliant order, as she had entered it. Women whispered with envy that she planned and wore as many different dresses as parties she attended in one evening.
On this occasion, Madame de Vaudremont was not destined to leave when she wished. Pausing on the threshold, she shot swift, observant glances at the women present, hastily scrutinizing their dresses to assure herself that her own eclipsed them all.
The illustrious beauty presented herself to the admiration of the crowd at the same moment as one of the bravest colonels of the Guards’ Artillery and the Emperor’s favorite, the Comte de Soulanges. The transient association of these two had an air of mystery. Upon hearing the names announced, a few women sitting by the wall rose, and men hurried in from side-rooms, pressing forward to the principal doorway. One of the jesters in the assembly remarked that “women have as much curiosity about seeing a man faithful to his passion as men do in studying a woman difficult to enthrall.”
Though the Comte de Soulanges, a young man of about thirty-two, possessed a nervous temperament that gives rise to fine qualities, his slender build and pale complexion were not initially attractive. His black eyes revealed great vivacity, but he was taciturn in company, and nothing in his appearance hinted at the oratory that would later distinguish him in the legislative assembly under the Restoration.
The Comtesse de Vaudremont, a tall woman, somewhat plump, with dazzlingly white skin and a small head carried well, inspired love through her gracious manner. She was one of those beings who keep all the promise of their beauty.
The pair, for a few minutes, became the center of general observation, but did not long give curiosity a chance to exercise itself. The Colonel and the Countess seemed to understand that accident had placed them in an awkward position. Martial, as they approached, hastened to join a group of men by the fireplace, watching Madame de Vaudremont with the jealous anxiety of a first flame of passion from behind a barrier of heads. A secret voice warned him that the success he prided himself on might be precarious. However, the coldly polite smile with which the Countess thanked Monsieur de Soulanges and her little bow of dismissal as she sat down by Madame de Gondreville relaxed the rigidity of his face, which jealousy had made tense. Seeing Soulanges still standing near the sofa where Madame de Vaudremont sat, apparently unaware of the glance that conveyed to him that they were both playing a ridiculous part, the volcanic Provençal knitted his brows and smoothed his chestnut curls to maintain his composure, watching the faces of the Countess and M. de Soulanges while still chatting with his neighbors. He then took the hand of Colonel Montcornet, who had just renewed their acquaintance, but he listened without hearing him; his mind was elsewhere.
Soulanges gazed calmly at the women, sitting four ranks deep around the immense ballroom, admiring the display of diamonds, rubies, gold, and shining hair, which almost outshone the blaze of waxlights, the cut glass of the chandeliers, and the gilding. His rival’s indifference unsettled the lawyer. Unable to control his impatience, Martial approached Madame de Vaudremont with a bow. Upon seeing the Provençal, Soulanges cast him a covert glance and impertinently turned away his head. A solemn silence reigned in the room, where curiosity peaked. Eager faces wore strange mixed expressions; everyone anticipated an outbreak that men of breeding carefully avoid. Suddenly, the Count’s pale face turned as red as the scarlet facings of his coat, and he fixed his gaze on the floor to hide his agitation. Spotting the unknown lady humbly seated by the pedestal of the candelabrum, he moved away with a melancholy air, passing in front of the lawyer, and took refuge in one of the cardrooms. Martial and the company thought Soulanges had publicly surrendered his position out of fear of the ridicule that follows a discarded lover. The lawyer proudly raised his head and looked at the strange lady; then, as he took his seat near Madame de Vaudremont, he listened so inattentively that he missed her words spoken behind her fan:
“Martial, please don’t wear that ring you took from me. I have my reasons and will explain them when we leave. You must give me your arm to go to the Princess de Wagram’s.”
“Why did you come in with the Colonel?” asked the Baron.
“I met him in the hall,” she replied. “But leave me now; everybody is looking at us.”
Martial returned to the Colonel of Cuirassiers. It was then that the little blue lady became the object of curiosity that agitated the Colonel, Soulanges, Martial, and Madame de Vaudremont.
When the friends parted after their challenge, the Baron flew to Madame de Vaudremont and led her to a place in the most brilliant quadrille. Favored by the intoxication dancing produces in a woman and the turmoil of a ball, where men appear in all the trickery of dress, which enhances their attractions as much as it does women’s, Martial thought he might yield to the charm that drew his gaze to the fair stranger. Though he managed to hide his first glances towards the lady in blue from the Countess’s watchful eyes, he was soon caught in the act; and while he excused himself once for his absent-mindedness, he could not justify the unseemly silence that followed the most insinuating question a woman can ask:
“Do you like me very much this evening?”
The more dreamy he became, the more the Countess pressed and teased him.
While Martial danced, the Colonel moved from group to group, seeking information about the unknown lady. After exhausting the good humor of even the most indifferent, he resolved to take advantage of a moment when the Comtesse de Gondreville seemed at liberty to ask her the name of the mysterious lady. He noticed a little space cleared between the pedestal of the candelabrum and the two sofas, which ended in that corner. The dance had left several chairs vacant, forming rows of fortifications held by mothers or middle-aged women; the Colonel seized the opportunity to make his way through this palisade of shawls and wraps. He began by making himself agreeable to the dowagers, and from one to another, through compliments, he finally reached the empty space next to the stranger. At the risk of catching on to the gryphons and chimeras of the huge candelabrum, he stood there, braving the glare and dripping wax, to Martial’s annoyance.
The Colonel, too tactful to speak suddenly to the little blue lady on his right, began by addressing a plain woman seated on the left:
“This is a splendid ball, madame! What luxury! What life! Every woman here is pretty! You’re not dancing—because you don’t care for it, no doubt.”
This vapid conversation aimed to induce his right-hand neighbor to speak; however, she, silent and absent-minded, paid no attention. The officer had prepared several phrases leading to: “And you, madame?”—a question from which he hoped for great things. But he was surprised to see tears in the strange lady’s eyes, which seemed wholly absorbed in gazing at Madame de Vaudremont.
“You are married, no doubt, madame?” he asked hesitantly.
“Yes, monsieur,” replied the lady.
“And your husband is here, of course?”
“Yes, monsieur.”
“And why, madame, do you remain in this spot? Is it to attract attention?”
The mournful lady smiled sadly.
“Allow me the honor, madame, of being your partner in the next quadrille, and I will ensure not to bring you back here. When so many are ready to ascend the throne, and Royalty is the mania of the day, I cannot imagine you will refuse the title of Queen of the Ball that your beauty may claim.”
“I do not intend to dance, monsieur.”
The curt tone of her replies was so discouraging that the Colonel found himself compelled to withdraw. Martial, guessing what the officer’s last request had been and the refusal he had met with, began to smile, stroking his chin and making the diamond on his finger sparkle.
“What are you laughing at?” asked the Comtesse de Vaudremont.
“At the poor Colonel’s failure, who just put his foot in it—”
“I begged you to take your ring off,” said the Countess, interrupting him.
“I did not hear you.”
“If you can hear nothing this evening, at least you see everything, Monsieur le Baron,” said Madame de Vaudremont, vexed.
“That young man is displaying a very fine diamond,” the stranger remarked to the Colonel.
“Splendid,” he replied. “The man is the Baron Martial de la Roche-Hugon, one of my most intimate friends.”
“I thank you for telling me his name,” she said; “he seems an agreeable man.”
“Yes, but he is rather fickle.”
