“He seems to be on the best terms with the Comtesse de Vaudremont?” asked the lady, looking inquiringly at the Colonel.
“On the very best.”
The unknown turned pale.
“Hallo!” thought the soldier, “she is in love with that lucky devil Martial.”
“I fancied Madame de Vaudremont had long been devoted to M. de Soulanges,” said the lady, recovering from the suppressed grief that had clouded her beauty.
“For a week past, the Countess has been faithless,” replied the Colonel. “But you must have seen poor Soulanges when he came in; he’s still trying to disbelieve in his disaster.”
“Yes, I saw him,” said the lady. Then she added, “Thank you very much, monsieur,” in a tone that signified dismissal.
At that moment, the quadrille was coming to an end. Montcornet had only time to withdraw, telling himself, “She is married.”
“Well, valiant Cuirassier,” exclaimed the Baron, drawing the Colonel aside into a window-bay to breathe the fresh air from the garden, “how are you getting on?”
“She is a married woman, my dear fellow.”
“What does that matter?”
“Oh, deuce take it! I’m a decent sort of man,” replied the Colonel. “I have no intention of courting a woman I cannot marry. Besides, Martial, she expressly told me she did not intend to dance.”
“Colonel, I will bet a hundred napoleons to your gray horse that she will dance with me this evening.”
“Done!” said the Colonel, putting his hand in the coxcomb’s. “Meanwhile, I’m going to look for Soulanges; he might know the lady, as she seems interested in him.”
“You’ve lost, my good fellow,” cried Martial, laughing. “My eyes have met hers, and I know what they mean. My dear friend, you owe me no grudge for dancing with her after she has refused you?”
“No, no. Those who laugh last, laugh longest. But I’m an honest gambler and a generous enemy, Martial, and I warn you, she is fond of diamonds.”
With these words, the friends parted; General Montcornet made his way to the cardroom, where he saw the Comte de Soulanges sitting at a bouillotte table. Though there was no friendship between the two soldiers beyond the superficial comradeship from the perils of war and service, the Colonel of Cuirassiers was alarmed to see the Colonel of Artillery, known to be prudent, playing a game that could lead to ruin. The heaps of gold and notes piled on the fateful cards showed the frenzy of play. A circle of silent men stood around the players at the table. Occasionally, a few words were spoken—pass, play, I stop, a thousand Louis, taken—but the five motionless men seemed to converse only with their eyes. As the Colonel, alarmed by Soulanges’ pallor, approached him, the Count was winning. Field-Marshal the Duc d’Isemberg, Keller, and a famous banker rose from the table, completely cleaned out of considerable sums. Soulanges looked gloomier than ever as he swept up a quantity of gold and notes; he didn’t even count it; his lips curled with bitter scorn, defying fortune rather than being grateful for her favors.
“Courage,” said the Colonel. “Courage, Soulanges!” He tried to pull him from the gaming table. “Come with me. I have good news, but on one condition.”
“What is that?” asked Soulanges.
“That you will answer a question.”
The Comte de Soulanges rose, gathering his winnings; his look was so fierce no one stopped him; the players seemed relieved at his departure.
“Those soldiers are always as thick as thieves at a fair!” observed a diplomat who took Soulanges’ place. A single tired face turned to the newcomer and said, “When we say military men, we do not mean civil, Monsieur le Ministre.”
“My dear fellow,” Montcornet said to Soulanges, drawing him aside, “the Emperor praised you this morning, and your promotion to field-marshal is certain.”
“The Master does not love the Artillery.”
“No, but he adores the nobility, and you are an aristocrat. He said that men who married in Paris during the campaign are not in disgrace. Well?”
Soulanges looked confused.
“And now I hope you will tell me if you know a charming woman sitting under that huge candelabrum—”
At this the Count’s face brightened; he seized the Colonel’s hand. “My dear General,” he said, his voice altered, “if anyone else had asked me that, I would have cracked his skull with this gold. Leave me, I beg you. I feel more like blowing out my brains tonight than— I hate everything I see. I am going. This gaiety, this music, these stupid faces, all laughing, are killing me!”
“My poor friend!” Montcornet replied, squeezing the Count’s hand gently. “You are too vehement. What would you say if I told you that Martial is thinking so little of Madame de Vaudremont that he is quite smitten with that little lady?”
“If he speaks to her,” Soulanges shouted, trembling with rage, “I will thrash him flat, even if he’s in the Emperor’s lap!”
He sank into an easy chair; seeing Soulanges beyond consolation, Montcornet withdrew.
Back in the ballroom Montcornet noticed Madame de Vaudremont, agitated. He sat beside her.
“I wager something vexes you?” he said.
“A mere trifle, General. I want to leave for a ball at the Grand Duchess of Berg’s, but I must first stop at the Princesse de Wagram’s. Monsieur de la Roche-Hugon amuses himself by flirting with the dowagers.”
“That’s not the whole secret. I bet a hundred louis you will stay here all evening.”
“Impertinent man!”
“You are afraid of seeing Martial at the feet—”
“Of whom?” she exclaimed, feigning surprise.
“Of that candelabrum,” he replied, glancing at the fair stranger.
“You have guessed it,” she said, hiding her face behind her fan. “Old Madame de Lansac, as malicious as an old monkey, told me that Monsieur de la Roche-Hugon flirts with that stranger, who sits like a skeleton at a feast. I would rather see a death’s head than that face—so cruelly beautiful and pale. She is my evil genius. Madame de Lansac, who only comes to a ball to watch everything while pretending to sleep, has made me miserable. Martial shall pay dearly for this trick. Urge him, since he is your friend, not to make me unhappy.”
“I just left a man who promises to blow his brains out if he speaks to that lady. And he is a man, madame, to keep his word. But I know Martial; such threats encourage him. Besides, we have wagered—” The Colonel lowered his voice.
“Can it be true?” asked the Countess.
“On my word of honor.”
“Thank you, my dear Colonel,” Madame de Vaudremont replied, her glance inviting. “Will you do me the honor of dancing with me?”
“Yes; but the next quadrille. During this one, I want to find out what will come of this little intrigue and ascertain who the little blue lady is; she looks intelligent.”
Pleased to have made a start, the Colonel retired.
At such entertainments, older women like Madame de Lansac gather to watch younger people’s affairs. The Duchess of Lansac, a shrewd, mischievous dowager from an earlier generation, read hearts as if reading a book: a faint frown, a quiver, the slightest curl of the lips revealed impressions. From her large armchair she observed the young coquette who disguised annoyance as cheerfulness; Madame de Vaudremont felt as much sorrow as she feigned. She had believed Martial a man of talent who could adorn her life with power; now she realized her mistake. Her sudden passion for Martial, though recent, caused acute pain, like the amputation of a healthy limb.
Seizing the opportunity to interfere, the old Duchess dismissed her Ambassador and turned all her attention to the couple. Her sardonic glance frightened the Countess, who suspected the dowager’s hand in the stranger’s presence. Madame de Lansac, tall and tastefully dressed with diamonds and rouge, had eyes that promised epigrams; her politeness softened a mischievous mind. Her triumphant glance across the room brought hope to the pale young woman under the chandelier; the alliance between the Duchess and the stranger did not escape Madame de Vaudremont’s practiced eye, and she was determined to uncover the mystery.
The Baron de la Roche-Hugon, having questioned the dowagers without learning the blue lady’s name, turned in despair to the Comtesse de Gondreville, who said the lady had been introduced by the “ancient” Duchesse de Lansac. By chance the Baron caught the intelligence in the Duchess’s look aimed at the stranger and, despite their previous coldness, approached. The Duchess, smiling with sardonic irony, looked at Madame de Vaudremont; Montcornet laughed.
“If the old witch pretends to be friendly,” thought the Baron, “she is certainly going to play me a spiteful trick.” He addressed her: “Madame, you have, I am told, undertaken the charge of a very precious treasure.”
“Do you take me for a dragon?” said the old lady. “But of whom are you speaking?” she asked sweetly, reviving Martial’s hopes.
“Of that little lady, unknown to all, whom the jealousy of these coquettes has imprisoned in that corner. You, no doubt, know her family?”
“Yes,” said the Duchess. “But what concern have you with a provincial heiress, married some time ago, a woman of good birth, who goes nowhere.”
“Why does she not dance? She is such a pretty creature. May we conclude a treaty of peace? If you tell me all I want to know, I promise to urge a petition for the restitution of the woods of Navarreins by the Commissioners of Crown Lands on the Emperor.”
The Baron’s suggestion to petition for an estate belonging to the elder branch of Navarreins—an appeal to the Duchess’s past influence—was trickery revealing his ambition. The younger branch of Navarreins used the arms of Lansac; the duchess’s past favors had once secured her husband a title. The Duchess, with deceptive gravity, answered: “Monsieur, bring the Comtesse de Vaudremont to me. I promise to reveal the mystery of the interesting unknown. You see, every man in the room shares your curiosity. All eyes are turned to the corner where my protégée has modestly seated herself; she reaps the homage the women wished to deprive her of. Happy the man she chooses for her partner!” Fixing her eyes on Madame de Vaudremont she added, with a look that plainly said, “We are talking of you. I imagine you would rather learn the stranger’s name from your handsome Countess than from me.”
There was such marked defiance in the Duchess’s attitude that Madame de Vaudremont rose, approached her, and took the chair Martial had placed for her. Without acknowledging him she said, “I can guess, madame, that you are talking of me; but I admit my lack of perspicacity; I do not know whether it is for good or evil.”
Madame de Lansac pressed the young woman’s hand in her dry fingers and replied in a low, compassionate tone, “Poor child!”
The women looked at each other. Madame de Vaudremont understood that Martial was in the way and dismissed him with an imperious “Leave us.” The Baron, displeased to see the Countess under the spell of the dangerous sibyl, gave one of those decisive looks men use—potent over a blinded heart but ridiculous to a woman beginning to judge the man who attracted her.
Martial, a man of the world with wit and acumen, would not risk breaking with a woman favored at Court whom the Emperor wished to see married. He counted on jealousy as the surest means of discovering the secret of the Countess’s coolness and withdrew willingly as a new quadrille began.
The Baron leaned against a marble console, watching the two ladies talk. Comparing the Countess with the new beauty, made attractive by mystery, he fell prey to the self-interest common to adventurous lady-killers, hesitating between a fortune within reach and indulging his caprice. The light outlined his anxious face against white silk hangings, making him resemble an evil genius; observers might have said, “There is another poor wretch who seems to be enjoying himself!”
Meanwhile the Colonel, leaning in the doorway between ballroom and cardroom, laughed under his moustache. He enjoyed watching the dance’s turmoil, reading the Countess’s and Martial’s agitation, and wondering about the connection between Soulanges’ gloomy looks and the fair unknown’s face, which alternated between hope and dread. Montcornet, like a king of the feast, received inviting smiles; as Colonel of the Imperial Guard he was a fine match in the army.
It was nearly midnight. Conversation, gambling, dancing, flirtations, rivalries, and scheming reached a fervor that made a young man exclaim, “A fine ball!”
“My sweet little angel,” Madame de Lansac said to the Countess, “you are now at an age when I made many mistakes. Seeing you endure a thousand deaths, I thought I might give you some charitable advice. To go wrong at two-and-twenty means spoiling your future; it is like tearing the gown you must wear. My dear, it is not much later that we learn to go about in it without crumpling it. Go on, sweetheart, making clever enemies and friends without sense of conduct, and you will see what a pleasant life you will lead!”
“Oh, madame, it is very hard for a woman to be happy, do you not think?” the Countess eagerly exclaimed.
“My child, at your age, you must learn to choose between pleasure and happiness. You want to marry Martial, who is not wise enough to be a good husband, nor passionate enough to remain a lover. He is in debt, my dear; he is the man to squander your fortune; still, that would be nothing if he could make you happy. Do you not see how aged he is? He must have been ill; he is making the most of what is left him. In three years he will be a wreck. Then he will be ambitious; perhaps he may succeed. I do not think so. What is he? A man of intrigue, who may have business acumen and be able to gossip agreeably, but he is too presumptuous to have any real merit; he will not go far. Besides—look at him. Is it not written on his brow that, at this very moment, what he sees in you is not a young and pretty woman, but the two million francs you possess? He does not love you, my dear; he is calculating you as if you were an investment. If you are bent on marrying, find an older man with an assured position who is halfway in his career. A widow’s marriage should not be a trivial love affair. Is a mouse to be caught a second time in the same trap? A new alliance should be a good speculation on your part, and in marrying again, you ought at least to hope to be addressed as Madame la Maréchale!”
Her advice combined worldly calculation with pity: prudence, rank, and financial security over a romantic match with Martial, whom she painted as indebted, aging, and opportunistic. The Countess listened, torn between feeling and practical counsel. The salon’s intrigues continued around them—wagers, furtive glances, jealous men and calculating women—while the mystery of the pale stranger and the designs of the Duchess, the Colonel, the Baron, and Martial revolved like the dance itself, each player seeking advantage among love, ambition, and social maneuvering.
As she spoke, both women glanced toward Colonel Montcornet’s handsome face.
The Duchess bluntly rebuked the Countess: if she preferred flirting to marriage, she must know how to stir and then calm trouble, but she must not wreck families or destroy wives’ happiness. The Duchess admitted she had played that dangerous game and learned too late that fleeting vanity can ruin virtuous women and leave one mortally hated. “One salmon is worth a thousand frogs,” she quoted the Duc d’Albe: genuine affection brings far more happiness than transient passions. She had come to preach to the Countess and to explain why she visited this modest house; they had once received actors in their boudoir, never in the drawing-room. If the Countess must toy with men, the Duchess urged, play only with those whose lives are unsettled and who have no duties—others will never forgive you for errors that displace their happiness.
To make her point she pointed to Soulanges. For fifteen months the Count had been a lovesick fool, neglecting his devoted wife of two years; the wife lived in tears. The Count’s remorse outweighed any pleasure the Countess might have given him, and the Countess had abandoned him. “Come and see the result,” the Duchess said.
She led the Countess and Madame de Vaudremont toward a pale, trembling figure by candlelight: the Comtesse de Soulanges, finally persuaded to leave her sorrowful room where her child offered little consolation. The Countess looked away, imagining how the woman’s blighted face must once have glowed with happiness. Scanning the crowd, the Duchess found Soulanges himself: pale, rigid in an easy chair, his indifference and the tension in his brow revealing deep suffering. Players passed him as if he were dead. The tearful wife and the morose husband, separated amid festivity, struck the Countess with foreboding about her own future. Though not wholly hardened, she pressed the Duchess’s hand with a childlike gratitude.
The Duchess whispered, “Remember, we can repel a man’s attentions as easily as we attract them.” Then, nodding to Montcornet, she told Madame de Vaudremont, “She is yours if you’re not a fool.” The Countess smiled.
Alone, Martial asked the Countess if she knew the name of the unknown woman. She played coy; Martial, still believing she loved him, adopted a charming air and teased about caring for that name. She warned him not to approach the young lady; it could be dangerous—her husband would kill any rival. Martial mocked the inconsistency: the Colonel could desert a man he had taken from another woman, yet would fight for his wife. He begged to dance with her, convinced her icy heart could not love the Colonel. She replied that the woman loved her husband; Martial found that a delicious obstacle to conquer. She remarked that they were punished for both faults and for repentance. He begged forgiveness and promised to think no more of Madame de Soulanges; she half-wished she could send him to the woman as a lesson. He laughed, vowed to return more devoted, and teased Montcornet about winning the Countess’s favor. The Colonel smiled and joined them. The Baron, believing he had piqued the Countess and harmed Montcornet, sardonic, gave Martial a seat near her—unaware that both men were drawn toward one another.
At that moment the lawyer approached Madame de Soulanges, who sat pale and timid, alive only in her eyes. Her husband entered the ballroom in a flash of anger. The Duchess, watching, rushed to her nephew, asking for his arm to find her carriage and feigning boredom to prevent a scene. Before leaving she shot a signal at her niece, prompting a vengeful play.
Madame de Vaudremont noticed the exchange and suddenly realized the duplicitous Duchess—preaching morality—might be plotting. Pride, more than curiosity, stirred her to unravel the intrigue; in her distraction she lost control, which the Colonel misread as encouragement and took as license to grow more ardent. Old diplomats, amused by the unfolding drama, perhaps had never seen so many simultaneous intrigues; the passions of the two couples were visible in the crowded rooms and the faces that reflected and amplified their impotence.
Finally the Baron sat beside Madame de Soulanges and admired her beauty up close—the fresh neck, the graceful figure emphasized by the fashionable sash—and was captivated. She replied coldly that she rarely attended parties. Martial, adjusting the diamond on his hand, caught the Countess’s eye and felt a spark of hope. She blushed and, when he asked to be her partner for the next quadrille, hesitated only until Martial identified the tall cavalry colonel she had declined; then she acquiesced, her tone revealing a new, deep emotion that stirred Martial’s world-weary heart. Overcome with shyness, he regained his southern charm. Standing beside her in the quadrille eased his nerves; for men like Martial, dancing expressed what words could not—a bid to win a woman’s heart by grace and presence.
He led his conquest to the quadrille, where the most brilliant women prized dancing. As the orchestra played, Martial surveyed the ladies forming the square with pride. Madame de Soulanges’ dress briefly diverted attention from Madame de Vaudremont’s; her blush added to her charm. When she looked at her partner she seemed to transfer that glory to him, mixing innocence, vanity, and the first stirrings of affection. Modest and inexperienced, she nevertheless knew how to glance.
A new figure by a dancer named Trenis brought Martial face to face with the Colonel, who quipped, “I have won your horse.” Montcornet retorted, “Yes, but you have lost eighty thousand francs a year!” Martial shrugged: “What do I care? Madame de Soulanges is worth millions!”
After the quadrille the room buzzed. Less attractive women moralized about Martial and the Comtesse; prettier ones admired her apparent ease. The men, puzzled by Martial’s luck, did not find him especially handsome. Some sympathetic women warned that a single glance or graceful dance could compromise a young wife. Only Martial truly grasped his happiness. During the last figure, as the ladies formed the moulinet, he clasped the Countess’s hand and felt her response through the thin glove. As the dance ended he urged her not to hide in a corner but to enjoy the scene and herself.
Yielding to his charm, Madame de Soulanges drifted with him through the crowded rooms, hesitating at each doorway until he reassured her, “He is not here.” They reached a grand picture gallery where a lavish collation served three hundred guests. As supper neared, Martial led her to an oval boudoir overlooking the garden, draped in flowers and blue hangings. The noise faded; the Countess, startled, resisted at first, then sat on an ottoman after a look in the mirror that seemed to reassure her they might be seen.
“This room is charming,” she murmured. Martial watched her shifting face—unease, modesty, eagerness—and was captivated. Calming herself, she flirtatiously took his left hand and slipped the ring from it, admiring the diamond. Martial, intoxicated by her touch, urged her to wear it as a memento and an emblem of love. She asked if he’d ever take it back; he swore he would not. She set the ring on her finger, then abruptly rose and said, “I accept the diamond, monsieur, with less scruple because it belongs to me.”
She explained that Monsieur de Soulanges had taken it from her dressing-table and told her he had lost it. Martial protested that it had been given to him by Madame de Vaudremont. “Precisely,” she replied with a smile. “My husband borrowed it, gave it to her, and she gifted it to you. This ring has made a little journey.” Touching a secret spring, she revealed a lock of M. de Soulanges’ hair. Stunned, she fled into the crowded rooms, leaving Martial confounded.
Nearby the Colonel and Madame de Vaudremont laughed; the Colonel teased, “Will you have my horse to chase after your prize?” Martial took their banter in stride, relieved they kept silent about the evening’s intimacies. He fancied he had exchanged his horse for a young wife.
On the drive home to the Faubourg Saint‑Germain, the Comtesse was consumed by anxiety. Before leaving the hotel she had failed to find her aunt and husband, who had left without her. She now feared having been complicit in her aunt’s scheme: she had witnessed her husband’s torment at Madame de Vaudremont’s hands and had hoped for his repentance, but consenting to the plot felt like a mistake. The evening darkened her innocent spirit; alarm at her husband’s suffering gave way to distress at her rival’s beauty and the corruption she saw around her.
Crossing the Pont Royal, she cast away the lock from the diamond and wept, recalling old grief and thinking that a woman seeking peace must bury her pain. “What can women do when they do not love? What sustains their devotion? I cannot believe, as my aunt claims, that reason alone is enough.”
Arriving home, she found her husband by the fire. He reproached her for attending balls without him; she soothed him, explaining her aunt had taken her and that she had been happy. His sternness, born of fear she might have discovered his infidelity, softened. Relieved and enchanted by her kindness, Soulanges took her hand and kissed it. “Hortense, what is that on your finger that hurt my lip?” he asked, laughing. “It’s my diamond, which you said you lost, and which I have found.”
General Montcornet did not marry Madame de Vaudremont despite their brief understanding; she later perished in the tragic fire that marred the ball given by the Austrian ambassador for Napoleon’s marriage.
################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
In 1835 Clémentine married Polish exile Comte Adam Laginski; settlements offset her father's losses. Facing Parisian prejudice, buying a house gave him bourgeois standing. She noticed Paz, the factotum. Adam said Paz was of poor origin and had fought for Poland; he refused a gift, showing pride and loyalty. She grew fond of both, suggesting ties transcended norms.
Thaddeus, long reclusive in Paris, became visible when Comtesse Clémentine Laginska was seen riding with him in the Bois de Boulogne. Invited to dine with her household, he dressed formally but behaved like a subordinate—reserved, answering little, deferring to Clémentine. Adam urged him to be livelier, unaware that Thaddeus’s coolness hid a deep, unrequited love intensified after Clémentine’s return from a trip. He took a bittersweet pleasure in being indispensable to their household, knowing they would falter without him even as his devotion caused him pain.
Clémentine, newly confident and absorbed in her Paris position, left little room for him. On an impulsive drive home she invited him along, a moment that flustered him and revealed charged, unspoken emotion: gratitude and longing met by deliberate distance. Routine resumed; he found solace in managing affairs and in silent worship, admiring small signs of her happiness while feeling the torment of his love. At a reception his absence went unnoticed; Clémentine, preoccupied with rivals and status, increasingly neglected Adam, while Thaddeus remained a steadfast, quiet overseer privately wrestling with his feelings.
In May, preparing to visit her uncle’s estate, she saw Thaddeus again and insisted he join them for dinner. He hesitated but engaged; their talk hinted at a deeper connection, yet loyalty to Adam restrained him. When she confessed a desire for passion he nearly replied in kind but held back, inventing a romance with Malaga, a circus performer. Visiting Malaga became an escape—he provided for her without love. Her despairing letter longing for him finally pierced him, underscoring the constancy and sacrifice of his devotion to Clémentine.
Clementine returned home more beautiful than ever. After dinner the count and countess discussed Thaddeus, who had left a letter from Malaga with Adam. Adam regretted Thaddeus’s misfortune, fearing that association with a circus performer would harm his standing; Clementine scorned such women, while Adam defended Thaddeus for the financial sacrifices he had made for Malaga.
Paz, who had asked Adam for money to help Malaga, was seen by the countess as a threat to Adam’s stability. Adam suspected Thaddeus might abandon Malaga if it suited him; the countess doubted it.
Paz noticed Clementine’s changed manner and felt her contempt, sinking into despair. Still he took quiet pleasure in admiring her at gatherings and devoted himself to her comfort and the household. His love was so deep he fell ill from anguish before recovering.
After Adam lost heavily gambling, he asked Thaddeus to cover the debt by blaming Malaga. Thaddeus reluctantly complied, burdened by obligations. When Clementine learned of his generosity to Paz she complained about his loyalty. Overwhelmed by expectations, Thaddeus resolved to teach her household management so she could be independent.
In spring Adam fell gravely ill. Thaddeus nursed him devotedly while Clementine sought the best medical care. Adam recovered and praised Thaddeus. Tormented by unrequited love and sure that staying in Paris would only prolong his pain, Thaddeus left for the Caucasus after writing Clementine a heartfelt letter confessing his sacrifices. Clementine and Adam never fully grasped his devotion; years later she wondered about his fate, a bittersweet, unfulfilled memory.
################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
The Marquise de Listomere embodies the Restoration ideal: sincere religious devotion paired with worldly elegance. Seven years into a marriage with a bland but politically useful marquis, she preserves a virtuous façade to support his ambitions; acquaintances reserve judgment until he attains the peerage. Her respectable, sometimes scornful reserve rebuffs flirtations, sustains guarded conversation, and signals principle rather than coquetry.
The narrator admires her: not classically beautiful but possessing a captivating presence—animated eyes and effortless charm. This charm surfaces in her encounter with Eugène de Rastignac, a clever, moderately vivacious young man. At a ball they talk of opera and women’s duties; he abruptly leaves. The next morning Rastignac, distracted and writing letters, imagines pleasures until his friend Horace plans an expedition. Meanwhile the Marquise reads one of Rastignac’s affectionate letters; initially intrigued, she grows offended by its impertinence and implications, a clash between outward virtue and a stirred feeling. She forbids her porter to admit Rastignac and burns the letter; Caroline, the housemaid, comments on the fine love‑letter. That evening she attends the Marquis de Beauseant’s party expecting Rastignac; by two a.m. she realizes he will not come—Stendhal calls this hardening of resentment “crystallization.”
Four days later, when Rastignac scolds his valet Joseph for misplacing letters, he learns one was for the Marquise and another for a lawyer. Aware his affections have wandered, he laughs; his trouble began when he tried to amuse her with the blunder and worsened by failing to visit. When he finally calls the marquis, unaware, admits him. The meeting is awkward: the Marquise blushes, gives guarded answers, promises to forget while remaining dignified; mention of the letter provokes feigned offense and hidden amusement, and the name Madame de Nucingen hardens her until she rings for wood to signal his departure. Embarrassed, he leaves. Days later she confirms the letter’s true recipient and withdraws from society; the marquis blames a stomach ailment, her physician calls it nervousness. The episode exposes social misunderstandings, intense but constrained feeling, and rigid romantic roles.
################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
In Paris, balls often split into a formal round of polite small talk and a later, intimate salon where genuine conversation and wit flourish—hosted by women like Madame d’Espard and Mademoiselle des Touches. At one such evening, guests including the Minister de Marsay shared lively discourse.
De Marsay told of a youthful, passionate affair with a woman six years older who outwardly seemed indifferent but privately signaled affection with the flowers she carried. When illness made him cancel a meeting, jealousy drove him there anyway and he found her with another man. Her calm tenderness briefly soothed him, but the discovery ignited a storm: his innocence felt ended, he wrestled with thoughts of revenge, and concluded that true love must rise above vengeance—prompting reflection on love, jealousy, social expectation, and fragile trust.
Freed from naïveté, the narrator began a new affair with a charming heroine whose practiced beauty contrasted with his earlier deceptive angel. He avoided quarrels, suspecting refined hypocrisy could masquerade as virtue and fearing genuine love might not match the pleasures of her practiced deceit. Visiting a hair artisan in the Rue Boucher to commission a keepsake, he saw astonishing hair mementos and learned of passing trends. Suspicion flared when he recognized a handkerchief the artisan had worked on, confirming doubts about the authenticity of women’s affections. He left resolved to pursue pleasure but skeptical of love.
Two months later in the heroine’s boudoir he tested her. Charlotte professed exclusive devotion and claimed he was the only man in her world, but when he bluntly asked, “When are you to marry the Duke?” her startled reaction betrayed guilt. He suggested she should accept the Duke—rich, devout, proper. Kneeling, she swore before God she loved only him; he pressed her, revealing he'd seen her attend mass with the Duke. She alternately supplicated and raged, lamenting that had he spoken sooner they might have been happy. He insisted it was too late—that she had gone too far.
She feared the Duke’s suspicions; he counseled her to show devotion while he feigned the distressed lover until she asked his fate. Then he admitted making advances to another woman, discomfiting Charlotte, who accused him of unfaithfulness. He retorted that taking love seriously in their society was like romanticizing an actress. When she could not shake bitterness even as a potential duchess, he felt he had successfully distanced himself—having learned how successful men withdraw.
Back in the salon, the women sympathized with Charlotte while de Marsay smiled. Conversation turned to the erosion of social distinctions: Lady Dudley observed that women often did not view marriages as inconstancy; de Marsay lamented the aristocracy’s decline—duchesses and marquises fading as the Civil Code leveled hierarchies and created a new class. The Comte de Vandenesse and Lord Dudley noted anyone could claim nobility under the new order; unsettled property hastened the fall. Joseph Bridau lamented a decline in womanly beauty; Daniel d’Arthez argued society lost women who might have shaped it.
They debated the modern “perfect lady”: tasteful, graceful, poised but lacking the grandeur of past great ladies. Mademoiselle des Touches questioned whether this was progress; Madame de Nucingen argued a woman’s worth now lay more in character and presentation than lineage. Émile Blondet defined the perfect lady as effortlessly elegant and refined, commanding respect by bearing and demeanor. The dialogue captured modern femininity’s complexity—how charm, intellect, and presentation now often replace inherited splendor.
Blondet begins with a caustic portrait of the modern “perfect lady”: married yet effectively concealing her husband, she performs delight rather than possession. At a ball a decorated middle-aged man bows and departs; she orders her carriage and leaves, leaving an impression of brightness instead of conjugal solidarity. At home she keeps strict hours, receives no one before afternoon, and stages her life—warm staircases, fresh flowers (accepted only from favored suitors as tokens), tasteful comforts, and ever-renewed trifles. She sits by the fire and lets visitors come to her; in public she dazzles, in private she calculates. Her salon becomes a tiny world where conversation clings to safe generalities because women are isolated and hungry for a sphere in which to shine.
Blondet contends that a woman’s intelligence in this milieu often shows as cultivated doubt: uncertainty becomes a social grace. The straightforward townswoman is openly virtuous; the lady doubts and negotiates virtue, debates religion without practicing it, and invokes moral rhetoric when it suits social aims. Her speech becomes a tangle of modern political and religious catchphrases—neo-Christian, neither fully Catholic nor Protestant—moralistic and self-contradictory. Women laugh at Blondet’s satire, but he persists: the “perfect lady” feigns ignorance to extract confidences and arranges her passions like a musical score—careful pauses, modulations, anonymous compromises—because exposure threatens ruin. Name, rank, and wealth no longer guarantee safety; the old aristocratic grandeur that let great ladies dominate has faded. The modern woman must master subtleties and Jesuitical compromises, living between public propriety and private liberties. She is seldom independent except in secrecy or theory; her chief task is to preserve her status.
Blondet imagines a repudiated wife deprived of carriage, opera box, and social role: neither wife, maid, nor townswoman—adrift and vulnerable to calumny, with religious orders rejecting her as scandalous. Thus the modern lady may provoke gossip but seldom outright slander because her cautious arrangements offer more protection than frankness ever did. Blondet judges the contemporary blend of English hypocrisy and eighteenth-century frankness a degraded transitional system: present manners resemble nothing that has truly vanished. Great figures are receding; distinctions have become merely personal. He doubts that any woman, even of high birth, can before twenty-five acquire the encyclopedic refinements—subtlety of manner, wit, innocence, mischief, silence, speech, diplomacy, and artful balance—that made the perfect lady of the past.
Mademoiselle des Touches asks where the female author fits. Blondet replies with a stinging quip—an ungenial female author is “a woman of whom there should not be one,” an echo of Napoleon. Canalis interrupts to defend Napoleon against the epigram, recognizing his paradoxical genius: a phenomenal will who created empires, laws, public works, and literature, yet remained capricious, contradictory, and humanly weak. Canalis portrays Napoleon as brute force coupled with subtle intellect—able to found codes and crowns, to seize Europe, yet unable to secure lasting civilization—and admires the extraordinary, if inconsistent, greatness.
De Marsay calls him “all despotism and all justice” at the right moments, “the true king.” Joseph Bridau observes that witty conversation is a rare, fleeting luxury—each flash unique and unrepeatable, like music one could not afford if sold by the note. The Princesse de Cadignan challenges Blondet’s caricature: fashions and small forms do not mean the human heart has shrunk. Passions and sublime devotions persist, though less publicly staged. The lack of a grand public theater does not diminish depth. Contemporary women may rival historical figures—Madame Récamier beside Mme. de Sévigné; Marquise d’Espard with Madame Doublet or du Deffand; Taglioni and Malibran with Camargo and Saint-Huberti; modern poets matching or exceeding eighteenth-century ones. The Empire had its own splendor. Comte de Vandenesse amplifies this defense, praising Frenchwomen’s enduring significance and asserting that Blondet’s “perfect lady” can, when necessary, become heroic. Providence sometimes directs events where feelings cross legal bounds, and Vandenesse has seen women rise to greatness beyond their faults.
This praise provokes convivial banter and then leads to General Montriveau’s grim story, which reveals the brutal realities behind honor, passion, and conscience. He recounts an episode during the 1812 retreat near the Beresina, when discipline collapsed and survivors sought shelter. Montriveau, cold and starving, found a poor Polish farmhouse—one room piled with hay and a small meal laid out. Among the officers gathered there was a colonel who dominates the tale: Italian by temperament, tall, powerful, dark-haired, alternately tender-eyed and ferociously irascible, able to inspire both fear and devotion. His passions erupted in swift violence: when another colonel’s battery struck their captain and badly wounded him, Montriveau watched the Italian colonel lose control—foaming, roaring, and in two seconds split the other man’s skull with his sword. The soldiers were stunned.
Present also was the wounded captain’s wife, Rosina, a striking but ragged Sicilian. Her husband—a phlegmatic Piedmontese—had seemed oblivious to scandal, but Montriveau sensed tension. At the farmhouse the colonel summoned Rosina with brusque possessiveness; she responded with a gesture of humiliation or foreboding. When he summoned her again more brutally, she rose and went to him amid awkward silence; Montriveau’s nervous laugh drew a rebuke from the husband. They slept that night and resumed the dreadful march next morning. A short distance on, they saw the farmhouse in flames, barricaded, the air full of smoke and cries. The captain returned to the caravan and, with a pointed gesture to his breast and a wave toward the burning house, said “Son’io” (I am the one). He walked on; no one pressed him. Montriveau’s account leaves the precise deed implied but unstated—an act of vengeance or desperate protest—illustrating how easily dignity is crushed and how humiliation can provoke savage reactions. De Marsay comments bitterly that “there is nothing more terrible than the revolt of a sheep,” an image that haunts the company.
Conversation lightens with laughter at English foibles and then closes on a softer note from Doctor Bianchon. He recounts a moving bedside scene: a duchess dying of consumption, thin and luminous, having taken the sacrament. The Duke—an immense public figure, tired and complex—had fallen asleep at her bedside; when he awoke about dawn, despairing at his lapse and fearing he had missed her last moments, the dying duchess, in a tenderly altered voice, took his hand and said, “My poor dear, who is left to understand you now?” She died looking at him. Bianchon calls her end one of the most beautiful he has seen; the Duke’s sorrow and the serenity of the death reveal a private, sacramental tenderness that outlasts display.
The assembled company reflects on these vignettes: Blondet’s satire, Canalis’s defense of Napoleon, the Princesse’s praise of women, Montriveau’s wartime violence, and Bianchon’s bedside charity together sketch a society where appearances, passions, public roles, and private heroics interweave. Women, constrained by manners and rank, still have depth and capacity for sublimity; men of genius display paradoxes of grandeur and triviality; sudden violence and rare tenderness punctuate public life. The dialogues and episodes portray contemporary Parisian life—its hypocrisies, private loyalties, sudden cruelties, and occasional nobility.

######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
“Ah! madame,” said the doctor, “I have some appalling stories, but each has its proper hour in conversation—you know Chamfort’s joke to the Duc de Fronsac: ‘Between your sally and the present moment lie ten bottles of champagne.’”
“But it is two in the morning, and the story of Rosina has prepared us,” the hostess replied.
“Tell us, Monsieur Bianchon!” they all cried.
The doctor bowed and began.
About a hundred paces from Vendôme, on the bank of the Loir, stands an old brown house with very high roofs, solitary and utterly neglected—no tannery or tavern near it. A garden slopes to the river where once-clipped box shrubs now run wild; willows rooted in the stream form a half-hidden fence. Weeds have taken the bank; fruit trees, neglected for years, no longer bear, their suckers forming a thicket; espaliers look like a little copse. The gravel paths are overgrown—indeed there is no trace of a path. From the hilltop where the ruins of the Dukes of Vendôme cling, one can see that this spot must once have been the pride of a country gentleman devoted to horticulture. An arbor’s wreck and a partially standing table testify to that bygone care. To complete the mournful air, one wall bears a sundial with the motto “Ultimam cogita” (think on your last).
The house itself is dreadfully dilapidated: the roof leaking and warped, shutters always closed, swallows nesting on balconies, doors shut. Grasses green the flagstones of the steps; ironwork is rusty; moon, sun, winter, and snow have eaten into the wood, peeling paint and warping boards. The silence is broken only by birds, cats, polecats, rats, and mice; to the observer an invisible hand has inscribed one single word over it all: Mystery.
From the street you see a large gate with a round-arched top, full of children's holes; the gate, as the doctor later learned, had been blocked for ten years. Through breaches you view the courtyard: the same ruinous state as the garden side—paving-stones choked with weeds, cracked walls, blackened coping laced with pellitory, disjointed stone steps, a rotten bell-cord, broken gutter-spouts. What fire from heaven, what decree, has struck this dwelling? Reptiles crawl over it but give no answer. The empty, deserted house is a vast enigma.
It was formerly a small fiefed estate called La Grande Bretèche. During the doctor’s stay in Vendôme—where he had been left to attend a rich patient—this odd dwelling became one of his keenest pleasures. Better than a ruin, it carried a secret, an unrevealed thought: the slow destruction of a standing house seemed like an avenging hand at work. Many evenings he climbed the wild hedge surrounding the enclosure, braved scratches to enter the ownerless garden, and lingered for hours in its disorder. He would weave romances and indulge in melancholia there, relishing the unwritten poetry of ignorance; had he known the reason for the neglect, the charm would have been gone. To him the place represented various phases of human misfortune: sometimes a graveyard without the dead, sometimes a lepers’ home, sometimes the house of the Atridae—but above all provincial life, contemplative and like an hour-glass. He often wept there; he never laughed.
Occasionally he felt involuntary terrors at the dull hum of a passing wood-pigeon overhead. The damp earth required watching for lizards, vipers, and frogs; a cold would seem to settle on your shoulders like the Commendatore’s hand on Don Giovanni’s neck. One evening a weathercock turned in the wind, its creaking like a cry from the house just as the doctor was finishing a gloomy drama to account for this enormous air of woe. He returned to his inn in a pensive mood. After supper, the hostess came into his room with a mysterious air to announce, “Monsieur, here is Monsieur Regnault.”
“Who is Monsieur Regnault?” the doctor asked.
“What, sir, do you not know Monsieur Regnault?—Well, that’s odd,” she said and left.
A tall, slim man in black appeared: hat in hand, receding forehead, small pointed head, colorless face like dirty water—a sort of usher. His coat was old, worn at the seams, but he wore a diamond in his shirt frill and gold rings in his ears.
“Monsieur,” the doctor asked, “whom have I the honor of addressing?”
The man sat by the fire, rubbed his hands and answered, “Dear me, it is very cold. I am Monsieur Regnault.”
“My curiosity is piqued,” the doctor said.
“I am notary at Vendôme,” Regnault replied. He quickly informed the doctor that the latter’s habit of walking in La Grande Bretèche’s garden constituted a misdemeanor since the estate was enclosed property, and as executor under the will of the late Comtesse de Merret he begged the doctor to desist. He insisted he meant no offense and would be glad to allow free access if he were not bound by the testatrix’s instructions: he had never set foot in the house since the will was read. They had done nothing there but verify doors and windows to assess taxes; administering Madame de Merret’s estate had been the most important event of his life. His account was long and voluble, but the doctor pressed him for the reason that La Grande Bretèche was left to ruin.
Regnault delighted in relating his own history and the tale of Madame de Merret. He had been head-clerk in a Paris office that went bankrupt; unable to buy a practice in Paris in 1816, he came to Vendôme, bought his predecessor’s business, and married into local relations. Three months after being licensed, just before his marriage, he was sent for to the Chateau of Merret by Madame la Comtesse. The Count had gone to Paris and died two months previously after falling into dissipation. On the day he left, Madame la Comtesse had quitted La Grande Bretèche, having dismantled it; some said she had burned the furniture and hangings, perhaps in the Merret meadow. Regnault had been summoned: the Countess was very ill and seemed unlikely to survive the night, having received the Sacrament. The maid who met him at the door was now a servant in the inn.
When Regnault reached the chateau about eleven, he climbed the great staircase, crossed large, lofty, dark rooms—cold and damp—and reached the state bedroom where the Countess lay. Rumors had painted her as a coquette; reality confounded that image. The enormous, old-fashioned room, papered with brown and filled with dust, had only an old Argand lamp for light. The bed was antique, the tester hung with flowered chintz; on a small table lay an Imitation of Christ and the lamp; a deep armchair for the maid and two small chairs formed the whole furnishing; there was no fire. If you had seen that vast room you would have felt transported into a romance; it was icy, funereal.
Madame de Merret lay propped up, waxen and narrow-faced. Her hair, abundant but white as linen, was shown by a lace cap; her large black eyes, dimmed by fever, hardly moved beneath bony brows. Her forehead was clammy; her fleshless hands looked like bones covered with soft skin; veins and muscles showed—she must have once been striking, but disease had consumed her to a phantom. Her lips, pale-violet, scarcely moved when she spoke. Though Regnault’s work had exposed him to many deaths and last wishes, this solitary figure in her vast chateau was the most affecting: no sound, almost no breath, yet an immense presence. When her eyes moved, she tried to raise a hand, then, with difficulty, drew from under her pillow a sealed paper. “I place my will in your hands—Oh! God! Oh!” she uttered, then clutched a crucifix, kissed it, and died. Regnault remembered a joy stamped on her last glance, an expression that made him shudder, and he took away the will: she had appointed him her executor.
The will proved extraordinary. She left most of her property to the Vendôme hospital, with a few legacies. As for La Grande Bretèche, she ordered that, for fifty years from her death, it should remain precisely in the state it was at her death: nobody, whoever he might be, should enter the apartments, no repairs were to be made, and watchmen were even to be paid if necessary to secure this injunction. At the expiration of the term, if the will had been dutifully executed, the house would become the executor’s heirs’ property; otherwise it would revert to the heirs-at-law under terms set in a codicil not to be opened for fifty years. The will had not been contested. Regnault’s account thrilled him and he enjoyed the triumph of such a legal contrivance.
The doctor, vivid imaginings spinning into a gothic romance, pressed Regnault for the specifics of the Countess’s eccentric injunctions, but the notary affected comical reticence. Eventually he regaled the doctor further with town gossip—diffuse, contradictory opinions that nearly put the doctor to sleep. Regnault’s monotone and self-satisfaction were too much for curiosity, and the notary took his leave, teasingly suggesting that many would wish to live fifty years longer, and hinting cryptically about age.
After Regnault left, the doctor, still stirred, sat in his armchair when his landlady, Madame Lepas, entered. A buxom, florid Flemish woman with the keen scrutiny of an innkeeper, she had overheard the notary’s history. The doctor repeated the story of Madame de Merret, and Madame Lepas leaned close, an instinctive mix of constable, spy, and dealer in her look.
When asked about Monsieur de Merret, she described him as extremely tall, a good gentleman from Picardy, taciturn and hot-tempered but generous in paying his debts, and well-liked by the common folk despite his haughty manner. He and Madame de Merret had once been a handsome couple; she was pretty, sweet, and wealthy—about twenty thousand francs a year. They were a splendid match and their wedding had filled the town. Were they happy? “So-so,” Madame Lepas said—the common folk were not intimate with their life. Madame de Merret was kind and pleasant but must sometimes have endured her husband’s temper; being of noble birth, his ways passed as his place required.
“But there must have been some catastrophe for Monsieur and Madame de Merret to part so violently?” the doctor pressed.
“I did not say there was any catastrophe,” she replied. Yet she then confided what she knew. During the Emperor’s time, when Spaniards and prisoners of war were sent here, she was required to lodge a young Spaniard sent to Vendôme on parole. Despite the parole, he had to present himself daily to the sub-prefect. He was a Spanish grandee with a fanciful name—Bagos de Férédia or something alike—which she had entered in her books. For a Spaniard he was notably handsome though short—no more than five feet two or three—but well made, with small, fastidious hands; he kept his hands, she said, as carefully as a woman keeps brushes. He had thick black hair, a flame in his eye, a somewhat coppery complexion the hostess admired, and the finest linen she had ever seen—finer than anything even princesses had worn at her inn, despite having housed personages like General Bertrand, the Duc and Duchesse d’Abrantes, Monsieur Descazes, and the King of Spain. The Spaniard ate little but had such polite, amiable ways that he was impossible to dislike; he spoke scarcely at all, answering rarely if spoken to—a manner the hostess guessed might be a national affectation.
He was devout: he read his breviary like a priest and went to mass regularly. Most notably, he always took his station within two yards of Madame de Merret’s chapel. At his first attendance there no one suspected intent; he never raised his eyes above his book, poor young man. In the evenings he walked the hill among the ruins of the old castle—a pastime that must have reminded him of Spain, which, they said, is all hills.
Thus the scene closes on the picture of La Grande Bretèche, the Countess’s strange last will that sealed the house for half a century, and the shadow of a Spanish exile whose daily position near the Countess’s chapel and his solitary walks among the ruins suggest a link between foreign passion and domestic catastrophe—an implication the doctor, Regnault, and Madame Lepas allow the listener to supply, while the dilapidated house stands mute and enigmatic by the Loir.
One evening soon after his arrival he stayed out late, and I grew uneasy when he did not return until midnight. He lived in a house we owned on Rue des Casernes. One of our stable-boys thought he had seen the Spanish grandee swimming in the river. When he did come back I warned him about the weeds; he seemed annoyed at being seen.
Eventually one morning we discovered he had not come back at all. Searching his things I found a note in his drawer, fifty doubloons—about five thousand francs—and a sealed box of diamonds worth ten thousand francs. The note said that if he did not return he left the money and diamonds to fund masses in gratitude for his escape.
My husband went searching and returned with the Spaniard’s clothes, found under a stone on a breakwater near la Grande Bretèche. After reading the letter my husband burned the clothes and, keeping Count Férédia’s wishes, we announced that he had escaped. The sub-prefect sent the constabulary after him, but he was never caught. Lepas thought the Spaniard had drowned. I suspected otherwise, believing him implicated in Madame de Merret’s affair; Rosalie had told me the crucifix her mistress cherished and was buried with was ebony and silver, and early in his stay Monsieur Férédia had owned a similar crucifix that I never saw again. “Should I feel remorse about the Spaniard’s fifteen thousand francs?” my hostess asked. “Aren’t they truly mine?”
“Certainly. But have you ever questioned Rosalie?” I asked.
“Oh, I have, sir. But what can be done? That girl is like a wall. She knows something, but it’s impossible to make her talk.”
After that brief chat la Grande Bretèche occupied my imagination: its overgrown grass, shuttered windows, deserted rooms. I felt compelled to explore the house and uncover the story that had claimed three lives.
Rosalie became the most intriguing person in Vendôme. Observing her, I sensed something inward—sorrow or hope—beneath her blooming health; she seemed to keep a secret. Yet she was simple and clumsy; her vacant smile showed no malice. I resolved not to leave Vendôme without knowing la Grande Bretèche’s full story, and decided to worm it out of Rosalie if necessary.
“Rosalie!” I called one evening.
“Your servant, sir?”
“You’re not married?” She flinched.
“Oh! There’s no shortage of men if I ever fancy being miserable!” she laughed, then composed herself.
“Yes; you’re attractive enough to never lack suitors. But tell me, Rosalie, why did you become an inn-servant after leaving Madame de Merret? Didn’t she leave you a small annuity?”
“Oh yes, sir. But my position here is the best in all Vendôme.”
Her answer seemed evasive. Rosalie appeared bound to the affair’s core; she contained the last chapter of a romance. From that moment my attention fixed on her. She was neat and attractive; soon she embodied every charm. A fortnight after the notary’s visit, one morning I said:
“Come, tell me all you know about Madame de Merret.”
“Oh!” she replied, “I will tell you, but keep it a secret.”
“Of course, my child; I will guard your secrets.”
“If it’s all the same to you,” she said, “I’d prefer it be with your own.”
She adjusted her head-kerchief and settled in to tell the tale; she had a diffuse eloquence that would fill pages, but I will summarize her account, which lay between the notary’s gossip and Madame Lepas’s version.
Madame de Merret’s bedroom at la Grande Bretèche was on the ground floor; a cupboard set into the wall, about four feet deep, served as her wardrobe. Three months before the evening in question Madame de Merret had been seriously ill; her husband had taken to sleeping on the first floor. One night he returned late from the club, delayed by discussions and losses at billiards. He had previously relied on Rosalie to tell him if his wife was in bed; that evening he decided to check on her himself. He entered thinking he heard a closet door shut; Madame de Merret stood alone by the fireplace, looking anxious.
“You’re very late,” she said huskily.
Monsieur de Merret remained silent as Rosalie entered, which startled him. He began pacing, arms crossed.
“Have you received bad news, or are you unwell?” his wife asked timidly while Rosalie helped her undress. He did not answer.
“You can go, Rosalie,” Madame de Merret said; “I can manage my curl-papers myself.” She wanted to be alone with her husband. Once Rosalie left—or lingered in the passage—Monsieur de Merret confronted his wife, saying coldly, “Madame, there’s someone in your cupboard!” She calmly replied, “No, monsieur.”
That “No” pierced him; he did not believe it, yet his wife appeared more saintly than ever. He moved to open the closet door, but Madame de Merret took his hand and, with strange emotion, said, “Remember, if you find no one there, everything must end between us.”
Her extraordinary dignity filled him with deep respect and inspired a resolve like a legend. “No, Josephine,” he said, “I won’t open it. Regardless of the outcome, we would be forever parted. I know your soul’s purity; you wouldn’t commit a deadly sin to save your life.” She looked haggard.
“Here is your crucifix,” he continued. “Swear to me before God that there’s no one in that closet; I will believe you—I will never open that door.”
Madame de Merret took the crucifix and swore, “I swear it.”
“Louder,” he insisted; “repeat: ‘I swear before God that there is nobody in that closet.’” She repeated the words without flinching.
“That will do,” said Monsieur de Merret coldly. After a moment, observing the ebony-and-silver crucifix, he remarked, “You have a fine piece of work there, which I’ve never seen before.”
“I found it at Duvivier’s; last year, when that troop of Spanish prisoners passed through Vendôme, he bought it from a Spanish monk.”
“Indeed,” he said, hanging the crucifix back on its nail, and he rang for Rosalie.
He waited for her to arrive. “Go to bed, all of you,” he instructed, beckoning Jean, his coachman and confidential servant, to come close. He whispered to Jean: “When they’re all asleep—mind, asleep—you understand?—come down and tell me.”
Monsieur de Merret kept his gaze on his wife while giving orders, returning to her at the fireside to talk of billiards and the club. When Rosalie returned she found them conversing amiably.
Not long before, Monsieur de Merret had had new ceilings installed in all the ground-floor reception rooms. Plaster was scarce in Vendôme; he had ordered a large quantity, knowing he could sell any excess. This circumstance inspired his plan.
“Gorenflot is here, sir,” Rosalie whispered.
“Tell him to come in,” he replied aloud.
Madame de Merret paled at the sight of the mason. “Gorenflot,” said her husband, “go fetch some bricks from the coach-house; bring enough to wall up the cupboard door; you can use the leftover plaster as cement.” Then, pulling Rosalie and the mason close, he whispered: “Listen, Gorenflot, you’ll sleep here tonight; tomorrow morning I’ll provide you a passport to take you abroad to a place I’ll specify. I’ll give you six thousand francs for your journey. You must live in that town for ten years; if you don’t like it, you may settle elsewhere, but it must be in the same country. Go through Paris and wait there until I join you. I’ll give you an agreement for six thousand francs more, to be paid upon your return, provided you fulfill the conditions of the bargain. In return, you must keep silent about what you do tonight. Rosalie, I’ll secure ten thousand francs for you, to be paid on your wedding day—contingent on your marrying Gorenflot; but to marry, you must remain silent. If not, no wedding gift!”
“Rosalie,” Madame de Merret said, “come and brush my hair.”
Her husband quietly paced the room, watching the door, the mason, and his wife without appearing suspicious. Gorenflot made some noise; Madame de Merret seized a moment when he was unloading bricks to say to Rosalie: “My dear child, I’ll give you a thousand francs a year if you tell Gorenflot to leave a crack at the bottom.” Then she added casually, “You’d better help him.”
Both husband and wife remained silent while Gorenflot walled up the door; the silence on Monsieur’s part was deliberate—he did not want to let his wife speak ambiguously. When the wall was half built the mason broke one of the top panes with his pick. They all then saw the face of a dark, gloomy-looking man with black hair and fiery eyes. Before her husband turned around the poor woman nodded to the stranger the signal of “Hope.”
At four in the morning the work was completed. The mason was placed in charge of Jean, and Monsieur de Merret slept in his wife’s room.
The next morning he casually mentioned, “Oh, by the way, I must go to the Maire for the passport.” He donned his hat, took a few steps toward the door, paused, and grabbed the crucifix. His wife trembled with joy—she thought he would go to Duvivier’s.
As soon as he left Madame de Merret rang for Rosalie and cried, “The pick! Bring the pick! We must work. I saw how Gorenflot did it yesterday; we have time to make a gap and build it up again.”
In an instant Rosalie brought a cleaver and began demolishing the wall with a frenzy. She had removed a few bricks when, turning to strike again, she saw Monsieur de Merret behind her. She fainted.
“Lay madame on her bed,” he said coldly.
Foreseeing what would happen in his absence, he had set this trap; he had merely written to the Maire and summoned Duvivier. The jeweler arrived just as the disorder in the room was being repaired.
“Duvivier,” Monsieur de Merret asked, “didn’t you buy any crucifixes from the Spaniards who passed through town?”
“No, monsieur.”
“Very well; thank you,” he said, casting a fierce glare at his wife. “Jean,” he added to his valet, “you can serve my meals here in Madame de Merret’s room. She is ill, and I won’t leave her until she recovers.”
The cruel man remained in his wife’s room for twenty days. During the early days, when there was some noise in the closet and Josephine wanted to intercede for the dying man, he would cut her off, saying without allowing her to speak, “You swore on the Cross that there was no one there.”
After Bianchon finished this story, all the ladies rose from the table, shaken by the last words.
################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
During the Restoration the Archbishop of Besançon frequently attended Baronne de Watteville’s drawing‑room because of her devoutness.
Monsieur de Watteville, a quiet, unassuming descendant of the notorious Watteville, lived in La Franche‑Comté. He married Mademoiselle de Rupt, an heiress, in a match arranged in 1802 and formalized in 1815 after the Second Restoration. After her grandparents’ deaths Madame de Watteville became more pious and influential in Besançon. Thin and plain, he worked as a turner and collected geological specimens. Admirers praised his noble soul: he accepted that he would not dominate his wife and devoted himself to work and hobbies. The Rupt residence reflected both families’ standing—spacious rooms, elegant furnishings, and fine cuisine—while his own estate remained modest. Their intimacy with the Archbishop attracted several notable abbés.
At a ceremonial dinner in September 1834 Abbé de Grancey announced a favorable lawsuit verdict; the Vicar‑General, surprised by the outcome in the present political climate, credited a young lawyer, Monsieur Savaron. Amedée de Soulas, present that evening, would later prove significant.
France and England long exchanged cultural influences—language, fashion, and customs crossed the Channel. The English anthem “God Save the King” was linked to Lulli; dresses shifted from hoops to altered waistlines; and English terms like “dandy” and “lion,” popularized by Alfred de Musset, launched a vogue for stylish young men that reached the provinces.
In 1834 Besançon’s “lion” was Amedée‑Sylvain de Soulas, of Spanish descent. Ill at ease in the dull military town, he kept a spare apartment, lived frugally while cultivating an ostentatious appearance, and maintained a fashionably dressed servant, Babylas, as his “tiger.” Clever at economizing—often dining out—he used grooming and clothes to impress the local aristocracy and to court Mademoiselle Rosalie de Watteville, a modest eighteen‑year‑old zealously guarded by her mother. Amedée’s life thus illustrates social ambition, cultural exchange, and provincial constraint.
“Madame,” Monsieur de Soulas began while his soup cooled, “one morning a gentleman from Paris took rooms in Mademoiselle Galard’s house on Rue du Perron and registered at the Mairie as ‘Albert Savaron.’ He left his card with the local officials.”
“The Savaron name is well known,” Mademoiselle de Watteville observed.
“He's a Frenchman and no man’s son,” Amedee de Soulas replied. “If he claims the Savaron arms he must add a bar-sinister.” Rosalie noted that a bastard of a Comte de Savarus would still be noble, but the Baroness cut the discussion as Monsieur de Soulas continued.
“In a town like Besançon everything is catalogued,” he said. “Albert Savaron was quickly accepted by local lawyers, who wondered who sent him and why—he sent cards to judges instead of calling. Three days later he was mostly forgotten. He hired Jérôme, the late Monsieur Galard’s servant; no one saw him much.”
“He goes to mass?” asked Madame de Chavoncourt.
“He attends early service at Saint-Pierre on Sundays. He rises between one and two, works until eight, breakfasts, works again, walks in his garden, dines, and goes to bed between six and seven,” Monsieur de Soulas said. Living across the way, he had learned details from Jérôme and village gossip.
“How did you come to hire a stranger for your defense?” the Baroness asked the Vicar-General. The President had assigned Savaron to defend a half-witted peasant accused of forgery; Savaron not only proved the man’s innocence but exposed the true culprits. A merchant later entrusted him with a difficult case, which he also won. Monsieur Berryer advised employing him for the Chapter’s matter; the Vicar-General met Savaron and felt reassured.
“Is he extraordinary?” Madame de Chavoncourt asked.
“Certainly,” replied the Vicar-General, and Abbe de Grancey, who had also met Savaron, elaborated. He described a plain outer room lined with law books: an old writing table, six armchairs, gray curtains, a green carpet. Savaron was younger than expected, dressed in a black dressing gown edged with red and wearing a red smoking cap—the “devil’s colors,” joked Madame de Watteville. But the Abbe praised his head: black hair streaked with gray, thick and curly; a round white throat; a splendid forehead furrowed by thought; an olive complexion; a square nose; fiery, eagle-like eyes. He had passion with calm, a sweet voice that could thunder, average height, with hands like a prelate. Subsequent visits found him in a shabby bedroom or taking a frugal breakfast in his library. He seemed to carry a heavy burden.
“Why leave Paris for Besançon?” someone asked. The Abbe had watched him at the bar and in court and thought Savaron would make an impact. His portrait of Savaron enthralled Rosalie. For the first time she encountered someone exceptional, unlike the ordinary Count who had courted her. She replayed the Abbe’s words—“What a fine forehead!”—and, recalling the prelate-like hands and the window she had seen from her bed, felt an urgent desire: she must see him.
That night she planned to wake between two and three to glimpse his windows. “While the world sleeps, he is awake—like God!” she thought, watching candlelight through the trees and imagining his furniture. The household lectured that girls’ education should be limited lest they mature too soon; Rosalie’s sudden inward stirring showed how an Abbe’s portrait could transform the ordinary into the extraordinary for a young mind.
The next morning she saw Albert walking in the garden beside the Hotel de Rupt and resolved to enter his house, uncover his mystery, hear his voice, and glimpse his eyes. All day she embroidered with the concentration of someone who appears idle but is deeply reflective. She contrived to go to confession at half-past seven that Sunday, planning to see him at early service.
She visited her father in his workshop and proposed a grotto or belvedere in the garden from which she might look into Albert’s windows. Her father delighted; she begged him not to tell her mother. Sunday came: she dressed carefully, quickened her pace across the cross-court, checked the confessional, and positioned herself to see Albert as he entered church. No man could seem unattractive to Rosalie in that state. Albert, strikingly handsome, made a profound impression—solemn, burdened, his gaze conveying intense thought. In boldness she stood in his path to exchange glances; heat rushed through her. After he sat, she found a place to observe him during confession; time in the confessional seemed fleeting and, by the time she emerged, Mass had ended and he had gone. “The Vicar-General was right,” she thought. “He is unhappy. Why has this eagle come to Besançon? I must know everything!” From then on she masked her preoccupation with an innocent air and supported her father’s belvedere plan.
“Mamma,” she said, “Father has a unique idea for a belvedere in the garden.”
“I approve of everything your father does,” Madame de Watteville replied dryly. Rosalie described a cool grotto with a place to take coffee; her mother inspected the proposed columns and approved a site not visible from Monsieur de Soulas’ windows but commanding a view into Savaron’s rooms. A builder was hired to construct a grotto with a path of periwinkles, clematis, ivy, honeysuckle, and Virginia creeper. The interior would be rustic, with a mirror, an ottoman, a table of inlaid bark, and a chandelier carved by Monsieur de Watteville. Rumor spread through Besançon about the Wattevilles’ new garden feature.
“They’re wealthy and can afford such whims,” people said. “A thousand crowns!” Madame de Chavoncourt exclaimed. The cost and the Parisian craftsman were gossiped about—no one suspected that Rosalie’s motive was romantic.
Meanwhile Savaron’s legal brilliance continued to rouse envy. He avoided social gatherings, which helped him remain a modest stranger even as he won cases. He had argued three complex matters at the Commercial Tribunal and gained four prominent merchants as clients who recognized his skill.
On the day the Wattevilles inaugurated their belvedere, Savaron was launching another enterprise: a fortnightly paper, the Eastern Review. Quietly, through merchant connections, he raised capital—forty shares of five hundred francs each from his first ten clients—who hoped to promote Besançon as a trade and enlightenment center to rival Strasbourg and Dijon. Savaron’s vision appealed: to make Eastern France a hub of commerce and letters. He kept his name out of the spotlight; his chief client, Monsieur Boucher, handled finances while Savaron retained editorial control and a share of profits.
To manage the literary side he invited Alfred Boucher, the eager son of his main backer, to oversee contributions, while Savaron subtly guided content. The Review, with a low subscription price, quickly drew five hundred subscribers. The merchant-shareholders, pleased with the Review’s success, had paid only a fraction of their shares; with two hundred more subscribers, the publication would yield dividends for shareholders while the unpaid editor retained influence.
After the third issue the Review gained recognition among French publications. The third number included a tale attributed to Savaron that sparked comment in drawing rooms for its liberal tone; higher circles dismissed it as too forward. Rosalie, hearing of the Review, persuaded her father to subscribe.
“Papa,” she said, “subscribe and keep it in your room—mamma wouldn’t let me read it, but you can lend it to me.” Monsieur de Watteville, eager to please his daughter, subscribed and lent Rosalie the four issues already published. That night she devoured the tale—the first story she had ever read. Its confession-like quality made her certain it reflected Savaron himself; others noted that his work drew on his life. The story’s intimacy deepened Rosalie’s fascination, confirming that the man she had glimpsed and the author on the page were one and the same.
In 1823 two young men left Lucerne to tour the lake of the Four Cantons. Passing Gersau, Rodolphe was arrested by a newly built house and a woman at the window and stayed behind while his friend Leopold continued. They had studied law together: Leopold was bound for a notary’s post in Paris; Rodolphe, the illegitimate son of a nobleman, had a harder past—his mother had sacrificed everything for his education. Passionate and impulsive, he contrasted with Leopold’s calm temperament.
Lodging in Gersau with a couple, Rodolphe learned the woman was Fanny Lovelace, who had come with her elderly father for his health. Of modest means, she supported him by translating and writing; Rodolphe was taken by her beauty and musical gift. Visiting the Bergmanns’ gardens—the family renting to the Lovelaces—he noticed a deaf-mute girl, Gina, who hinted at an unusual backstory, and found the Bergmanns protective of the household.
That evening he heard Fanny sing and was enchanted. After an unknown assailant wounded him, Fanny and Gina nursed him. While he recovered he learned of his mother’s death and found solace in Fanny. They bonded and confessed love, though Fanny had been forced into marriage with Emilio, who was not as old as first thought. Their passion grew, but Rodolphe’s jealousy—especially when Tito, a youth from Fanny’s past, reappeared—along with social ties and obligations, forced them to weigh love against duty and sacrifice.
For twenty days Rodolphe stayed with the Bergmanns, studying Francesca with a mix of fascination and puzzlement. He saw both youthful imprudence and a resolute, sometimes resistant character. The old man treated her like a father; her gratitude suggested an innate nobility that deepened the mystery.
Those days blended secret joys and melancholy, quarrels and harmony. Rodolphe was captivated by Francesca’s constant tenderness and jealous over trifles. When she complained of Gersau’s lack of luxuries, he asked if she valued such things. She answered passionately, linking a love of luxury to an appreciation of art and beauty while insisting she had borne hardship without complaint; her playful claim that ordinary people aspired to greatness added to her charm.
Francesca then insisted Rodolphe not accompany them to Geneva, citing gossip and the need to protect her husband’s reputation. She professed love but demanded secrecy, kissed him, and left him astonished.
The next day Rodolphe went to Vevay and then Geneva, renting a room in Eaux-Vives and inquiring after Italian refugees—hoping for a bookseller named Lamporani—but found only the arrivals of Prince and Princess Colonna and Prince and Princess Gandolphini. On his first walk he saw Francesca at Villa Diodati in an elegant carriage; learning she lodged with the Colonnas revealed her noble rank and made him fear he had been a passing whim.
He left his card with Prince Gandolphini and met a bogus Lamporani who invited him to a concert. Though curious, Rodolphe did not intrude. That evening his passion intensified and he felt driven to raise his social standing. At the concert Francesca was radiant; their meeting of eyes convinced him she had remained true despite her rank.
Afterwards he was introduced to the Colonnas and Francesca, who teasingly called herself “Princess Rodolphini,” reviving memories of Gersau. She acknowledged his faithful affection; he admitted his ambition—to achieve greatness so she could be proud.
Francesca was full of contradiction: meant for the cloister but forced by family tragedy into marriage with Prince Gandolphini to preserve status, she prized independence, liberal ideas, and sympathized with revolutionary sentiments. Winter in Geneva passed in her intelligent, beautiful company, but by spring Rodolphe grew restless; her calm clashed with his need for more demonstrative feeling. On a terrace he bitterly lamented others’ happiness; she, wounded, withdrew. Finding her in tears, he kissed her; she rebuffed him, asserted her dignity, ordered him to leave—declaring they would not meet again until Naples—and he, recognizing her will, complied.
In Paris Rodolphe found a portrait of Princess Gandolphini by Schinner. Though claiming indifference, Francesca persuaded Schinner to paint originals for Rodolphe and Emilio, and correspondence began.
Driven by love and ambition, Rodolphe threw himself into a fraudulent affair that consumed three years. When Villele’s ministry fell he was ruined; after three months’ recovery at Villa Gandolphini in Naples he turned to politics. His talents were praised and a high post promised, but the July 1830 upheaval destroyed those hopes. Only Francesca and God witnessed his courageous efforts—a noble heart ill-favored by fortune. Sustained by love, he endured trials. Lovers! Pray for him!
