When Mademoiselle de Watteville finished, Rosalie flushed and wept. The novel—notable for a man narrating his own love—was her first encounter with that voice and convinced her Francesca Colonna was Albert’s beloved.
Curiosity hardened into resolve. Insecure about her looks and lack of musical talent, Rosalie resolved either to win Albert or learn the truth, dreaming schemes: sneaking into his garden, writing, visiting her father’s land to force a meeting.
From Albert’s window she saw Jerome helped over the wall by Mariette. Mariette’s plainness and relative freedom explained Jerome’s interest and showed servants could evade her mother’s rules. Rosalie escorted Mariette to Mass, coaxed access to letters Albert gave Jerome, and won her trust. Reading them, she learned Albert’s lineage, struggles, ambition, and steadfast devotion to Francesca. Though ashamed of the invasion, she clung to the letters as proof. When Mariette later brought a letter meant for Francesca, its fidelity moved Rosalie to step back—recognizing their noble bond and yearning for selfless love.
Rosalie spent the winter of 1834–35 in turmoil; by April, at nineteen, she fantasized about triumphing over the Duchesse d’Argaiolo. Alone, that struggle fueled her passion and dark plans; her story is presented as a cautionary example.
That winter Albert de Savarus advanced his position in Besançon, eager for the Chamber’s dissolution. Backed by a wealthy contractor allied with moderates, he revived the old Arcier aqueduct issue. Though the Doubs supplied water, Savarus attacked its quality and promoted “Roman” water; the local Review echoed him and townspeople across parties accepted the idea. The controversy, driven by hidden interests, dismissed opponents as old-fashioned while Savarus’s projects dominated discussion. After eighteen months he had stirred the previously stagnant town, gaining influence without broad popularity.
He also won lawsuits for local priests; ambition consumed him. His apparent indifference to personal gain concealed the expectation of larger rewards. In October 1834 he helped a struggling merchant with Léopold Hannequin’s funds, a move that also qualified him for election. The Abbé de Grancey, who discerned Savarus’s designs, called him remarkable and suggested he had taken a wrong path.
Meanwhile Rosalie resolved to introduce Savarus to the society of the Hôtel de Rupt, initially to observe him. The Wattevilles’ estate, les Rouxey, produced ten thousand francs yearly but was poorly managed by the inattentive Baron and an old steward, Modinier. The family often retired there, a picturesque spot improved by the famous Watteville, who built a dam and lake. Watteville’s heirs nonetheless pressed a dubious claim over the village of Riceys, whose inhabitants had long-held rights; the Baron's death left a legacy of usurpation his descendants continued.
Rosalie’s maneuvers were subtle. In May 1835 she left Besançon with her father for les Rouxey, enchanted by the lake and linking it with love. On a boat trip her father confessed legal troubles with Riceys; Rosalie urged him to hire Savarus as lawyer, stressing the need to secure the Dent de Vilard and insisting Savarus be brought to les Rouxey—clearly aiming to fix her future there.
Four days later the Abbé visited Savarus to win the Watteville case; Savarus hesitated, fearing loss of neutrality, but the Abbé promised political advantages. Rosalie’s letter to the préfet exposing Savarus’s ambitions was ignored. As the election approached, influential men unexpectedly nominated Savarus; he had been unaware. The Abbé warned of betrayals and urged honesty about his past. Savarus’s passionate speech impressed many, yet the préfet undermined him and the first vote disappointed him. In despair he reflected on his love for Francesca and an uncertain future—until the Abbé reported behind-the-scenes support, the Rouxey case’s likely resolution, and proposed marriage to Sidonie de Chavoncourt to consolidate votes. Savarus, reluctantly appreciative, prepared to navigate the choices of love and politics.
By nine the next evening Madame la Baronne de Watteville’s rooms were full of Besançon’s aristocracy, debating the surprise nomination of a candidate connected to the Rupts, kin of a former Master of Appeals. Madame de Chavoncourt arrived with her younger daughter Sidonie; her elder sister stayed away. The Abbé de Grancey moved among the guests, probing an elderly statesman about what the Elder Branch might field; they feared Berryer, noted the Duc de Fitz‑James’s Toulouse nomination, and worried that supporting Monsieur Savarus might alienate Republicans rather than attract Moderates.
Albert had not appeared by nine‑thirty; Madame de Watteville took it as an insult, while Madame de Chavoncourt counseled patience. Rosalie, observing hostess and mother, noted the indulgence toward Albert and hinted at a possible match between him and Sidonie. By ten some guests played cards. The Abbé, uneasy, went to the window where Rosalie had withdrawn and suddenly cried, “He must be dead!” The Vicar‑General, Monsieur de Watteville, and Rosalie went to Albert’s dark rooms; the servant Jérôme said Albert had left in a post‑chaise. The Abbé exclaimed ambiguously, “He is ruined! Or he is happy!”—and Rosalie’s face betrayed a joy the Vicar‑General pretended not to see.
Back in the drawing‑room Monsieur de Watteville announced Albert’s unexplained departure. By half‑past eleven about fifteen guests remained, including Madame de Chavoncourt and her daughters, the Abbés de Godenars and de Grancey, Monsieur de Vauchelles, Rosalie, Amédée de Soulas, and a retired magistrate who had supported Albert. Grancey stationed himself near the Baroness to watch Rosalie, now pale and agitated. A servant brought a letter on a silver tray; the Abbé read it, recognized the handwriting, folded it into his pocket, and declared, “She is in love with Albert Savarus!” Rosalie then confessed the letter was Albert’s and begged him to remain silent when he announced Albert’s withdrawal—knowing that withdrawal would doom any hope of her sister’s marriage to him.
On her way to Mass Mademoiselle de Watteville learned from Mariette that Albert had vanished after a Parisian visitor called on Monsieur Savaron; Savaron left with a large package, hurried to the Prefecture, bought a carriage with post horses, quit his post, and obtained a passport. His abrupt departure inflamed local prejudice against outsiders. Most soon forgot Albert—except Girardet, the Vicar‑General, and Rosalie, who alone knew the truth about the Duc d’Argaiolo’s death.
By April 1836 there was still no news. The Baroness pressed Rosalie to marry; the Baron promised the estate les Rouxey, though his wife fretted about finances. A match with Monsieur de Soulas was arranged; Rosalie refused. After a tense summer at les Rouxey the servants Mariette and Jérôme married and the Baron invested in the estate, increasing the Baroness’s resentment. Rosalie confessed to the Vicar‑General that she had intercepted letters between Albert and the Duchess Francesca d’Argaiolo, causing their separation and the tragic sequence tied to the Duc’s death. A Parisian clerk later sold Albert’s belongings and revealed he had renounced the world and entered a monastery. The Vicar‑General obtained Albert’s letter: he forgave Rosalie and wished to remain in solitude. Urged to reconcile with her mother and accept de Soulas, she refused. After the Vicar‑General’s death the Baroness married de Soulas, believing Rosalie’s support. Now of age and financially independent, Rosalie went to Paris, publicly confessed at a ball her part in Albert’s downfall, and returned to Besançon having exposed the truth—at heavy emotional cost, leaving a tale of love, betrayal, and the lasting consequences of ambition.
Mademoiselle de Watteville lived at Les Rouxey, managing the estate. Madame de Soulas, mother of two, contrasted with her husband, who told Chavoncourt that marrying a bigot had cost him dearly. Eccentric, she visited the Grande Chartreuse annually, recalling a grand‑uncle who broke monastery walls to escape. In 1841 she left Besançon to marry but returned after a Loire steamboat boiler explosion that cost her right arm and left leg and scarred her face. Failing health and constant pain led her to devote herself to religion at Les Rouxey's Chartreuse.
PARIS, May 1842.
ADDENDUM
Reappear: Vicomtesse de Beauseant; Genovese; Léopold Hannequin; Jeanrenaud; Gaston de Nueil; Duc Alphonse Rhetore; Savaron de Savarus; Hippolyte Schinner; Clarina Tinti.
MIDDLE OF THE STORY — CENTRAL SECTION OF THE NARRATIVE.
################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
Sweetheart, I too am free! I write first, unless you have already written to Blois.
Do not be alarmed; leaving the convent was simple. The conscience that tormented me yielded to gentler reasons: my aunt feared I would waste away, and my mother relented. I am in Paris thanks to you. If you had seen me when I left you, you would have been proud of your influence on my young heart. We were so intimate at the convent that when you left I sank into melancholy. I felt like a turtle‑dove widowed of her mate; death seemed sweet, and I nearly succumbed. To be at Blois, frightened of taking vows and without you, made me ill.
My aunt could not understand our private life or why the convent routine ill suited girls of our temperament. She had sacrificed for a brother; to expect the same of us was too much. For a fortnight I suppressed everything, but letter‑writing became my lifeline. I began a journal to mirror yours and dreamed of Gemenos as you will dream of Paris.
One morning a lady companion and Philippe, my grandmother’s last valet, arrived to take me away. My aunt kissed me and warned that pride might lead me to heaven or hell. I kissed her hands and promised not to return unless my heart were broken.
At Beaugency I still felt stunned. No one met me—my mother at Bois de Boulogne, my father at council, my brother late to dress. Philippe led me to my grandmother’s suite, the Princess de Vauremont, whose fortune had been left to me. Entering the rooms where she died brought a wave of grief; though she died in 1817, the place seemed recently occupied. It pained me that so little had been done: panels empty, marbles broken. Philippe said the Princess’s furniture was left to me, and my father awaited a law restoring lost property before renovating—an estimate of three hundred thousand livres had been set.
At first despair gripped me: had my father meant to send me to the convent while sparing no expense on my household? But memories of the Princess softened me; the rooms preserved her spirit. I set Philippe to polishing and rearranging, giving the old furniture a youthful air.
The bedroom and drawing‑room, hung in yellow damask and scarlet and filled with family relics—Savonnerie tapestry, a timepiece from Marshal de Saxe, china from Marshal de Richelieu, my grandmother’s portrait—breathed another century and her coquetries. I remembered how she tended her appearance to the end: traces of nocturnal visitors, disarranged furniture, card‑tables, snuff remained. When I was sent to the convent in 1816 she bade me go without display, promising I should remain independent and marry whom I pleased. Six months later she died; her will, entrusted to the Prince de Talleyrand, hinted she disapproved of the vows. I hope to meet the Prince and learn more.
Thus, though no one in flesh met me at first, the Princess’s presence comforted me, and I resolved to keep our pact: to tell each other the smallest domestic details. Send me every minute picture of your life.
October 19.
At half‑past five Rose announced my mother’s return. Her suite corresponds to mine and we share a secret staircase. I descended anxious about our reunion, wondering if I should feign affection. Her tact surprised me: neither distant nor effusive, she greeted me like a friend and kissed my forehead. She said she preferred my living with family to dying at the convent and wished to be more sister than mother. I found her charming at thirty‑eight—beautiful, with dark‑blue eyes and elegant hands—and responded kindly. She advised I not appear in society before visiting the dressmaker.
My father arrived and greeted me affectionately, calling me his rebel daughter and praising my looks; he predicted future pride. My brother, the Duc de Rhetore, arrived leisurely and kissed me; I was conscious of his indifference to my convent absence.
Thus ended the return of an eighteen‑year‑old after nine years at a convent, to a noble household where the old ways linger. Tired, I kept convent hours and slept early.
November 25.
The next morning the rooms were ready; Philippe had put flowers in the vases. Breakfast was troublesome—convent habits leave one hungry early. About one, my father found me writing to you and gave me a purse containing twelve thousand francs as the annual allowance he intended for me. He also told me my grandmother left me five hundred thousand francs in government stock, yielding about forty thousand francs a year. He had planned to use this to set up my younger brother but now left the decision to me, trusting my good sense. He warned that a Chaulieu daughter must comport herself with pride; any slander might have dire consequences. His frankness pleased me and spared my pride.
Philippe spent the morning arranging my transformation: dressmaker Victorine, corset‑maker, shoemaker, glove‑maker—all to fashion me out of convent attire. I was impatient to be made presentable; even a glovemaker was called. At lunch my mother proposed we go to the milliner; I felt dazzled by this independence—like a blind man who had recovered sight. I began to sense how far I had been from society.
My father often seems absent: deeply engaged in state affairs, he refused a Minister of State appointment and prefers an embassy. Family life is fragmentary at home: my brother and father are often away, my mother devotes mornings to dressing and keeps a busy social calendar of drives, receptions, and theatre evenings. We meet mainly at lunch and dinner; otherwise each pursues his own life. I see why family affection is so seldom; in Paris people find no time for private intimacy.
Miss Griffith, my governess—recommended by the English ambassador—is a Scotch clergyman’s daughter, thirty‑six, well‑bred and meant to teach me English and chaperon me. She is cautious but accommodating; I quickly see she will be governed by me. She advises against being dazzled by society and against being seduced by what attracts me most—her moral lectures are brief and repetitive.
I have not yet seen the streets; I must first be made presentable. The Italian opera opens soon, and my mother has a box. I read avidly; modern literature often dwells on love—the theme that once obsessed us at the convent. Corinne and Adolphe delighted me. When I asked if I might meet Mme. de Staël, my parents laughed and informed me she was dead.
December.
My Darling—tomorrow I make my debut. I have rehearsed every folly: stays, shoes, curls, dress, ornaments—all inspected in private with Griffith as witness. In short, I have left the convent and been reborn as a frivolous society girl. I may be raw in some ways—shoulder lines not yet rounded—but there is strength, health, and a clear, vivid complexion. My features are correct, my hair pale gold with a darker center, my eyes bright and insolent, the mouth expressive. I have grace of gait and voice; my grandmother’s manner and voice are in me. If I wish, I can be innocent, witty, or melancholy enough to command admiration.
My father has provided me a trousseau—ribbons, shoes, gloves, dressing‑case, scent‑box, fan, prayer‑book, cashmere shawl—and promised riding lessons. I can dance; tomorrow evening I will appear in white muslin with a Greek garland of white roses and intend to play the simpleton to win the other women. My mother underestimates me and sees me as a simpleton; my brother treats me with polite contempt. I suspect Alphonse harbors secret discontent and jealousy toward our father, which none of the household understands.
We are so unlike my parents: my father absorbed in public life and pleasures, my mother devoted to her toilette and society. They both know how to wear their parts; the show of unity conceals indifference. It amazes me how Paris life can consume people from morning until the small hours, draining energy and money. I am newly curious about this spell of society that keeps people enslaved and separate. For now I have sixteen hours a day to think, while those around me barely have minutes for reflection. There is much to learn in this world I sought; I am eager and anxious at once.
Yesterday at two o’clock I drove down the Champs-Élysées and through the Bois de Boulogne on a beautiful autumn day that reminded me of the Loire. At last I felt I had experienced Paris; the Place Louis XV seemed a testament to human artistry.
I was impeccably dressed, pensive, and poised, yet no one smiled. The carriage moved slowly, matching my demeanor. Only my father, a duke, acknowledged me with satisfaction; my mother blew a butterfly kiss. Griffith scanned indiscriminately. A young woman should know where to look.
I was furious. A man inspected my carriage without seeing me—probably a carriage-maker. I had misjudged my appeal; beauty, it seems, is common in Paris. Hats were tipped to the less deserving while my mother drew admiration. I resolved to learn why.
The men generally disappointed: unattractive, mere echoes of former elegance. Their fashion lacked the color and comfort of earlier days; hats perched like truncated columns. It seems easier to incite a revolution than to make a stylish hat, yet the French cling to ridiculous styles. Most faces looked worn, betraying thwarted ambition and bruised vanity; a fine forehead was rare. “And these are the products of Paris!” I said to Miss Griffith. “Most cultivated and pleasant men,” she replied. I was silent; a thirty-six-year-old spinster’s heart practices tolerance.
That evening I went to a ball, staying close to my mother, who received all attention. I was merely a pretext for compliments aimed at her. She found me partners who all praised the heat and beauty of the ball as though I were freezing and blind; each man pretended it was the first time he saw me.
My dazzling dress paled beside the extravagant attire of married women, each surrounded by admirers and watching one another closely. A few shone with genuine beauty—my mother among them. At a ball a girl is merely a dancing machine—insignificant.
The men impressed me no more there than on the avenue: exhausted, bland, uniform. The proud, vigorous men of our ancestors seemed vanished. One strikingly handsome man failed to stir me; he was not noble. No matter his genius, a commoner could not move me. He seemed absorbed in himself—an intellectual who views women as objects. I learned he is a professor and author, driven by ambition.
I resolved not to quarrel with society for failing to recognize my merits and instead embraced the simple joy of dancing, which I thoroughly enjoyed. I overheard trivial gossip about strangers; perhaps my ignorance spared me. Society is a web of intrigue, and I am perceptive.
Returning home pleasantly tired, I began sharing my experiences with my mother. She interrupted: “My dear child, it requires as much tact to know when to be silent as when to speak.” Her words revealed feelings that should remain concealed even from a mother. I realized the vast realm of feminine duplicity. In our innocence we might seem astute gossipers; a mere gesture or glance can teach profound lessons. Suddenly I felt overwhelming shyness. Must I never again express my joy in dancing? What of deeper emotions?
I went to bed sorrowful, reeling from the clash between my candid nature and society’s harsh rules. Already the hedges of my life are flecked with white wool! Farewell, beloved.
How deeply your letter moved me, especially as our paths diverge: you to Parisian brightness, I to a quiet retreat.
At the Castle of Maucombe my room remains the same, now opening on the Gemenos valley I once saw without understanding. Soon after my arrival my parents took me to dine with M. de l'Estorade, a wealthy, frugal old gentleman who lost his only son to Bonaparte’s conscription in 1813. The youth evaded the draft, joined the Emperor’s bodyguard, and vanished after Leipzig. The Baron clung to hope and hoarded his income for him. Only later I learned my future husband had been slowly returning from Russia, Poland, and Germany; his fortunes turned in Berlin where the French Minister helped him home. The Baron, a small Provençal landowner with about ten thousand livres income, remains obscure enough in Provence despite these adventures.
The late Mme. de l'Estorade’s estate combined with the father’s savings amounts to roughly two hundred and fifty thousand livres—excluding property—a considerable fortune here. The Baron has just bought a poorly run estate he plans to plant with ten thousand mulberry trees. Now that his son is found, his one desire is to marry him to a girl of good family.
My parents approved once they learned Renée de Maucombe would be accepted without a dowry and that her prospective inheritance would be acknowledged in the marriage contract. My younger brother Jean signed a document recognizing an advance on his inheritance. This is how the nobles of Provence work within Bonaparte’s Civil Code, which otherwise forces many girls into convents rather than marriage.
That dinner was the first meeting of your sweetheart and the exile. My father-in-law-to-be has no castle, only a simple country house at the entrance of a beautiful valley: unpretentious, with pebble walls and a red-tiled roof, a typically Provençal garden enclosed by low walls. Still, the house did its best for the dinner, serving us on old silver.
The exile is emaciated and pale, marked by suffering. At thirty-seven he looks much older, with white streaks in once-beautiful hair; he is a little deaf, like the Knight of the Sorrowful Countenance. Despite his ravaged appearance, he seems pleased to be marrying the fair Renée de Maucombe.
I have graciously agreed to become Mme. de l'Estorade, bringing the two hundred and fifty thousand livres, on condition that I may transform the grange into a park. I have asked my father for a water grant to help this. In a month I will be Mme. de l'Estorade, having made a good impression.
While you prepare to enjoy Paris’s vibrant life, I descend from our shared dreams into the humble realities of country existence. I am determined to bring joy to this young man, who knew little youth, going straight from his mother’s arms to war and Siberia. I will take pleasure in improving the little oasis around our home with fine trees and greenery: a hillside park with perhaps a kiosk from which I can glimpse the Mediterranean, orange and lemon trees, and the poetry of the surrounding nature. My father-in-law and the Chevalier share my religious sentiments.
My life will run like a smooth highway, free from the turmoil of another Bonaparte. If I have children they will be mine to nurture; if you remain true to your destiny, your children will have in me a devoted protector. I bade farewell to the romances and adventures we once imagined: my milestones will be the care of the young Masters de l'Estorade, and the mischief they bring. Perhaps one day I may visit Marseilles; otherwise provincial dangers are minimal. We will interest ourselves in the silkworms whose mulberry leaves we sell and share in Provence’s ordinary ups and downs. Quarrels seem unlikely: M. de l'Estorade has promised to leave household matters to me, and I shall see that he does.
You, dear Louise, will provide the romance in my life. Tell me of your balls, your dresses, and the men you meet; your other self will always be with you, listening and feeling the excitement. If only I could enjoy some Parisian fun while you managed La Crampade—poor M. de l'Estorade thinks he is marrying one woman; will he discover two?
I must conclude—I am foolish only through you. One kiss on each cheek; my lips remain untouched, he has only taken my hand. P.S. I have just opened your third letter. I have about one thousand livres to spend; buy me pretty things we cannot find here or in Marseilles. Think of the recluse at La Crampade; neither family has anyone in Paris with taste to buy what we need. I will answer your letter later. Goodbye, dear.
VI. DON FELIPE HENAREZ TO DON FERNAND PARIS, September.
The heading assures you our house lives. The massacre of our kin in the Court of Lions forced me into a double life and taught me an Arab cunning that probably saved me. Ferdinand’s terror and his acting deceived Valdez; had I not intervened, the Admiral would have died. The Liberals misunderstand kingship; I know this Bourbon too well—his constant vows of protection only confirm my distrust. A true Spaniard need not repeat promises; too many words betray falsehood.
Valdez sailed away on an English ship. Watching beloved Spain’s cause collapse in Andalusia, I set my escape in motion. I sent my steward and arranged to meet coral fishers on the coast; while Ferdinand tried to seize me, I was already on my barony of Macumer among brigands who defy authority. The last Hispano‑Moorish family of Granada has found refuge in an African desert; I keep a Saracen horse on an estate inherited from Saracens. Those same brigands who once feared my rule now shelter me from the King’s vengeance, proud to defend the Duc de Soria, a Henarez—the first of our name to visit them since the Moors ruled.
I had imagined living off the estate’s income, but Quevedo was right: my possessions are mostly nominal—vast prairies without houses, virgin forests without furniture. It would take a fortune and half a century of care to render them productive. That problem looms.
Seeing my noble country left a carcass for monks to exploit moved me to tears; it is an omen of Spain’s fate. In Marseilles I learned of Riego’s fall. It struck me that my life would linger as unnoticed martyrdom. Is existence worth living when one can neither die for his country nor live for a woman? To love and to conquer was our law. Now my sabre is broken, the palace in ruins, the spring dried to sand.
Here is my last will.
Fernand, this explains why I checked your ardor and urged loyalty to rey netto. As brother and friend I implore you, as master I command: approach the King and claim my dignities, properties, offices, and titles. He may hesitate; tell him you are loved by Marie Heredia, and that she will marry only a Duc de Soria. This will gratify the King: the Heredia fortune has always warded off my ruin. Your proposal will seem to deprive me of a last resource, so he will gladly hand over my spoils.
You will marry Marie. Her secret love for you was no secret to me; I have prepared the old Count for your succession. Marie and I did our family duties; all favors you. You are handsome and possess Marie’s heart. I am an ill‑favored grandee whom she holds an unconfessed aversion toward; any reluctance caused by my misfortunes you will soon overcome.
As Duc de Soria, you will not regret my predecessor nor lose a maravedi. I will retain my mother’s diamonds for independence; the gap they leave may be filled by Marie’s jewels. Send them with my nurse Urraca—the only servant I shall keep. No one makes my chocolate like her.
During our brief revolution my needs shrank to necessities, covered by my salary. You will find two years’ income with your steward. This sum is mine, but a Duc de Soria cannot wed without expense, so we shall share it. You will not refuse this wedding gift from your brigand brother; I intend it.
The barony of Macumer, not on Spanish soil, remains mine. Thus I still have a country and a name should I ever re‑enter the world.
Thank Heaven, our business is done, and the house of Soria is saved!
Just as I resigned myself to being merely Baron de Macumer, the French cannon announced the arrival of the Duc d’Angoulême. You will understand why I must stop here.
October.
When I arrived I had not ten doubloons. A poor leader finds means to live within calamities he cannot avert: for the vanquished Moor, a horse and the desert; for the frustrated Christian, the cloister and a few coins. My resignation is weariness, not surrender. I am not prepared to abandon all thought of life. Ozalga gave letters of introduction, including one to a bookseller who serves our compatriots as Galignani serves the English. He found me eight pupils at three francs a lesson. I teach every other day, earning twelve francs—more than I need. When Urraca arrives I will pass my connection to another Spanish exile.
I lodge in Rue Hillerin‑Bertin with a poor widow who takes boarders. My room faces south, with a little garden. It is quiet; I have green trees to gaze on, and I spend one piastre a day. From sunrise until ten I smoke and enjoy chocolate, contemplating two Spanish plants—a broom rising from jasmine—colors that thrill any scion of the Moors. At ten I give lessons until four, return for a frugal dinner, then read and smoke until bed.
I can endure this life of work, meditation, solitude, and modest society for a long time. Be happy, Fernand; my abdication brings no regrets. Five days have passed since I wrote my will; they feel like five centuries. Honor, titles, wealth—as if they never existed.
Now that conventional barriers are down, I open my heart to you, dear boy. Though outwardly grave, my heart is full of tenderness and devotion—never granted to any object, not even she who was to be my bride since childhood. Spain became my mistress and received my homage. Now Spain is lost. Beggared of all, I look on the ruins of my former self and ponder the mystery of my existence.
Why was life given to this carcass, and when will it depart? Why did a race famed for chivalry infuse all its virtues into this last offshoot only for the seed not to sprout—no stem, no flower to scatter its Eastern perfumes? What crime did I commit before birth to inspire no love? Was I destined to be stranded, a useless hulk on a barren shore? In my soul I see deserts where my ancestors roamed under a scorching sun. Proud remnant of a fallen race, vain force, love wasted—an old man in the prime of youth—I shall await death’s last grace. Under this murky sky no spark will rekindle these ashes. Thus my last words may echo Christ’s: “My God, Thou hast forsaken me!”—a cry of agony and terror whose depth few mortals have explored.
You can now understand, Fernand, the joy I find in living through you and Marie. I will watch you with the pride of a creator satisfied with his work. Love one another well and continue to do so; any discord between you would pain me more than it would you.
Our mother had a premonition that events would one day fulfill her wishes. Perhaps a mother’s longing is a pact with God. She often revealed a heart that rightly belonged to both her sons, yet was given to you alone by an irresistible passion. Her spirit will hover above you as you bow at the altar. What I have done pleases our womenfolk, the dead, and the King; it is God’s will. Do not resist, Fernand; obey and remain silent.
P.S. Tell Urraca to call me nothing but M. Henarez. Do not mention me to Marie. You must be the sole living soul to know the secrets of the last Christianized Moor, whose veins carry the blood of a great family that began in the desert and is now about to die out in solitary exile.
Farewell.
VII. LOUISE DE CHAULIEU TO RENEE DE MAUCOMBE
What! To be married so soon? After a month you become engaged to a stranger about whom you know nothing. He may be deaf, sickly, tiresome—anything. Don’t you see what they want from you? You’re needed to continue the l’Estorades line. You’ll be buried in the provinces. Were these the promises we made? If I were you, I’d rather risk a voyage to Hyères, capture by Algerian corsairs, and a life as a Sultana—at least an adventure!
You’re merely leaving one convent for another. I know you: timid, prone to family docility. But I will guide you; come to Paris. There we will make men frantic and hold court like queens. Your husband may enter the Chamber in three years; you will play that world well and live as a woman of fashion. I won’t let you rot in your grange.
Monday.
For a fortnight I’ve been living society’s life: evenings at the Italiens, the Grand Opera, always a ball. Opera music enchants me; I am the focus of admiration. Yet no young man has stirred me; none has evoked the feeling García rouses in Otello. I am jealous—jealousy pure as Rossini made it. What a wretched dramatist Shakespeare is! Othello wins battles and leaves Desdemona meekly neglected. Such a sheep deserves slaughter. Let the man I love beware of thinking of anything but loving me!
Give me the long trials of chivalry: the lover sent among lions for a glove—what exquisite prizes are lost by such impertinence! But I ramble. My father is likely to represent our master the King in Madrid, and I shall accompany him. My mother wishes to remain; my father may take M. de Canalis as secretary—if the King doesn’t appoint another. Canalis, the poet, courts my mother and studies diplomacy with her. Diplomacy must be a fine art; he pursues it like a gambler at the Stock Exchange.
Our elder brother, the Duc de Rhétore, will stay in Paris. Miss Griffith has discovered Alphonse is in love with a ballet girl at the Opera—how can one love legs and pirouettes? Two ballet girls in a family are ruinous. My second brother is with his regiment; I have not seen him. Thus I must act as Antigone of His Majesty’s ambassador. Perhaps I may marry in Spain; perhaps my father imagines a marriage without dowry. He teased me, asking my opinion of young men; I dissimulated with trivial remarks about hair and faces. He seemed inwardly disappointed.
Saturday.
One thing I left out: the love we imagined is extremely well hidden; I have seen no trace. Quick glances occur in drawing‑rooms, but how colorless it all is! Love as we pictured it—wonders, dreams, sorrows that awaken sympathy—does not exist. The bewitching words, constant interchange of happiness, the misery of absence—where are they? What soil produces these radiant flowers of the soul? Which is wrong: us or the world?
I have seen hundreds of men; not one has moved me. No proof of admiration or devotion on their part would touch me. Love is so rare one might live a lifetime without meeting the being who can make one happy. The thought chills; if found too late, what then?
For days I’ve trembled at women’s destinies. Marriage is mere chance. Look at yours. I dreamed a storm of wild thoughts: to be loved as much after ten years as on the first day—such love demands years. The lover must languish; curiosity must be piqued and satisfied. Is there a law for the heart’s inner fruits, as for nature’s? Can joy be made lasting? In what measure should love mix tears with pleasure? Only God can solve this.
I begin to think I’ll return to the convent. If I stay in society I may do foolish things, for I cannot reconcile what I see with delicacy and principle.
My mother is the happiest, adored by Canalis, that “great little man.” I sometimes crave to know what passes between them. Griffith says virtue is burying savage instincts, but then what becomes of the heart? It becomes a sink for the worst in us.
It’s humiliating that no admirer has yet appeared for me. I am marriageable, but family honor may keep men away. If that’s why, they are cowards.
The parts of Chimène in the Cid and the Cid himself delight me. What a marvelous play! Farewell.
VIII. THE SAME TO THE SAME, January.
Our master is a poor refugee, hiding for his role in the revolution quashed by the Duc d’Angoulême—a triumph that has led to brilliant fêtes. Though Liberal and perhaps a man of the people, he has aroused my interest; I suspect he was condemned to death. I try to make him talk to uncover his secret, but he has a Castilian taciturnity—prideful yet angelic in patience and gentleness. His pride is not petulant; it’s part of his nature, imposing civility through perfect manners and keeping us respectfully at a distance.
My father jokes there is much nobility in Señor Henarez, whom he calls Don Henarez. I took the liberty of addressing him so. He raised his eyes—usually cast down—and flashed a look that abashed me; he certainly has the most beautiful eyes. I asked if I had offended; he replied in grand Spanish that he was there only to teach us Spanish. I blushed and felt snubbed. I nearly retorted sharply, but remembering our mother’s counsel, I begged his pardon.
A tremor passed through him; his olive cheeks flushed. He said religion must teach respect for the unhappy better than I could; had he been a don in Spain who lost everything in Ferdinand VII’s triumph, my witticism would be unkind. If he were but a poor language teacher, would satire be heartless? Neither description suits a young lady of rank.
He bowed, opened my Don Quixote, and began. That little incident disturbed me more than any compliment. During the lesson I watched him, safe because he rarely looks at me.
From appearances, the tutor—whom we assumed forty—is under thirty. My governess admired his black hair and pearly teeth. His eyes are velvet and fire, but his face is plain and singular—smallpox scars give his ugliness piquancy; a prominent forehead, shaggy brows, a stoop, and an oddly large head give him a harsh, plaintive look, like a sickly child needing superhuman care. As my father noted, his features recall a shrunken Cardinal Ximenes. The natural dignity of his manners unsettles the Duke, who dislikes him and never feels at ease before such superiority.
Once my father knows enough Spanish we’ll go to Madrid. When Henarez returned two days after his reprimand, I said, out of gratitude, “I have no doubt you left Spain because of political events. If my father is sent there, as seems likely, we might help you obtain a pardon, if you are under sentence.”
“It is impossible for anyone to help me,” he replied.
“But,” I asked, “is that because you refuse help or because the situation is impossible?”
“Both,” he answered, bowing and closing the subject.
My father’s blood boiled. I was offended by his haughty posture and promptly dropped Señor Henarez. Yet something admirable lay in his refusal of aid. “He would not accept even our friendship,” I thought as I conjugated a verb. Suddenly I spoke to him in Spanish. Henarez said equality of sentiment is necessary between friends, which did not exist here, making the matter pointless.
“Do you mean equality of feeling, or equality in rank?” I demanded, eager to provoke.
He raised those awe‑inspiring eyes, and mine fell. Dear, he is a hopeless enigma. The glance asked whether my words implied love; the mingled joy, pride, and doubt in it moved me. It was clear that advances, taken lightly in France, might complicate with a Spaniard, and I retreated, feeling foolish.
The lesson ended; he bowed, and his eyes expressed a humble prayer: “Don’t trifle with a poor wretch.” This sudden variance from his usual grave dignity made a deep impression. It seems horrible to say, but I can’t help believing there are treasures of affection in that man.
All is over, my dear child; Mme. de l'Estorade writes. Between us nothing has changed; it is only one girl the less.
Do not be troubled; I did not consent rashly. My life is planned, and certainty suits me. A moral resolve has removed chance. We have property to develop and a home to beautify; I must manage a household, reconcile my husband to life, and probably educate our children.
Everyday life cannot be cast in a heroic mould, yet the infinite can still be explored within familiar tasks. My humble duties allow passion; restoring faith in happiness to the suffering is a noble way to relieve life's monotony. I see little room for suffering and much good to be done. My love for Louis de l'Estorade does not make my heart race, yet nothing in him offends me.
My instincts for greatness and my mental energies remain mine, concealed and devoted to the family's welfare. The time for a woman to seek admiration is short; if my life is not grand, it will at least be calm and free from shocks. Nature gave a choice of love or motherhood; I have chosen motherhood: my children will be my gods, this spot of earth my Eldorado.
Thank you for what you sent; see the enclosed list of needs. I am determined to live with refinement and some luxury, taking from provincial life only what adds charm. Solitude never vulgarizes a woman—she remains herself. My father-in-law is delighted and is sending workmen from Paris to renovate the house.
X. MLLE. DE CHAULIEU TO MME. DE L'ESTORADE
January.
Oh Renée, you have made me miserable. Your proud beauty and fine heart will be wasted in a dull marriage; your children will be monsters. You seem resigned—neither hopeful nor afraid—but what if one morning a man awakens you? The thought chills me. At least you will have a friend and become the guardian of your valley; I will remain a heart untouched by petty society, all yours.
Monday.
My Spaniard bewilders me with calm, severe dignity. His solemn silence and reserve provoke curiosity; how strange that a language teacher should fascinate me more than any nobleman. His coldness, his distance, wrapped in pride and mystery, both amuses and irritates me.
Yesterday I said something that struck M. Henarez; he did not answer, and the lesson ended with a bow implying he might not return—an outcome I prefer, for I dread sentimental catastrophe. I’ve been reading Rousseau and find his moralizing passion detestable; Richardson’s Clarissa captures English women better. Love is, for me, a subjective poem; everything written about it is both false and true. Since you will be an authority on married love, I feel called to remain unmarried and seek a grand passion to enrich our shared experience. Tell me every detail of your first days with that strange creature called a husband; I promise to do the same if I ever find love.
Farewell, poor martyred darling.
XI. MME. DE L'ESTORADE TO MLLE. DE CHAULIEU
La Crampade.
Your Spaniard and you make me shudder. Dismiss him. Everything you describe fits a dangerous adventurer who risks all. He cannot be your husband or lover. I will write more about my married life when my heart is less anxious from your letter.
XII. MLLE. DE CHAULIEU TO MME. DE L'ESTORADE
February.
This morning my father visited, more thoughtful than usual. After flattering me and admitting he’d misjudged my intelligence, he launched into politics: France remains shaken—its Revolution persists in law and thought; the King rules without force, nobles are weak before the elected, and the family has been undermined by individualism and state finance. He argued a state cannot survive without paternal authority and half-jokingly asked me to persuade him to sacrifice my inheritance for my younger brother; then reminded me a husband will expect a dowry. We parted in good humor.
Your warning about Henarez made me cautious. I hesitated to end my lessons, yet I’m drawn to him. His voice thrills me; his manners are simple and genuine. After a lesson he almost kissed my hand, stopped, and looked terrified; my smile encouraged him, his eyes were wet, and I felt a strange happiness. He fascinates me.
Friday.
He is handsome and eloquent, revealing intellectual power and a new world. He told of a valet who died for a queen’s glance and I was captivated. At a ball M. de Vandenesse hinted Henarez might be of noble descent—the Duke de Soria, his titles lost under Ferdinand—raising suspense.
In our next lesson I suspected him; he denied it, calling himself a servant of the King of Spain. His reticence provoked me; I devised a test: I had him translate a letter I wrote describing my ideal lover. He was visibly moved and asked to keep the letter as a remembrance. He left asking to speak with my father; from my father I learned Henarez is now the Baron de Macumer, with property in Sardinia.
Two weeks have passed since I saw him; I wonder what he is doing. He has become necessary to me, and I am torn between love and the realities of my situation. Sunday at the Italian Opera I felt his fiery gaze. He has a genius for love.
XIII. MME. DE L'ESTORADE TO MLLE. DE CHAULIEU
La Crampade, February.
My dear Louise, I waited to write until I had learned enough for your happiness. The difference between girl and married woman is vast; one cannot fully understand the other. I chose to marry Louis de l'Estorade rather than return to the convent. The seriousness of marriage terrified me, yet I realized friendship might be the key to a happy marriage. I accepted his love believing it would not be slavery but a reign.
We agreed to separate marriage from certain marital rights to preserve independence: a compact to keep my freedom while being his wife. I watched Louis to see if suffering had softened or hardened him. His love proved a passion, and I judged I could risk much. I proposed a friendship in which we would share life’s burdens; I asked to be left free, promising to attend to his feelings. He was overjoyed, and we married soon after.
I am nearly happy now, though I must conceal my full contentment from him, for he is sensitive. For three months we lived much as before; I studied our relationship and saw how my presence changed him. I believed I could bring new life to my husband before bearing him children. His happiness brightened our home; even my father became generous. I manage the household with economy—seeking pleasure without excess.
I harbor ambitious plans for Louis, hoping to see him in politics; he has embraced my designs, showing the depth of his affection. To conclude, here is a letter he wrote expressing his love: he sees me as a treasure, and I feel proud of our relationship.
XIV. THE DUC DE SORIA TO THE BARON DE MACUMER
Madrid.
My dear brother, I did not make you Duc de Soria to betray the name. I cannot tolerate your exile while we enjoy happiness. Marie and I will delay marrying until you accept the money Urraca offers. We have prayed for your contentment; I believe Heaven will send you the love you seek. Marie and I will always be grateful to you. Write through the Nuncio. Farewell; take pride in the happiness you have brought us.
XV. LOUISE DE CHAULIEU TO MME. DE L'ESTORADE
March.
Ah, my friend, marriage has made a philosopher of you! Your prudent calculations trouble me. Do you think you can convert me to matrimony by cautious arithmetic? We left Blois innocent, armed with meditation, and now your weapons have turned against you. If I did not know you were pure, I would suspect vice.
You have made a holocaust of your youth—becoming a miser before your time. If Louis loves you, he will never realize your actions spring from necessity. Love is the first principle of all virtues; I cannot accept justifying marriage by calculation. You act a man's part; Louis must play the woman!
Your letter plunged me into thought; I see now the convent cannot replace a mother. Please do not turn away from my cries!
Monday.
Last night I looked at the stars and pictured my Spaniard in a tree by my window. He blew a letter to me through the window, but I burned it unread—it felt improper to read such an offering. At the opera he seemed sad; I learned he had renounced a rich heiress for another man’s sake. I was wretched for burning his letter—what might he have confessed? To be loved in secret, to know a man is devoted—how can I resist?
When I returned home I reopened my window, hoping he might be there. He was, and I received another letter, which I read and cherished.
XVI. THE SAME TO THE SAME
March.
I am dressed in white, with camellias in my hair. I feel an odd desire to delay my decision and make him wait. What a vision—driving my barouche, seeing him, the thrill. I complimented him on his horse; he said it was a gift from his steward. Henarez is no longer just a simple man; he grows more distinguished. Do my love affairs amuse you? I am a little off my head and must stop.
XVII. MME. DE L'ESTORADE TO LOUISE DE CHAULIEU
April.
My angel, you have grieved me. You do not realize the force of the word indissoluble in marriage. Each marriage has laws shaped by place and character: a wife of a leader must resign to his guidance; a wife of a fool must take control. You speak of vice, but passion can be admirable when it springs from the heart. We are answerable to God and ourselves for our happiness.
You will find a wife’s calculation necessary to maintain happiness. I have reflected on the duties of wife and mother, and I believe a sublime hypocrisy may be required to reach the ideal of a perfect woman. I persist in my principles, convinced virtue varies with surroundings. If I wished to make Louis unhappy, I would leave him the helm entirely.
I may not have married a man of rank, but I hope to further him toward it. Five years from now we will see if I was mistaken. You taught me what a woman sacrifices in marriage; regret is not remorse, but I feel the loss of freedom. You say marriage has made you a philosopher; I feel far from it. I have read Bonald, who revealed the strength of the family.
Farewell, my dear friend.
Well, my Renee, you are a wonderful woman, and I now agree that we can only be virtuous by cheating. Will that satisfy you? The man who loves us is our property; we may make a fool or a genius of him as we please—though the former is common. You will make yours a genius and keep the secret—two heroic acts, if you will.
Ah, if there were no afterlife, how nicely you would be sold, for this is the martyrdom you are choosing. You want to make him ambitious and keep him in love! Child, surely keeping him in love alone is punishment enough.
Tell me, to what extent is calculation a virtue, or is virtue calculation? You won’t say? We won’t quarrel; we have Bonald to refer to. We are, and intend to remain, virtuous; still, I believe you, with all your little knavery, are a better woman than I.
Yes, I am shockingly deceitful. I love Felipe and conceal it with hypocrisy. I long to see him leap from his tree to my balcony—and if he did, how I would wither him with my scorn! I am frank with you.
What restrains me? Where is the power that prevents me from telling Felipe how supremely happy he has made me with his pure, absolute, overflowing love?
Mme. de Mirbel is painting my portrait, which I intend to give him. Love animates life; every hour, every trifle matters, blurring past, present, and future. Is it still so at the heights of happiness? What is happiness—soothing or exciting? I am restless, dragged to him despite reason and propriety. At least I can better understand your feelings.
Felipe’s happiness rests in feeling himself mine; his aloofness and strict obedience irritate me, just as his profound respect did when he was my Spanish master. I want to cry out, “Fool, if you love me as a picture, what will it be when you know the real me?”
Oh, Renee, you burn my letters, don’t you? I will burn yours. If anyone else read these thoughts, I would send Felipe to silence them; perhaps even kill the owners for security.
Monday.
How can one fathom the human heart? My father should introduce me to M. Bonald; I would ask him. I envy God, who can read the heart’s undercurrents. Does Felipe still worship me? That is the question.
If I ever detect the slightest fall in the respect he used to show me as my instructor, I believe I would have the strength to end it. “Why these fine words, these grand resolutions?” you will say. Dear, I will tell you.
My charming father, devoted as an Italian cavaliere servente, had my portrait painted by Mme. de Mirbel. I managed to get a copy for the Duke and sent the original to Felipe with these lines: “Don Felipe, your single-hearted devotion is met by a blind confidence. Time will show whether this is to treat a man as more than human.”
It was a rich reward—both promise and challenge—given without fully committing. If his reply included “Dear Louise!” or even “Louise,” he would be lost!
Tuesday.
No, he is not lost. The constitutional minister is perfect as a lover. Here is his letter, in essence:
Every moment away from me was filled with ideal pictures; his eyes closed to the world, fixed on my image. My portrait is a talisman; my painted blue eyes haunt him. If he delayed writing, it was because he could not tear himself from my presence, which wrung from him secrets he struggled to keep.
Closeted with my likeness, he surrendered to complete happiness for the first time. He speaks of placing me between the Virgin and God in his thoughts, of an agony he will not voice because a single altered glance from me would be torture. “Queen of my life and soul,” he calls me, longing for but a fraction of the love he bears.
He describes doubt that wrecks him, like a criminal awaiting a verdict. A smile from me alone could calm his dread of displeasing me. From birth, no one—even his mother—smiled on him; the woman meant for him rejected him for his brother. In politics he has suffered—thwarted, distrusted, regarded almost as an enemy. To be loved had become a dream lost to him until I appeared.
Knowing his graceless exterior and the odds against him, he joined my service because devotion alone could justify his passion. Looking at my portrait alternates hope with fear: hope that, seeing his constancy, I might yield; fear that I will never love him. He promises that if his daring offends, he will return to being my humble servant; but if I consent to try to love him, he begs mercy in breaking it gently to one who has placed his happiness in serving me.
My dear, reading this, he rose before me—pale, humble, but full of a tenderness that was not the ordinary flourish of lovers. It was the breath of happiness.
The weather has been atrocious; impossible to go to the Bois without arousing suspicion. Even my mother, who often goes out regardless of rain, remains at home.
Wednesday evening.
I have just seen him at the Opera; he was another man. He came to our box, introduced by the Sardinian ambassador.
Having read in my eyes that his audacity was well taken, he seemed awkward and addressed the Marquise d’Espard as “mademoiselle.” A light brighter than the chandeliers flashed from his eyes. He left with the uncertain air of a man unsure of his next move.
“The Baron de Macumer is in love!” exclaimed Mme. de Maufrigneuse.
“Strange, isn’t it, for a fallen minister?” replied my mother.
Outwardly I kept my composure, regarding Mmes. de Maufrigneuse and d’Espard and my mother as if they spoke a foreign language; inwardly my soul rose in waves of intoxicating joy. There is only one word for what I felt: rapture. Such love as Felipe’s makes him worthy of mine. I am the very breath of his life; my hands guide his thoughts. I have a mad longing to see him clear every obstacle and come boldly for my hand. Then I would know whether this storm would calm at a glance from me.
Ah, my dear, I stopped here, and I am still trembling. As I wrote, I heard a noise outside and rose to look. From my window I saw him coming along the wall at the risk of his life. I went to the bedroom window and made him a sign; it was enough. He leaped from the wall—ten feet—and then ran along the road, showing me he was not hurt. That he thought of my fear moved me so much that I am still crying; I don’t know why. Poor ungainly man! What was he coming for? What had he to say to me?
I dare not write my thoughts and will go to bed joyful, imagining all we would say if together. Farewell, fair silent one. I have no time to scold you for not writing; it has been over a month since I heard from you! Does this mean you are finally happy? Have you lost the “complete independence” you were so proud of, which nearly played me false tonight?
If love is life’s essence, why is it excluded from marriage? Society demands women sacrifice for family, breeding marital discord. Anticipating this, men built defenses against women; without those defenses, theories justifying female subordination could be overturned by love or aversion. Marriage now embodies opposing forces that the law should reconcile. Your letter challenges the foundations laid by the sage of Aveyron, under whose logic I once sought comfort: laws made by men accept conjugal love without passion, allowing women to yield without desire, reflecting a Nature that emphasizes procreation.
Once a person, I now feel like property. I have swallowed many tears alone, aching for a comforting smile. Why are our fates so different? You thrive in lawful love where virtue aligns with pleasure, while I face an uncertain future.
You are loved and adored. Embrace the noble romance we long dreamed of. Your beauty—refined, spiritual—was made to enchant. Guard your heart’s secret and test Felipe’s love—and more importantly, test your own. Novelty and the illusion of happiness easily mislead.
Remain independent, dear friend; do not rush into marriage without assurance. In intimate conversation, a gesture or word can reveal hidden depths. You have the courage to explore where others falter.
I watch you anxiously from afar; my heart is in sync with yours. Write to me about everything; your letters ignite passion in my otherwise mundane life. I oscillate between despair and hope—perhaps expecting too much. In youth we often believe ideals and reality will align.
Seated beneath a rock in my park, I concluded love in marriage is a fortunate accident, not a universal rule. The philosopher rightly views the family as society’s unit, placing women in subservience as history shows. The solution to our dilemma lies in motherhood, which I yearn for to channel my energy and heart.
Louis remains cheerful; his love is active while mine is passive. He enjoys the flowers of my affection without acknowledging the toil behind them. I play the devoted mother, sacrificing for his happiness, and that sacrifice gives me joy. I await the rewards of my efforts, hoping motherhood will fulfill my aspirations and compensate for what I surrender.
---
Dear wedded friend, your letter arrived as I contemplated a bold step. A strange yearning for the unknown revealed the conflict between societal and natural laws within me. I want to speak with Felipe alone, under the lime trees in our garden. This suits the modern girl I’ve become, my father observes.
There is method in this seeming madness: I wish to reward Felipe for his nights beneath my window while testing his reaction to my boldness. If he respects my folly, he may imagine me as his wife; if not, farewell, Don Felipe.
Meeting him this way seems less risky than encounters in society with prying eyes. I longed for your company to share the anticipation of our first clandestine meeting. I sent Felipe a tracing of the garden key with a note urging him to stop his reckless behavior, warning that if he fell it would tarnish my reputation. Yesterday I took Griffith to the garden, and after a delicious moment of tension, I saw Felipe approach like a shadow.
When he reached the garden, I introduced him to Griffith, who withdrew a few steps. The night was mild and heavy with scent; I felt intoxicated by the thrill of being almost alone with him. We savored the moment before I urged him to abandon his reckless climbing, fearing scandal. His pallor revealed fear; he apologized, and I reassured him my actions were not as mad as they seemed. He expressed joy and reverence for me, acknowledging that my actions were sacred. I told him I was on the path to loving the man who understands me, but that my heart would belong only to someone who truly knows it. He expressed gratitude for my acceptance; I noted he should prove himself worthy of my affection.
Once again I met Felipe; he appeared timid and reverent, which I found appealing. He seemed to sense my power, yet remained unaware of the depth of my feelings. I am still fond of him, but I keep my composure.
Love appears in two forms: one that commands and one that obeys. Can both coexist? Will Felipe ever become my master? These questions unsettle me. Suspense has thrilled me, but now fulfillment seems elusive. Imagination knows no bounds, yet pleasure has limits. How can a woman reconcile these two aspects of her existence?
I am displeased with you. If you did not weep over Racine's Berenice and feel it as a profound tragedy, we have no connection and should part. Answer me satisfactorily, or I will forget you; you will be M. le Baron de Macumer to me, or cease to exist.
At Mme. d'Espard's yesterday your self-satisfied air disgusted me and your confidence alarms me. You have lost the humility of your first letter; instead of the absent-mindedness of a lover you polished epigrams. That is not the behavior of a true believer, who should stand in awe of his divinity.
If you do not see me as the very breath of your life, a being above others, then I must be less than a woman in your eyes. You have bred a spirit of mistrust that drowns my tenderness. Reflecting on our past, I feel justified in my suspicions. Understand, Prime Minister of all the Spains, that I have contemplated the vulnerable state of our sex. My innocence has lit my path, and I have emerged unscathed.
Let me repeat what youth taught me: deceit and broken promises are often punished, but love answers only to its own law—the victim is accuser and judge. The bitterest betrayals remain secret between two hearts; how can the wounded reveal them without wounding herself? Love gives its own justice, which the world ignores.
I have resolved never to forgive a serious offense; in love, what is not serious? Yesterday you looked like a man who had already succeeded. If that certainty has stripped you of the charming uncertainty that once characterized you, I blame you. I want you neither timid nor presumptuous; I do not want you to fear losing my affection—that would be an insult—yet I also do not want you to treat your love lightly. Your heart should never be freer than mine. If you cannot grasp the torment a single stab of doubt can inflict, beware the lesson I might teach you.
In a single glance I entrusted my heart to you, and you understood it. The purest feelings of a young girl are yours. My reflections have enriched my mind; yet if a wounded heart seeks counsel from reason, it may ally itself with darker knowledge and daring.
I swear to you, Felipe: if you love me as I believe, and if I sense even the least decline in the fear, obedience, and respect you have shown, or if the pure flame that ignited my heart grows dim, expect no reproaches. I would not burden you with letters of weakness, pride, or rage. But if I became silent, my face would announce that death was near. I would not perish, however, until I had branded you with infamy and sown eternal sorrow in your heart; you would behold the girl you loved dishonored and lost, condemned to suffering.
Do not make me envy the old, happy Louise, the object of your pure worship, whose heart thrived in happiness—Senza brama, sicura ricchezza! I have searched the Inferno for the most terrible punishment, a mental torture to link with divine vengeance.
Yesterday, as I observed you, doubt pierced me like a cold dagger. I mistrusted you, and the pain was unbearable. If my service is too exacting, leave it; I would not retain you. I do not need proof of your cleverness. Keep your wit for me. Do not present a polished façade to others for flattery or praise. Come to me burdened with scorn or hatred, the target of calumny, told that no woman loves you; then you will discover the treasures of Louise's heart.
We are truly rich only when our wealth lies so deep the world could trample it without knowing. If you were merely handsome, I doubt I would have noticed you. We understand little of why we love; one cause I reveal: your character and individuality give meaning to your face, a mystery to all but me. My love transforms you into the most lovable of men, and that is why I wish to keep your mental gifts for myself. To others they should be as meaningless as your eyes and features. Let it be mine alone to ignite your intelligence, as I bring light to your eyes.
I envision you as a proud Spanish grandee, cold and haughty, a monument to be admired from a distance—ruins of barbaric power—rather than a figure of Parisian affability. Have you forgotten my ideal portrait? Your excessive cheerfulness betrayed your love. Had I not restrained you, you would have revealed in the sharpest salons of Paris that your inspiration came from Armande‑Louise‑Marie de Chaulieu.
I believe too much in your greatness to think your love is merely strategic; yet if you did not show childlike simplicity with me, I could only pity you. Despite this fault, you are still deeply admired by LOUISE DE CHAULIEU.
When God sees our faults, He also sees our repentance. My beloved mistress, you are right; I thought I had displeased you but did not know why. Now that you have explained, I find new reasons to adore you. Like the God of Israel, your jealousy is a holy and precious sentiment. Be jealous of your servant, Louise; the more you strike, the more contrite I will be, proving my devotion to you.
If the effort to overcome my timidity was invisible to you, will Heaven reward it? It cost me much to show you my true self, as I was before love. In Madrid I was thought a good talker; I wanted you to see whatever gifts I had. If that was vanity, I have been punished.
Your last glance unnerved me. I quailed more than when I faced danger at Cadiz. I sought the cause of your displeasure; the lack of sympathy between us was intolerable. I wish only to act by your will, think your thoughts, and share your joys and sorrows. The torment lay in a breach of the feelings I believed we shared.
"I have vexed her!" I repeated, distraught. Noble, beautiful Louise, your words have illuminated my feelings. If this is chastisement, what sweetness lies in your rewards?
To be your servant is happiness enough. You have given me life I thought lost. I no longer draw a useless breath; I have something to devote myself to, even suffering for you. I will always remain as when I first offered myself as your bondman. If you were dishonored, my heart would cling to you even more, seeking to heal your wounds and pray for your innocence.
Did I not tell you my feelings are more than a lover’s? I will be your father, mother, sister, brother—anything or nothing, as you wish. If the father and brother sometimes fade behind the lover, they are still there, though hidden. If you could read my heart when you appear radiant in beauty, you would know how free from selfishness my pride is when I hear praises of your loveliness. When you greet me, I feel God’s blessing on me, and the joy lingers long after.
Can you be unaware of how you are loved? After seeing you I return to my study; the glitter of its ornaments pales before your portrait. I lose myself in thought, linking my happiness to verse. Have you never heard the whispers of my prayers reaching you?
Through silent contemplation of your face I have deciphered every feature and its connection to your spirit's grace, and I have penned sonnets in Spanish about your dual beauty—unfit perhaps for your eyes, yet constant offerings. Not a moment passes without thoughts of you; my whole being is bound to you, and without you I would ache.
Now, Louise, can you realize the torture of knowing I displeased you without knowing why? My ideal life was cut short, and I feared you had ceased to love me. Your letter turned my despair into joy. Oh, might I listen forever to such chiding!
Once, a child, after falling, turned to his mother and said, "Forgive me." Hiding his hurt, he sought pardon for the pain he caused her. Louise, I am that child, and I remain so. Here is the key to my character, which I humbly place in your hands.
But fear not, there will be no more stumbling. Keep tight the chain that binds me to you, so that a touch may convey your lightest wish to your devoted slave, FELIPE.
---
My dear friend, how can you, who married a broken-down invalid to mother him, understand the shifting drama of love, where death lies in a glance or a light reply?
I reserved one last test for Felipe, to see whether his love was as blind and unwavering as a Royalist’s for his King. He walked with me all night without suspicion; the next day he loved me more, with reverence and humility. He is a true Spaniard, scaling my wall to kiss my hand from my balcony, risking his life.
But this is nothing; Christians suffer for heaven. I spoke to my father, the royal ambassador-to-be, with a smile and a jest: friends say he is marrying Armande to an ambassador's nephew for wealth and title. Your daughter weeps but bows to her father's authority. Some say she hides a worldly soul behind her tears.
We are going to the gentleman's box at the Opera tonight; M. le Baron de Macumer will visit us there.
"Macumer needs a spur," my father said, smiling.
"You mistake Clarissa Harlowe for Figaro!" I replied scornfully. "When you see me with my right hand ungloved, you will mark your displeasure at this gossip."
"I can rest easy about your future. You have no more a girl's head than Jeanne d'Arc had a woman's heart. You will be happy, love no one, and allow yourself to be loved."
This was too much; I laughed.
"What is it, little flirt?" he asked. "I tremble for my country's interests..." I added, with a smile, "At Madrid!"
"You have no idea how this little nun has learned to make fun of her father," he told the Duchess.
"Armande makes light of everything," my mother replied.
"What do you mean?" I asked.
"You are not even afraid of rheumatism on these damp nights," she said, glancing at me meaningfully.
"Oh!" I answered, "the mornings are so hot!"
"It's high time she were married," my father said.
"If you wish it," I replied demurely.
Two hours later my mother, the Duchesse de Maufrigneuse, and Mme. d'Espard bloomed in the box. I sat aside, unseen, to observe.
Macumer arrived and put up his opera-glasses to look at me. In the first interval the Comte Henri de Marsay, always ready with an epigram, entered smiling. He greeted my mother and the others, then turned to me: "I do not know whether I shall be the first to congratulate you on an event that will make you the envy of many."
"Ah! a marriage!" I cried. "Is it left for me, a girl fresh from the convent, to say that predicted marriages never come off?"
M. de Marsay whispered to Macumer; I sensed he warned him about me. Macumer met him with noble scorn, saying he was "not in love with any little coquette." His demeanor delighted me; I caught sight of my father ready to act.
Felipe showed no fear or doubt. His faith is in me; society’s lies cannot hurt him. Not a muscle of his face stirred.
The two young counts left, and I laughed at Macumer: "M. de Marsay has been treating you to an epigram on me."
"He did more," he replied. "It was an epithalamium."
"You speak Greek to me," I said, smiling.
My father spoke with Mme. de Maufrigneuse about the scandalous gossip surrounding me; he intended to take a turn to avoid suggesting the marriage. The Duchess and Mme. d'Espard exchanged glances—women scent love like perfume.
Having enjoyed Felipe’s composed response, I signaled him to come to my window by the dangerous route. Later I found him, upright as a statue, studying the light in my room.
"My dear Felipe," I said, "You acquitted yourself well tonight; you behaved as I would have if told you were marrying."
"I thought you would hardly tell others before me."
"And what right have you to this privilege?"
"The right of one who is your devoted slave."
"In truth?"
"I am, and shall ever remain so."
"But suppose this marriage was inevitable; suppose I had agreed..."
His eyes flashed, measuring the danger. A power stronger than passion held him back.
"An Arab cannot take back his word," he said. "I am your slave; my life is not mine to destroy."
I placed my hand on his as I said, "Felipe, my beloved, from this moment I am your wife in thought and will. Go in the morning to ask my father for my hand. He wishes to retain my fortune, but if you acknowledge receipt of it in the contract, his consent will be given. I am no longer Armande de Chaulieu. Leave me; no breath of scandal must touch Louise de Macumer."
He listened, pale and trembling, then cleared the ten feet to the ground in safety. It was agonizing, but he waved goodbye and disappeared.
"I am loved then," I thought, "as never woman was before." I fell asleep, content, my destiny fixed.
The next day my father summoned me to his private room; I found the Duchess and Macumer. After civilities I said that if my father and M. Henarez agreed, I had no reason to oppose their wishes. My mother invited the Baron to dinner; we drove in the Bois de Boulogne, and I smiled ironically at M. de Marsay as he passed.
Felipe has had his cards reprinted as follows:
HENAREZ
(Baron de Macumer, formerly Duc de Soria.)
Every morning he brings me a splendid bouquet, hidden within which is a letter containing a Spanish sonnet in my honor.
To keep this letter concise, I send you only the first and last of these sonnets, translated for your benefit.
FIRST SONNET (essence)
I have braved bulls and battles, scaled redoubts, staked life on dice. Yet before your sweet eye my courage deserts me; I tremble, shrink, and lose my nerve as a child with ghosts.
SECOND SONNET (essence)
I sought sleep to dream of thee, but jealous wakefulness kept me on the balcony. Heaven itself seemed robbed of color and light by thy beauty: the moon’s silver and the stars’ sparkle appear pale beside you.
What a gracious way to tell a young girl she fills a man’s life! Tell me what you think of this love, which lavishes earth and soul. Only in these ten days have I understood Spanish gallantry.
Ah, dear, what is happening at La Crampade? How often I stroll there, inspecting our crops! Do you have news of our mulberry trees and last winter's plantations? Does everything prosper as you wish? While buds open on our shrubs, have they also blossomed in the bosom of the wife? Does Louis continue his madrigals? Do you enter each other's thoughts? I wonder if your wedding peace is better than the raging torrent of my love! Has my sweet lady professor taken offense? If so, I would send Felipe post-haste to plead for my pardon—or her head. Sweet love, my life here is a splendid success, and I want to know how it fares with life in Provence. We have just added a Spaniard with the complexion of a Havana cigar to our family, and your congratulations still await.
Seriously, my sweet Renée, I am anxious. I fear you may be eating your heart out in silence, afraid to cast a gloom over my sunshine. Write to me at once, naughty child! Tell me your life in every detail; tell me whether you still hold back, whether your "independence" stands erect, or has fallen on its knees, or is sitting comfortably, which would indeed be serious. Can you suppose the incidents of your married life are without interest for me? I muse over all you have said. Often, at the Opera, while absorbed in watching the dancers, I think, "It is half-past nine; perhaps she is in bed. What is she doing? Is she happy? Is she alone with her independence, or has it gone the way of other dead and cast-off independences?"
A thousand loves.
---
Saucy girl! Why should I write? What could I say? While your life is varied by social festivities and the passions of love, mine is as monotonous as a convent.
We go to bed at nine and rise with daybreak. Meals are served with maddening punctuality. Nothing ever happens. I have adjusted to this routine; perhaps such fixed laws give the world stability.
I have set rules of dress, which occupy my mornings. I feel it is my duty as a wife to look charming, and it pleases both Louis and the good old man. After lunch we walk; when newspapers arrive I disappear to manage household affairs or read—often writing to you. I return before dinner; after, we play cards or receive visits. Thus my days pass between a contented old man, who has done with passions, and the man who owes his happiness to me. Louis’ joy warms my heart.
For women, happiness cannot consist solely in satisfying desire. In the evenings, when I can relax, I imagine your life, its riches and passions, and wonder what such a stormy preface can lead to. May I not swallow the book itself? For you, my darling, love's illusions are possible; for me, only the facts of homely life remain. Your love seems a dream!
I find it hard to understand why you insist on romanticizing it. Your ideal man must have more soul than fire, more nobility and self-command than passion. You demand sacrifices to be rewarded by them, testing Felipe to see if desire, hope, and curiosity endure. But behind your stage scenery lies the altar where everlasting bonds are forged. The day after your marriage, the graceful structure raised by your strategy may fall before the reality that transforms a girl into a woman, a gallant into a husband. There is no exemption for lovers; for them as for ordinary folk like Louis and me, the great "Perhaps" of Rabelais lurks beneath wedding rejoicings.
I do not blame you for your rashness in speaking with Felipe in the garden or spending a night with him; but you play with life, and I fear it may turn the tables. I dare not give you the counsel my experience suggests, but let me repeat from my valley that the viaticum of married life lies in resignation and self-sacrifice. Despite your tests, I see that marriage will mean to you what it has meant to me. The greater the passion, the steeper the precipice we have hewn for our fall—that is the only difference.
Oh, how I wish to see the Baron de Macumer and talk with him! Your happiness lies so near my heart.
---
As Felipe has acknowledged my fortune, my mother has become friendlier, calling me sly-boots and a woman of the world.
"But, dear mama," I said before the contract was signed, "you attribute cunning to what is truly the outcome of the most unselfish love! I assure you I am not the 'woman of the world' you think."
"Come, Armande," she said, drawing me close, "you did not want to return to the convent or die an old maid. Like a noble Chaulieu, you recognized the need to build up your father's family."
I glanced at my father and said, "I love Felipe de Soria with all my soul. Though this love sprang up without my knowledge, I swear I never yielded until I recognized in him a character worthy of mine."
"But, my dear," she interrupted, "he is as ugly as..."
"As anything you like," I retorted, "but I love his ugliness."
"If you love him, Armande," my father said, "and can master your love, do not risk your happiness. Happiness in marriage depends largely on the first days—"
"Days only?" my mother interrupted. "You had better leave us to talk."
"You are to be married in three days," the Duchess began, "so I must give you the serious advice every mother owes her daughter. You are marrying a man you love, and there is no reason for pity. I have only known you for a year, but it is long enough to love you. You have gratified maternal pride and shown yourself a sweet daughter. I promise you will always find a staunch friend in me."
"You smile? Alas! it often happens that a mother who has lived well with her daughter as a girl comes to cross purposes with her as women."
