DEDICATION
To Madame la Comtesse Bolognini.
Madame, remembering our pleasant talks in Milan, I dedicate this work to you, trusting you will shelter it with your name as you once protected a beloved writer’s tales. You have a daughter, Eugénie, whose beauty and wit promise a happier upbringing than the rigid one of the Eugénie here. Though called fickle, I remain Italian in constancy and memory; writing “Eugénie” revives Milan’s warmth and makes you a distant, treasured masterpiece.
Your devoted servant,
De Balzac.
A DAUGHTER OF EVE
CHAPTER I. THE TWO MARIES
In a lavish blue-velvet boudoir on rue Neuve-des-Mathurins, two young women—Madame du Tillet and her sister Madame Félix de Vandenesse—weep by the fire. The room belongs to Ferdinand du Tillet, a wealthy banker who has bought social standing but lacks aristocratic ease. Reared by a strict, pious mother and married without youthful freedom, both entered marriages innocent of passion; what they imagined as escape reveals unforeseen realities.
CHAPTER II. A CONFIDENCE BETWEEN SISTERS
Madame de Vandenesse confides marital despair: a husband indifferent to her inner life. Madame du Tillet, equally helpless, describes similar cold domesticity. Both crave passion and excitement suppressed by society and duty, and find consolation in each other.
CHAPTER III. THE HISTORY OF A FORTUNATE WOMAN
Félix de Vandenesse, of noble lineage, married hoping to revive an idealized love. The duties and constraints of his class cool their relationship; though kind, he cannot supply the romantic intensity his wife desires. Monotony awakens in her longing for deeper emotional fulfillment and thoughts of love beyond marriage.
CHAPTER IV. A CELEBRATED MAN
Raoul Nathan, an ambitious literary figure with talent, audacity, and biting wit, attracts Madame de Vandenesse at Madame de Montcornet’s salon. Their exchanges suggest an unspoken attraction. Nathan, balancing career struggles and private troubles, sees her as a possible muse though social decorum forbids an open liaison.
CHAPTER V. FLORINE
Florine, a gifted actress and Nathan’s long companion, sustains his spirits and career while depending on him for protection. As Nathan’s admiration for Madame de Vandenesse grows, Florine senses the shift and grows jealous.
CHAPTER VI. ROMANTIC LOVE
Nathan and Madame de Vandenesse bond over art and literature; their meetings grow intimate, bringing exhilaration and peril. She is torn between guilt, loyalty, and the lure of a passionate life, aware of social consequences.
CHAPTER VII. SUICIDE
Crushed by setbacks, Nathan contemplates suicide. A chance meeting with Madame de Vandenesse revives him; seeing his despair, she resolves to protect and encourage him.
CHAPTER VIII. A LOVER SAVED AND LOST
After his crisis she becomes more involved. Their secret liaison is both strength and turmoil as social risk and Florine’s jealousy complicate matters. Nathan, improving in fortune, is torn between Florine’s practical devotion and the countess’s inspiration.
CHAPTER IX. THE HUSBAND’S TRIUMPH
Félix, sensing his wife’s attachment, moves to preserve their marriage. Determined to uncover the truth and shield her from scandal, he acts to defend her honor and their union, setting desire against duty.

######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
On a Sunday morning in April 1813 a luxurious cabriolet drew up before the Tuileries carrying an anxious, aged father and his beautiful daughter, Julie. Proud of the attention she attracted, he accompanied her to the gardens to see the military review commanded by Napoleon—the last before a disastrous campaign. Though he urged them to leave, Julie, afraid of missing the Emperor, insisted on staying. An officer admitted them to a prime vantage point. The spectacle—the Old Guard preparing, the crowd’s buzz, the mounting anticipation—was magnificent. When Napoleon appeared, cheers rose. Julie was captivated, but her eye soon fixed on a young officer on the Emperor’s staff, Colonel Victor d’Aiglemont. A pang of jealousy and an unexpected attraction seized her, while her father’s anxious manner reminded her of their precarious situation. As the review ended, he warned her that youthful dreams could lead to sorrow and advised against falling for a man like Victor, whom he deemed unworthy. Despite his cautions, Julie felt drawn to Victor and their attachment deepened.
Nearly a year later, in March 1814, Julie and Victor were traveling together. The once-vibrant girl had become a shadow; her health faltered as Victor, now a colonel, was absorbed in military duties and later in adapting to the shifting political scene. Isolated and yearning for affection, her need grew while Victor’s attention waned. During their travels they encountered an Englishman, Arthur, detained in France. Julie felt an unexpected kinship with him; Arthur’s quiet admiration and gentleness contrasted sharply with Victor’s indifference, and she was drawn to him as a steady presence. Torn between marital duty and desire, she wrestled with feelings that deepened her inner conflict.
As Napoleon fell and the political landscape shifted, Victor’s ambitions altered—he grew more involved with the new regime and increasingly self-interested. When his infidelities surfaced, Julie’s heartache deepened; she recognized his selfishness and felt the weight of betrayal. In desperation she sought solace in Arthur, though the moral and social consequences pressed upon her. Trapped in a loveless marriage and isolated, she resolved to refuse passivity and defy societal expectations to reclaim her happiness. The account is a poignant exploration of love, sacrifice, duty, and the quest for fulfillment.
Between the Seine and the Loing lies a flat, dreary country edged by the Forest of Fontainebleau, its southern limits marked by Moret, Montereau, and Nemours. A monotonous horizon breeds melancholy. At its center stands the old château of Saint-Lange, once a sign of wealth now diminished.
In late 1820 a young woman known in Paris for her beauty and wit arrived at Saint-Lange, startling villagers unaccustomed to gentry. The Marquise d’Aiglemont’s frailty and withdrawal fueled gossip: some blamed her husband’s gambling in Spain and feared the estate’s sale.
The Marquise lived in isolation, rarely leaving her room and only dining briefly with her daughter. At twenty-six she bore a profound, unacknowledged grief that had extinguished maternal instinct and made her feel like a ghost. Her anguish sprang from a lost love—a man who had died to preserve her honor—and from social demands that forbade open mourning. Youth and beauty meant little without the true love she had imagined; her respectable marriage offered no emotional fulfillment.
One day the village priest, M. le Curé, visited and, sensing her suffering, spoke of the universality of grief and the sustaining power of faith. At first she dismissed him, convinced her pain unique. His tale of a bereaved father placed her sorrow in a broader context and offered unexpected solace. Gradually she confided in him, revealing her emptiness: she saw her daughter Hélène not as the object of maternal love but as a living reminder of the lost man; duty, not affection, defined their relationship. The priest urged her to find strength in duty, but she wrestled with inadequacy and bitterness toward social constraints that stifled her true self.
Spring’s work on the estate brought temporary reprieve. By October she left Saint-Lange with some regained vitality, though scars remained; she encountered the curé again but avoided him, signaling a desire to move forward without the château’s painful memories. Her quiet struggle—between public expectation and private desire, duty and identity—reveals the costs of rigid social structures and the burdens placed on women.
At Madame Firmiani’s ball, M. Charles de Vandenesse, a promising young diplomat nearing thirty and about to leave Paris for the Congress of Laybach, took his leave. Accustomed to a transient life, he regarded existence philosophically, preferring ambition to romance and finding Parisian society shallow. Madame Firmiani introduced him to the enigmatic Marquise d’Aiglemont, also thirty, whose delicate beauty hid a deep melancholy. Vandenesse noted the contrast between her sensibility and her dull, self-interested husband; beneath her calm he sensed unfulfilled dreams.
Their conversation moved from banter to talk of love and feeling, creating an immediate bond that both excited and disturbed him. He asked to visit again; she soon occupied his thoughts. Their meetings blurred friendship and unspoken desire, forcing Vandenesse to face the consequences of loving a married woman. The Marquise was drawn to him but torn by loyalty to the memory of a deceased lover and by social constraint. As their intimacy deepened, obstacles—her past, social expectations, his ambitions—produced moments of joy and anguish. A tender kiss marked a turning point, only to be shattered by her husband’s intrusion. Their bond showed love’s power to cross boundaries yet remained weighed down by duty and unfulfilled longing.
Between the Barrière d’Italie and the Barrière de la Santé, the boulevard toward the Jardin des Plantes presents a striking Parisian scene: a tree-lined avenue dropping into a valley where factories and the brown Bièvre mingle with greenery; the squalor of Faubourg Saint-Marceau set against the domes of the Panthéon and Val-de-Grâce; the canal Saint-Martin beside lime trees and granaries; Belleville’s smoke-streaked slopes rising toward the clouds. In sunlight whole quarters seem briefly at peace.
One morning, while admiring this view and brooding over wealthy travelers who mocked France, I was startled by a sudden kiss. A neatly dressed young woman, a handsome man and a lively little boy shared a moment; nearby stood a second child, a dark-haired, grave girl of seven or eight, apart and scowling. As the couple played the girl’s indifference hardened; when the boy approached she pushed him and he fell into the river and vanished. The mother’s frantic grief and the knowledge that the daughter’s act would forever stain the family left an indelible mark.
Years later the now Marquis de Vandenesse, mourning his father, hosted a dinner dominated by a notary who monopolized conversation. The Marquise, anxious to leave for the theatre with their children, hinted repeatedly, but the notary persisted. When the husband returned with the upset children, the notary’s thoughtless remarks heightened tensions; the Marquise sent her daughter away and the Marquis demanded the notary leave. The notary later dismissed the evening as aristocratic whim; his wife saw the deeper truth and warned him to stick to business.
One of Napoleon’s orderly staff-officers, the General (or Marquis), after making a fortune during the Restoration, bought a country house near Montreuil, a former nobleman’s retreat. One winter evening the household was alone: the servants had gone to Versailles for a wedding, and with Christmas revelers still about the General grew uneasy as the clock struck eleven; outside there was only wind and a distant footfall.
Inside, the scene was warm. The rugged soldier sat by the fire while his youngest son resisted his mother’s attempts to undress him, preferring to play with his sister. Laughter filled the room; the Marquise watched them with affection. At a table their thirteen-year-old read the Arabian Nights while Hélène, striking and thoughtful, worked at a tapestry. The children’s play contrasted with the older siblings’ more serious reflections, and the General took simple pleasure in the domestic calm.
Sudden knocks brought a breathless, disheveled man pleading secrecy and water, his hands bloodied from a duel. Despite misgivings the General hid him in a secret room. The Marquise suspected and was rebuffed; Hélène overheard, saw the wounded stranger, and felt an unsettling connection. When gendarmes later arrived seeking the man, the confrontation exposed the moral cost of the General’s choice and the fragility of their happiness.
On a June day in 1844 the Marquise d’Aiglemont, about fifty, walked in her daughter’s Paris garden, pausing by the windows to look toward the dome of the Invalides and her own grounds. Having lost her two sons, Gustave and Abel, she had given her fortune to her daughter, the Comtesse Moïna de Saint-Héreen. Her devotion to Moïna contrasted painfully with the faint memories she kept of her dead children. Society shrugged at the Marquise’s attachment as superstition, ignoring her past sacrifices and her coolness toward her daughter-in-law.
Moïna, absorbed in social life, left the Marquise often alone; her love was unmistakable but shaded by sorrow. Gossip suggested the Marquise might regret the bequest, though most praised her loyalty. She felt eclipsed as Moïna’s youth drew attention and distrusted the Countess’s flirtation with Alfred de Vandenesse, fearing it would end in heartbreak; with the Comte away for months, her warnings were unlikely to be heeded.
Moïna’s growing distance showed in small slights—indifference in conversation, curt replies, loss of respect. Overwhelmed, the Marquise collapsed on a bench and was carried inside; physicians could not save her. Struck by remorse as her mother failed, Moïna realized too late she had taken that love for granted. In the Marquise’s last moments she gave a forgiving smile; Moïna was left hollow with regret.
################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
In spring 1822 Parisian doctors sent M. le Baron Gaston de Nueil to Lower Normandy to recover from an inflammatory illness, probably from overwork. Seeking rest, light diet, and fresh air, he settled in Bayeux with welcoming relatives.
After a few evenings with his cousin, Mme. de Sainte‑Severe, Gaston met the local elite, who embodied provincial French types. One was an ancient but impoverished noble family: a patriarch, a resolute sportsman contemptuous of modernity and the new political order, and a loud, domineering wife who insisted their daughters’ prospects rested on the family name. They clung to obsolete customs—nobility without wealth or influence.
Opposite them stood a wealthier, more recent family attuned to Parisian trends: a society-minded wife a step behind the latest fashions and a shrewd husband on the Departmental Council who managed finances and political gossip. Their Royalism was lukewarm; they embraced modern life while keeping ancestral ties.
The local bishop, a former Vicar‑General, mediated between factions—respected for his office yet often recalled to plebeian origins. Retired officers maintained a veneer of aristocracy; women dressed for court but lived by routine; a few prosperous townspeople were viewed skeptically. Conversation replayed the same themes, judgments were fixed, intellectual life stagnated, and critics were dismissed.
His cousin had mentioned that at twenty‑three Gaston had eighteen thousand livres from Auge valley land and that his father sought more, omitting his elder brother’s consumption. Initially amused sketching their quirks, weeks of monotony left him weary and trapped; ennui and growing discontent threatened to fossilize him among the fossils.
One evening Gaston de Nueil sat between a dowager and a vicar-general in a gray-paneled drawing-room where portraits looked down on four card tables and sixteen people playing whist. After dinner he noticed provincial habits—old cards, threadbare cloths—and a philosophic indifference to luxury that made Paris seem remote.
A remark about Mme. de Beauseant struck him: the Vicomtesse, unwell, had declined an invitation; gossip said she was involved with M. d’Ajuda-Pinto and had retired to Courcelles. Though noble, she was shunned after separating from her husband, becoming for him a bright point amid provincial stagnation.
Obsessed, he wandered near Courcelles, then asked M. de Champignelles to arrange an introduction. The Marquis’s message met resistance but piqued her curiosity; after reflection she agreed to receive him. Excited and anxious, Gaston prepared and, when announced at Courcelles, entered to meet the enigmatic Vicomtesse.
A bright fire burned on the hearth despite mild weather, throwing soft light over a young woman in a high‑backed bergère. The Vicomtesse, graceful as she stirred the coals and picked up a fallen glove, motioned to a chair for M. de Nueil. Her poised, fastidious ease—so unlike the automata he had seen during two months’ exile in Normandy—made him feel transported back to Paris.
Mme. de Beauseant’s fair hair, dark eyes, and pale complexion gave her a regal, almost fallen‑angel air; a crown braid intensified this bearing. Beneath that beauty lay sadness—traces of past unhappiness and lost love. Alone in her silent salon she seemed cut off, haunted by memories of a once‑vibrant youth now emptied. M. de Nueil felt both humbled and captivated by the blend of nobility and misfortune.
When she acknowledged him he was struck by the weight of his deception. He had come to deliver a message from M. de Champignelles, but awareness of his duplicity made him flush. Summoning courage he confessed, “Madame, I do not deserve the happiness of seeing you. I have deceived you basely.” Her manner turned icy; she rang for a servant to escort him out.
As he left he realized that going now would mean never returning and that she would regard him a fool. On impulse he turned back, saying he had forgotten something; the servant followed unaware. A half‑sad smile—“Jacques lighted me to the door”—softened her and she invited him to sit. He pleaded, admitted his blunder, blamed youthful enthusiasm and exile’s weariness, and owned that he had idealized her and intruded foolishly. She rebuked him as ridiculous, insisting she would rather be scorned than pitied, warned of impropriety, and commanded him not to return. He begged indulgence for his fault.
An impassioned voice thawed Mme. de Beauseant’s chill solitude: she was moved by M. de Nueil’s sincerity and captivated by his noble youth. Gaston, in the bloom of life, seemed free of ambition and vanity. Both concealed and reflected on their feelings: he saw in the Vicomtesse not only beauty but depth and goodness; she discerned in him a purity resisting society’s corruptions. His approach was instinctive, hers deliberate—she tested him with opposing opinions and put him at ease with wit and grace, reviving a sense of innocence in herself and offering, in effect, absolution from past shame.
When separation loomed, Mme. de Beauseant, smiling gently, insisted on preserving her solitary life, fearing any tie would tarnish her reputation. She spoke candidly of a forsaken love and the lessons solitude had taught her. Gaston longed to declare himself but feared rejection; torn between admiration and dread of appearing foolish, he finally confessed his love and vowed to heal her old wounds. She, moved yet resolute, insisted they remain strangers. She described the pain of being unable to follow a beloved man through life’s changes and reminded him she could not yield to blind impulse.
Gaston left unsettled, unsure whether her charm was genuine or coquetry. Diffident at twenty-three, he wrote faltering letters, then returned at night to Courcelles and sent a heartfelt plea. Mme. de Beauseant’s reply, sealed with the arms of Burgundy, was plain: she refused his advances—not to wound him but to be candid. Though she cared for him, she did not share his love and would not be moved by youthful passion: she was nearly thirty, he just over twenty, and feelings might change with age. Vows made lightly could become burdens; she feared he might one day resent the sacrifices she required, and she could not bear desertion. Her consolation was in God rather than earthly ties; she could offer only the friendship of an older woman. For both their sakes she urged him to remain in his country and not wreck his life for an illusion.
Gaston answered with fervor: if he could cease to love her and accept an ordinary life, he would deserve his fate; his oath of fidelity could be absolved only by death, and if she feared his remorse she might take his life. When the messenger returned, he reported he had delivered Mme. la Vicomtesse’s reply as she was leaving in a carriage. Realizing she was departing, Gaston followed her to Geneva, convinced that if she truly loved him she would seek the freedom of Switzerland.
Mme. de Beauseant had rented a small house by the lake. One summer evening Gaston surprised her; she dropped her book and embraced him as he explained he had followed her. For three years they lived in the villa on Lake Geneva—alone and apparently in a dreamlike happiness: a simple home, beautiful views, quiet days cherished together. Weighty obligations soon called Gaston back to France after the deaths of his father and brother. They refrained from selling the villa; instead they bought the family house, and Mme. de Beauseant sold her property to purchase an estate near Manerville adjoining Gaston’s lands. His mother took the family house at Manerville, and the lovers resumed the secluded happiness they had known.
For nine years they enjoyed almost indescribable bliss, but a legal obstacle remained: Mme. de Beauseant’s husband, the Marquis, was still alive, so any lawful union was impossible. The situation was precarious: Gaston’s mother, the dowager comtesse, would never accept Mme. de Beauseant; the Marquise feared the dowager’s influence might one day pull Gaston away. Though tempted to sell her estate and return to Geneva, Mme. de Beauseant could not act in a way that might betray distrust.
Their fragile peace was disturbed by the arrival nearby of a young heiress, Mlle. de la Rodiere, and by Gaston’s meeting with her. Mme. de Beauseant replied with a letter that laid bare her turmoil and pride. She confessed she had not known love until she met him and that, for nine years, she had felt no jealousy and had experienced infinite happiness. But now she anguished at the thought that, at forty, she might be a burden to his future: he was thirty and might desire a marriage that would bring children and security. Too proud to bind his future to her own fading life, she urged him to be frank: if he still loved her, he should come after reading the letter and she would love him more than ever; if he sought freedom, she would not display her suffering, because she loved him enough to put his happiness first.
When Mme. de Beauseant learned her letter had reached Gaston she was overwhelmed and stunned into numbness. Reading her words, M. de Nueil found himself in a painful dilemma—torn among passion, duty, pride, and the prospect of a conventional future.
Gaston had almost yielded to his mother’s urgings and to the attraction of Mlle. de la Rodiere, a plain young woman with a forty‑thousand‑livre dowry. Mme. de Nueil praised the respectability of such a match, pointed out that future rents might raise his income to eighty thousand livres, and argued that if Mme. de Beauseant truly loved him she would encourage his marriage. Her pressure left him torn.
Then a letter from Mme. de Beauseant changed everything: Gaston resolved to end their liaison and marry. He told himself “one must live a man’s life,” aware that his choice would wound her. Vanity and conscience deepened his sorrow; he felt real pity. He intended to prepare her gently, at first sacrificing Mlle. de la Rodiere to ease the transition and marrying later to respect Mme. de Beauseant’s wishes. He meant to send a calming letter, though he feared a woman of such perception might be devastated by a mere note.
When Jacques delivered the letter Mme. de Beauseant trembled, dread of loss closing in. If he did not come in person, she feared all was lost; yet a woman in love can endure much. Reading it, an inner cry—“He lies!”—rose within her: the message struck her as calculated, not sincere. In despair she tore it and cast it into the fire, feeling betrayed and abandoned.
M. de Nueil summoned Jacques, who returned with a brief note: “Madame la Marquise has left the chateau.” In Mme. de Nueil’s desk M. de Nueil found his own letter from nine years earlier, in which he had sworn eternal fidelity; at the bottom Claire de Bourgogne had written, “Monsieur, you are free.” Stunned, he went to his mother and, within three weeks, married Mlle. Stéphanie de la Rodiere.
If the tale ended there it might seem trivial, but its notoriety cuts deeply for those who have known love’s joys and losses. After their parting Mme. de Beauseant remained at Valleroy in seclusion, rarely seen except by servants, attending mass daily.
Soon after the wedding M. de Nueil sank into emotional indifference—interpreted by observers as contentment or misery. His wife was amiable but shallow; a month after the wedding she expected a child. Two months on he grew increasingly pensive. Seven months later a small incident revealed deeper turmoil: returning from a hunt he crossed Mme. de Beauseant’s park and had Jacques set aside whatever game he shot for her; for days he came home with an empty bag while Jacques secretly delivered the birds to the Marquise.
He dared to write, but the letter was returned unopened. One evening, while his wife played the piano, he rushed to the Marquise’s room and found her in despair; she threatened to throw herself from the window if he approached. Terrified, he fled. Back home he wrote again, sent his servant, and an hour later the note was returned unopened. In despair M. de Nueil retired and shot himself.
The tragic end, though at odds with fashionable conventions, seems inevitable to those who have experienced a profound soul‑connection. Love requires time and nurture; abandoning deep passion for a loveless, conventional marriage brings either despair or a cold, material existence. Mme. de Beauseant probably did not foresee his extreme act; she may have thought herself the greater sufferer, yet she rightly refused humiliation. A wife may compromise for social reasons, but a mistress cannot submit if the purity of her passion demands respect.
ANGOULEME, September 1832.
ADDENDUM
Characters appearing elsewhere in the Human Comedy: Beauseant, Marquis and Comte de; Beauseant, Marquise de; Beauseant, Vicomte de and Vicomtesse de; De Champignelles; Jacques (M. de Beauseant’s butler); Nueil, Gaston de.
#### MIDDLE OF THE STORY ####

La Grenadiere is a quaint house on the right bank of the Loire, about a mile downstream from the bridge of Tours. The broad river, dotted with green islands, runs between cliffs topped by white-stone country houses, gardens and terraced vineyards warmed by southern exposure. A church spire marks Saint-Cyr; further on the Choisille joins the Loire.
Halfway up the hillside and a hundred paces from the church, La Grenadiere—an old Touraine house of two to three centuries—is reached by a fissure in the rock with steps down to the dike of the Paris–Nantes causeway. A gate opens onto a narrow footpath between terraces; trees partly conceal the house until the river view widens. A crumbling Gothic arch leads into a small garden hewn from rock beside a wooden summer-house draped in jessamine and honeysuckle. Vine-clad steps lead to an arched doorway into cellars carved in the rock. Trellised vines and pomegranate trees frame the yellowwashed front with two large windows, a rustic door, three dormers in a steep slate roof, and green shutters.
Inside, a crooked staircase rises to a lobby; to the right a large dining room with white tile floors, to the left a sitting room with saffron walls and exposed walnut rafters. The first floor has two whitewashed bedrooms with simple stone chimneypieces. Most openings face south; a later timber-framed kitchen with a pump and well suggests the house began as a vintner’s retreat.
Two acres of vineyard, a steep back slope and a vinedresser’s cottage adjoin the western gable. Stone walls and espaliers maximize the land while neglected corners fill with figs, wildflowers and strawberries. Modest yet dignified, La Grenadiere yields grapes, figs, peaches and melons; Spanish broom and Italian oleanders add exotic notes. With the Loire below and Tours and its châteaux in view, it remains a peaceful retreat for lovers and dreamers, where river sounds invite quiet contemplation among perennial flowers and fruit.
If an Englishman rents La Grenadiere for the summer he pays a thousand francs for six months; orchard fruit doubles the rent, and the vintage doubles it again. With a stone staircase, triple terrace, two acres of vineyard, flowering roses, well-head and a view over three valleys of Touraine and cathedral towers, its health and beauty seem beyond price. Unsold since 1690 when it fetched forty thousand francs, it has remained a cherished family possession.
In a bright Restoration spring Mme. Willemsens arrived in Tours with her housekeeper and two children, thirteen and eight, and chose La Grenadiere—perhaps because it lay some distance from town. She turned the drawing-room into her bedroom, gave the children the two rooms above, and had the housekeeper sleep in a closet. The dining-room served as sitting and drawing room, simply furnished in plain walnut; the house’s charm lay in neatness and harmony rather than luxury.
Mme. Willemsens resisted easy classification: her plain dress invited assumptions while polished manners suggested higher rank. Tall and slender with delicate features and a melancholy expression, her dark eyes sometimes revealed secret anguish and premature wrinkles. She always wore a long black gown with a silk ribbon and an English-style bonnet, a sign of mourning. Her reserve provoked local curiosity; she visited Tours only twice—to inquire about teachers and arrange lessons—and her rare appearances on the bridge attracted attention. The owner said she signed the lease as Augusta Willemsens, Countess of Brandon, implying her husband’s name; the remark circulated.
Natural manners and a sweet voice charmed the local elite, but her sadness and beauty kept her untouchable. Several young men fell in love yet kept away; she burned all letters, underscoring her wish for solitude.
The three tutors admired the close bond between Mme. Willemsens and her children. The boys resembled their mother—Louis-Gaston dark-haired and robust, Marie-Gaston almost golden and delicate—with contrasting temperaments; Louis was protective. Well-behaved and attentive, they kept a scrupulous routine of rising early and prayer. Their interactions with their mother were affectionate and intuitive, though Louis often noticed small signs of her declining strength and remained watchful.
Mme. Willemsens dressed during her children’s early breakfast and play to be a pleasing sight for them. From ten until three she listened to their lessons, breaking for a midday meal in the summer-house where they all ate together. After lunch the children played while she rested on a sofa, enjoying the Touraine landscape.
They ranged through the orchard and garden, chasing lizards and studying plants and insects, often returning with eager questions. Mme. Willemsens embroidered during lessons but followed them attentively, encouraging her eldest, Louis, and taking little notice of little Marie. She walked tutors to the gate and frankly queried Louis’ progress; they reported his strengths and weaknesses honestly.
Their days balanced work and play, leaving little room for quarrels. Her steady love smoothed difficulties and taught her sons moderation and submission. She often wept for joy watching them, treasuring the laughter in the garden-house. Thanks to their English nurse they spoke both languages, and their mother guided their learning with engaging biographies of great figures to teach ambition and success.
Little Marie often fell asleep on her mother’s knee. Afterwards Mme. Willemsens would grow pensive and tearful; one evening she confessed fears of leaving them alone and without resources. Louis, puzzled, promised to care for Marie. She reassured him that love sustained her and hoped Touraine’s air might aid recovery.
As she spoke more of possible death, Louis adjusted the household to her melancholy. The lively home grew quieter; the old servant Annette watched with concern as Mme. Willemsens’ health declined. By August she remained beautiful but frail; in autumn she spent nearly all her time with the children, while Louis, encouraged by his mother, prepared for the École Polytechnique, listened to a retired naval officer’s tales, walked, climbed and learned to swim, his sunburned face showing renewed purpose.
When October came, Mme. Willemsens could only rise at noon. The Loire’s warmth, likened to the Bay of Naples and advised by the faculty, softened her days. At midday she sat in the shade with her two children, Louis and Marie, who never left her. After lessons the elder lay beside her while restless Marie gathered flowers. With tired eyes and languid movements, she smiled at them—an autumnal, poignant scene.
The doctor soon confined her to her room, brightened daily by favorite flowers and her children at her side. One early November day she played the piano for the last time, fingers moving beneath a Swiss landscape painting; outside her children played. When ordered to stay in bed, she asked Louis to take her to the terrace for a final view of her homeland. She looked up at the clouds, then, anguished, clutched her children’s hands and worried about their future and how they might judge her. Seeing them kneel beside her, she returned to bed and never rose again.
Days blurred with Annette and Louis keeping vigil. On the fifth day the doctor banned flowers—an ominous sign. One quiet Sunday night she drew the curtain and told Louis tomorrow would be her last. She had him fetch important papers—birth certificates and a letter to Lord Brandon, her estranged husband—which she dictated, hesitating over her signature before choosing “Louis-Gaston.” She revealed twelve thousand francs saved and urged him to keep it safe, warning everything would be sealed after her death. Louis vowed to care for Marie, send her to school, and apprentice at sea, determined to return successful.
Her eyes brightened at his resolve. Growing weak, she requested a priest and, surrounded by loved ones, received the sacrament. As her breathing slowed the room fell silent; she died.
Annette and the vinedresser’s wife prepared the body. Only a few mourners attended; Marie, too young to grasp finality, cried as they lowered the coffin marked by a simple cross. The children returned to La Grenadiere for a last look, then left with Annette to her cousin’s house in Tours. Louis entrusted Annette with Marie’s birth certificate and the money, sent Marie to school, gave earnest advice, and departed to apprentice on a warship. A month later, watching the French coast fade, he embraced life at sea, resolved to honor his mother’s memory and comforted by an old sailor’s words.
################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
I have always wanted to tell a simple true story that would terrify two young lovers and drive them to seek refuge in one another, like children clinging together at the sight of a snake. That may sound vain; I played a part in this almost ordinary tragedy—if it fails to move you, blame me and the banality of reality.
In 1819 I was traveling from Paris to Moulins and, for want of money, took an outside seat on the coach. An Englishman joined me and we quickly bonded over fresh air and the landscape. Conversation turned to women and love; we agreed a birth certificate meant little—some women at thirty-five or forty were younger in spirit than girls at twenty. We praised “the woman of a certain age.” He said his mistress was thirty-eight; I confessed mine was forty. Freed by this, we traded romantic follies: one had traveled six hundred miles for a brief meeting; the other had risked danger for a secret rendezvous. Both countesses were adored by their husbands, whose simplicity lent enough danger to heighten our pleasure. Then the wind took away our laughter.
At Pouilly I examined my companion more closely: a vicomte, well-made, dark-haired, blue-eyed, pale, with shapely hands and a clever air—many girls would have desired him.
About a league out of Pouilly the coach overturned. He jumped to escape, misjudged, and was crushed beneath the vehicle. We carried him to a peasant’s cottage where, in agony, he entrusted me with a sacred task: inform his mistress of his death so she would not learn it from a newspaper. He asked me to retrieve a key worn around his neck; I found it embedded in his flesh. He gave directions to his home in La Charite-sur-Loire for the love letters, then grew speechless and died.
At La Charite I fulfilled his wishes. His mother was away; an old servant fainted at the news. I took the sealed letters, burdened by the sorrow of a woman who had lost her last love. The countess’s chateau lay eight leagues beyond Moulins and I had little money, yet youthful resolve propelled me to walk fast, eager to bring the news before anyone else. Nearing the Chateau de Montpersan, unease grew about my role as bearer of such tragic news.
When I reached the chateau, the open doors unsettled me. Inside, barking dogs and a servant-maid directed me to the park, and a playful little girl guided me through winding paths as she called for her mother.
I paused behind a shrub on the avenue, straightening my shabby clothes so the lady would not mistake me for a local rate collector. I spotted Juliette and her husband among the flowers; at her child's ambiguous remark her pace quickened. Surprised by my presence, she regarded me coolly, disappointment visible. I fumbled the elegant phrases I had rehearsed, and the hesitation let her husband approach. I asked if they were indeed M. le Comte and Mme. la Comtesse de Montpersan, using the question to size them up. He was a typical provincial nobleman: large, self-important, badly mannered, more pretension than polish. The Countess was the opposite: petite, graceful, in white muslin with rose accents, youthful and lively.
Choosing diplomacy, I took the Count aside with an air of mystery and requested a private word. He obliged, confident his wife could learn his secrets at will, and left her to wander. I told him of my traveling companion’s death and of his sincere attachment to Juliette. Concerned for his wife, the Count urged caution; I explained I could not approach her without first informing him, stressing the sacredness of the message entrusted to me. Flattered, he allowed me to proceed.
As we neared the house, Juliette glanced at us with curiosity and disdain, likely due to my appearance and our unexpected presence. I recalled her beauty in the sunlit alley and sighed. “Alas, madame, I have made a difficult journey solely for you,” I began; her face paled. She asked if he was ill; I answered that he was but urged her to control herself, saying he had entrusted me with secrets concerning her. Pressing for details, she demanded whether he still loved her. I could only nod. Distress overwhelmed her; she cried out and leaned against a tree.
“Your husband is coming!” I warned.
“Do I have a husband?” she fled.
The Count called me to dinner; the Countess was absent. The canon of Saint-Denis inquired after her, but the Count shrugged and hastily served the lavish meal. After a while a maid reported the Countess missing. Alarmed, I rushed out with the canon and the maid to search the garden and park. As we called, I recounted the tragic accident; the maid’s concern for her mistress was evident.
At last we heard a muffled cry from a granary. Inside we found Juliette buried in hay, sobbing. The maid and I helped her to her room; I instructed that she not be disturbed, claiming she had a headache. Returning to the dining room, I was surprised the Count still ate, seemingly unfazed. His indifference was explained: a serious medical condition had left him ravenous, overshadowing his concern for Juliette. The evening was somber; exhausted, we retired early.
Passing the Countess’s room, I asked the servant for news. Juliette called me in, tried to speak but could not. I left, feeling the weight of her agitation.
Later that night I was awakened by the sound of curtain rings. The Countess sat at the foot of my bed, pale and drawn. “Is it true?” she asked, voice trembling. “I don’t know how I can live after this blow, but I want to know everything.” Her eyes were red and swollen. I related the accident briefly, avoiding distressing details. When I described the dying man’s fears and the message he had entrusted to me, her tears dried into a deep pallor. I handed her letters from him; she took them mechanically, lamenting that she had burned all his other letters and now had nothing. “I cut this lock of his hair,” she said, revealing a precious token. I gave it to her; her tears fell on my hands, a moment of profound gratitude and connection. “Ah! You love! May you always be happy,” she cried, clutching the lock and fleeing with her treasure.
The next morning felt surreal. I searched under my pillow for the letters but found nothing. I spent the day with Juliette, witnessing her noble spirit and selflessness. That evening the Comte de Montpersan accompanied me to Moulins and asked if I could deliver a sum of money to a contact in Paris. I agreed, taking a rouleau of twenty-five louis d’or, which covered my journey back. On arrival I appreciated the delicate way Juliette had arranged the loan, revealing the depth of her love and thoughtfulness. What a joy to share this adventure with a woman who cherished me, saying, “Oh, my dear, not you! You must not die!”
