To M. le Baron Barchou de Penhoen.
Of all the pupils of the Oratorian school at Vendôme, we are, I think, the only two who have since met in mid‑career of a life of letters. When we met, you were engaged on your works on German philosophy, and I on this study. So neither of us has missed his vocation; and you, seeing your name here, will feel as much pleasure as I do in inscribing my work to you. Your old schoolfellow, 1840 De Balzac.
It was one o’clock one winter night in 1829–30 in the Vicomtesse de Grandlieu’s salon; only two family guests remained. A young man took his leave; as his carriage died away, the Vicomtesse turned to her daughter Camille, who pretended to watch a fire‑screen while listening anxiously.
“Camille,” she said, “if you continue to treat young Comte de Restaud as you have this evening, I shall have to prevent him from visiting. Trust my love and let me guide you. At seventeen one cannot judge the past or the future or social standings. M. de Restaud has a mother, Mlle. Goriot, who squandered her father’s fortune and is of no birth. Though he behaves admirably toward her and his siblings, any family would hesitate to entrust their daughter’s future to him while she lives.”
Derville, a family friend who had overheard, interposed: “I have won, M. le Comte. I shall assist your niece.” On being challenged, he said he had read the Vicomtesse’s looks and settled by the fire. Camille’s uncle approached and the Vicomtesse sat between them.
“It’s time for a story that may change your view of Ernest de Restaud’s prospects,” Derville said.
The tale concerned the Vicomtesse’s restored fortune and explained why an attorney could be intimate with her. After the Revolution and return of the Royal family, the Vicomtesse had been reduced to a pension from Louis XVIII and saw her Hôtel de Grandlieu sold. Derville had discovered defects in the title, pursued legal action, and reclaimed it. He obtained judgments restoring property and indemnities, raising her income to about sixty thousand francs. He did this without exploiting his new popularity: a man of integrity, he refused preferment to keep his practice and his social ties, becoming a close family confidant.
His management of her affairs won him esteem and many aristocratic clients, yet he did not use politics to profit. His steadfast service revealed his talents; since Ernest de Restaud’s arrival—especially as Camille showed interest—Derville had visited the Hôtel de Grandlieu more often.
At a recent ball Derville had teased Camille: “It’s a pity that young man doesn’t have two or three million francs, isn’t it?”
“Is it a pity? I don’t think so,” she replied. “M. de Restaud is capable and well educated. He will have as much money as he wishes when he comes into power.”
“But what if he were already rich?” Derville persisted.
“Rich already?” Camille blushed. “All the girls would be vying for him,” she said.
“Then Mlle. de Grandlieu might not be the one he favors,” he teased. Her blush betrayed her. Camille rose and left. Derville thought, “She loves him.”
Since that evening Camille had been unusually attentive to Derville, who approved of her feelings. Though grateful for his services, she showed respect rather than warmth; gratitude often weighs on the next generation, which may seek to escape it.
“This adventure,” Derville began, “recalls the one romantic episode of my life. It may seem odd that an attorney speaks of romance, yet once, at twenty-five, having seen strange things, I met a man I shall describe—a usurer whom you cannot know.
Imagine a sallow, wan face—what I would call lunar. His iron-gray hair was sleek and carefully combed; his features were impassive, his little yellow eyes without eyelashes peering from under a shabby cap as if afraid of light. Thin lips, a gimlet nose, and a low, suave voice that never rose made him look either prematurely aged or unnaturally preserved. His room matched him—clean but threadbare, like an elderly spinster’s. In winter the fire smoldered in ashes, never making a flame. He moved with pendulum regularity, hoarding every sensation; his life flowed soundlessly like hourglass sands. Yet toward evening his money seemed to turn to a heart: when he finished his day’s business he would rub his hands; inward glee creased his face into a silent laugh. Even then he spoke in monosyllables with an inscrutable expression.
He was my neighbor in the Rue de Grès while I was a second clerk finishing my legal studies. The house was damp and dark; its gloom smothered even the eldest sons’ gaiety before they reached his door. He and his lodging were alike; the oyster resembled its rock. I was the only one he spoke to—borrowing a light or a book, sometimes permitting me to visit in the evenings. These small courtesies had grown out of four years of neighborly contact and my sober conduct. I lived nearly like him through lack of funds. No one knew if he had relatives or friends, whether he was rich or poor; I never saw money in his room—his capital was in the Bank. He collected debts himself, running all over Paris on skinny legs. Once, when gold fell from his pocket, another lodger returned it.
“That isn’t mine!” he cried, surprised. “If I were rich, would I live as I do?”
He made coffee on a cast-iron chafing dish; his dinners came from a cookshop, and the porter’s wife tidied his room faithfully. A whimsical chance named him Gobseck. Later, when I did business for him, I learned he was about seventy-six when we met—born about 1740 near Antwerp, son of a Dutch father and Jewish mother. His full name was Jean-Esther Van Gobseck. When I mentioned the murder trial of La belle Hollandaise, he said without interest, “She is my grandniece.” That was the only remark he made about the death of his sole surviving relative, Sara Van Gobseck; when I asked why his grandniece bore his name, he smiled, “Women never marry in our family.” He had never cared to trace relatives beyond four generations of the female line. The thought of heirs disgusted him; the idea of his wealth passing to others after his death was intolerable.
Sent away at ten as a cabin boy to the Dutch Straits, he spent twenty years abroad. The inscrutable lines on his sallow brow concealed adventures, panics, sudden luck, and squandered fortunes. He had known Admiral Simeuse, M. de Lally, Tippoo Sahib, and notorious pirates in St. Thomas, had hunted treasure in Buenos Ayres, and observed the American War of Independence. Yet when he spoke of the Indies or America, he did so reluctantly, as if it were indiscreet. If sociability is a religion, Gobseck was an infidel; despite my efforts, his nature remained opaque.
Was he faithful to his mother’s religion? Had he converted? Did he view Gentiles as prey? I never learned his religious opinions; he appeared indifferent rather than unbelieving.
One evening I visited him. He sat motionless in his armchair, staring at the mantel as if reading his statements. A lamp burned behind him, making his face even paler. He gestured to a chair but said nothing. What could such a being think? Did he know God, feeling, women, happiness? I felt pity for him, like pity for a diseased creature. Yet I knew that while millions were at his command, he possessed the world in idea—having explored, ransacked, appraised, and exploited it.
“Good day, Daddy Gobseck,” I said.
He turned, contracting his bushy black eyebrows in an expression as expressive as a Southern smile. “You look as gloomy as when you heard that bookseller you admired had failed, though you were one of his victims.”
“One of his victims?” he repeated.
“Yes. You refused composition at the creditors’ meeting until he promised to pay you in full. He gave you bills accepted by the bankrupt firm. When he resumed business, he paid you the dividend on those bills.”
“He was sharp, but I got it out of him.”
“Do you have bills to protest? Today is the 30th, I believe.”
It was the first time I’d mentioned money to him. He looked at me ironically, then said blandly, “I am amusing myself.”
“So you amuse yourself now and again?”
“Do you think the only poets are those who print their verses?” he replied, pityingly.
I, knowing little of his life, thought, “Poetry in that head!” Then he surprised me.
“What life can be as glorious as mine?” he asked, eyes lighting. “You are young; your visions are colored by blood. You see women’s faces in the fire; I see coals. You have beliefs; I have none. Keep your illusions if you can. I’ll show you life with the discount taken off. You can go wherever you like or stay with your wife, but eventually you settle into a groove. Happiness consists in applying your faculties to realities. Anything more is false. My principles have varied with different men and latitudes. What we admire in Europe is punishable in Asia; a vice in Paris becomes a necessity elsewhere. There are no hard-and-fast rules, only conventions adapted to climate. Throw a man into various social melting pots and convictions become meaningless. The only constant is self-interest. If you lived as long as I have, you would know the only concrete reality worth caring about is GOLD. Gold means every form of human power. I’ve traveled and found hills or plains everywhere; place is irrelevant. Manners are universal; the battle between poor and rich is inevitable. It is better to exploit than be exploited. Pleasures are the same, and when sensations are exhausted, only Vanity remains. Vanity is satisfied only by gold. Our dreams need time and means to be realized; gold contains everything in embryo; it realizes all things for us.
“Only fools find pleasure in shuffling cards for a few pence. Only dupes think they’re useful making political rules amid unpredictable events. Only simpletons delight in discussing actors and repeating their sayings. What is all this but Parisian life? Let us seek a higher outlook: happiness in strong emotions that exhaust us or in methodical occupation that makes existence clockwork. A higher happiness is noble curiosity to learn Nature’s secrets, or to imitate Nature. In two words: Science and Art, or passion and calm. Every human passion, heightened by the struggle for existence, parades before me while I live in calm. As for scientific curiosity, I replace it with insight into the springs of action in man and woman. In short, the world is mine without effort and holds no claim on me. Listen,’ he continued, ‘I will tell you about my morning, and you’ll see my pleasures.”
He rose, bolted the door, drew a tapestry across it, and sat again.
“This morning,” he said, “I had only two amounts to collect; the rest of the bills due I gave away rather than cash yesterday. So much saved—when I discount a bill I always deduct two francs for a hired brougham; it’s absurd for my clients to make me trudge all over Paris for a few francs of discount when no man is my master, and I only pay seven francs in taxes.
“The first bill for a thousand francs was presented by a dandy—spangled waistcoat, eyeglass, tilbury, English horse. The bill bore the signature of one of the prettiest women in Paris, the wife of a Count, a great landowner. How did that Countess come to sign a bill of exchange, legally worthless but practically sound? Such women, poor things, fear the scandal of a protested bill and would rather pay than fail. I wanted to know what that bill truly meant—stupidity, imprudence, love, or charity?
“The second bill, signed ‘Fanny Malvaut,’ came from a linen-draper near bankruptcy. No one with bank credit comes to me. The first step to my door means despair; the news of failure will soon break, and he has been everywhere else first. When I see the stag at bay, a pack of creditors follows. The Countess lived in the Rue du Helder, and my Fanny in the Rue Montmartre. I made conjectures as I set out. If these two women could not pay, they would have more respect than their fathers. What tricks would the Countess try for a thousand francs? She would be so nice to me, speaking in that ingratiating tone of endorsers, pouring out coaxing words, while I...’ (he turned his pale eyes on me)—‘and I would remain unmoved, inexorable! So much for hypotheses. I reached the house.
“‘Madame la Comtesse is asleep,’ said the maid.
“‘When can I see her?’
“‘At twelve o’clock.’
“‘Is the Comtesse ill?’
“‘No, sir, but she returned home at three o’clock this morning from a ball.’
“‘My name is Gobseck; tell her I’ll call again at twelve,” I said, leaving muddy footprints on the carpet. I like leaving mud on a rich man’s carpet—not spitefully, but as a reminder of Necessity. In the Rue Montmartre, I entered a poor house and looked into a dark courtyard where sunlight never reached. The porter’s lodge was grimy, the window like a shabby gown—greasy and full of holes.
“‘Mlle. Fanny Malvaut?’
“‘She’s gone out; but if you’ve come about a bill, the money is waiting with the porter’s wife.’
“‘I’ll come again,’ I said.
“As soon as I knew the porter had the money, I wanted to know what the girl was like; I pictured her as pretty. I spent the rest of the morning looking at prints along the boulevard; just as it struck twelve, I entered the Countess’s antechamber.
“‘Madame has just rung for me,’ said the maid; ‘I don’t think she can see you yet.’
“‘I’ll wait,’ I said, sitting in an easy chair.
“Venetian shutters opened and the maid returned.
“‘Come in, sir.’
“From the soft tone I knew the mistress was not ready to pay. What a woman I saw! She had thrown an Indian shawl over her bare shoulders—covering herself while revealing the outline beneath. A loose gown trimmed with ruffles hinted that her laundress’s bills amounted to some two thousand francs a year. Dark curls escaped a bright handkerchief knotted carelessly on her head. The bed lay disorderly, suggesting broken slumber. A painter would have paid to see the scene: a crushed pillow in an eiderdown quilt, satin slippers on a bearskin rug, a crumpled gown, twisted stockings, ribbon garters, a half-open fan on the chimney-piece. Drawers stood open; flowers, diamonds, gloves, a bouquet, a girdle were scattered about. A vague sweet perfume filled the room. Beneath luxury and disorder, I saw Misery lurking—she felt those fangs. Her tired face epitomized the room strewn with relics of past revelry. The scattered gewgaws, pitiable this morning, had turned heads the night before.
“What efforts to drink from the Tantalus cup of bliss I read in these traces of love struck by remorse—in this visible account of a life of luxury and riot. There were faint red marks on her young face, signs of fine skin; her features were slightly hardened, and dark circles ringed her eyes. Yet Nature was vigorous; these traces of folly did not mar her beauty. Her eyes glittered. She resembled a da Vinci Herodias: magnificently alive and energetic; nothing in her was starved. She awakened desire; it seemed a passion stronger than love burned in her. I was taken with her. It had been long since my heart had throbbed; I was paid then and there—for I would give a thousand francs for a sensation that brought back youth’s memory.
“‘Monsieur,’ she said, finding a chair for me, ‘will you be so good as to wait?’
“‘Until this time tomorrow, madame,’ I replied, folding the bill. ‘I cannot legally protest this bill any sooner.’ Inwardly I thought, ‘Pay for your luxury, your name, your comfort, your monopoly! The rich have invented judges and courts to secure their goods; the guillotine gapes under silk, and remorse grinds teeth beneath smiles; the fantastical lions’ jaws await to sink into your heart.’
“‘Protest the bill! Can you mean it?’ she cried, eyes on me. ‘Could you have so little consideration for me?’
“‘If the King himself owed me money and did not pay, I would summon him even sooner than any other debtor.’
“While we spoke, someone tapped softly at the door.
“‘I cannot see anyone,’ she cried imperiously.
“‘But, Anastasie, I particularly wish to speak to you.’
“‘Not just now, dear,’ she answered softer, but without relenting.
“‘What nonsense! You are talking to someone,’ said the voice, and in came a man who could be only the Count.
“The Countess glanced at me. I saw how it was; she was utterly in my power. There was a time when I might have been foolish enough not to protest. At Pondicherry in 1763, I once let a woman off and she repaid me. I deserved it; why trust her?
“‘What does this gentleman want?’ asked the Count.
“I could see the Countess trembling; the white satin of her throat stood rough with gooseflesh. I laughed inwardly without moving a muscle.
“‘This gentleman is one of my tradesmen,’ she said.
“The Count turned his back; I drew the bill halfway from my pocket. After that inexorable movement, she came over and placed a diamond in my hand. ‘Take it,’ she said, ‘and be gone.’
“We exchanged values, and I bowed and left. The diamond was worth twelve hundred francs to me. Outside, a swarm of flunkeys brushed their liveries, polishing sumptuous equipages.
“‘This is what brings these people to me!’ I thought. ‘They steal millions with all due formalities to maintain this lifestyle. The great lord and the little man who apes him bathe in mud to save a splash or two when walking through the streets.’
“At that moment the great gates opened and a cabriolet admitted the young fellow who had brought the bill.
“‘Sir,’ I said as he alighted, ‘here are two hundred francs, which I beg you to return to Mme. la Comtesse, and please tell her I hold the pledge she deposited with me this morning for a week.’
“He took the two hundred francs and smiled ironically, as if to say, ‘Aha! So she’s paid, has she? … Faith, so much the better!’ I read the Countess’s future in his face. That fair, handsome young man is a heartless gambler; he will ruin himself, her, her husband, and the children—devouring their portions and wreaking more havoc in Parisian salons than a battery of howitzers.
“I returned to see Mlle. Fanny in the Rue Montmartre, climbed a steep staircase, and reached a neat two-room dwelling on the fifth floor. Everything was as tidy as a new ducat. In the first room Mlle. Fanny sat—neatly dressed, with a delicate fresh face and a winning look. Her neatly brushed chestnut hair formed a double curve on her forehead, lending refinement to her clear blue eyes. Daylight through short curtains softened her girlish face. A pile of shaped linen showed she was a sempstress. She looked like a spirit of solitude. When I offered the bill, she said the porter’s wife had the money. I pretended not to understand.
“‘You go out early, mademoiselle, it seems.’
“‘I very seldom leave my room; but when you work all night, you must bathe sometimes.’
“I looked at her. A glance revealed a life of toil and misfortune—honest farmer folk origins, a freckle or two, an air of goodness. There was an indefinable sincerity about her; it was refreshing. Poor child, she had faith in something; a crucifix and a sprig of green box adorned her little painted bedstead. I felt inclined to charge no more than twelve percent, to help her establish a good business.
“But perhaps she had a cousin who would raise money on her signature and sponge on her,’ I thought.
“So I restrained my generosity; benevolence, when unchecked, often ruins those who take it. I have often thought Fanny Malvaut would make a nice wife; I compared her pure, lonely life with the Countess’s—already sunk to a bill of exchange; she will sink further before she is done!
“Well,’ he said, ‘do you think it nothing to have this power to see into the deepest recesses of the human heart, to embrace many lives, to see the naked truth? There are no two dramas alike: hideous sores, deadly griefs, love scenes, misery soon lying under the Seine, young men’s joys leading to the scaffold, laughter of despair, and sumptuous banquets. Yesterday was a tragedy: a father drowned himself because he could not support his family. Tomorrow will be a comedy: some young man will attempt a theatrical scene. You have heard people praise eloquent preachers; I have wasted time on them; they might change opinions but not conduct. These priests and your Mirabeaus and Vergniauds are mere stammerers compared to my orators.
“Often a girl in love, a gray-headed merchant on the verge of bankruptcy, a mother concealing a son’s wrongdoing, a starving artist, or a great man whose influence wanes comes to me. Their pleading makes me shudder. Sublime actors play for me, for an audience of one, and they cannot deceive me. I read their inmost thoughts as God reads them. Nothing is hidden from me. The holder of the purse-strings can loose and bind. I am rich enough to buy the consciences of those who control ministers, from office boys to mistresses. Is that not power? I can possess the fairest women, receive soft caresses; is that not Pleasure? Your whole social economy is summed up in Power and Pleasure.
“There are ten of us in Paris—silent, unknown kings, arbiters of destinies. What is life but a machine set in motion by money? Methods are confounded with results; you cannot separate soul from senses, spirit from matter. Gold is the spiritual basis of society. We ten meet at the Café Thémis near the Pont Neuf; we reveal finance’s mysteries. No fortune deceives us; we possess family secrets in all directions. We keep a Black Book noting important bills, drafts on public credit, or bank instruments. We are the casuists of the Paris Bourse, an Inquisition weighing every man of fortune’s actions; our forecasts are infallible. One of us watches the judicial world, another the financial, another the administrative, another commerce. I keep an eye on eldest sons, artists, high society, and gamblers—on Paris’s most sensational side. Everyone who comes reveals neighbors’ secrets. Thwarted passion and mortified vanity are great babblers. Vice, disappointment, and vindictiveness are the best detectives. My colleagues and I have had all things, are sated, and love power and money for their own sake.
“Here,” he said, indicating his bare, chilly room, “the most high-mettled gallant who would draw a sword elsewhere will entreat with clasped hands. No city merchant is so proud, no woman so vain, no soldier so bold, but they all entreat me here, with tears of rage or anguish. Here they kneel—the famous artist, the man of letters whose name may go down to posterity. In short, all inheritances and concerns of Paris are weighed in the balance. Do you still think there are no delights behind that blank mask which has so often amazed you?” he asked, stretching out his livid face that reeked of money.
I returned to my room feeling stunned. The little wizened old man had grown great. He had become a strange visionary symbol—the power of gold personified. I shrank from life and my kind.
Is it really so? must everything be resolved into gold? It was long before I slept that night. I saw heaps of gold surrounding me. My thoughts were filled with the lovely Countess; I confess, to my shame, that she eclipsed another quiet, innocent figure—the woman who toiled in obscurity. But through the clouds of slumber the next morning Fanny’s sweet face rose before me in all its beauty, and I thought of nothing else.”
“Will you take a glass of eau sucrée?” the Vicomtesse offered, interrupting Derville.
“I should be glad of it.”
“But I see nothing here that concerns us,” Mme. de Grandlieu said, ringing the bell.
“Sardanapalus!” cried Derville. “Mademoiselle Camille’s happiness once depended on Daddy Gobseck; but with his death at ninety, M. de Restaud will soon inherit a fortune. This needs explanation. As for poor Fanny Malvaut, you know her; she is my wife.”
“Poor fellow,” the Vicomtesse smiled. “He would admit that, with his usual frankness, in front of a crowd!”
“I would proclaim it to the universe,” answered the attorney.
Derville then told his story. After consulting the miser Gobseck, he completed his law studies, became a licentiate and then an advocate. Gobseck’s opinion of him grew; the old man began consulting him gratis on delicate matters. Derville rose from clerk to head-clerk, moved into his employer’s house, and earned 150 francs a month. Gobseck’s peculiar attention persisted; even after they drifted, the miser continued to seek his counsel.
When Derville’s pleasure-loving employer fell into debt and sold his practice for 150,000 francs, Derville—the seventh child of a tradesman with no money or connections—could not buy it. Refusing to beg, he went to Gobseck, who now occupied Derville’s old room and watched callers through a grated window. After listening, Gobseck judged the young man—no guarantees, age under twenty-five—and agreed to think it over. The next morning he consented but on harsh terms: for acquaintances he would take twelve and a half—then corrected himself—thirteen percent interest. He would pay the seller directly to secure the lien and demanded that Derville give bills for the amount—fifteen bills of ten thousand francs each—payable to a third party unnamed. Derville puzzled over the need for his birth certificate; Gobseck, with a malicious smile, explained that youth’s integrity and brains are commodities one can mortgage—after thirty a man’s value becomes uncertain.
Further conditions followed: legal advice without fees for life so long as it cost no outlay; unrestricted visits by Gobseck; no ostentation; dinners on Wednesdays and Saturdays after the “Change” at five. Gobseck promised recommendations, business, and lessons “to know men, and what is more—women!” He delighted in investing capital in Derville’s head and watching it prosper.
Derville prospered. He became a respected attorney, recovered Mme. de Grandlieu’s estates, and his name spread. Despite the usurer’s enormous interest, he repaid the loan in under five years. He married Fanny Malvaut, whom he loved; through hard work, shared resources, and a timely inheritance—one of her uncles left her seventy thousand francs—they cleared the debt. Derville summed up his happiness modestly and returned to the company.
About a year after buying the practice, Derville was drawn into a bachelor breakfast lost as the forfeit of a bet. Among the guests was M. de Trailles, the consummate dandy: a master of taste, a gambler and gourmand of society’s pleasures, spending perhaps a hundred thousand francs a year with no visible capital. Brilliant but frivolous, Trailles could charm or ruin; he skimmed life’s surface.
Derville explained a bachelor breakfast: it begins as sumptuous display and dissolves into chaotic orgy—neatness giving way to broken glasses, ruined linen, loud jesting, songs, quarrels, smashed bottles—an exhausted Bacchanal. Amid that pandemonium Trailles, cool and calculating beneath an affected drunken air, engaged Derville and by evening secured his promise to introduce him to Papa Gobseck. Trailles’s golden tongue mixed words of honor, virtue, and urgent misfortune; he painted a young woman’s reputation and marriage at stake unless fifty thousand francs could be procured that morning.
Uneasy but bound by his word, Derville accompanied Trailles to Gobseck the next day. Trailles, outwardly courtly, was inwardly agitated; on the Rue de Grès he grew nervous, glancing about with sweat on his brow. At Gobseck’s, Derville vouched for him; Gobseck said he had no money except client cash. Trailles boasted of fashionable partners and foreign revenues, quipping that spendthrifts and usurers were interdependent. Gobseck answered coldly: other creditors—Girard, Palma, Werbrust, Gigonnet—were full of Trailles’s paper and offered it at heavy losses; he would not lend a stiver to a man who owed thirty thousand francs and had nothing.
A cab arrived; Trailles returned with a woman on his arm—one of Goriot’s daughters, the Countess. Derville watched from the window; he saw the Countess enter Gobseck’s damp room, proud on the surface but distraught beneath. She shuddered when she recognized Derville; he felt pity despite her social brilliance and moral failings. Trailles had become her evil genius, ruling her by vanity and other strings.
The Countess asked whether it was possible to obtain the value of her diamonds while retaining the right of repurchase, and handed over a jewel-case. Derville explained this would be a sale with faculty of redemption—a formal transfer with a stipulated term allowing recovery by payment. The Count breathed freer; her companion looked black, fearing Gobseck would undervalue the stones.
Gobseck, impassive, took his magnifying glass and inspected the jewels. Derville never forgot the look that came over the old man—an unnatural glitter in his eyes as he rose and went to the light, holding the diamonds close to his toothless mouth, as if to devour them. He handled bracelets, sprays, necklaces, and tiaras like a child or a doting miser, mumbling, letting the light draw out every fire. That flash of greed, the only time Gobseck’s heart seemed to open, shocked Derville and left a lasting impression.
Earlier, when Trailles first argued, Gobseck had been ironic and calculating, mocking the ruin of a man who seemed penniless. He made plain he wouldn’t be fooled. Yet when Trailles returned with the Countess and her jewels, Gobseck’s mood changed; he rose in triumph, stretching out his arms to Derville and calling the arrival “Life” itself, as if the security had saved him. He admitted he had been guarded, unwilling to trust the elegant scoundrel alone. Now, having the jewels, he felt exultant—his ecstasy frightful in its intensity.
Gobseck rejoiced at the prospect of outwitting rivals Werbrust and Gigonnet and anticipated laughing at them that night. He sat again before his bureau, face reverting to a pale, impassive mask. Yet the sight of the Countess—beautiful, suffering, controlled by Trailles—had registered in him hardly beyond the value of her jewels. Derville reflected that Trailles had become the Countess’s tormentor: an angel-faced youth who, by charm and calculation, governed her life and misled her into degrading necessity.
Mme. de Grandlieu lamented how the Countess’s virtues had been subverted—how Trailles turned devotion into a weapon and made her pay for pleasures. Derville shuddered at the glittering youth whose angelic look masked moral ruin. In Gobseck’s room they stood before the old man as if before a judge; he peered at them like an inquisitor, measuring guilt and bargaining worth.
The Countess’s plea and Gobseck’s examination revealed what Derville had learned: Gobseck’s judgments were never sentimental—people were valued for what they could secure and what they promised. The bargain he struck with Derville—usurious interest, anonymous bills, visits, unpaid counsel—underscored a system in which personal ties and youthful promise were commodified, and financial life depended as much on shrewd calculation and social maneuver as on law or sentiment. Gobseck’s power came not only from capital but from a cold, almost scientific knowledge of men’s weaknesses—knowledge he wielded to acquire dominion and to profit emotionally as well as financially from the commodities people brought him, whether practices, promises, or jewels.
“Fine stones! This set would have fetched three hundred thousand francs before the Revolution. What water! Genuine Asiatic diamonds from Golconda or Visapur. Do you know their worth? No one in Paris but Gobseck can appreciate them. In the Empire, such a set would have cost another two hundred thousand francs!”
Gobseck’s eyes shone as he handled the case, alternately rapture and calculation. The Count sought a loan of one hundred thousand francs for three years, offering the diamonds as security. The Countess claimed them as hers, but I pointed out that, as a married woman, she could not legally alienate property without her husband’s consent; a deed of purchase would be void. Gobseck shifted: possession being nearly as good as title, he offered eighty thousand francs if they left the diamonds with him.
Pride flared in the Count; the Countess seemed stupefied. Maxime, her lover, blustered; Gobseck, unmoved, produced a cheque for fifty thousand francs on the Bank of France and thirty thousand in protested drafts, even two pistols to show his implacable temper. Cornered, they accepted. Gobseck exulted at obtaining such stones “tolerably cheaply”; his ferocious delight in possession chilled me.
Soon after, the Count burst into Gobseck’s room, furious that his family diamonds had been bought. Gobseck replied coldly: if Mme. la Comtesse could sign bills, she could engage in business; whether they were the Count’s could not be proven without bringing the wife into court and exposing her. He mocked the Count’s authority. We debated law and equity; I advised the Count to settle—either pay for the diamonds or secure a redemption clause—since litigation would be ruinous and humiliating, and the buyer could claim good faith. With my help the Count signed an acknowledgment of receipt of eighty-five thousand francs, including interest, in consideration of Gobseck’s promise to return the stones.
Days later the Count, agitated and fearful, asked me to draw papers to transfer his estate to Gobseck as a simulated sale, Gobseck to hold it in trust and later restore it to the eldest son. He feared his son’s favoritism for his mother and distrusted the boy’s future loyalty. I warned that such a counter-deed risked disinheriting other children; he wept and agreed to provide for them in the instrument. It was a desperate precaution.
The deed was signed but not delivered to me. Months later, Gobseck, dining at my house, hinted the Count was dying and that he and I were the only ones trusted by him. Fearing for the counter-deed, I sued the Count for a sum ostensibly due to Gobseck so that, on the man’s death, I could attach the estate and secure the deed; I bribed a servant to tell me when the Count was gravely ill. My plan was to intervene quickly, threaten to affix seals, and secure the document before the Countess could act. Money governed these manoeuvres at the bedside.
For two months the Count lay prostrate, living in squalor he seemed to prefer. He hated daylight, rejected nurses, and reveled in discomfort. One morning he summoned his son Ernest and, in a lucid moment, entrusted the boy with a sealed packet addressed to me, begging him to carry it to the post and to keep the secret even from his mother. Ernest promised.
When Ernest returned, the Countess drew him to her knee and pressed him until he refused to repeat what his father had said. Furious, the Count confronted her. The scene was brutal: he accused her of filling his life with trouble and trying to corrupt their son; she begged for mercy and vowed to expiate her sins if the children could be spared. The Count, exhausted of affection, called her a bad wife and mother; she fainted. Priests administered the sacraments. At midnight he died.
Gobseck and I arrived soon after. The house was in chaos. In the death-chamber and adjoining salon the Countess had been ransacking drawers and trunks; her search had been violent—mattress overturned, drawers emptied, papers and furniture strewn. A paper bearing the Count’s arms lay at her feet; in panic she had thrust some documents into the grate and was burning them when we entered. I snatched an addressed paper from the hearth: it was to me. Confronted, the Countess was near collapse, torn between shame and fear. Gobseck, cold and triumphant, reminded her—without mercy—that he was the legal owner of what her husband had sold. He asked if I would judge him; I could not deny that under the law he had rights. I left her beside her husband’s corpse while Gobseck declared himself trustee of the estate.
From then on Gobseck managed the property with the hard efficiency of a usurer turned landowner—leasing the mansion, spending summers on the estates, repairing mills, planting timber. He became, in all but name, lord of the manor, showing no softness. When I suggested he help young Ernest, he scoffed: adversity is the best teacher; let the youth fend for himself. My attempts to move him to honor the Count’s intentions and provide for the children met the old man’s icy thrift.
Time passed. Gobseck grew feebler but retained his iron will. He kept his rooms exactly as always and received clients even from his bed. During the financial reorganizations that followed political changes—when claims and fortunes were reshaped—his knowledge of assertions, agencies, and negotiated slices of transactions made him influential; he took fees, arranged deals, and amassed presents—a snuff-box here, a piece of plate there—while parting with little or no cash. He remained as thin and avid as ever, his avarice concentrated to a point.
Then Gobseck’s death came. Summoned to his bedside by his old porter, I found him kneeling by the grate, muttering bequests and naming people—half delirious, half lucid. He died with a final enumeration of riches. At the hearth, amid cinders, I dug and found metal: coins, receipts, tucked papers—proof of the vast hoard he had amassed. I ordered the justice of the peace to seal everything at once.
The rooms revealed the scale and eccentricity of his thrift: one chamber reeked of putrid pies and moldy fish among chests of tea and coffee; others were packed with furniture, silver-plate, vases, pictures, books, and curios—many likely presents or pledged goods. Jewel-cases bore ciphers and coats of arms; fine linen and weapons lay in confusion. Every item was the result of bargaining, barter, and hard bargaining; some bargains remained unsettled, and dealers who expected presents or returns balked at Gobseck’s stingy terms. The stench and clutter bespoke a miser’s final degradation.
There was talk of Gobseck’s intended bequests: he had given an address for a supposed heiress, a woman called “La Torpille” in the Rue Vivienne; I foresaw the absurdity of pouring an immense fortune into such a character’s hands. But more immediate was the question of the Restaud estate: the Count’s counter-deed, the burned fragments, the tormented Countess, and young Ernest’s uncertain inheritance. Despite Gobseck’s harshness, Ernest would, in time, inherit a fortune sufficient to marry Mlle. Camille and to provide for his mother and siblings—assuming the legal knots could be untangled and the provisions enforced.
Thus closed the arc of transactions, betrayal, and legal scrimmage: Gobseck’s cold mastery of money had altered an aristocratic family’s fate; the Count’s plans were thwarted by rage and sickness; the Countess’s frantic attempts to save her children had degenerated into crime; and a miser’s death revealed treasures amid squalor. The outcome promised both restitution and new trials, as the rightful heirs would have to navigate tangled transfers and the old usurer’s secreted wealth.

######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
Monsieur de Manerville, a Norman who married a wealthy Bordeaux heiress, became attached to her Gascon estate, the château de Lanstrac. He fled the Revolution for Martinique in 1790 and returned to find Lanstrac well run by Mathias, a progressive notary’s clerk. After Madame de Manerville’s death in 1810 the old man grew miserly and deprived his only son, Paul, of resources.
Paul came home from college in 1810 and endured three years of strict paternal control that stifled him. Though physically brave, he lacked the will to oppose his father and became timid in society, isolated at royalist gatherings in the old mansion and unable to connect with peers. To relieve monotony he practiced fencing and riding—skills his father demanded—and read novels.
His father’s death freed him: he inherited substantial, well-kept estates but disliked Bordeaux and Lanstrac. He invested in consols, traveled, and served at embassies; indulgence and poor management depleted much of his fortune. Resolving to return as a country gentleman and mindful of rank, he felt he must marry well. De Marsay warned marriage brings ridicule and responsibility; Paul replied he sought companionship and was ready for family duties.
Despite earlier doubts, the Comte de Manerville returned to Bordeaux in winter 1821 determined to carry out his plans. Restoring the family mansion and furnishing it in Parisian taste burnished his reputation for elegance; his manners soon earned him a leading place in Bordeaux’s royalist society. An old marquise called him “the pink of fashion,” a jibe liberals mocked and royalists admired.
Paul de Manerville enjoyed attention. Charming, witty, solicitous toward women, he blended foppery with a genuine concern for his appearance. Of medium height and graceful movement, he preferred receiving happiness to bestowing it and sought in marriage the understanding and encouragement he felt lacking. Young and averse to domestic routine, he nonetheless accepted that provincial life demanded matrimony and variety. His inclination was sharpened by a secret love for Mademoiselle Evangelista.
Years earlier an affluent Spaniard, Evangelista, had settled in Bordeaux and, through fortune and connections, entered noble salons. His Creole wife, from an illustrious Spanish family, sustained their standing with extravagant tastes, indulging whims regardless of cost and backed by her devoted husband. After Evangelista’s death in 1813, Madame Evangelista—now a wealthy widow with a beautiful daughter—continued to live lavishly even as her fortune declined.
By Paul’s return Natalie was striking yet unmarried, largely because her mother’s extravagance discouraged suitors; gossip claimed only a prince could afford her. Accustomed to luxury and adulation, she knew little of life beyond privilege. Six months after Paul’s arrival they met at a high-society event and found mutual charm. Madame perceived his interest and hoped he would be her son-in-law; Paul, long aware of the Evangelista fortune, began to picture Natalie as his future wife, brushing aside warnings with Parisian disdain.
Spending time with the Evangelistas, he felt proud of their company; society soon treated him as Natalie’s suitor. Royalist circles deemed the match prudent: Natalie’s beauty and her mother’s intelligence and connections made the union advantageous. A year after his return people concluded he was courting Natalie, though neither pressed for marriage. Natalie’s reserve intrigued him; she was attracted but inexperienced, and he, enamored yet cautious, wondered if he could manage someone who might not fully appreciate him.
The narrative widens to note that French marriages are often governed by negotiations over marriage contracts—frequently overlooked in literature but crucial in shaping couples’ lives—and that those discussions will influence Paul and Natalie’s future.
In winter 1822 Paul de Manerville sought Mademoiselle Natalie Evangelista’s hand through his great-aunt, the Baronne de Maulincour of Médoc. After discussing the dowry with Madame Evangelista, the baroness reported the match likely: Natalie’s dowry would be her patrimony, her mother contributing nothing. She urged Paul to consult Maître Mathias to protect his interests.
Paul, anxious, visited Madame Evangelista, who privately admitted she owed her daughter a large sum from her late husband’s estate and could not pay. Fearing Paul might withdraw and damage Natalie’s prospects, she summoned Maître Solonet, who promised they could conceal the difficulty and craft a favorable contract.
That evening Mathias and Solonet met. Mathias, cautious and traditional, sought to safeguard Paul; Solonet favored practical flexibility. While Paul and Natalie exchanged tender moments at dinner, the notaries debated risk: Mathias warned the family had squandered wealth; Madame Evangelista and Solonet suggested selling her house. They compromised: Madame Evangelista would place an entail on her property, protecting Natalie while limiting Paul’s exposure, allowing the marriage to proceed amid lingering tensions.
The next day Elie Magus, in Bordeaux, appraised Madame Evangelista’s jewelry—necklace and earrings at 250,000 francs and the “Discreto” at 100,000—cautioning sales would incur heavy loss. Staff rumors and society gossip, amplified by Madame de Gyas and Paul’s hunting plans, questioned the marriage. To counter them and display means, Madame Evangelista planned a grand fête of the camellias and to finalize the contract. At signing the notaries revealed that if Paul died first Natalie would inherit only a fraction of her expected fortune. Struck, Madame Evangelista resolved—despite personal cost—to protect her daughter’s future, shifting from careless spender to determined strategist.
The fête had briefly distracted Paul from his anxieties, which returned that night. He feared Madame Evangelista might have deceived him without Mathias’s intervention, though he comforted himself that in two days Natalie would be his wife and their finances settled. He failed to see his future had already shifted: the astute woman he trusted had become an adversary, contemplating withdrawing her interests from their shared life. He did not perceive the cunning of his Creole mother-in-law—part European intellect, part tropical passion, part Eastern indifference—awakened by the prospect of losing her daughter. With a day until the marriage, Madame realized she could shape the couple’s fate: Natalie trusted her completely, and a single conversation might alter everything.
The next morning, seated on Natalie’s bed, Madame recalled their harmony and mourned the thought of her daughter bound to a man she might have to obey. Natalie dismissed obedience, expecting love to rule. Madame warned that conjugal love can fade if not guarded and urged Natalie to assert authority from the start. She proposed leaving to avoid complicating the household and counseled Natalie to feign obedience while subtly maintaining dominance.
Their talk turned practical: keep an active social life to hold Paul’s interest; avoid constant proximity that breeds indifference; preserve allure and independence; and keep him entertained so the relationship stays vibrant. Overwhelmed, Natalie sought guidance. Madame promised continued contact and visits but insisted some separation was necessary for Natalie’s happiness and prospects.
When Paul arrived he tried to hide his unease; Madame noticed and offered to sell her jewelry to support the couple, framing it as a sacrifice for Natalie’s vanity and future. Paul, moved, accepted. Solonet, the notary, reported potential buyers for Madame Evangelista’s property, which could provide a substantial financial boost; Natalie agreed to the sale.
Determined to secure her daughter’s prospects, Madame resolved to step back from Parisian life and support them from afar so the newlyweds could form their own dynamic. As the midnight wedding neared, preparations carried a foreboding air and guests wondered at her absence. After the ceremony she played the grieving mother, extracted promises from Paul to secure her influence, and sent the couple to Paris. Alone with Paul, Natalie began to assert her authority, setting the stage for the complex dynamics of their marriage.
Five years later, in November, Comte Paul de Manerville, cloaked and somber, arrived at his old friend Mathias’s home in Bordeaux. The retired notary had preserved the estate titles though the usufruct was lost; public notices of Paul’s financial ruin embarrassed him. Paul revealed he had left Natalie without her knowledge and intended to sail to Calcutta to rebuild his fortune—no longer considering himself a count, he planned to succeed under another name. He outlined enormous debts from lavish spending and gifts to Natalie, sales and heavy borrowing to sustain their lifestyle.
Despite Mathias’s doubts and hopes about de Marsay’s promised funds, Paul insisted India was his only chance and vowed to return wealthy by forty. The next day Mathias received de Marsay’s bill of exchange confirming some relief, but Paul remained determined. At sea, seasick and reflective, Paul read Natalie’s letter begging support—she pledged her jewels and promised to economize—and de Marsay’s letter exposing Paul’s ruin and the mother-in-law’s profiteering. Confronted with love and treachery, Paul felt anger, helplessness, and remorse as he sailed away from France uncertain of the future.

######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
Railroads threatened to make many industries obsolete and change travel around Paris; elements of the Scene were already becoming relics. The once-common "coucous" of 1830 were fading by 1842 and survived mainly at suburban spectacles. In 1820 many districts still lacked regular stage-coach service, so short-route local vehicles remained vital.
The Touchards, father and son, held a near-monopoly on travel within about forty-five miles of their rue du Faubourg-Saint-Denis base, but local coucous continued to compete on shorter routes. Parisians’ appetite for the countryside let small, familiar enterprises—often run by innkeepers who knew the roads—thrive: they undercut fares, bent rules, and earned local loyalty. These coucous could challenge the Touchards’ formal coaches until omnibuses later carried more passengers more efficiently.
One lucrative contested line ran from Paris to Beaumont-sur-Oise; in 1822 multiple rivals operated despite Touchard efforts to cut fares and improve equipment because stops like Saint-Denis and Saint-Brice guaranteed demand. The Touchards extended to Chambly while rivals reached Beauvais. By contrast, the road to Isle-Adam, prized for its quarries and picturesque terminus, had only two coaches and discouraged speculation, giving Pierrotin and a colleague a virtual monopoly that locals often preferred to the crowded Beaumont coaches.
Pierrotin, in his forties, left the cavalry in 1815 to take over his father’s coucou, married an innkeeper’s daughter, enlarged the business, and ran it with military precision, treating passengers like cargo. He ordered a larger coach while keeping an inherited yellow coucou—built for six but habitually overloaded—and a noisy four-wheeler nominally for seventeen (limited to fourteen), paying taxes only on the coucou via a special permit.
On a Saturday morning in autumn 1822, outside the Lion d’Argent, Pierrotin fretted over scarce passengers and a thousand-franc debt, even weighing a loan from Monsieur Moreau at Presles. A footman then sought a discreet ride for his master, the Comte de Serizy, who had countermanded his carriage. Pierrotin seized the opportunity, hopeful the fare would ease his debts; the count’s secrecy made him extra careful.
Monsieur Huguet de Serizy descends from the eminent President Huguet, ennobled under François I; his family arms and motto link modesty to the estate’s name. His father was president of parliament before the Revolution. The count became a councillor of State at twenty-two, noted for persuasive diplomatic memoranda. He remained at Serizy through the Revolution, sheltered by his father’s stature, and after that father’s death in 1794 joined the Council of Five Hundred to distract himself from grief. After 18th Brumaire he won the First Consul’s favor, rejoined the Council of State, and was charged with reorganizing a chaotic department—work that shaped much of Napoleon’s administration. In 1806 he married the widow of a notable Republican general, improving his fortune. By 1814 years of labor and ill health compelled his resignation; though Napoleon treated him with some mistrust he retained rank. Louis XVIII later trusted him, appointing him a cabinet minister. During the Hundred Days he remained loyal to the Bourbons, staying at Serizy. After Napoleon’s final defeat he resumed duties as privy councillor and vice-president of the Council of State, influencing affairs without seeking acclaim. His life—early to rise, early to bed—reflected relentless, quiet duty; he received many honors, including the grand cross of the Legion of Honor, yet his devotion carried an unspoken sorrow.
He had long been passionately attached to his wife; despite strains their bond endured. Work was his refuge from heartache; he treated her with respect and allowed considerable freedom. Though she acknowledged some virtues, her ingratitude often wounded him; only occasionally did she soothe his pain.
A pressing business matter then occupied him: the Presles farm was encroaching on his estate. Leased by tenant Léger from the burgher Margueron, the lease was expiring and the property would be sold. The count intended to buy it to end disputes and instructed his notary and lawyer to negotiate rapidly.
His steward, Moreau, had served faithfully seventeen years—saved by the count’s father during the Revolution and later spared by the count himself. Moreau asked to manage Presles and at first was diligent, but after securing the post he grew lax and corrupt, taking bribes and arranging shady deals, already striking a bargain with Léger. His conduct drew the retired officer Monsieur de Reybert’s ire; Madame de Reybert brought the count detailed accusations. Initially skeptical of her motives, the count’s doubts grew and he ordered his notary to accompany her to Presles. What began as a straightforward purchase thus became an investigation fraught with intrigue and the threat of betrayal by trusted servants.
As Pierrotin left the Café de l’Echiquier he met Madame Clapart and her son Oscar, eighteen and anxious on his first trip alone. Ruined by a first husband’s suicide and a poor second marriage, Madame Clapart entrusted her overprotected, vain son to Pierrotin’s care. Moreau had proposed testing Oscar by placing him with the steward. Mocked and humiliated en route, Oscar’s struggle with identity simmers as the coach—carrying a cross-section of society and an incognito Comte de Serizy—sets the stage for his trial.
Passengers on public conveyances sit silent until the most talkative breaks it. National types: taciturn English, somber Germans, cautious Italians, Spaniards, Russians hampered by poor roads, and lively French.
Georges, a clever young man, sized up his companions, felt superior, and set out to amuse himself. Descending from La Chapelle to Saint-Denis he tried identities—posing as a Russian prince or soldier. When dust was complained of he steered talk to the Levant, claiming service under Ali Pacha and inventing a plague scare that made the count stop the coach. He claimed to have fought in 1813 and at Waterloo, promoted to captain.
Pressed about decorations, Georges said he didn’t wear them; the count displayed his, and Georges professed admiration for Napoleon, spinning tales of Egypt and a harem from Ali Tebelen. At Saint-Denis the count found a portfolio identifying Georges as Crottat’s second clerk. Despite exposure, Georges charmed Père Léger and Oscar—buying treats and telling Eastern tales—keeping the party amused though the count noted contradictions.
Pierrotin’s coach stopped at Saint-Brice about half past one to rest the horses. The innkeeper welcomed the passengers by name and they stepped down for a brief breakfast. Georges, ostentatious and boastful, demanded a hearty meal, twirled his cane, produced a straw cigar case and offered cigars to Oscar and Schinner. Oscar, flattered but clumsy, puffed ineptly and became the butt of teasing: Schinner smoked easily, Georges fumbled theatrically, and Mistigris mocked Oscar’s discomfort. Oscar watched Georges’s display with thinly veiled resentment, mentally tallying the bills he suspected Georges was burning through.
Georges suggested a bottle of Bordeaux shared with Père Léger. The innkeeper, echoing François from Touchard’s coach, mentioned that Monsieur Margueron had been invited by the Comte de Serizy to dine at Presles. Père Léger laughed and boasted of a plan to preempt such patronage: he intended to buy Les Moulineaux cheaply with newly minted coin, snapping up the farm before the count could act. The innkeeper calculated returns—Les Moulineaux produced six thousand francs—and mused about lending twenty thousand. Père Léger explained he’d made Moreau a partner and, by leveraging royal favor and new coin, secured the estate. The scheme sounded shrewd even if the count tried to intervene.
The count, listening nearby, grew uneasy about discretion. When Pierrotin stepped out to water the horses, the count drew him aside and reminded him of a bribe: ten louis to keep the matter secret. Then, raising the inducement dramatically, he offered a thousand francs the next day for a new coach if Pierrotin would conceal his name. He instructed him not to enter the inn, not to speak of the incident, and to stay with the horses. Pierrotin returned curt reassurance, hurried his customers on, and insisted he must deliver fish by three to a big dinner in Stors.
Père Léger proposed harnessing the horse Pierrotin meant to sell into the cabriolet so they could breakfast calmly and overtake Pierrotin. But Pierrotin rebridled and set off; Schinner and Mistigris walked. Georges kept up talk about the hostess and the landscape, joking about spare cigars. Oscar, stung by being called “the little young man,” defended his dignity: he denied being Mina’s aide-de-camp, denied Eastern travels, and said his family intended him for diplomacy. His companions ridiculed his pretensions, tutors, and “household” language—Oscar proudly noting his mother ran their household—and taunted his social standing and vocabulary.
Pressed to name his tutor, Oscar named the Abbé Loraux, vicar of Saint-Sulpice, hinting at family influence that might aid the abbé’s ecclesiastical rise and saying Abbé Frayssinous owed favors to his father. Then, unexpectedly, Oscar declared he was bound for the Château de Presles to visit the Comte de Serizy and claimed intimacy with the count’s son. His confident talk about the Serizys—detailing private matters—caught the count’s attention.
Seizing the chance, the count questioned Oscar, saying he sought information on Monsieur de Serizy to support a claim he intended to press with the American government. Oscar advised approaching the count’s wife rather than the count himself: he said the count was madly in love with her, while she secretly despised him. He described the count’s disfiguring skin disease and humiliating remedies—sulphur and steam baths, even an iron box—portraying a man reduced and ashamed; in Oscar’s telling, such afflictions left the count vulnerable at home. He recommended instead seeking the Marquis d’Aiglemont, an old admirer of Madame de Serizy, for influence.
The young man’s ease in speaking of intimate matters shocked the company. Schinner scolded him for airing friends’ affairs in public; Mistigris feigned indifference. Georges advised that one should talk without saying anything in a public coach. When conversation returned to the Comtesse, the count interrupted sharply and forbade disparagement. Asserting his friendship with her brother, the Marquis de Ronquerolles, he vowed that speaking ill of the countess would not go unpunished. The painter agreed: no man should mock women. The count hinted at influence—knowing ministers, blocking unworthy men from decorations, and acquaintance with the architect of Presles—and asked Pierrotin to stop at the next inn so he could step out.
Once clear of the others, the count had Pierrotin show him aside in the inn-yard and asked about the talkative young man. Pierrotin explained the boy was the son of a poor woman in the rue de la Cerisaie, Madame Husson, whom he often supplied with fruit and game from Presles. Pierrotin had driven the youngster only once and thought he might be the prince of Maffliers; the boy had told him to leave him near Maffliers, not to go on to Isle-Adam. The count was reassured by this account. When he rejoined the party, Père Léger and the others exchanged awkward glances, feeling exposed; the three young men kept a guilty silence. Mistigris quipped about suffering for loose talk, and Georges reminded Oscar that public conveyances required speaking artfully.
They rode on into the Carreau woods in a heavy silence; the count noted their quiet as if led to the scaffold. Oscar, trying to assert himself, declared he was Oscar Husson and would be famous within ten years. The companions teased him further, and the count refined his name to Husson de la Cerisaie, lending Oscar’s modest boast a veneer of dignity. The coach descended toward the grand château of Presles.
Before disembarking, the count delivered formal, almost theatrical farewells: the colonel should make peace with the king; Schinner’s fame was assured; Oscar, the budding diplomat, needed no protection since he possessed “the secrets of statesmen;” Père Léger was urged to pluck the Comte de Serizy and to do so firmly. Asking Pierrotin to set and fetch him at the same spot the next day, the count left the coach to the others’ bewildered speculation about his identity. Père Léger guessed he was the count who’d leased Franconville; Schinner swore never to blague again in a public coach. Mistigris, unrepentant, shrugged with a proverb.
As they neared the château gate, Georges warned Oscar that if the man indeed had been the Comte de Serizy, Oscar should be wary—a chill reminder of his mother’s cautions. Pierrotin called out they had arrived at Presles. Some of the young men feigned familiarity, but Georges, not wishing to reveal his destination, pretended he would continue to Les Moulineaux. Père Léger then triumphantly disclosed that he was the farmer at Les Moulineaux, forcing Georges to admit it was so.
Oscar asked Pierrotin to leave his belongings at the steward’s; he intended to go straight to the château. He plunged forward along a narrow path until Père Léger corrected him: the little gate, not the forest path, led to the château. Guided thus, Oscar entered the courtyard. While Père Léger watched, Georges slipped away upon realizing Père Léger truly possessed Les Moulineaux. Pierrotin, having obeyed the count’s injunction to keep the matter secret for now, drove in with swagger, deposited the painter’s packages and Oscar’s bundles, and handed a small leather portmanteau to the concierge’s wife. Mistigris and the painter took rooms in the château itself—a discovery that stunned Oscar.
The scene closed on Pierrotin’s satisfaction: he had kept the count’s secret and now looked forward to the promised reward. With a sly peasant’s calculation, he anticipated receiving a thousand francs the next day and buying the splendid new coach he long desired. The coach rolled on, the passengers dispersed through the estate, and the social ambitions, pretensions, and private bargains that had surfaced in their talk settled back beneath the polite surface of conversation.
Oscar hid as Monsieur Moreau emerged from the guard-room in a blue overcoat, riding-whip in hand. Moreau greeted the painters—Mistigris and Bridau, sent by architect Grindot—told the concierge to show them their rooms, invited them to dinner at five, and mentioned hunting on the estate. Leading Oscar through the park, he sent his son Jacques ahead to tell Madame Moreau of their arrival and, stern but kindly, urged the inexperienced Oscar to learn to ride and hunt. Oscar’s mother had allowed only a two-week stay because Madame Moreau was present, though Moreau seemed inclined to extend it.
Their steward’s house, attached to the chateau and predating the Revolution, stood in a large garden and contained a salon, dining room, and a modern boudoir arranged to Madame Moreau’s taste. The family lived comfortably on estate provisions. Madame Moreau, once a maid, retained an air of elegance and worked to be treated as the chateau’s mistress, often patronizing villagers; an old slight from Madame de Reybert stung her, and she hoped the visiting artist would flatter her.
On the painters’ arrival day Madame Moreau dressed elegantly, though the Paris coach brought only Mistigris’s packages. When Mistigris and Bridau reached the house she professed a love of art; the two artists quickly perceived her pretensions and humored her. Their talk was cut short when a maid announced the count’s unexpected arrival, and Madame Moreau grew flustered. Oscar, uneasy, was fetched by his younger brother and sharply rebuked for his appearance; a joking remark by Mistigris about Oscar’s future heightened the awkwardness. The maid then said the count wanted dinner promptly.
The count, angered to find Moreau absent and having overheard talk suggesting Moreau’s disloyalty, confronted him—outraged that private matters had been spoken of, especially in front of Oscar. Moreau’s explanations failed; he returned home distraught over having lost the stewardship at Presles because of Oscar’s careless words. Estelle, his wife, was furious and grasped the family’s peril. Moreau demanded an explanation from Oscar, who remained silent from shame and fear. In anger Moreau dragged him back to the count to apologize; the count dismissed Oscar and doubted his promise. Humiliated, Moreau took him home to face uncertain consequences.
Moreau wrote to Madame Clapart, distraught that Oscar had revealed Madame de Serizy’s secrets to an incognito count, costing the family its position. At Clapart, Monsieur fretted about Oscar’s character while Madame defended him; when Brochon returned with Oscar and the letter, Madame Clapart fainted. The next day she urged Oscar to be discreet, hardworking, and prudent, and appealed to Uncle Cardot, a modest self-made businessman who generously supported his children. Cardot praised Oscar’s promise and, moved by Madame Clapart, offered to fund his legal studies on condition of integrity, discipline, and effort.
Madame Clapart felt renewed hope. Moreau later visited, noting he had begun speculating in land and could place Oscar as a pupil in a law office; he and Madame Clapart insisted on disciplined study to repair Oscar’s reputation and secure the family’s future.
Ten days later Monsieur Moreau brought Oscar to Maître Desroches, a twenty-six-year-old solicitor on the rue de Béthisy who had opened a roomy but inexpensive office. Raised in poverty, Desroches immediately read Oscar’s situation: the boy’s gaunt look and curt manners unnerved him. “We work here day and night,” he said, placing his head clerk, Monsieur Godeschal, in charge of training Oscar so he would not lose time on the way to law school and would master the Code. Oscar was lodged in a small attic beside Godeschal’s room; Desroches plainly intended to make him a capable clerk.
For two years Oscar lived at the office under exacting, petty work. Under Godeschal’s strict oversight he kept a monk-like schedule: rising at five to prepare, running errands, studying the Code under rigorous tests, returning to office tasks after classes, and subsisting on plain meals. His social life was minimal — monthly breakfasts with his uncle Cardot and Sundays with his mother — interrupted only by occasional outings Moreau could arrange. His attempts at elegance met the disapproval of his superiors.
Godeschal counseled a modest wardrobe and frugal living, insisting a good clerk should not spend more than seven hundred francs a year; he himself lived by those principles. He embodied honest, unostentatious leadership: when a colleague failed, Godeschal discreetly guided him to resign rather than suffer public dismissal, showing practical humanity.
By twenty, Oscar had become the third clerk, doing the second’s duties without pay but receiving room and board. His legal knowledge surpassed many licensed graduates, and he even began arguing cases in chambers. Yet he felt constrained by a life at odds with his desires: he envied well-dressed young men and yearned for freedom and pleasure, feelings he habitually suppressed. Godeschal, aware of these struggles, tried to shield him and sometimes covered the cost of outings to give him enjoyment; Moreau supported him but could not connect as Godeschal did. Both feared youthful follies might jeopardize his future, though they hoped to steer him aright.
“How is he getting on?” Moreau asked. “Too much vanity,” Godeschal replied, noting Oscar’s taste for fine clothes and suggesting his talents might suit a barrister rather than a solicitor’s clerkship.
In November 1825 a new clerk, Frédéric Marest, arrived — well-to-do and aiming for the magistracy, though he claimed a desire for hands-on practice. The clerks amused themselves with practical jokes: Godeschal kept a fictitious register of receptions for new clerks filled with absurd entries. When Oscar asked for it, Marest read, laughed, and revealed his inexperience.
The next day Georges Marest, now head-clerk to notary Hannequin, greeted Oscar and recalled past mischief that had led to his transfer. Georges invited the clerks to the Rocher de Cancale and an evening with “the Marquise,” promising high society. Desroches interrupted to assign Oscar the task of fetching a crucial document from the Palais and warned him to beware of Derville, who might obstruct the errand. Eager to prove himself, Oscar accepted.
Frédéric maintained the ruse that the Marquise was a wealthy widow from Mexico; in truth she was Mademoiselle Agathe-Florentine Cabirolle, a celebrated danseuse at the Gaîté supported by Père Cardot. At the lavish breakfast the intoxicated clerks went to Florentine’s salon. Oscar, charmed, joined a game of vingt-et-un with Florentine and the actress Fanny Beaupré, quickly losing the money meant for the legal document. Florentine offered him a thousand francs; he accepted but lost everything, sank into despair and stupor, and slept in the boudoir while the party continued into the early morning.
A loud noise wakes clerk Oscar Husson. His uncle Cardot scolds him for late revelry and, after the danseuse Florentine reveals Oscar is his nephew, is urged to help. Oscar admits he lost gambling money belonging to his employer, Monsieur Desroches; ashamed, he begs Cardot, who reluctantly lends 500 francs and warns it will be the last aid. Mariette sends a note and money; Godeschal hurries to fetch papers for Desroches. Angered, Desroches resolves to dismiss Oscar despite Godeschal’s defense, who recalls past lapses and blames the influence of Georges Marest. At home Clapart taunts his wife over their son’s failures; Madame Clapart defends Oscar. Oscar confronts his father, asserting independence but is ignored.
Determined to take responsibility, Oscar faces conscription; Clapart hopes military service will reform him. He draws a number and, through the Comte de Serizy’s influence, enters the cavalry. Madame Clapart turns to religion. Oscar applies himself, rises to lieutenant-colonel, and years later—decorated—stands with his changed mother outside the Lion d’Argent, meeting a ruined Georges Marest who does not recognize him. Military discipline, connections, and hard lessons have transformed his errors into respect, security, and a future marriage to Georgette Pierrotin.
