######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
If the French travel less eagerly than the English, they have reasons: foreign lands may offer scenery and comfort, but France’s particular wit, conversation, and tacit social understandings are hard to find, so many French take pleasure in recognizing home abroad.
In 1836, while the Sardinian Court was in Genoa, two Parisians lodged in a palazzo there. Genoa’s evenings, especially after rain, lent themselves to intimate company and Italian fare. At a dinner given by the French Consul-General were Mademoiselle des Touches (Camille Maupin), the painter Léon de Lora, and the critic Claude Vignon. Camille, returning from business in Florence and traveling with Léon, welcomed an informal evening rather than ceremony. Conversation was lively but light until the Consul-General, a thirty-four-year-old with a Byron-like look, began a personal story.
He had married Onorina Pedrotti, the wealthy banker’s only child, after reluctance and at the ambassador’s urging; her grace brought harmony, yet a persistent melancholy marked him. A debate about women’s morality split the guests, and to change the mood he recalled his youth: at twenty-two his uncle, the Abbe Loraux, secured him a post with Comte Octave, a statesman whose outward piety masked deep sorrow. The Count’s tender, almost fragile love of beauty blurred mentor and friend, revealing unrest beneath a seemingly perfect life—an image that lingered with the company.
“On a certain Monday,” he resumed, “M. le Président de Grandville and M. de Serizy (then Vice‑President of the Council of State) met at Comte Octave’s house. They formed a committee of three with me as secretary. The Count had secured my appointment as Auditor to the Council of State. All the documents for their inquiry lay in the library; MM. de Grandville and de Serizy relied on the Count for the preliminary examination. To avoid carrying all the papers to M. de Serizy, they agreed to meet first in the Rue Payenne. The Tuileries valued this work, and it led to my appointment as Master of Appeals that year.
“Though the Comtes de Grandville and de Serizy, who rarely dined out, were still discussing late into the evening, a servant interrupted: ‘MM. the Cures of Saint‑Paul and of the White Friars have been waiting in the drawing‑room for two hours.’
“It was nine o’clock.
“‘Well, gentlemen, you find yourselves compelled to dine with priests,’ said Comte Octave. ‘I do not know whether Grandville can overcome his horror of a priest’s gown—’
“‘It depends on the priest.’
“‘One of them is my uncle, and the other is the Abbe Gaudron,’ I said. ‘Do not be alarmed; the Abbe Fontanon is no longer second priest at Saint‑Paul.’
“‘Well, let us dine,’ replied the President de Grandville. ‘A bigot frightens me, but there is no one so cheerful as a truly pious man.’
“We entered the drawing‑room. The dinner was delightful. My uncle’s wit was refined and reserved, the manner one expects of a man in black. The Abbe Gaudron, as M. de Grandville observed, was simple and devout, a guileless peasant whose ignorance enlivened the talk. They began discussing adultery. My uncle pointed out the contradiction between civil and religious law left by the Revolution.
“‘In the eyes of the Church,’ he said, ‘adultery is a crime; in your tribunals, it is a misdemeanor. Adultery drives to the police court in a carriage instead of standing at the bar. Napoleon’s Council of State, touched with tenderness towards erring women, was inefficient. Should they not have harmonized civil and religious law and sent the guilty wife to a convent, as of old?’
“‘To a convent!’ said M. de Serizy. ‘They must first have created convents; monasteries were being turned into barracks. Besides, think—give to God what society would have none of?’
“‘Oh!’ said Comte de Grandville, ‘you do not know France. They had to leave the husband free to take proceedings; well, there are not ten cases of adultery brought up in a year.’
“‘M. l’Abbe preaches for his own saint,’ said Comte Octave; ‘in the East, woman was merely a luxury, regarded as a chattel; no virtues were demanded of her but obedience and beauty. By exalting the soul above the body, modern Europe—daughter of Christ—invented indissoluble marriage and made it a sacrament.’
“‘Ah! the Church saw the difficulties,’ exclaimed M. de Grandville.
“‘This institution has given rise to a new world,’ the Count continued with a smile. ‘But its practices will never match a climate where women marry at seven and are old at twenty‑five. The Catholic Church overlooked half the globe. Let us discuss Europe only.
“‘Is woman our superior or inferior? That is the real question. If woman is inferior, placing her on such a high level as the Church does required severe punishments for adultery. Formerly, cloister or death summed up legislation. But practice has since modified the law. The throne served as a hotbed for adultery, and the growth of this inviting crime marks the decline of Catholic dogmas. Nowadays, where the Church demands only sincere repentance from the erring wife, society is satisfied with a brand instead of execution. The law still condemns the guilty, but it no longer terrifies. In short, there are two moral standards: that of the world and that of the Code. Where the Code is weak, the world is audacious and satirical. Few judges would not gladly have committed the faults they condemn. The world punishes a blunder after encouraging hypocrisy. The entire marriage law seems to require reconstruction. The French law would be perfect if it excluded daughters from inheriting.’
“‘We three know the question thoroughly,’ said Comte de Grandville with a laugh. ‘I have a wife I cannot live with. Serizy has a wife who will not live with him. As for you, Octave, yours ran away from you. So we represent every case of the conjugal conscience, and if divorce is ever brought in again, we shall form the committee.’
“Octave’s fork dropped, breaking his glass and plate. He turned pale and shot a thunderous glare at M. de Grandville, hinting at my presence.
“‘Forgive me, my dear fellow. I did not see Maurice,’ the President continued. ‘Serizy and I, after witnessing your marriage, became your accomplices; I did not think I was committing an indiscretion in front of these venerable priests.’
“M. de Serizy changed the subject, recounting his efforts to please his wife without success. The old man concluded that human sympathies and antipathies cannot be regulated; social law is most perfect when closest to natural law. Nature does not consider the affinities of souls; her aim is the propagation of the species. Thus, the Code, in its current form, wisely leaves latitude to chance. The incapacity of daughters to inherit while male heirs exist is an excellent provision, whether to prevent degeneration or to make households happier by abolishing scandalous unions.
“‘But then,’ he exclaimed, lifting his hand in disgust, ‘how are we to perfect legislation in a country that insists on bringing together seven or eight hundred legislators! After all, if I am sacrificed,’ he added, ‘I have a child to succeed me.’
“‘Setting aside the religious question,’ my uncle remarked, ‘Nature only owes us life; society owes us happiness. Are you a father?’ he asked.
“‘And I—do I have any children?’ said Comte Octave in a hollow voice, making such an impression that the topic of wives and marriage was dropped.
“When coffee was served, the two Counts and the two priests quietly left, noticing that poor Octave had fallen into a melancholy fit, oblivious to their departure. My patron sat in an armchair by the fire, crushed.
Augustine Guillaume's cousin, the widow of Maelien, lived a very miserable life. 
“‘You now know the secret of my life,’ he said to me when we were alone. ‘After three years of marriage, I came home one evening to find a letter from the Countess announcing her flight. The letter was dignified; women often retain some virtues even when committing such a sin. The story now is that my wife went abroad on a ship that was wrecked; she is presumed dead. I have lived alone for seven years! Enough for tonight, Maurice. We will discuss my situation when I am ready to speak of it. When suffering from a chronic disease, it takes time to adjust to the idea of improvement. That improvement often seems merely another aspect of the complaint.’
“I went to bed greatly agitated; the mystery seemed more obscure than ever. I anticipated a strange drama, realizing that the events leading the Countess to leave such a noble and loving man must have been extraordinary. M. de Grandville’s remark had illuminated the caverns I had been navigating, and though the light was dim, I could perceive their vastness. I could imagine the Count’s suffering without grasping its depths. His sallow face, parched temples, overwhelming studies, and moments of absentmindedness stood out vividly during the twilight of mental questioning before sleep.
“Oh! how I loved my poor master! He seemed sublime. I read a poem of melancholy, seeing perpetual activity in the heart I had accused of being torpid. Must not supreme grief eventually lead to stagnation? Had this judge, with so much power, ever sought revenge? Was he feeding on her long agony? Is it not remarkable to harbor anger for ten years in Paris? What had Octave done since this great misfortune—when the separation of husband and wife has become a social question?
“We allowed a few days to pass, for great sorrows have their own diffidence; but eventually, one evening, the Count said gravely:
“‘Stay.’
This, as nearly as may be, is his story.
My father had a ward, Honorine, rich and lovely, who was sixteen when I returned from college to live in our old house. Raised by my mother, she greeted life with an innocent, poetic view of religion and happiness, seeing the future as a perpetual fête. We lived like siblings until, by the garden basin, I asked if she would marry me. We waited eighteen months at my mother’s insistence; when I was twenty-six and Honorine nineteen, we married. Out of respect for my parents we kept the old house unchanged; I introduced her to society more from duty than delight.
I later learned how treacherous such marriages can be: a husband becomes teacher and moralist, and love may wither under authority. I trusted her innocence too much and reproached myself like an anatomist seeking the cause of a disease. After my parents’ deaths, eighteen months later, Honorine left me—she wrote a farewell letter. The shock stunned me; for a month I was numb, then I resolved to live on memory. I loved Honorine and never ceased to worship her; since her departure I have subsisted on recollections of pleasures she perhaps never appreciated.
Do not make a hero of me; I sought no diversion. I was too young or too in love and met no other like her. After struggling to forget, I almost fell into infidelity, but the thought of Honorine restrained me. My life became a relentless agony of fears, joy, and despair; I felt no anger toward her, only compassionate, paternal sorrow. I realized I had idealized her and perhaps exhausted her with my uncritical passion. Too young to understand the unwritten laws of a woman’s heart, I blamed myself and absolved her. Love held me captive.
Society mocks a husband who cannot keep his wife; I had to remain silent. Serizy is happy—able to see and protect his wife—while I have nothing but a love that starves. I loathe prostitution and remain faithful under a kind of spell. Without religion I might have killed myself; instead I threw myself into work, feverish and sleepless.
The interior drama of my life interests no one; one can only vaguely perceive another’s sorrow. I calm despair by contemplating a miniature of Honorine, kissing her brow, breathing her perfume. I suffer fits of collapse and wild hilarity, and terrors like a criminal meeting the police. The public believes I am absorbed in official duties—Council of State, Chamber, Courts, Politics—but seven hours of nightly work suffice for those. Honorine is my true occupation. To recover my wife, to guard her without her knowing, to satisfy small pleasures she allows, to be always near her like a sylph unseen—that is my life. For seven years I have never gone to bed without checking her night-lamp and the shadow on her curtains.
Honorine left my house with only the dress she wore. Eighteen months later she had been deserted by her lover, who recoiled at poverty. She had a private income of sixty thousand francs a year. In November 1820, I secured the services of a skilled accoucheur disguised as a humble suburban apothecary and induced a parish priest to act as charity when she needed help. To hide her, save her from discovery, and find a devoted housekeeper who would be my confidante—this was a task worthy of Figaro. Locating where my wife had taken refuge was not difficult.
After three months of despair, I devoted myself to Honorine with God as my witness. Love needs daily nourishment; should I not protect this child whose guilt sprang from my imprudence against further ruin? At seven months her infant died—mercifully for both of us. For nine months she lay between life and death, deserted when she most needed a man’s support; but my arm was extended over her head. Honorine was cared for as she would have been at home. When she recovered and asked who had assisted her, she was told it was the Sisters of Charity, the Maternity Society, and the parish priest.
Honorine’s pride, almost a vice, has at times become obstinacy in misfortune. She determined to earn her living. For five years I lodged her in the Rue Saint-Maur, in a charming little house where she made artificial flowers. She believes she sells her creations to a shop, earning twenty francs a day and living as if on fifteen thousand francs a year. She adores flowers, pays a gardener handsomely—an expense I secretly meet. I promised the gardener a market-garden with a house near the porter’s lodge in the Rue Saint-Maur, held in the name of a law-clerk; the smallest indiscretion would ruin him. Honorine has her little house, a garden, and a splendid hothouse for a rent of five hundred francs a year. She lives under the name of Madame Gobain, her housekeeper, an old woman of impeccable discretion who has won Honorine’s affection. For three years Honorine has been happy, believing she owes her comfort solely to her own toil.
Yes, I have tried to reconcile with her. Once, believing there was a chance, I wrote her a letter—rewriting it twenty times. I will not describe my agonies. It was dark and foggy; I went to meet Madame Gobain. Honorine, recognizing my handwriting, threw the letter into the fire without reading it, declaring she would leave the next day. It felt like a dagger in my heart. I had just succeeded in deceiving my wife once—sending an Indian shawl presented as a gift from an actress, which I had wrapped myself for a night. My life may be summed up: I love, and I wait.
Madame Gobain is my faithful spy. I visit her every evening for reports of Honorine’s day; a single exclamation might reveal the secrets of a soul that remains willfully deaf and mute where I am concerned. Honorine is pious—she attends church and prays—but she has never confessed or taken Communion, anticipating what a priest would say. She will not return to me; the horror I inspire overwhelms her, though I have never harmed her and have always been kind. Even if I was hasty in teaching her, is that a reason for her implacable determination? Her silence is absolute: she has never told Madame Gobain who she is; the housekeeper cannot advocate for me. The others know nothing; they live in awe of the Prefect of Police and respect the power of a Minister. Thus it is impossible for me to penetrate her heart. I have no means of action; any violence would ruin me forever.
How can I argue against reasons I do not know? Should I write and have my letter copied and delivered, risking a third removal, which cost me fifty thousand francs previously? The last purchase of a house had been made in the name of my second secretary. The poor man, unaware of my vigilance, was caught opening a private box; I intimidated him into selling the house and dismissed him. If I did not feel my noblest faculties satisfied by this divine fatherhood—if I did not drink delight in every pore—I might think myself a monomaniac. Sometimes at night I hear the jingling bells of madness and dread the violent transitions from feeble hope to complete despair. Recently I thought of the tragic ends of Lovelace and Clarissa Harlowe; if Honorine were the mother of my child, she must return under her husband’s roof.
I have such faith in a happy future that ten months ago I bought one of the finest houses in the Faubourg Saint-Honoré. If I win her back I will not let her see the room from which she fled; I will place my idol in a new temple where life will seem entirely new. That house is being transformed into a marvel of elegance and taste. I was inspired by an anecdote of a poet’s extravagant purchase for an actress; great passions share such impulses. I love like a poet—like an emperor!
From the vehemence of my speech you might imagine a burning physical passion, but it is nothing compared to my worship for her soul, mind, and heart. By retrospection I now see the graces of Honorine’s mind and heart I overlooked in happiness. Day by day I appreciate my loss, discovering gifts in that capricious and proud young creature who has grown strong under poverty and the cowardly shock of desertion. That heavenly blossom is fading in solitude and hiding. The law we discussed cannot compel love—seizing my wife by force would be triumph over a corpse. Religion has little hold on her; she craves its poetry but does not heed its commands. I have exhausted mercy, kindness, and love; I am at my wit’s end. Only one chance remains: the cunning and patience of bird-catchers who finally trap the wariest birds.
When M. de Grandville’s indiscretion revealed my secret life to you, I regarded it as fate’s decree. Have you affection for me to show romantic devotion? I replied with a question about danger: my first secretary had tried to open my deed box; my second might fall in love with my wife. Can anyone put his hand in a brazier without burning it? The Count answered that he would send me well-gloved. It would not be his secretary who lodged in the Rue Saint-Maur but his distant cousin, Baron de l’Hostal, a lawyer high in office—an appearance of respectability.
Shortly after, Madame de Courteville and her daughter called; the Count had family ties on his mother’s side. A young woman of twenty might suit the Count, but he did not propose that match. Instead, three weeks later, I moved into the gardener’s cottage, repaired and furnished by Paris workmen and money. I was as much in love as the Count could desire for security. Would a young man of twenty-five play this part faithfully for his friend’s cause? I confess I relied on my uncle’s advice; the Count had authorized me to confide in him if needed. I engaged the garden, devoted myself to horticulture, and worked frantically—obsessing over varieties of flowers as part of my disguise. The Count dictated the tragi-comedy enacted in the Rue Saint-Maur. Each night, after Honorine went to bed, Octave, Madame Gobain, and I held council; Octave lived only for that hour.
For two months I worked in the garden without ever seeing the little house where my fair neighbor lived; I had not even inquired if I had a neighbor. Her garden front was divided from mine by a paling, her cypress trees already four feet high. One morning Gobain warned me that a neighbor intended to build a wall between the gardens. My curiosity burned to see the Countess—my wish nearly extinguished the budding affection I had briefly felt for Amélie de Courteville. Building a wall would close off fresh air from Honorine’s garden and turn her plot into a narrow alley. The cottage, once a summer villa, imitated a trellis with flowers up to the second floor—a charming, fragile place. My wall would cut away three-quarters of it.
“My good Gobain,” the Countess asked, “what sort of man is this florist?”
Gobain replied that he disliked women, was the nephew of a Paris cure, and perhaps a little cracked. A harmless lunatic is the only man no woman ever distrusts in matters of sentiment—so the Count had chosen a wise disguise for me.
When Gobain informed the Countess that the florist wished to be left in peace, she replied she would speak to him herself. The next day as I walked the path I saw the curtain parted and a face peering out. Gobain announced me as the eccentric neighbor. I had dressed as a countryman—gray flannel trousers, wooden shoes, shabby coat, peaked cap, hands soiled with mold—to strengthen the part. The Countess did not shrink.
It was early May and the air was pure; seeing Honorine, I understood Octave’s passion. Her pallor was of a particular white, with pink flushes at emotion. Sunlight through the acacia gave her a pale gold halo. Her brown eyes were tender and lively, framed by long lashes; a glance could enchant. Light-brown hair framed a high brow; her mouth was voluptuous; her head bore a stamp of nobility. Slight but not thin, she embodied mignonne—pliant, ready to be petted, yet proud and imposing. Her walk had a proud carriage, and even humble people made way. Gay and tender, haughty and commanding, she combined contradictions and remained childlike. She could be as implacable as an angel if hurt.
The violet perfume she wore made sense of the Count’s adoration: she inspired devotion that demanded chivalrous sacrifice. Many women study their expressions; in her it flowed from a nature that went straight to the heart. If I sketch her it is because her soul and exquisite heart are central to this tale, and you would blame me had I not done so.
I was about to forget my ridiculous role as half-crazy rustic when I asked her, with assumed gravity, if she loved flowers. She replied she made artificial flowers—after growing them she imitates them, as a mother paints her children—an occupation that proved she was poor. I pretended to be a gardener with an interest in blue dahlias and blue roses; I argued that blue is the color of superior souls. I proposed open railings to unite our gardens. She, who loved solitude and independence, allowed me the right to enter her garden anytime and urged I be treated as a neighbor with whom she would keep good terms. With a leap I cleared the paling.
For a fortnight I seemed indifferent; by late May on a lovely evening we walked on opposite sides of the paling and exchanged civil words. I leaped the fence and joined her. She led me into the house—a charming sanctuary of eighteenth-century taste: frescoed dining-room, tasteful staircase, faded but elegant drawing-room, a bedroom, dressing-room, and workroom used as a library. The kitchen lay below. Everything harmonized with the woman I had come to love.
Exquisite simplicity reigned. Her little house was like a box of 18th-century art; but occasionally a bitter smile hinted at deeper sorrow. In days we reached intimacy born of proximity and her conviction of my indifference to women. A look would have spoiled everything; I kept my feelings masked. Honorine saw me as an old friend and treated me with pity rather than coquetry. She soon allowed me into her pretty workshop, full of books and curiosities—a boudoir where elegance softened the vulgarity of the tools of her trade. She had poetized manufacture.
Perhaps making artificial flowers allows a woman to remain herself: she can chat, laugh, sing and still work. Honorine had an artist’s sense in arranging petals and shades; white china saucers held colors; a cabinet with drawers held molds; a Japanese bowl held the paste; a Venetian glass held the living model she strove to imitate. She pursued difficult tasks—tiny corollas, wildflowers—moving like an accomplished pianist. Her fingers seemed fairy-like in their deft economy of action.
I could not tire of admiring her; she displayed the genius of a painter in bold attempts, copying faded flowers and yellowed leaves to create poetic crowns suitable to women’s moods. She explained a fanciful botany of feelings—flowers for bacchantes, for bigots, pensive flowers for bored women. She employed me to stamp leaves, cut material, and prepare wires. My affected desire for occupation made me useful. We talked as we worked; when idle I read to her to keep my role. My gloomy, misanthropic persona amused and matched her antipathy to love; she was content with a companion on her desert island.
I spared you the shame I felt as I feigned wounds in my heart to excite her compassion. Her renunciation of love, her dread of happiness, she proclaimed with vehemence. These happy days convinced me that a woman’s friendship can be superior to her love. She resisted any trap of sentiment; mulish obstinacy is common among women, and she was stubborn.
One evening, playing the cynic, I argued that women, once they have inspired passion, hold men sacred forever, while women forget. She rebuked my generalization and denied being like those who give themselves to successive loves. She declared herself a nun, seventy-two years old in spirit, not a woman. Her disgust for life made her invulnerable. She often spoke of the angel Gabriel singing in her heart; if rich she would work still to prevent herself from soaring on “many-tinted wings.” She found some solace in her flowers but sometimes they failed to occupy her. She felt some great event impending; her life seemed about to change.
After three months of this diplomatic struggle between my assumed melancholy and her proud indifference, I told the Count I could not coax her out of herself; the shell must be broken. The Count left me free to act.
One Saturday in July, she gleefully showed me five gold pieces she had earned for her flowers and caps. It was late, a moonlit evening. The room smelled of perfumes; the Countess jingled the coins that Octave’s accomplices had provided as part of the ruse. She said she earned her living by amusing herself and felt transports of pride every Saturday. I suggested the image of a pretty child running among the flowers calling her “mother,” and her reaction was immediate and extreme: she suffered an attack—what she called “a cramp of the heart.” Madame Gobain tended her; I walked the path weeping and doubting whether I should abandon the bird-catcher’s role I had assumed. Gobain returned saying I cried like a child, and the Countess, roused by the danger of misinterpretation, wore a wrapper and came down to me.
“You are not the cause of this attack,” she said, and when I asked about her troubles she declared her heart a poem laid before God. I threatened, lightly, that I could know all her secrets by tomorrow. She defiantly challenged me to be curious and told me to reveal what I discovered and the means by which I discovered it, for the preservation of the small happiness she so dearly bought depended on my discretion.
“I defy you,” she said; she wanted me to find out who she really was. I warned that if I learned anything carelessly, others would be informed, and then—she would fly “on wings” to the New World. I interrupted that Paris is a place where those who must work can hide. She persisted: find out who I am. I promised to tell her what I might discover the next day and pleaded she not hate me. She replied coldly, almost imperiously, that only two words would suffice to destroy her peaceful fraud and she must be allowed to keep her carefully arranged retreat. After a few more words, she dismissed me abruptly.
Thus the Count’s scheme had reached a crucial point: I was to be the Count’s instrument, the gardener-spy and companion, someone who could win Honorine’s confidence without revealing his identity. The house’s delicate balance—Honorine’s love of flowers, her pride, her vow of solitude, and the secret charity that sustained her—would be the stage for whether pity, cunning, or patience could redeem what force and authority could not.
“The battle is to-morrow, then,” I said, forcing a smile; the phrase haunted me as the reunion approached. Octave shared Honorine’s agitation. The Count and I walked until two in the morning like generals before a decisive engagement, aware that victory might hinge on a single instant. We lay awake—one hopeful, the other dreading a meeting that would decide lives. For Octave and Honorine the true drama was interior, in a spiritual world of lofty souls.
The next evening I arrived at ten and entered Honorine’s white-and-blue bedroom, the refuge of this wounded dove. The Comtesse seemed about to speak but held back at my respectful manner. I told her plainly she was not living in her own house but in Count Octave’s: M. Lenormand, the usher, was only a front; the seclusion, the money, and the protection all came from him. Her disappearance had been plausibly explained; her husband had even hoped she had been saved from a shipwreck. He had hidden her as she hid herself, obeying her through seven years of magnanimity.
The Countess trembled, defiant, eyes dry and fierce that later yielded helpless tears. She had believed herself free; marriage weighed on her like a prison. “I will go!” she cried. “He forces me to it; I will go where no one will come after me.” When I asked if she meant suicide, she denied it and insisted she had powerful reasons to refuse returning to Comte Octave.
I urged her to tell me and my uncle—both devoted advisers. My uncle was a priest but not a pedant; we would listen and try to find a solution. If she were the victim of a misunderstanding we could clear it; if guilty, expiation could be sought. I promised friendship and refuge from Octave’s tyranny if she proved she could not and ought not return. She spoke of the convent as a last hope, but I warned that as a Minister of State the Count could block many convents; nonetheless I pledged to save her if she had no other life to lead. I offered peace, solitude, independence, and vowed not to see her without her consent.
She answered that no man could understand her conscience, “like circles of Dante’s hell.” Another man, though unworthy, had had all her love; for her there could never be two loves. The cup of happiness once broken could not be refilled. She would not be a prostitute to Octave—she esteemed his generosity and respected him but could never love him. Expecting tenderness from him would be impossible; every time her eyes met his she would see her sin reflected. To return would be a snare: she feared killing him with coldness or provoking suspicion, and could not bear to kneel while he stood. She clung to the memory of a child conceived in rapture and nursed for seven months; other children would draw tears in their milk. For her there was no situation in the life he offered that did not hide a precipice. Religion offered solace, but she lacked the strength to face that suffering. She had chosen between this hell—where God did not forbid blessing Him—and the hell awaiting her in Count Octave’s house.
I asked where she would go. Could a woman at thirty, beautiful and with undiscovered powers, live unprotected in the wilderness where I could hide her? I reassured her the Count, who had not shown himself at her house for nine years, would not go anywhere without her permission; she had his devotion, a guarantee for her tranquillity. I urged her to discuss the future in confidence with my uncle, a man whose moral influence equaled a minister’s. He would be tender before her flowers.
I left at midnight; Honorine seemed calm but depressed, harboring a secret purpose I could not divine. I found Octave a few paces away in the Rue Saint-Maur—drawn by irresistible attraction, he had left our agreed spot. On hearing of my visit he fretted: “What a night my poor child will go through! Suppose I were to go to her! What if she saw me suddenly?” I warned that in such agitation she might throw herself from a window; she was one of those Lucretias who could not survive such violence. He countered that passions change like a tempest; tonight she might as well rush into his arms as she might throw herself out. He joked of opium to sleep away the torment.
Next day Gobain brought a letter informing me the Countess had taken a chemist’s draught at six and fallen asleep. Gobain read Honorine’s anguished letter aloud. She acknowledged conscience condemned her: conscience is God’s interpreter to man. If she did not reconcile with Octave she would be damned; civil law condemned her to obey; if her husband did not reject her the world would deem her pure. Legally, religiously, and socially she ought to return; it would be cruel to deprive him of happiness, of children, to blot his name. Her sufferings and repugnance she admitted were selfish and ought to be sacrificed to family. She should be a mother; a child’s caresses might wipe many tears. God, law, and society were in accord.
Yet she declared she could not live false to herself. She would become “white and innocent again” only in death. It was not obstinacy but a nature that could not renew passion once consummated. “A young bride is like a plucked flower; a guilty wife is a flower trodden underfoot,” she wrote. She could not love Octave. Every time their eyes met she would see her sin reflected; his generosity would measure her crime. Neither esteem nor sacrifice could survive. She feared she would by coldness kill him or provoke suspicion. She could not bear to be treated as chattel, to kneel while he stood. She clung to the memory of a child conceived in rapture; other children would draw tears from her in their milk. Religion offered solace but she lacked energy for that particular suffering. She had chosen between a hell where God did not forbid blessing Him and a hell under Count Octave’s roof. She closed asking me to tell her where to find the solitude, peace, and silence I had promised.
After copying the letter I went to the Rue Payenne. Opium’s power had been overcome by anxiety; Octave paced his garden like a madman. I gave him Honorine’s letter and urged him to answer; he must reassure modesty with experience, for it is harder to restore conviction than to seduce ignorance. He cried with joy on reading her words: “She is mine!” Ecstasy and torment possessed him; he would act. I visited other friends and, comparing Mademoiselle de Courteville with Honorine, reflected that some women inspire perfect, inevitable fidelity while others, like Honorine, demand extraordinary generosity. Such comparisons are fatal at marriage’s threshold. Life teaches that marriage excludes passion and family cannot be founded on tempests of love. Having dreamed impossible passion and tasted its torment, I saw modest reality and took a manful resolution.
When I returned to the Count to announce his relations’ arrival I saw him grow young with hope. He asked what ailed me; I replied with a crucial condition: if he succeeded in bringing the Countess back, she must never see me again and must never know Maurice was his secretary. “Never mention my name to her, or all will be undone.” I told Octave to get me a diplomatic post abroad instead of the position he had obtained for me at home; I asked not to marry Amélie and to be sent away. He pressed my hand, understanding. We agreed on evening arrangements at Honorine’s.
That August evening the Countess sat under an arbor in a loose white muslin dress with blue bows. She did not rise but signaled me to sit. “Is not life deadlocked for me?” she asked. I told her it need not be if she chose differently. She asked how. I told her her letter was in the Count’s hands; she started like a frightened deer and accused me of being a traitor, a spy sent by her husband. I explained I had forwarded her letter through my uncle and would bring the Count’s reply. There is a law, divine and human, that even hatred obeys: do not condemn without hearing the defense. She must read the Count’s answer in the presence of my uncle and me, or she would be a mere wilful child. Make this sacrifice to the world, to the law, and to God.
She consented. Months of effort had led to this instant. The Count had pinned all hopes on it and had reached it. My uncle’s entrance into the little Pompadour drawing-room at ten was formidable: his fine head, silver hair against black, and divinely calm face soothed the Countess. My uncle, the Cure of the White Friars, handed Honorine Octave’s letter.
In that letter Octave confessed that if she had trusted him and read a letter he had written five years earlier she would have spared five years of privations; he admitted faults and ignorance at the marriage’s start; an angel had been in the house and he felt punished for his audacity. He begged mercy, saying he had so appreciated her susceptibilities he would not bring her back to Rue Payenne where he could not bear to see her. He was decorating another house in the Faubourg Saint-Honoré and hoped to conduct not a wife secured by ignorance and law but a sister who would allow him to press a fatherly kiss on her brow. He promised not to force passion on her until sure of his strength to leave her free. He argued she could live as a sister or daughter presiding: unapproachable, alone, occupied as she pleased, under legitimate protection and with fortune for good works. If she longed for absolution it would wait on her pride; he would not use ecclesiastical threats or legal force; he wanted her happiness and would not take peace except from her consent. If she persisted in condemning him to loneliness he would yield; she would be troubled no more.
Honorine folded the letter, pressed it to her bosom, and, soothed, said she would avail herself of the Count’s permission to remain here. My involuntary cry betrayed my feeling and revealed to her that I was more than a spy; she gave me a mischievous glance and took my hand at the door, pressing it affectionately. I replied that I would leave tomorrow—“You love your husband,” I said after a squeezing farewell. I rushed away, leaving my uncle to explain my agitation as a touch of madness.
Six days later I left with an appointment as vice-consul in Spain. After I established myself, Count Octave wrote to me: if I were happy he would not write; he had entered a new life of suffering, grown young again in desire but governed by a diplomat’s prudence. When I left, Octave had not yet been admitted to the pavilion in Rue Saint-Maur, though a letter promised permission. He waited more than a month and finally called. Gobain stalled, saying “Madame had to dress,” flattering Octave’s hopes. Once admitted, he spoke with trembling: he told her he had no crime but being in love with his wife and proposed she no longer work, offering her certificates worth twelve thousand francs each—an offer which was freedom more than money. She took them, languidly, and understood the gift. She conceded and said she would be seen as often as he liked.
Two months of habit brought out her true character; it was a delicious springtime of love that gave Octave ineffable bliss. Yet when he proposed she go to England to resume rank and life in their new residence she feared to leave their experiment. “Why not live always as we are?” she asked, and he submitted, wondering if she were making an experiment. He painted his case in a letter and begged her answer; she made none and seemed sad. When she finally received him in her blue-and-white room she was bewitching in dress, but her face was grave, fires beneath ice. She told him she would return as his wife when he wished, but warned that submission had dangers: she could be resigned but could not give the love he wanted. She loved his soul enough to die in his service—an expiation—and knelt, sobbing she might not die.
For two months he struggled. His heart was too full; he sought a friend and cried, “What shall I do?” I did not answer that letter. Two months later newspapers announced the Comtesse Octave had returned on an English vessel, restored to her family after contrived adventures. When I moved to Genoa I received formal notice of the birth of a son to the Count and Countess; I held the letter for two hours on the terrace bench. Two months later, urged by Octave and friends and broken by my uncle’s death, I agreed to marry.
Six months after the Revolution of July I received a final letter concluding the story. The Countess wrote that she was dying though already a mother—perhaps because she was a mother. She had played her part as a wife well; she had deceived her husband; she had had happiness as genuine as an actress’s tears. She was dying for society, family, marriage, as early Christians died for God. She did not know of what she was dying; she tried to find out but could not. She was not perverse; she had been bent on explaining her malady to me—the man who had brought the heavenly physician, my uncle, at whose word she surrendered. He was her director; she had nursed him in his last illness; he had shown her the way to heaven, bidding her persevere in duty.
She had done her duty. She did not blame those who forget; she admired them as strong natures. But she had the malady of memory. She could not twice feel that love which identifies a woman with a man. To the last moment she cried in the confessional and to her husband, “Have mercy!” but there was none. She died with stupendous courage, playing the actress’s cheerful mask while the invisible rival—her dead child—came every day to claim her. She was rent and smiled; two children existed, but the elder, the dead one, would triumph. She told me she had said this before: the dead child calls her. The intimacy of marriage without love degraded her soul every hour. She could only weep or dream when alone. Social duties and care of her child left no moment to renew herself as she could in solitude. The constant need for watchfulness starved her heart. She lacked tender lips to cool her burning eyes; her soul acted a part and so she died. The doctors, who discovered her secret, allowed a plausible complaint as cause. Octave believed in her apparent adoration; she feared he might follow her. She begged me to be her little Count’s guardian; she had placed a codicil expressing her wish and told me not to produce it unless needed. She asked me not to mourn; she had been dead these many days if Saint Bernard was right that where there is no love there is no life.
The Consul closed the reading by putting away the letters and locking his pocket-book. The guests asked about the Count. The Consul-General recalled seeing him a white-haired man, an old man of forty-five, passing through Genoa in search of health and amusement; the man had placed his will in the Consul’s hands and appointed him guardian of his son. He suspected the truth of Honorine’s death and that suspicion was killing him. At the steamboat Octave, poor friend and patron, had entrusted his will and looked down at the Mediterranean, pondering the mystery of why human nature sacrifices exquisite beings to fugitive pleasures. He confessed he was consumed by remorse. In the Rue Payenne he had been dying of joys not had; now in Italy he would die of joys he had had. He wondered where the discord lay between two equally noble natures.
A profound silence followed. The Consul then asked the two women present, “Was she virtuous?” Mademoiselle des Touches took his arm and answered by turning the question to men: are they not equally wrong when they take a young girl to wife while cherishing an angelic image and then compare us to unknown rivals? She argued that if marriage were based on passion her point would be valid—that kind of marriage would be Paradise. Others remembered such passions devastating men and making women like nuns or suicides. Camille Maupin and others reflected that such exceptional souls find peace only in solitude or in religious life. As the guests dispersed along the seashore at eleven, the conversation lingered on the rarity of Honorine: perhaps the most extraordinary intellect, a pearl whose tragedy was that life alternates pain and pleasure and that to ask Dante’s Paradise of life is to demand a luxury nature resists. Those great souls still existed, they agreed, though to live by such intensity often meant sacrifice beyond the world’s comprehension.

######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
Simonnin, the lawyer’s clerk, leaned from a window and flung a breadcrumb at a shabby stranger in Maître Derville’s courtyard, jeering, “There is that old Box-coat again!” Boucard, the head clerk, admonished him—however poor, a client is a man—while others, including Godeschal, taunted the visitor and plotted a prank. Laughter filled the cluttered, musty office; such disorder was routine.
When the weary man entered and politely asked for M. Derville, Boucard brusquely told him to come back at one. The clerks jeered—nobleman or porter?—and laughed when he insisted he was Colonel Chabert, reported killed at Eylau. About one in the morning he knocked again. The porter said Derville had not returned, but the head clerk explained Derville preferred to work at night. When Derville arrived he was struck by the man’s thin, pale appearance and scars. The claim to be the colonel—presumed dead at Eylau—made Derville and Boucard suspect madness, but Chabert insisted on telling his story.
Chabert recounted Eylau: gravely wounded and buried among corpses, he had miraculously survived because his horse’s body shielded him. He woke in a grave, struggled free, and was rescued by a woman who took him to a hospital. After months he recovered but had lost all papers. Without documents, his attempts to reclaim his life met disbelief and ridicule; he wandered, was treated as mad, and spent two years confined in an asylum. Upon release he learned his wife, the Countess Ferraud, had remarried and borne children.
Sensing the gravity though skeptical, Derville offered help: he would obtain papers from Germany, gave a small sum for subsistence, and provided a letter to a notary. Overwhelmed, Chabert felt hope for the first time in years, voiced a longing for vengeance, and declared he would rather die than live in obscurity. Derville, recognising the case’s complexity, vowed to treat it seriously, restoring Chabert’s purpose.
About three months after the interview, in Derville’s room, the notary Crottat—who had taken the post after his chief fled in bankruptcy—demanded the six hundred francs the old soldier had received be refunded. Crottat mocked Derville’s philanthropy; Derville admitted his generosity had only reached twenty-five louis.
Seeing German-stamped papers on Crottat’s desk, Derville had his clerk Boucard translate them. A Berlin notary reported that requested documents would arrive and were legally stamped; witnesses to the recorded facts still lived in Eylau, Prussia, and the woman who had saved Comte Chabert was alive in Heilsberg. “This looks like business,” Derville observed.
Crottat then outlined the background and the villainy of Roguin—mockingly called “the unfortunate Roguin”—who had absconded with eight hundred thousand francs of clients’ money. As third clerk, Crottat had copied the papers and recited the family and estate history: Rose Chapotel, wife of Hyacinthe (known as Chabert, Count of the Empire), had married without settlement, so they held property in common. The personalty had been about six hundred thousand francs. Before marriage Colonel Chabert’s will bequeathed one-quarter to hospitals and one-quarter to the State; after the will was contested and assets sold, the widow was left only with the portion bequeathed to the treasury, and Chabert’s personal fortune was reduced to roughly three hundred thousand francs.
Augustine Guillaume's cousin was born in the same city, Paris, in the same year as Augustine, and grew up happily alongside her, until she got married. 
Derville found the Colonel in the Faubourg Saint-Marceau with an old quartermaster named Vergniaud. The house was squalid—bare, damp, nearly empty—yet Chabert, smoking his pipe, retained a soldier’s calm. When asked why he had not moved to Paris, Chabert said the local folk had fed him for a year and he could not leave them now that he had a little money. He insisted he had done no one wrong and slept in peace.
Derville told him the Heilsberg papers were expected and that the woman who saved him was alive. Chabert, desperate for money he did not possess, reacted with frustration. Derville outlined the legal difficulties: proving identity would require costly, lengthy proceedings; the supposed bigamy—Comtesse Ferraud’s remarriage—would provoke inquiries, translations, and foreign formalities that could drag on for years and exhaust resources. Overwhelmed, Chabert despaired; Derville reassured him a favorable outcome was possible and proposed a friendly suit to annul the death certificate and the subsequent marriage. He offered a power of attorney and financial help; Chabert, hopeless but trusting, agreed.
As Derville left, Louis Vergniaud asked for a loan to improve the Colonel’s lodgings. Derville agreed but insisted Chabert should help his friends. He then planned to visit Comtesse Ferraud to gauge her position and leverage.
Madame Chabert, now Comtesse Ferraud, had married Monsieur le Comte Ferraud after the Revolution and converted her share of Chabert’s fortune into a comfortable income. Her marriage was socially accepted; Ferraud was politically ambitious and uneasy about the match. Derville found her at the Hotel Ferraud amid luxury. She greeted him lightly until he revealed that Colonel Chabert lived. Her laughter faded as he warned of the legal and social danger if Chabert’s identity were proven and hinted that Ferraud might not support her. Struck by the implications, she asked, “Does he still love me?”—already considering how to turn affection into a settlement. Derville left confident he had gained the upper hand.
A week after Derville’s visits, Colonel Chabert and Madame la Comtesse Ferraud arrived at their lawyer’s office from opposite ends of Paris. With Derville’s help Chabert had been restored—dignified in a blue suit, wearing the Legion of Honor ribbon, his soldierly bearing returned. The Countess, made up to seem youthful, followed. Clerks noticed; sensing tension, Derville separated them and presented a memorandum: Chabert would formally acknowledge his identity in exchange for financial support from his wife. The Countess, aghast at public exposure, resisted.
Chabert confronted her about their past, exposing the pain of their separation. She feigned surprise and tried to leave; Derville urged her to stay. A heated exchange ensued. The Countess then took him in her carriage to her country house in Groslay, confessing she loved another man, Comte Ferraud, and feared scandal that would destroy her dignity. Hurt, Chabert offered a measured understanding, aware of the social consequences she dreaded.
At Groslay she treated him kindly and they reminisced; he found temporary solace though resentment lingered. Meanwhile she moved to secure her position, writing to Delbecq to neutralize Chabert’s claim. Discovering this betrayal, Chabert resolved to confront her but ultimately sacrificed his identity to protect her and their children, renouncing his name and withdrawing from society.
Months later Derville found him reduced to a vagabond in a courtroom, poor yet retaining dignity and scorn for society’s indifference. In June 1840 Godeschal accompanied Derville to Ris; near Bicêtre they found an old pauper under an elm, drying his handkerchief. In shabby clothes with a carved, grotesque look, he was recognized as Comte Chabert—likely consigned to the workhouse because he reminded the Countess of their past. Insisting he was now “Hyacinthe,” a number in the institution, he envied a condemned man for not being married, accepted snuff gratefully, saluted them as “brave troopers,” and cried “Vive Napoleon!” Derville reflected on Chabert’s tragic arc—from foundling to Napoleonic soldier to workhouse inmate—and on human baseness: betrayal, greed, and moral decay. An addendum lists characters from Balzac’s Human Comedy and their other appearances, including Napoleon and Comtesse Ferraud.

######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
Desplein trained Horace Bianchon as a physiologist. He paired surgical brilliance with atheism hardened by corpse dissection and drew criticism for odd dress, moodiness, contempt. Bianchon earned his mentorship; Desplein’s care for a destitute water‑carrier revealed unexpected compassion.
Some time passed before Dr. Bianchon, despite their friendship, found an opening to ask Desplein about a pivotal episode. Their meetings rarely allowed private talk, but seven years later, after the Revolution of 1830, Bianchon saw Desplein entering Saint‑Sulpice and knelt beside him; they attended mass together.
“Can you explain your sudden piety?” Bianchon asked as they left. Desplein at first deflected, noting outward devotion that masks skepticism and criticizing politicians; pressed, he replied plainly: nearing the end of his life, he could trace its beginnings. Standing in the Rue des Quatre‑Vents, he pointed to the house where he had lived two desperate years and recalled hunger, poverty, isolation, and an irritable temper that made friendship difficult. He had no money for books or medical training and struggled alone to rise from destitution.
He spoke of meager meals and tireless labor without sympathy or support. The privileged could not imagine his plight and treated debt as a choice. Early hardships made him expect selfishness and envy, and to see mediocrity trying to pull down those who strove for excellence.
A turning point came when, at the depth of his poverty, he contemplated enlisting. He had hoped for a box of linen from a relative, but when the porter demanded too much to release it, despair set in. Then his neighbor Bourgeat—a humble water‑carrier—appeared and changed his fate.
Bourgeat, though poor himself, paid Desplein’s debts and secured new lodgings. He devoted his savings to Desplein’s future, understood his mission, and supported him selflessly. Desplein described Bourgeat as nurturing, almost maternal, proud of his protégé yet seeking no recognition. When Desplein became a house surgeon, leaving Bourgeat pained him; Bourgeat was proud nonetheless.
Later Desplein repaid him by buying a horse and a barrel, fulfilling a long‑held dream. Bourgeat was angry that Desplein had spent his money on him, yet delighted; he never claimed credit for Desplein’s rise, though Desplein felt he owed him everything. When Bourgeat fell ill, Desplein tended him devotedly; Bourgeat died in his arms, leaving him all he had. He had strong faith, and Desplein honored his wishes by having masses said for him after his death. The mass Desplein founded was a tribute to Bourgeat’s faith; Desplein hoped, if there is a paradise, that Bourgeat had found peace there and admitted he would give anything for such faith.
Bianchon, who had witnessed Desplein’s last days, could not say whether the great surgeon died an atheist. He wondered whether Bourgeat might have opened the gates of Heaven for Desplein, just as he had opened the doors to the earthly temple they had shared.
PARIS, January 1836.
ADDENDUM
These characters recur throughout La Comédie humaine: Bianchon appears in Father Goriot, César Birotteau, Lost Illusions and others; Desplein recurs in Cousin Pons, Lost Illusions, The Thirteen, Pierrette, and more.

######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
At one in the morning Dr. Horace Bianchon and Baron de Rastignac walked and talked about the Marquise d’Espard. Bianchon distrusted her; Rastignac, dazzled by rank and beauty, hoped she could advance him and settle his debts. Bianchon warned this was a mistake—likening her to a “one-eyed horse,” and, as a physician, he scorned fashionable women as insincere. Rastignac insisted a woman of fashion could be a useful ally. Still, Bianchon agreed to introduce him to his uncle, Judge Jean-Jules Popinot, though he doubted the rigid judge would help.
The next morning Bianchon visited Popinot’s house in a decayed quarter: a building bearing traces of former grandeur now reduced to practical poverty. Popinot himself was unkempt and unambitious, a supernumerary judge devoted to justice. He combined kindness with a remarkable ability to elicit confessions without force and upheld firm integrity, though frustrated by the legal system. Bianchon reflected on how hard it would be to persuade such an uncompromising man to seek social favors.
Twelve years earlier, during the 1816 famine, Popinot had been appointed President of the Commission Extraordinary to distribute food in his neighborhood. Direct contact with the destitute transformed him from a learned lawyer who noted legal outcomes into a protector of the poor, gradually becoming a sort of modern Saint Vincent de Paul. One charitable act led to another until Popinot became central to local benevolence: preventing crime, finding employment for the vulnerable, and providing discreet assistance. He lived largely unknown to most Parisians; gratitude from those he helped was often silent.
After two years he converted a storeroom into a modest parlor for the needy, furnished plainly. He kept meticulous records of donations and requests; his aide Lavienne managed pawn tickets and visited the hardest-hit districts. From dawn until late morning the parlor filled with women, children, and the destitute; even criminals respected the household. Popinot devoted mornings to the poor, afternoons to his practice, evenings to judicial duties. This close observation sharpened his judicial instinct: he learned to discern hidden virtues in the poor and to detect subtle signs of wrongdoing. With only a modest inheritance, his late wife’s savings, and a restrained salary, he lived frugally and lacked honors like the Legion of Honor.
His routine was interrupted when, at the Marquise d’Espard’s request, Popinot was commissioned to examine the Marquis d’Espard for a Lunacy Commission. Bianchon found the parlor a tableau of hardship; Popinot, in a threadbare nightcap and gown, paused his ledger to help a woman with groceries, having already attended to more than two hundred people that morning. As they prepared for breakfast, Bianchon urged Popinot to dress suitably for the Marquise’s meeting. Popinot hesitated—magistrates should not accept hospitality from parties in cases they oversee—but, persuaded that a visit would be revealing and kinder, he agreed and began to consider the Marquis’s mental state and its implications for the family.
The next evening at nine Bianchon climbed his uncle’s dusty stairs and found him bent over a complicated judgment. Because the tailor had not sent the coat Lavienne ordered, Popinot wore his old stained coat; Bianchon straightened his cravat and buttoned it, but the judge’s habitual movements revealed a gap between waistcoat and trousers. Bianchon noted the absurdity just as his uncle entered the Marquise d’Espard’s room.
A portrait of Madame d’Espard frames the interview. For seven years she had been a Paris fashion-figure. After separating from her husband in 1815, the Marquise—about thirty-three and mother of two boys, fifteen and thirteen—kept her vogue by elaborate coiffure, cold baths, and strict diet; emotionally detached, she admitted neither love nor hatred and parted from M. d’Espard with evident relief when he took the children, whom she regarded as impediments to her ambitions.
With an income of twenty-six thousand francs a year she lived quietly at first but by 1820 re-emerged socially, hosting gatherings and displacing other leading women. She cultivated ties with duchesses and countesses, turning her salon into a discreet center for political conversation. Adroit at social navigation, she attracted ambitious young men but was vain and instrumental—using intimacy as influence while avoiding personal compromise.
Rastignac, seeking an alliance, viewed her as a useful, clever patroness. Popinot’s arrival confirmed the household’s opulence; she received him with feigned weakness while the Chevalier d’Espard observed silently. Perceptive and probing, Popinot questioned the husband’s supposed mental change and the reasons for their separation. The Marquise supplied a polished story, but Popinot’s practical inquiries—about the children’s welfare and household arrangements—exposed inconsistencies: claims of hardship clashed with lavish living, suggesting she might be maneuvering to control her husband’s affairs for self-serving ends.
When she tried to deflect, Popinot warned that the court would scrutinize motives and transparency and that her façade might collapse. Afterwards Bianchon and Rastignac debated the scandal’s potential and Popinot’s character; Bianchon feared her influence. Popinot resolved to investigate further and summoned Madame Jeanrenaud.
The next day Madame Jeanrenaud arrived: stout, unrefined, a stark contrast to the Marquise. She denied wrongdoing and expressed plain concern for M. d’Espard’s welfare. Her straightforward honesty intrigued Popinot. As he traced M. d’Espard’s finances he realized the situation was more complex than it first appeared; untangling the truth would require careful examination of all parties.
Small incidents mattered: Popinot’s minor illness delayed a crucial visit—the judge stayed home instead of going to the Marquis d’Espard, losing a day whose absence mirrored larger historical delays.
Before following the lawyer to the Marquis’s residence, consider the father’s home life, which his wife’s petition branded as mad. On Rue de la Montagne-Sainte-Geneviève the Marquis lived in a once-grand, now weathered building partly occupied by a printing business. He took the ground floor, likely for his children, because the garden was unusually large; his rent was modest given the building’s decay.
The Marquis furnished his rooms in rich browns, creating a quiet, harmonious atmosphere. Few visitors were admitted; family life was secluded. The household—Marquis, his two sons, a long-serving cook and a man-servant—kept strict order. To shield his children from outside influence he avoided socializing with tenants, and that isolation bred gossip: neighbors called him mad and his servants untrustworthy, and his frugality suggested ruin. Noble by birth, the family embodied rare ideals; the Marquis, about fifty, was distinguished though eccentric in appearance, elegant in manner but frugal in dress. His sons—Comte Clément, nearly sixteen and fashionable, and Vicomte Camille—were handsome, and he focused their education on mathematics, Eastern languages, and history.
The day Popinot planned to question M. d’Espard fell on a Thursday holiday. Before their father rose the two boys were in the garden; the younger begged to visit the shooting-gallery and provoked Clément. The Viscount often took advantage of Clément’s indulgence and delighted in wrestling with him. Their noisy quarrel roused their father, who watched amused from the window. Neighbors joked that the Marquis seemed mad to make his children fight. Noticing them, the Marquis called the boys; they leapt into his room and, after a moment’s hesitation, Clément obtained permission from Camille.
Through the house people whispered about the Marquis’ supposed new madness. When Popinot arrived near noon with his clerk, the portress conducted him to the third floor, remarking that M. d’Espard had made his children fight that morning and had laughed when the younger bit the elder until he bled; she added that the Marquis rarely showed himself. The landing’s muddy floor and grimy banisters lent gossip some credibility and made Popinot receptive to the tale.
They entered an outer office where an old man, likely the clerk, sat amid shelves of published numbers; a grating separated a private room, and a cash-desk showed the enterprise’s commercial aspect. A shopman opened the inner room to reveal the Marquis, a venerable, simply dressed old man wearing the Cross of Saint-Louis, seated at a desk. Popinot explained he was a judge on the Inferior Court of Appeal for the Department of the Seine, charged with examining M. d’Espard regarding a petition for a Commission in Lunacy filed by the Marquise d’Espard. The Marquis reddened and trembled, then steadied himself.
“How is it,” he asked, “that I have had no notice of such a petition?” Popinot explained that notice is unnecessary because those deemed subject to such a commission are presumed to lack reason; the Court must verify the petitioner’s claims. The Marquis accepted this and asked what he must do. Popinot urged him to answer fully and confidentially, especially concerning delicate accusations Madame d’Espard might make. The Marquis feared that his explanations could blame his wife. Popinot promised discretion and the customary courtesy if the inquiry revealed a misunderstanding.
Popinot then confronted the major allegation: that the Marquis had dissipated his fortune for the benefit of Madame Jeanrenaud, widow of a bargemaster, or her son Colonel Jeanrenaud, for whom he allegedly procured an appointment and exhausted influence with the King. The Marquis flushed; tears came, but pride returned quickly. He said he was placed in a strange dilemma: revealing his motives would mean disclosing secret wounds and trusting his family’s honor to strangers. Popinot assured him of confidentiality. Freed from reserve, the Marquis spoke with clarity and passion.
After his marriage his wife had incurred heavy expenses that forced him to borrow. He could not afford a steward; most of his title-deeds had been taken to Paris by his father, who feared scrutiny. Their family name was Negrepelisse; the title d’Espard had been acquired through marriage in the time of Henri IV. An ancestor had lost the town of Negrepelisse during the religious wars and been ruined by property destruction. Though a later ancestor secured favor with Louis XIV and fortune, a stain of blood and disgrace remained, and the Marquis was determined to erase it.
He discovered that his grandfather had obtained the lands of Negrepelisse by means tied to the Revocation of the Edict of Nantes: a Protestant merchant was arrested and hanged, and the land passed to his ancestor by iniquitous means. The merchant’s family had the rightful claim; the victim’s name was Jeanrenaud. The Marquis could not bear the disgrace and resolved to find the heirs. Eventually he located M. Jeanrenaud, reduced to poverty and serving as a lieutenant under Napoleon. Believing the heir’s rights incontestable and their lack of means to sue, he offered to pay them the lands’ value at the time they were taken—eleven hundred thousand francs—payable at his convenience and without interest.
To effect restitution he lived on little for a long time. When he proposed to Madame d’Espard that they move to the country to live on half her income, she treated him as mad; her coldness convinced him she would have condoned his ancestors’ crimes. Terrified by her attitude, he resolved to leave her in control of her fortune after settling their debts, and to take charge of educating their children himself, determined to raise them as gentlemen. He invested prudently, repaid the debt sooner than expected, and had recently completed the payment of the eleven hundred thousand francs, making restitution without harming his children's prospects. These were the reasons for payments to Madame Jeanrenaud and her son.
Popinot asked whether Madame d’Espard knew his motives for retiring from public life. “Yes, monsieur,” the Marquis replied. Popinot, visibly moved, excused his clerk. He then inquired about other allegations, such as that the Marquis was engaged in a business unbecoming his rank. The Marquis declined to discuss it there and summoned Comte de Nouvion, an old family friend, explaining that he used the rooms as offices for the publication of the Picturesque History of China by a reputable firm, not as his private residence.
He showed Popinot his ground-floor apartment—a place of simple, touching harmony that struck the judge. The pleasant weather and garden convinced Popinot that a madman would hardly produce such beauty. The Marquis said he intended to remain until his younger son finished his studies, then travel with the children to European capitals to further their education and languages. He explained his China project: Comte de Nouvion, returned to France without fortune, was his partner; the Abbé Grozier, Keeper of the Books at the Arsenal, had been his tutor and taught him about China. Though enamored of China’s history, he remained a French gentleman. Financially, they had two thousand five hundred subscribers—twelve hundred in France—the book would cost about three hundred francs, and the Comte would earn six to seven thousand francs a year from it; the hundred thousand francs he hoped to make would cover his children’s education and pleasures. Twelve years of retirement to educate his children had cost him his memory at Court and political prospects, but he believed he had purified his family’s past and secured a glorious future for his children—a noble, if secret, task.
At that moment horses clattered in the courtyard. The two boys entered, robust and sunburnt, dressed for riding. They greeted their father and the lawyer. Popinot needed no further evidence of the Marquis’ bond with his sons. The boys reported shooting success and that they had ridden in the Bois, where they had seen their mother. “Why didn’t you speak to her?” the Marquis asked. “I think I noticed, father, that she doesn’t want us to speak to her in public,” Clément whispered. A cloud passed over the Marquis’ brow; Popinot felt deep sympathy at the scene of fatherly devotion. “Justice may look in here at any time,” the Marquis said. “If anyone is mad, it can only be the children, who are a little crazy about their father, and the father who is very crazy about his children—but that kind of madness rings true.”
Madame Jeanrenaud then entered, disregarding protests from the man-servant. “I take no roundabout ways!” she exclaimed. Bowing to those present, she announced she had come too late because the criminal judge was there. She declared that she and her son were of one mind to return everything, for their honor was at stake; they preferred to give back everything rather than cause trouble. “They must be as stupid as pans without handles to call you a lunatic,” she added. The boys clung to their father, alarmed.
“Silence, madame,” Popinot interposed. The Marquis sent his children to the garden. Popinot addressed Madame Jeanrenaud: the payments made by the Marquis were legally due, though prompted by an expansive sense of honesty. If every possessor of confiscated goods were compelled to make restitution every century and a half, few legitimate owners would remain in France. The law allowed M. d’Espard to dispose of his income without accounting for it. A Commission in Lunacy is appropriate only when actions lack reason; here, the remittances were driven by the highest motives. She could keep the money without remorse and let the world misinterpret a noble action. Popinot declared he would rather see the Marquis crowned than subjected to a Commission. Madame Jeanrenaud applauded his defense, calling it judge-like and warmly praising his words.
The Marquis extended his hand to Popinot, who pressed it gently; a private understanding passed between the humane judge and the noble, sublime man. “Monsieur le Marquis,” Popinot added, “from the first words of this inquiry, I regarded my clerk as unnecessary.” Leading M. d’Espard to the window, he said it was time to return home and that Madame la Marquise had likely acted under an influence the Marquis should counteract. Popinot left, the scene lingering with him; he even thought the rooms would suit him if the Marquis left them.
The next morning, having written his report the night before, Popinot went to the Palais de Justice to pursue prompt justice. As he prepared to put on his gown, the usher said the President wished to see him privately. “Good morning, my dear Popinot,” the President greeted. “I’ve been waiting.” Popinot asked if something was wrong. The President called it a silly trifle: the Keeper of the Seals, with whom he had dined, had suggested it would be best if Popinot did not sit on the case. Despite Popinot’s protest that he had left Madame d’Espard’s house before tea, the President cited propriety—“Caesar’s wife must not be suspected”—and the need to avoid any appearance of conflict that might expose the Bench to journalistic malice.
It was to be a matter of propriety, not discipline. Popinot was to signal his retirement from the case as a personal favor; in recompense, the President would secure the Cross of the Legion of Honor for him, long deserved. M. Camusot, a newly appointed judge from a provincial court, had been named in Popinot’s place. Popinot noted with ironic amusement Camusot’s pale ambition. He bowed and withdrew, choosing not to contest the implication.

######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
Dedicated to Lieutenant-Colonel Periollas, as a testimony of the author's esteem.
Have you felt unease and sadness in the crowded galleries since the 1830 revolution? Balzac argues the true Salon has been overwhelmed by quantity. Where careful selection once showcased the best and provoked debate—helping form reputations like Delacroix and Ingres—exhibitions now drown in numbers. Before 1817 only a few paintings were shown; today galleries overflow, individual works lose impact, shows close quickly, and the Salon’s role as arbiter of taste has become a tumultuous bazaar. Great artists are obscured by mediocrity, and talk of unrecognized genius increases when the Academy’s discriminating judgment is absent.
Into this swollen catalogue comes Pierre Grassou, nicknamed “Fougeres.” In 1832 he lives in a cramped rue de Navarin flat with a small studio overlooking Montmartre. A tidy, careful genre painter of modest means, he avoids grand historical subjects and produces small easel works. Dealer Elie Magus, who buys Grassou’s pictures cheaply, commissions portraits of the bourgeois Vervelle family—whose daughter’s dowry could secure Grassou’s future. Trained under Servin and Schinner but never mastering their art, ridiculed for mediocrity, his 1819 Salon entry was rejected and sold cheaply to Magus, launching a cautious, persistent career that typifies many of his contemporaries.
For days Pierre Fougeres waited near Elie Magus’s shop hoping his painting would attract notice; when it disappeared after a week, Elie said he had sold it and would buy more if Fougeres produced Flemish interiors, anatomy lessons, and landscapes. Overjoyed, Fougeres felt validated and ignored any ironic warning a more cynical artist might have offered.
He met his friend Joseph Bridau but, consumed with painting, could not enjoy the evening. Back home he sketched, bought supplies, hired models, and devoted himself to his craft. After two months he produced four paintings. Schinner and Bridau called them derivative and urged literature, but Fougeres retouched the canvases and sold them to Elie for twenty-five francs apiece, barely breaking even. He even noticed a strange mist over his works in the shop but dismissed it.
Over seven years he developed competent, if unremarkable, work. Elie sold the pieces and Fougeres earned a modest living. At the 1829 Exhibition, with friends’ help, he entered a striking piece—a young man in prison inspired by Gerard Dou—which drew attention even from Charles X. Madame bought the painting and commissioned more. Fougeres’s success rested more on imitation than innovation, yet his modesty, punctuality, and steadiness won supporters. He furnished a studio, completed commissions reliably, and by thirty-seven had produced around two hundred paintings that appealed to the bourgeoisie. Friends often preferred his character to his art, regretting his career choice, though he offered sound advice and appreciated beauty.
After the July Revolution he continued submitting to the Salon, lived frugally, and managed his finances with his notary Cardot. He dreamed of a comfortable artistic life and aspired to join the Institute. One day the wealthy Vervelle family came for portraits; the father admired Fougeres’ Legion of Honor cross, the family liked his work, Pierre was charmed by their daughter Virginie, and they began to see him as a suitable match, blending personal hopes with professional ambitions.
While painting Virginie, Grassou became part of the Vervelle household. Near the end of a sitting Bridau burst in, disheveled, begged five hundred francs to meet creditors, and criticized Grassou’s style, urging acceptance of nature’s imperfections. Bridau left; Madame Vervelle feared he had spoiled the portrait, but Grassou assured her he had improved it.
Flattered, Grassou accepted hospitality at Ville d’Avray and was shocked to find many canvases in their gallery were his earlier works he’d sold to Elie. Vervelle valued the collection at three hundred thousand francs; Grassou dryly noted his original low sale prices. Vervelle promised to double his daughter’s dowry if he could prove the claim. Grassou’s standing rose; he later gifted their completed portraits, securing their favor.
Today Pierre Grassou is a competent portrait painter, earning about twenty thousand francs a year, living on his wife’s six-thousand-franc dowry, painting bourgeois portraits for five hundred francs apiece, and even holding the Legion of Honor. Yet he remains insecure—sure other artists mock him and critics ignore him—still craving Academy recognition and secretly replacing celebrated paintings in the Ville d’Avray gallery with his weaker canvases. His mediocrity, tempered by kindness and generosity, renders him complex and oddly sympathetic.
################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
In some provincial towns a cloistered melancholy hangs over certain houses; silence seems to freeze life, broken only by a solitary figure at a window. Such an air brooded over a house in Saumur, down a steep, little-used street of pebble pavement and three-century-old buildings. These wooden houses, prized by antiquarians, show carved oak beams, slate façades, worn window-sills with neglected flower-pots, and heavy studded doors bearing traces of history—Protestant marks or forgotten bourgeois insignia—so that the history of France seems written in wood and stone.
Beside a dilapidated dwelling stood a country gentleman’s mansion; its stone arch faintly showed armorial bearings eroded by revolutions since 1789. Ground floors were not modern shops but plain dark rooms where goods lay exposed; a girl knitting would call her parents to serve customers. Local commerce was governed by climate: merchants, wine-growers, and coopers watched the weather anxiously, fearing frost or rain that could ruin them. On Saturdays vineyards called their owners away, streets fell silent, and neighbors observed one another’s doings.
The most melancholy mansion belonged to Monsieur Grandet. To understand the house one must know the man.
