Grandet began life as a master cooper in 1789 and married the daughter of a rich wood merchant. With savings and his wife’s dowry he bought prime vineyard land during the sale of church property and earned a reputation both as protector of the local nobility and supplier to Republican armies. By shrewd investments in vineyards and estates—and by serving as mayor in 1806—he steadily increased his fortune. That year he also inherited three fortunes, adding vast vineyards, farms, and an abbey, making him one of the region’s wealthiest men.
Townspeople speculated about the extent of his riches; he timed sales to secure maximum profit. By 1816 his fortune was estimated at nearly four million francs, and many believed he kept as much again in cash. Frugality governed his life; he spent only on necessities. Plain, taciturn, and stuttering, he consulted his wife yet kept her dependent. Few entered his house, among them the young magistrate C. de Bonfons, who hoped to win Grandet’s daughter Eugénie. The town’s social life centered on the contest for her—chiefly between the Cruchots and the des Grassins. In early 1811 the Cruchots gained advantage: when Froidfond was sold they negotiated its purchase for Grandet, who paid in gold—an ostentatious display confirming his wealth and influence throughout Saumur.
“The house of Monsieur Grandet” sits cold and silent above the town, sheltered by ruined ramparts. The entrance, flanked by two pillars and a weathered tufa arch, bears holes that give the façade a jail‑like look; above the arch a crumbling bas‑relief of the four seasons is topped by a plinth where plants, even a small cherry tree, have taken root.
A solid oak door, split and bolted with iron, has a rusted grating permitting a glimpse into a dark vault that leads to a garden enclosed by thick damp walls—the remnants of the ramparts between neighboring plots.
The principal ground‑floor room serves as antechamber, salon, office, boudoir and dining room. Wood‑paneled and gray with exposed beams, it has two street‑facing windows, a white stone chimney surmounted by an old brass clock and a greenish mirror, and antique chairs in faded tapestry. In each corner stand closets or buffets with dirty shelves. Between the windows sits an old card table with a barometer and two pastel portraits of Madame Grandet’s ancestors. Red gros de Tours curtains—a rare luxury—contradict Monsieur Grandet’s stinginess. A straw chair by the window lets Madame Grandet watch passersby; a work table and Eugenie’s armchair complete the room.
For fifteen years Madame and Mademoiselle Grandet’s lives have been governed by strict economy: fires lit only on specified dates, the foot‑warmer used through the cold months. La Grande Nanon, the family servant for thirty‑five years, earned sixty francs a year yet managed to invest in an annuity. Unattractive but indispensable, she cooked, washed and ran domestic affairs with tireless devotion; Grandet’s rare favors—an old watch, a pair of stockings—were treated as monumental largesse.
During the difficult 1811 grape harvest Grandet’s relation with Nanon combined dependence and domination: he relied on her labor while enforcing strict authority, and she, grateful for employment and meager kindnesses, remained devoted. Her kitchen was model‑clean and orderly, though she slept in a small closet‑like space and the upper floors were bare compared with the hall’s modest comforts.
On a November evening in 1819 Nanon lit the fire for the first time that season, coinciding with Eugenie’s birthday. The household prepared the ritual: Grandet to give Eugenie a gold coin, Madame Grandet a new dress; Grandet urged saving for marriage. Guests—including the pushy Madame des Grassins and her son Adolphe—arrived with gifts that provoked jealousy. Compliments masked rivalry as attendees subtly vied for a share of Grandet’s wealth. He watched with pride and possessiveness, manipulating relationships for his benefit. The evening blended familial warmth with cold calculation—until a loud knock at the door signaled an unexpected interruption that would change the night.
Nanon lit a candle and opened the door; Monsieur Grandet followed. His wife called anxiously, the players exchanged uneasy looks, and Monsieur des Grassins suggested leaving. A young man arrived with a porter and trunks. Grandet brusquely sent his wife back to the game and stepped out to speak with the newcomer.
The players sat but did not resume. Madame des Grassins asked if he was from Saumur; her husband said Paris. The notary checked the hour; Abbé Cruchot inquired if the gentleman was young. Eugénie noticed Nanon’s absence; others guessed the newcomer might be a relative. Madame Grandet, aware of her husband’s irritation, urged continuation.
Grandet returned alone; the traveler entered to polite bows and curtsies. Madame Grandet offered warmth, but Grandet, absorbed in a letter, dismissed concern. The stranger—Charles Grandet—thanked Eugénie and, though not tired, surveyed the room and the loto. At twenty-two, handsome and confident, he intended to impress provincial relatives with Parisian polish: a hunting costume, several waistcoats, a gold toilet set, and, despite instructions to travel modestly, a private coupé.
His elegance surprised the provincials: the shabby Cruchots were taken aback; locals met his nonchalance with mixed admiration and disdain. Eugénie was captivated, yearning for the world he represented and enchanted by his possessions.
The loto ended when Nanon returned with sheets; guests began to leave. Eugénie promptly prepared his room, adding refinements under the pretext of her birthday. Madame des Grassins vied for Charles’s attention; Abbé Cruchot made sly remarks. Grandet, having read a letter about his brother’s impending bankruptcy and a plea to protect his son, concealed his feelings, gave Charles a modest room, and downplayed his wealth. The evening closed with talk that Charles might unsettle Saumur; the Cruchots and des Grassins resolved to unite against any attachment between Eugénie and her cousin and to exploit the situation.
When the four relatives were left alone, Monsieur Grandet told his nephew Charles it was too late for business and set household hours: breakfast at eight, a light lunch at noon, dinner at five. He professed poverty despite appearances, insisting his fortune came from hard work. He told Nanon to bring candles; Charles at first refused but accepted one so worn it looked like tallow.
Grandet led Charles through the shabby house, avoiding the cold stairwell. As they climbed, Charles’s expectations fell—the staircase and decor were mean, and he wondered why his father had sent him. On the landing he noticed many doors, especially one walled up that hid Grandet’s office, the secret room where he did business and hoarded wealth. Across from it, Eugenie sat in her room; after a brief goodnight she and her mother retired. Grandet showed Charles to his attic room, warning him to call Nanon if needed because the dog could be dangerous. Nanon returned with a warming pan, which Grandet dismissed as unnecessary as he left.
Alone, Charles surveyed the shabby surroundings and questioned his father’s intentions; he felt out of place and uncertain. Eugenie, meanwhile, woke restless. Dressing, she admired herself yet felt inadequate beside her cousin. The garden and courtyard, once dull, now seemed charming; her heart swelled with unfamiliar feelings for Charles and she longed to please him.
Eugenie hurried to Nanon, asking her to prepare cream for Charles’s breakfast. Nanon reminded her they needed Grandet’s permission. When Grandet entered, he and Nanon quarreled over supplies, exposing his miserly nature; reluctantly he consented to give some flour and butter for a galette.
After breakfast Grandet took Eugenie to the fields, where they met Cruchot, the notary. Grandet spoke of his land and the recent sale of poplars, revealing his calculating side. Cruchot mentioned gossip that Charles might be a suitor for Eugenie. Grandet reacted vehemently, declaring he would rather throw his daughter into the Loire than marry her to her cousin—words that shattered Eugenie’s hopes.
Back at the house, news arrived of Grandet’s brother’s suicide — a development with dire consequences for Charles. The subsequent meal was heavy with unspoken grief. Eugenie, moved and tearful, felt deep sympathy; Grandet remained indifferent. Despite the risk, Eugenie and Nanon conspired to prepare Charles a better breakfast, and her affection for him deepened as she worked.
Charles arrived in Parisian fashion, cheerful despite a difficult journey. His ease and charm captivated Eugenie; their conversation emphasized the contrast between his life of luxury and her modest upbringing. At breakfast Charles praised Eugenie, showed her a ring, and their fingers briefly touched. When coffee came, Grandet returned, eyed the food—especially the sugar—and demanded its source. Charles asked for sugar for his coffee, but Grandet rebuffed him, suggesting more milk instead.
Grandet then revealed the devastating news: Charles’s father had died by suicide, leaving him bankrupt. Charles broke down. Eugenie and her mother were compassionate; Grandet was immediately practical, lecturing Eugenie about the dishonor of bankruptcy and declaring Charles must go to the West Indies to seek fortune. Eugenie offered to help; Grandet reluctantly agreed to fund the journey. The house remained heavy with grief as Charles mourned and Eugenie resolved to share his sorrow—her life irrevocably changed.
About four o’clock a sharp knock startled Madame Grandet. Grandet burst in, rubbing his hands, announcing he had “trapped them all”: the wine was sold to the Dutch and Belgians at two hundred francs a puncheon, half paid down. He showed six louis for his wife and predicted prices would fall in three months. News of the sale spread panic through Saumur. Eugenie, excited, asked if he had a thousand puncheons; he confirmed it. She added up the sum and asked, “Then, father, you can easily help Charles.” The mention of his neglected nephew stung Grandet. He scolded Eugenie for presuming to advise him, insisted any duty to Charles would be discharged on his terms, and threatened to send her to the Abbaye des Noyers with Nanon if she meddled.
When Madame Grandet said Charles had not come down, Grandet asked why; Eugenie answered that he was weeping for his father. Momentarily bewildered, Grandet withdrew to consider investing the proceeds in public funds. His thinning forest had already yielded six hundred thousand francs; with this sale his total reached nine hundred thousand. Tempted by dreams of twenty percent returns, he settled on a more modest eight, imagining that in two years he might withdraw a million and a half in gold. Nanon’s call to dinner interrupted his calculations; he accepted the eight percent plan, murmuring that hunger would bring his nephew from grief.
At table he learned Charles refused to eat; he commented curtly, and Nanon approved—“So much saved.” When Madame Grandet suggested mourning, Grandet mocked the expense and told her to buy it out of her six louis: a hat-band would suffice. Eugenie sewed in silence while he ran through arithmetic. The town buzzed about the sale; Nanon kept spinning, and Grandet kept repeating his maxim, “Nothing should be wasted,” as he pursued further schemes.
That night, preoccupied with present gain and dismissive of any future life, Grandet revealed how money governed his laws, politics, and morals. He bargained with conscience and religion, ordered Madame Grandet to her prayers, and finally growled, “The devil take your good God!” He visited Charles’s dark room to chide him in his own miserly way: allowing the nephew to weep as natural, insisting he was a “good uncle,” offering a little glass of wine, and scoffing at the theft of a small wax candle. Madame Grandet and Eugenie, overhearing, fled in fear.
Alone, Madame Grandet prayed for Grandet and feared the punishment he had threatened for Eugenie. Eugenie lay restless; her concern for Charles swelled into a deeply personal disturbance. In the early hours she rose and found him asleep in an armchair, exhausted by grief; his smoothed, sorrowful face moved her in a way she barely understood. He woke, apologized; she urged him to bed and fled, ashamed yet secretly happy—a turning point in feeling and fear of judgment.
Next morning Grandet arrived distracted but outwardly affectionate, kissed Eugenie, and sat at breakfast as if forgetting his threats. “What has become of my nephew? The lad gives no trouble,” he said; Nanon reported he was asleep. His unexpected softness was only surface: he had spent the night planning schemes to enlarge his fortune and dominate others, contriving to restore his brother’s honor without spending a franc by manipulating public opinion in Paris. In his absence, Eugenie found tender pleasure in caring for Charles, bringing him breakfast to spare him shame. Her innocent devotion and his gratitude hinted at changes money alone could not govern, suggesting obligations and feelings that would shape their lives beneath Grandet’s continuing calculations and the household’s routine.
Saumur was more stirred by the dinner Grandet gave the Cruchots than by the previous night’s sale of his vintage, which the wine-growers saw as a betrayal; Grandet, who fancied himself superior to those he deceived, paid little heed to their opinion.
When the des Grassins learned of Guillaume Grandet’s failure and death, they came that evening to offer sympathy and probe why he had invited the Cruchots. At five Monsieur C. de Bonfons and his uncle the notary arrived in their Sunday best. The dinner was sombre: Grandet grave, Charles silent, Eugenie mute, Madame Grandet speaking little—a fitting meal of condolence. Afterwards Charles asked to retire to write a letter.
Once the women left, Grandet showed the craftiness that earned him the nickname “the old dog.” Affecting a practiced stutter, he steered his guests toward bankruptcy. The president of the commercial court explained that a respected merchant facing insolvency might have a receiver appointed, distinguishing legal liquidation from absolute ruin; sometimes liquidation could be managed without courts: if the merchant did not file his schedule and no creditor appeared, the family or heirs could liquidate the business. Debts could also be bought at a discount so that, if titles to all debts were acquired, the heirs would owe nothing.
The notary urged this course to save Grandet’s reputation. Mindful of family standing and his nephew’s prospects, Grandet feigned ignorance of Parisian tricks. The president offered to go to Paris to negotiate with creditors and suggested Grandet provide some assets to facilitate deals. The des Grassins declared they would liquidate the debts honorably; Madame des Grassins even offered to go to Paris. Favoring the banker, Grandet pulled him aside to discuss a separate investment in government bonds, asking for discretion and arranging to meet the next day.
As word of his supposed generosity spread, the town forgave the vintage sale. Once the house was closed, Grandet ordered Nanon to keep silent and secretly prepared to sell gold in Angers while the market was favorable. In the early hours Eugenie, worried about Charles, thought she heard a cry but was distracted by her father and Nanon carrying a heavy keg; Grandet told Nanon to tell his wife he had gone to the country and hurried off, determined to reach Angers before morning.
“My father has gone,” Eugenie thought as she listened from the top of the stairs. The house was silent; the carriage’s rumble had faded and a cry from her cousin’s room cut the hush. A thin strip of light from the doorway lit the old staircase.
She ran up. Charles sat in an armchair, head bowed, a pen fallen from his hand; his breathing was labored. On the table lay sealed bills to tradesmen—evidence he was settling affairs to leave France—and two open letters. The address “My dear Annette” struck Eugenie to the core. Rooted by shame and curiosity, she yielded to the latter, adjusted his head, kissed his hair, and read.
Charles’s letter to Annette explained that misfortune had ruined him: his father had killed himself, their fortune was gone, and he was penniless. He could not remain in Paris where the scorn for a ruined man would be intolerable. He would not ask Annette to sacrifice comforts to keep him; better to part. He planned to try his fortune in the Indies or America—perhaps to go to sea—and might return aged and altered. He urged Annette to cherish their happiness but accepted that he could not demand fidelity; the letter ended abruptly, as if his hand had left the page.
Eugenie softened the harshness of these words, convincing herself that love might make a girl blind to selfishness. Young and pure, she read passion into his phrases; “My dear Annette” became music. She mourned his misfortune and thought, “I have gold; I will give it to him.”
The second letter, to his friend Alphonse, was practical: sell possessions, recover what could be saved, keep his horse Briton if possible, press builders over an unpaid carriage, and pay debts—including six louis lost at cards. It revealed a calculating mind intent on avoiding dishonor and salvaging what remained.
Overwhelmed with compassion and admiration, Eugenie ran to her room, opened an old oak cabinet where she had hidden a red velvet purse—a grandmother’s relic—and counted coins: various foreign gold pieces and rare rupees—about five thousand eight hundred francs in all. Joy and resolve filled her. Secret love for her cousin and pity for his plight overcame propriety. She returned upstairs at once, candle and purse in hand.
Charles, stunned awake, listened as Eugenie, trembling, confessed she had read the letters and, without hesitation, offered him her savings. She presented herself as a poor girl who wanted nothing, a sister bent on helping a cousin. Kneeling, she implored him to accept her gold and, by taking it, to show that he respected her and was generous. Tears fell from Charles onto her hands; moved, she poured the coins onto the table. “You will be rich,” she cried; “this gold will bring you happiness; someday you shall return it to me—are we not partners?”
He could not refuse. He rose, kissed her, and revealed he would reciprocate not with money but with an object he held dear: a leather dressing-case with a hidden drawer containing two portraits by Madame Mirbel, set with pearls—portraits of his mother and father, who were Eugenie’s aunt and uncle. He declared the case more precious to him than life and begged Eugenie to be its guardian. If she guarded it and, should his fortune vanish, the pearls and gold could repay her; yet he demanded it ultimately be destroyed to prevent its passing into other hands. This exchange of trust marked a turning point; Eugenie looked at him with her first deep, womanly affection. He kissed her hand, calling her an “angel of purity,” and vowed that feeling and sentiment must be everything between them henceforth.
Their parting was gentle. Eugenie, practical and blushing, suggested Charles keep the treasures in her old cabinet. He sighed in his mourning but kissed her hand and, as she closed the door, murmured the ruin of his fortunes. Naively she tried to reassure him by imagining family wealth elsewhere; he scoffed. They traded sweet, foolish remarks: from that moment, beneath his black, Charles began to wear roses.
Next morning, before breakfast, Madame Grandet found Eugenie and Charles together in the garden. He remained sorrowful; the three women of the house, mindful of his grief, allowed him a quiet morning in the hall while they tended chores and awaited tradesmen for neglected business. Around five, Monsieur Grandet returned from Angers, unexpectedly jubilant, having made fourteen thousand francs on an exchange of gold. He had left his groom at Angers to look after the exhausted horses.
“I am hungry,” he proclaimed upon entering. Nanon scolded him for not eating. Shortly Des Grassins arrived to take Grandet’s orders. Absorbed in his financial success and amused to baffle others, Grandet barely registered his nephew’s presence. Des Grassins, the banker, informed him that gold fetched high prices in Angers and that people had come from Nantes; talk turned to sending theirs. Grandet curtly refused, hinting at sparing his old friend wasted exertion. A startled consultation followed; they glanced often at Charles.
Des Grassins learned Grandet planned to invest a sum that might yield enormous returns—talk even floated of producing a hundred thousand francs a year—though deputies’ figures and Grandet’s actual plans remained shrouded in his dry, cautious talk. Des Grassins offered to go to Paris and asked Charles if he had any commissions. Charles declined stiffly. Des Grassins rebuked the nephew for the curt reply, reminding him of family ties and honor. Charles, pale, kissed his uncle and withdrew. Eugenie watched her father with secretly swelling admiration for his mastery over affairs.
Grandet, giddy from his gain, dismissed Des Grassins with a jocular “decoy those people; lead them by the nose,” and returned to his armchair, asking Nanon for black-current ratafia. His excitement made him fidget, then sing snatches of a ditty, pacing in his odd “dancing steps.” The women exchanged uneasy glances at his towering contentment; his gaiety frightened them when it rose to a peak. He chose to go to bed early, expecting others to follow. Madame Grandet, caught in his current, lived according to his will.
During the two hours before retiring, Grandet grew more talkative and philosophical about money, offering peculiar maxims that revealed the mindset governing his life: life’s pleasures are fleeting; gold won’t circulate and remain in your purse; if it did, life would be too fine. His cheerfulness brought small generosity—offering Nanon a share of ratafia—but an underlying calculation made him formidable. The evening passed under a mood blending domestic routine, latent anxieties about affairs, and the quiet bloom of a new intimacy between Eugenie and Charles, sealed by her sacrifice and his trust.
Thus the house resumed its ordinary hum: tradesmen, household tasks, and the unspoken commerce of feelings and fortunes. Eugenie’s secret gift and Charles’s confided treasures had altered their relation; Grandet’s profit from the Angers exchange had reawakened his habit of managing and tightening the household’s finances. Between these threads—Eugenie’s selfless devotion and Grandet’s thrifty triumph—Balzac sketches the beginnings of passion, calculation, and the mingling of sentiment with the sober arithmetic of money that will shape the family’s future.
The next morning the family breakfasted together in a private, intimate mood. Grief had drawn Madame Grandet, Eugénie, and Charles closer; even Nanon felt sympathy. The old man, absorbed in thrift and intent on sending Charles away to avoid expense, stayed aloof, busy with farm work, leaving Madame Grandet to watch over Eugénie and Charles as their relationship quietly deepened.
For Eugénie, love grew from their shared secret and took on the simplicity and tenderness of childhood: soft glances, gentle words, small consolations. She delighted in comforting Charles; he, hardened by worldly passions, found in her a pure, sincere attachment and prized their modest, unpretentious life. While Grandet worked in the fields after breakfast, Charles savored the company of Eugénie and her mother, charmed by virtues he had once thought limited to Germany. Eugénie became his ideal; she surrendered to their love without foreseeing the looming separation.
Three days after the des Grassins left, Grandet took Charles to the civil courts to renounce his claim to his father’s estate—a legal act that felt like betrayal. Charles signed powers of attorney, obtained a passport, and sold his belongings to prepare for departure. Grandet admired Charles’s new appearance in mourning and regarded him as ready to seek his fortune. When Charles gathered valuables to sell, Grandet offered to buy them, valuing them at nearly a thousand francs; Charles accepted gratefully and gave his mother’s thimble to Madame Grandet. Grandet then insisted on paying for Charles’s passage to the Indies, a generosity that paradoxically cost him little.
As Charles prepared to leave, Eugénie confessed her sorrow; he acknowledged his obligations and his coming-of-age. They wore mourning for a Requiem Mass for Guillaume Grandet, and Charles received letters from Paris about his affairs, which he shared with Eugénie. In the garden he assured her his affairs were in order, that his effects would be sent to Nantes, and urged her not to tie her future to his uncertain fate. Still, their love held firm and they kissed passionately.
On the day of departure Grandet unexpectedly packed Charles’s things for Nantes. The family saw him off at the diligence; after he left the house felt empty. Eugénie kept his room unchanged and conspired with Nanon; her beauty grew more radiant as she followed his voyage on a map and confided her constant love only to Nanon, who deeply empathized.
Two months later life was consumed by the secret: Charles filled their thoughts; Eugénie even looked for him in her aunt’s portrait. One Sunday Madame Grandet discovered the truth about the gold Eugénie had given Charles, and panic followed. They feared Grandet’s reaction and missed Mass. On the cold morning of January 1, 1820, Madame asked for a fire to avoid confrontation. Grandet, unexpectedly cheerful, revealed he had received a large sum from investments and boasted over breakfast, which increased their unease. He then demanded the gold’s return; Eugénie said she no longer had it. Angered, he accused her of deceit and threatened confinement on bread and water. Heartbroken, she fled to her mother. Nanon, fearing for Eugénie, vowed support and that evening secretly brought a hare pâté, strengthening their bond as they faced Grandet’s harshness.
For months the old wine-grower tended his ailing wife while never mentioning their daughter Eugénie, who remained confined to her room and given only bread and water—Nanon secretly supplied extra. Townspeople condemned Grandet; Eugénie’s meek attendance at Mass inspired sympathy. Her chief sorrow was her mother’s deterioration, which she often blamed on herself. Madame Grandet urged reconciliation and begged her husband to restore their daughter to the family, but he remained indifferent as she weakened.
In spring Madame confided in the Cruchots, who were appalled and discussed legal remedies. Eugénie overheard but, fearing scandal, begged them to stay silent and resolved to obey her father for the family’s honor. When notary Cruchot warned Grandet that, if Madame died, Eugénie’s inheritance might force sale of property and threaten his control, Grandet began to soften. Returning home to reconcile, he found Eugénie and her mother admiring a gold dressing-case sent by Charles. Possessive, he confronted her; she demanded it back and, in despair, threatened self-harm. Shocked, Grandet relented, allowed her to keep the case, scattered coins on the bed, and promised kindness. Despite this brief gentleness, Madame Grandet died peacefully in October 1822, urging Eugénie to seek happiness in heaven and warning of a selfish world.
Afterwards Eugénie felt a renewed attachment to the childhood home. Her father’s sudden tenderness—taking her to breakfast and watching her with an almost kind gaze—made the household suspect his mind was failing. But at the mourning dinner Cruchot revealed Grandet’s motive: to avoid dividing property or paying taxes on supposed funds, Grandet needed Eugénie to renounce her rights so he could retain control. Confused, she asked for the deed to sign; Grandet, visibly moved, proposed she trust him and accept a monthly allowance of one hundred francs. She agreed and the next morning signed away her inheritance. A year later he had not paid the promised money; when she reminded him he awkwardly offered jewels.
Over the next years Grandet trained Eugénie in running the household and his frugal ways; by the third year she was mistress of the house and their monotonous life continued. Her sadness was well known though she never spoke of it. In 1827 Grandet, now eighty-two, suffered a stroke; as he declined Eugénie devoted herself to his care. He died fixated on hidden wealth, urging Eugénie to take care of his riches.
Left alone with Nanon, Eugénie then learned from Maitre Cruchot that she had inherited seventeen million francs. Despite the fortune, her thoughts turned to finding her cousin Charles. She secured an annuity for Nanon—who married Antoine Cornoiller and remained Eugenie’s housekeeper while Cornoiller managed the estate—and at thirty Eugénie had never known joy: her childhood was shadowed by her mother’s suffering and an unfulfilled love for Charles, who had not contacted her in seven years, while local notables misread her indifference as interest.
While events unfolded in Saumur, Charles prospered in the Indies. Early ventures brought him six thousand dollars, and he found the slave trade most profitable. He went to Africa, combined enslaved people with other lucrative goods, and vowed to return to Paris rich and restore his standing. Hardened and amoral, he rationalized that what one society condemned another might praise. Ruthless, he engaged in exploitative trades, customs fraud, and resold cheaply bought pirate goods. Memories of Eugénie faded; he came to view his family with contempt and her as little more than a creditor. To conceal his identity he adopted the alias “Shepherd.” By 1827 he returned to Bordeaux aboard the Marie Caroline with nearly two million francs in gold-dust, planning further profit at the Paris mint.
On the ship he met Monsieur d’Aubrion, a gentleman with royal connections who, though once prominent, was now financially strained. Madame d’Aubrion, anxious to secure her family’s future, sought a wealthy husband for her plain daughter and saw Charles as suitable, promising to help him gain a title and income through marriage. Ambitious, Charles entertained the match; Annette encouraged him. When Des Grassins asked Charles to settle his father’s debts, the newly wealthy Charles refused, declaring his father’s failures no longer his problem.
In August, Eugénie—sitting where they’d once pledged love—received Charles’s trembling letter recounting his success, announcing the engagement as a pragmatic step to status, and declaring marital love a delusion. Devastated but resigned, she recalled her mother’s warning and resolved to a life of prayer and acceptance. She spoke with the parish priest—connected to the Cruchots and allied with Monsieur de Bonfons—who cautioned her against withdrawing entirely from the world and reminded her of duties to society and the poor.
Madame des Grassins arrived, driven by vengeance, to reveal Charles’s engagement to Mademoiselle d’Aubrion and produced a letter from her husband outlining Charles’s situation, including his father’s bankruptcy; Eugénie returned the letter unread, filled with contempt and sorrow. Tormented, she asked the priest whether remaining a virgin after marriage would be a sin. She secluded herself in her father’s secret room, skipped dinner, and avoided guests. Then, unexpectedly, she asked Monsieur de Bonfons to stay—an act taken as a pledge of marriage. Privately she said she could offer only friendship and asked him to settle her uncle’s debts in exchange for her hand. He accepted, hurried to Paris, paid creditors with interest, and carried Eugénie’s letter explaining her decision. He informed Charles, who was at first excited but then shocked when he learned of Eugénie’s fortune. Madame d’Aubrion remained confident of the match.
The president returned to Saumur and announced his marriage to Eugénie. Although wealthy, she lived simply and devoted herself to charity. Her husband entered politics but died soon after being elected deputy. Widowed, Eugénie lived piously, dedicating her fortune to the community and rejecting worldly desires. Rumors linked her to the Marquis de Froidfond, but she remained independent, supported by loyal servants Nanon and Cornoiller. Her story reveals the tension between wealth and personal fulfillment, showing her struggle to meet social expectations while staying true to her values.
################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
To Mademoiselle Anna Hanska:
Dear Child,—You, the joy of the household, whose pelerine flutters in summer among the groves of Wierzschovnia, how can I dedicate to you a story full of melancholy? Yet should not sorrows be spoken of to a young girl like you, since the day may come when your sweet hands will be called to minister to them? It is difficult to find a subject worthy of your eyes, so I have no choice; perhaps you will feel fortunate when you read this story sent to you by your old friend, De Balzac.
PIERRETTE
I. THE LORRAINS
One October morning in 1827 a sixteen-year-old boy in modest dress stood in a small square of Lower Provins, among mills and bourgeois houses. He found the neat white-stone house he sought; its tidy air belonged to a retired petty shopkeeper. As the sun lit a calico garret curtain, the boy sang a Breton ditty for newlyweds—recalling Brittany and life’s bittersweet turns. Pierrette, at the window, signaled; he offered a yellow broom-flower. He was Brigaut, her childhood companion, newly returned and planning to settle nearby. Alarmed by a grotesque old maid below, sharp-eyed and ready to imagine scandal, Pierrette bade him go.
Overwhelmed, she thought of her fragile childhood: illness, faded joys, and a tangled lineage. Her mother, born a Demoiselle Auffray, had endured several marriages and a complicated inheritance, then wed Major Lorrain, who died in battle leaving poverty. Sent to live with her uncle and aunt, the Rogrons, Pierrette remained largely ignorant of her lineage and its financial consequences, setting the stage for what follows.
Père Rogron, an indulgent, ruddy-faced innkeeper, and his unattractive, selfish wife moved into a dilapidated house inherited from old Auffray. Their children, Sylvie and Jérôme-Denis, were neglected and sent to Paris to work with little support. Sylvie became a forewoman on rue Saint-Denis and Jérôme a head-clerk; together they bought the retail “Family Sister,” achieving modest success but gaining a reputation for severity and stinginess. Sylvie ran the business shrewdly though plainly; Jérôme was less competent. Their parsimony and harsh treatment of clerks caused high turnover. Pleasant to customers, Sylvie was a sour spinster at home.
After their father’s death they learned of cousin Pierrette’s inheritance. They considered selling and returning to Provins but refused to take Pierrette in, delayed replying, and left for Provins four years before Brigaut arrived. Back home, their Paris airs faded. New wealth funded travel, politics, hunting, and ostentatious renovations intended to mimic aristocracy, but they failed to enter established circles led by Madame Tiphaine. Ridiculed and sidelined, Sylvie’s faith that money bought status collapsed; she became frugal and nostalgic for business. Bringing Pierrette back exposed their neglect, and they fell in with lower bourgeois figures who exploited them—showing provincial social codes outweighed money.
After her grandparents were hospitalized, Pierrette—ashamed to depend on charity—felt relieved when distant wealthy relatives were located. Brigaut, a childhood friend and her mother’s friend’s son, gave her sixty francs for the trip to Paris; she accepted without thanks, an intimacy that moved him to visit often and run after the coach in tears when she left. Penniless in Paris, a conductor briefly paid for her hotel; four days later a kinder one brought her to Mademoiselle Rogron’s, where her cousins received her with astonishment.
She arrived in Breton clothes with a small bundle. Sylvie and the maid Adèle were surprised; Pierrette, shy and overwhelmed, spoke little. Adèle and Sylvie showed her a modest room that felt luxurious to her, and Sylvie immediately fretted over Pierrette’s lack of clothing and resolved to buy a new wardrobe.
In Brittany Pierrette had known affection and freedom; with the Rogrons she found coldness and constant criticism. Exploring the house and garden the next morning, she was reproved for dirty shoes. Their concern for possessions, their continual fault‑finding, and their emphasis on appearances sharply contrasted with the love she’d known. Pierrette’s innocence made their disdain more painful; she began to suppress her joy and became passive for fear of scorn. When she tore a dress playing, she was devastated; the Rogrons treated such mishaps as proof she was a burden rather than a child.
Life in Provins worsened as Sylvie forbade visits to friends and pursued social ambitions encouraged by Madame Vinet and the colonel. As her cousins schemed for status, Pierrette was increasingly confined—transformed from a lively child into a shadow of her former self, enduring harsh, self‑interested care that was not love. At night Sylvie’s coldness made her cry; when Pierrette said her poor grandmother had been kinder, Sylvie countered that the grandmother had taken money while a rich cousin would leave her something—revealing their interest in inheritance over affection.
Accused of mischief and disobedience, she was punished and urged into schooling to be “hardened” and kept quiet. Vinet dubbed her a rough diamond and hired a writing master; the messy lessons and stained papers were blamed on Pierrette, who was told she must earn her keep. Only Adèle showed genuine kindness, secretly warming her bed. The colonel and Vinet blamed her supposed “Breton” nature while investing in social schemes like a local newspaper.
She considered escaping to her grandparents, but a death and Rogron’s appointment as guardian prevented it. Vinet hinted at an inheritance and Rogron secured a promise of eight thousand francs from her grandmother. Preparations for Pierrette’s first communion brought the zealous priest Monsieur Habert into her life; his influence led her to find solace in faith and abandon escape plans. Though Habert’s sister offered a school place, the Rogrons refused for fear it might lead to a convent.
Later social introductions and Sylvie’s ambitions intensified Pierrette’s drudgery. Bathilde and other visitors drew Rogron’s attention, and Sylvie forced Pierrette into domestic tasks. With Adèle’s protection diminished, she was treated worse than the staff—scolded for trifles, denied praise—and over the years her health and spirit faded into a silent shadow while her cousins remained oblivious.
Before recounting the domestic drama Jacques Brigaut’s arrival would provoke in the Rogron household, it is necessary to explain how he came to Provins, since he plays a significant yet passive role.
Brigaut had fled the Rogron house, shaken by Pierrette’s decline from a lively girl to a pale, sorrowful figure. From a high point in the Upper town he watched her home, consumed by concern. Their rare bond pushed him to better himself: he briefly went to Paris to learn a trade, then returned to Provins and found work as a journeyman cabinetmaker so he could stay near Pierrette. He kept his feelings hidden and learned more about the Rogrons after Madame Frappier revealed their dubious acquisition of Pierrette’s grandfather’s property. He often saw Pierrette at the market or near the church, where she signaled him to keep away, and worried about her frailty.
Pierrette sensed changes but did not grasp the Rogrons’ internal strife between Sylvie and Mademoiselle Habert (Céleste), both angling for advantageous marriages. Sylvie, forty-two, feared aging alone; Céleste, about thirty-two and urged on by her priest brother, sought a husband. A recent political shift favoring local Liberals intensified the scramble for social position. Sylvie’s consultation with a discouraging doctor heightened her anxiety; Céleste urged delay to remove a rival. Vinet, a lawyer who coveted Bathilde, exploited Sylvie—encouraging the colonel to court Pierrette while secretly plotting to divert the Rogron fortune to Bathilde. After overhearing Brigaut serenade Pierrette, Sylvie accused her of secret admirers; Pierrette’s frightened silence and increased toil worsened her health. Brigaut offered fleeting comfort, but love, jealousy, and ambition had become entangled, foreshadowing tragedy for all.
For a month Rogron had stopped bringing the Constitutionnel to Gouraud, so Gouraud came himself and lingered to gossip and walk with Sylvie. Eager to see him, Sylvie dressed her best; when Gouraud arrived Rogron excused himself, leaving them alone.
Gouraud’s questions about his cousin were met with evasiveness: the cousin was unwell. Uneasy about Vinet’s growing influence with the Rogrons, Gouraud—only a half-pay officer—felt sidelined. He feared Vinet controlling plans and Rogron’s infatuation with Bathilde de Chargeboeuf would ruin his prospects; he even suspected Vinet of manoeuvring to push him toward Pierrette to benefit Vinet and Bathilde. With Sylvie alone, he denied any interest in Pierrette and favored a more mature match. Encouraged, Sylvie hinted her brother had once thought Pierrette suitable; sensing her interest, Gouraud declared he would marry Sylvie, delighting Rogron. Sylvie began planning their home and watched Pierrette and the colonel with suspicion.
At dinner Sylvie scolded Pierrette for a stain but insisted she look presentable—suspecting collusion. The Chargeboeuf ladies arrived; Bathilde, gorgeously dressed under Vinet’s guidance, treated marriage to Rogron as a stepping stone to power, using beauty and connections for independence. The evening grew tense: Bathilde’s condescension and Vinet’s remarks sharpened rivalries. Pierrette’s innocent comments at cards provoked Sylvie, and Pierrette left in tears. That night Sylvie brooded on men’s duplicity and her precarious hold.
The next day Pierrette suffered a painful fall; Sylvie blamed her and showed no sympathy. A week of torment followed. Concealing Brigaut’s arrival, Pierrette longed for him; he arranged clandestine correspondence—slipping notes while buying fruit and asking her to lower a string from her window. Pierrette replied at midnight, pouring out love and describing her relatives’ abuse. Under Sylvie’s scrutiny her health declined; she fainted. Sylvie dismissed it and violently tried to seize Pierrette’s letters, wounding her. Brigaut’s urgent appeals brought Madame Lorrain, who witnessed the violence, aided Pierrette, and got medical care. As she recovered, her bond with Madame Lorrain deepened; the promise of protection and a future with her grandmother and Brigaut offered a hopeful glimmer. Pierrette resolved to reclaim her life.
At nine o’clock Monsieur Martener told Monsieur Tiphaine about Pierrette’s mistreatment by the Rogrons. Tiphaine brought in Auffray, a notary-relative; long-standing enmities between the Vinet and Tiphaine factions—fueled by damaging truths about Madame Tiphaine’s family—gave the dispute political force.
Tiphaine consulted the public prosecutor; with Auffray and Martener he arranged to petition the courts to make Auffray Pierrette’s guardian so a Family Council, backed by medical testimony, could remove her from the Rogrons’ control.
By midday the town buzzed: neighbors had heard Pierrette’s cries and rumors spread. Doctors Bianchon and Brigaut examined her and confirmed abuse. Vinet, the Rogrons’ lawyer, warned of criminal exposure; Sylvie, initially evasive, later admitted jealousy and the truth. To quiet the scandal Vinet proposed a marriage announcement; under pressure the Rogrons signed a marriage contract that served their political aims. Then Vinet filed a petition accusing Brigaut and the widow Lorrain of abducting Pierrette, trying to recast his clients as victims.
The hearing was postponed while Tiphaines rallied supporters; Pierrette, unaware of the turmoil, was cared for by her grandmother and Brigaut. When court reconvened, Auffray presented the physicians’ report and the judge ordered she be kept from the Rogrons until the Family Council met—a public rebuke. The Council removed Rogron as guardian and appointed Auffray; evidence prompted a civil suit for neglect and abuse.
As the legal battle continued, Pierrette’s health declined. Martener and the Auffray family grew fond of her; a critical operation in March 1828 offered little hope. She forgave her cousins and soon died. Her funeral filled the town; the Rogrons, fearing outrage, stayed away. Brigaut made her coffin. Community support hampered Rogron maneuvers and Vinet faced backlash, yet they largely retained political standing. The account closes on questions of justice, accountability, and Pierrette’s lasting impact.
################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
Dedication: To David, Sculptor—Balzac thanks sculpture for its permanence versus fickle fame.
In autumn 1826 Abbé Birotteau, short, corpulent, about sixty and subject to gout, was caught in a rainstorm after visiting a friend. He had hurried across the deserted Cloister behind Saint‑Gatien cathedral, careless of wet shoes because he was elated from winning at cards with Madame de Listomère and hopeful of securing a long‑desired canonry; that ambition consumed him so completely he even neglected his umbrella.
The Cloister, once for Chapter dignitaries, had become the narrow Rue de la Psalette. On its left stood a single house long occupied by priests and kept by the devout, discreet spinster Mademoiselle Gamard, who preserved it through the Restoration by taking two clerical boarders. For twelve years she sheltered Abbé Troubert and the late Abbé Chapeloud; when Chapeloud died Birotteau—who had long admired the apartment’s furnishings—succeeded him.
Chapeloud had been a tactful friend; his death left Birotteau bereaved and relieved. He inherited Chapeloud’s tasteful library and furniture—comforts and status he had envied—and imagined domestic ease, though his gratitude soon reverted to renewed obsession with preferment.
One rainy evening he found the door locked and, after ringing, was told Gamard assumed he was inside. Small omissions—no slippers, no fire—made him feel slighted. Gamard, who had hoped for a salon and craved recognition, nursed a quiet discontent that turned these domestic slights into a developing conflict of pride, naiveté, and provincial intrigue—a prologue to a wider tale.
The next morning Birotteau, absorbed by hopes of a canonry, briefly forgot the previous night’s ill omens. He rang for Marianne, but Abbé Troubert arrived first. This routine visit began with Troubert noting the absent fire, calling for Marianne, and inquiring after Birotteau’s health and the canonry. Unaware that Troubert resented Madame de Listomère for excluding him, Birotteau cheerfully recounted his efforts to obtain the position.
The two abbés contrasted sharply: Troubert, tall, thin, serious, with a sarcastic, wrinkled face and a dulled intellect shaped by subservience to Mademoiselle Gamard; Birotteau, plump, round, and jovial, amiable and popular. Neither seemed likely to rise high—Troubert, at fifty and ill, might at best become vicar-general; Birotteau’s good nature promised comfortable mediocrity. Troubert borrowed the Register of Bishops; carrying the heavy books downstairs they met Mademoiselle Gamard’s sharp disapproval, and she scolded him for cluttering her dining room.
Breakfast was tense. Gamard complained of disturbance and beset Birotteau with reproach; her bitterness, born of resentment and social frustration as an unmarried woman, showed in harsh features and abrupt movements. Oppressed, Birotteau retreated to the garden, then to church duties; returning, he found he had missed dinner—Gamard had set the clock ahead to avoid waiting, a petty manipulation to preserve her propriety.
Eight days later Birotteau realized Gamard’s hostility was part of a six-month plot against him. Anxiety worsened his health; he sought Troubert’s help, awkwardly describing provocations. Troubert listened with restrained amusement and advised understanding. Desperate, Birotteau stayed at Madame de Listomère’s country house, unaware Gamard would exploit his absence.
After ten days, Caron, Gamard’s lawyer, demanded clarification to avoid disputes. Under pressure Birotteau signed a deed relinquishing his residence and, he later learned, possessions Gamard claimed as board compensation. Devastated, he fled to Madame de Listomère, then was returned to the Alouette where friends consoled him. Monsieur de Bourbonne examined the deed, condemned the exploit, and urged legal action. The group debated suing to recover property versus compromising to avoid scandal and resolved to support Birotteau. An expert appraisal exposed Gamard’s fraud; as legal proceedings opened, town gossip intensified and the outcome remained uncertain against Gamard and Troubert’s alliance.
After Birotteau’s quarrel, Baron de Listomere—expecting promotion—received a letter warning he might be retired. He hurried to Paris; the minister dismissed it, but inquiries revealed a retirement order awaiting signature. His deputy uncle said the minister thought the baron had sided with Radicals and offended Abbé Troubert; urged to placate the clergy, he resolved to avoid conflict.
Fearing fallout, the family followed Bourbonne’s advice to appease Troubert and distance themselves. The baroness demanded Birotteau withdraw his suit against Gamard and leave her house; he complied, requesting Chapeloud’s portrait, which she promised.
When Gamard fell ill, gossip blamed Birotteau. The baroness offered his withdrawal; Troubert returned with Gamard’s refusal to surrender the portrait, and she accepted. Gamard died, left her fortune to Troubert, who eulogized her while Birotteau mourned alone. Troubert became Bishop of Troyes; despite an annuity from Madame de Listomere, Birotteau was sent to a distant parish, ill and bereft—revealing the moral cost of ambition and clerical power.
################################################
######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
DEDICATION
To Monsieur Charles Nodier, member of the French Academy.
Dear Nodier, this book reveals actions hidden behind domestic doors where divine intervention often replaces human justice. Its morals center on family and motherhood, and on the harm that follows when paternal authority—the traditional tribunal for domestic wrongs—erodes. A loving mother cannot substitute for patriarchal power. The story defends indissoluble marriage and warns against unchecked selfishness in a society that exalts wealth and success while the law is impotent. I hope education might steer such a society back to Catholic moral guidance.
In “Scenes from Military Life” I will show noble self-sacrifice and war’s corrupting effect on those at home who live as if on a battlefield. You have observed our times keenly; I entrust this tale to your name as a shield from criticism.
Your sincere admirer,  
De Balzac  
Paris, November 1842.
THE TWO BROTHERS
CHAPTER I
In 1792 the townspeople of Issoudun disliked Dr. Rouget; gossip blamed him for his beautiful wife’s unhappiness, though their private life remained hidden. Madame Rouget, née Descoings, had been frail since youth. They had a son, Jean-Jacques, and, ten years later, a daughter, Agathe.
Rouget’s conviction that men must display strength caused much female suffering. The Descoings family—wealthy wool merchants turned investors in forfeited estates—were rich and miserly. Descoings, Rouget’s brother-in-law, left Issoudun for Paris, failed as a grocer, and later became obsessed with a beautiful widow. Expecting his wife’s death, Rouget sent Agathe to Paris to secure Descoings’ favor and a share of the inheritance.
Agathe, the most beautiful girl in Issoudun and like her mother in looks, provoked a quarrel between Rouget and their friend Lousteau, who then left town. Madame Rouget, learning her daughter had gone, predicted she would never see Agathe again—a prophecy fulfilled as her health declined, worsened by her son’s neglect; Jean-Jacques offered little comfort.
Agathe arrived in Paris as Descoings was imprisoned for imprudent political remarks during the famine and later executed alongside the poet André Chénier—an event that stunned their community. Agathe married Bridau, a ministry secretary who, captivated by her beauty, supported Napoleon, earned promotion in 1804, and labored relentlessly. In 1808 he died of overwork just as another promotion was imminent—an honor the Emperor meant to bestow but belatedly.
After Bridau’s death Agathe received no letters from Issoudun. Her mother died shortly before the birth of her second son; her father died around the Emperor’s coronation; Jean-Jacques remained absent. Madame Hochon warned that Agathe would inherit nothing unless she granted power of attorney to her brother; Agathe hesitated. Bridau’s prudent investments left only a modest income; Agathe and Madame Descoings lived on twelve thousand francs. By 1809 Madame Descoings, sixty-five but fond of theater and fine food, was secretly gambling and accumulating debts. Devoted and refusing to remarry, Agathe took on household duties; her blind trust proved disastrous when the lottery debts were revealed, forcing Agathe to sell possessions and liquidate investments—a decisive turning point in their lives.
One of the bleakest stretches of Paris was rue Mazarin between rue Guenegard and rue de Seine, shaded by the high gray walls of the college and library given by Cardinal Mazarin. There, on the third floor of a damp, cold house, lived a poor widow, Madame Bridau (Agathe). Opposite were the Institute buildings, where aspiring artists worked amid the tumult of examinations; from her windows Agathe could see their cages and the students’ uproar. To brighten her room she defied police regulations and set boxes of earth on the sill to make little gardens.
The shallow house, reached by a winding staircase, contained three rooms: a dining room, a small salon, and a bedroom, plus a kitchen and two single rooms. Agathe had chosen the flat because it was cheap—four hundred francs a year—near her sons’ school and in a neighborhood she knew. The interior matched the building’s dreariness: yellowed wallpaper, unglazed tiles, and only essential furniture. The salon held an Aubusson carpet given to her and a mahogany sofa bearing a pastel portrait of her late husband, Bridau, which captured his noble essence; a vivid Napoleon hung above a card table.
After Bridau’s death Agathe’s life narrowed. She filled the room with two large birdcages—canaries and Java sparrows—a hobby that left her space littered with millet and chickweed, visible signs of grief and neglected personal care. She preserved everything belonging to her husband, even wrapping his last pen with a note. Grief seemed to strip away her sense of womanhood and redirect her entirely toward her children.
Agathe lived for her sons and worried ceaselessly over their finances. The Emperor provided three hundred francs a year for each child, and scholarships supplemented this. Her aunt, Madame Descoings, on the second floor, assigned three thousand francs from her annuity to help Agathe; the Descoings themselves now lived modestly on twelve hundred francs a year, employed a woman for morning chores, and shared dinners with the two widows. Madame Descoings doted on Philippe and Joseph, indulging them with treats and small sums. Distrustful of the future, Agathe scrimped and saved—she set aside three thousand francs from her pension—hoping to secure some inheritance for her boys. Thus both widows had passed from hollow opulence to voluntary poverty, each for different reasons.
The house, the students’ uproar, and her husband’s portrait would shape her youngest son, Joseph Bridau, into a notable painter of the modern French school. Philippe, the elder, resembled his mother in appearance but had a bold demeanor often mistaken for courage; Agathe hoped his looks promised similar moral qualities. At fifteen his charm reinforced her hopes. Joseph, three years younger, took after his father in less favorable ways: untidy, often scolded, and plainly less favored by his mother.
Joseph’s artistic bent was sparked by a visit to the Tuileries, where a caricature on an Institute wall captivated him and made him want to draw. He began sneaking into the Institute courtyard, enduring teasing from older students until persistence won their respect. He later visited the sculptor Chaudet’s atelier; Chaudet recognized and encouraged his talent. Agathe feared art meant hardship and tried to dissuade him, but Chaudet defended the vocation as a divine calling, insisting true talent must be followed.
Meanwhile Philippe, enchanted by military life, secretly petitioned the Emperor to enlist. He was sent to the Imperial Lyceum at Saint-Cyr and rose rapidly, becoming a captain by nineteen. Joseph, after his scholarship was withdrawn, tried to earn a living by art, encouraged by Madame Descoings. In 1816 Joseph persuaded his mother to let him convert the adjoining garret into an atelier—partly financed by Madame Descoings—with a skylight and dark walls; he continued studies under Gros and Schinner.
Political unrest simmered in France; Philippe was implicated in conspiracies, briefly arrested, then released for lack of evidence, though his military pay was cut and Agathe feared for him. Friends urged the widows to send him abroad; Philippe joined General Lallemand in the United States to help found the Champ d’Asile—an enterprise that failed. Agathe equipped him with ten thousand francs, and by late 1817 the household had adjusted to reduced means. Joseph lived frugally to support the family while his talent developed.
In 1818 the notary Roguin absconded with funds intended for Madame Descoings. Soon after, Philippe sent a bill for a thousand francs, having lost everything in the Champ d’Asile. Agathe, distraught, blamed herself for urging his departure. Despite her own difficulties, Madame Descoings continued to support them. Desperate, Joseph appealed to Gérard, who arranged for him to paint copies of a portrait of Louis XVIII; Joseph worked tirelessly, selling panels to cover Philippe’s debt.
When Philippe returned, Agathe welcomed him warmly but failed to notice how America had hardened him. He became self-centered, drinking, gambling, and swaggering. Agathe excused his faults as results of circumstance. The family’s finances deteriorated; friends discussed selling possessions to help him. Philippe refused to serve the Bourbons, lived off others, disappeared for days in cafés and gaming houses, and neglected family duties. A cashier’s post at a newspaper, secured by Agathe and Madame Descoings, brought temporary relief but did little to reform him. Joseph felt increasingly overshadowed and unappreciated. Philippe’s return and altered behavior strained relationships, exposing the consequences of choices, the limits of maternal devotion, and the fragile balance between ambition and family duty.
Three months on, the colonel—who ate enough for four—kept complaining about the food while the widows, unwilling to confront him, entertained him lavishly though he paid nothing. His mother and Madame Descoings avoided reminding him of his promise; months passed without a cent moving from Philippe’s pocket into the household purse. The colonel soothed his conscience by seldom dining at home. “Well, he is happy,” his mother said, “he has a place.”
Propelled by a feuilleton, Mariette made a triumphant debut at the Porte-Saint-Martin with Begrand. A wealthy general, acquainted with Philippe and Giroudeau, became her impresario. Bixiou soon told his grandmother and Agathe that Philippe, their cashier, was in love with Mariette. The widows were appalled: Agathe’s piety condemned all stage women, and they feared such women demanded extravagance. “Do you think my brother will spend on Mariette? Such women ruin rich men,” Joseph protested. Bixiou countered that Mariette might draw higher admirers; he’d even heard an ambassador was infatuated.
Partly from vanity and partly to be near her, Philippe proposed; Mariette refused—perhaps suspecting his intent or hoping for the Grand Opera. Over the next year Philippe visited his mother rarely, spending his days at the office, the theatre, or with Mariette, enjoying pleasures and mixing with Tullia and other entertainers.
The carnival of indulgence lasted eighteen months but grew stormy. After her Opera debut in January 1821 Mariette attracted a distinguished duke. Philippe, maddened by passion, began dipping into the funds he managed—taking eleven hundred francs by May. Mariette left for London to seek patrons while a new opera house was prepared. Philippe, despite her infidelities, remained deeply in love; to her he was merely a stepping-stone.
Forced to relinquish Mariette, Philippe returned to his mother’s apartment, unable to imagine life without the recent pleasures. Suicide crossed his mind—not only because of the deficit but because he could not bear losing Mariette’s company. He entered Joseph’s studio faint and subdued, asked how long a portrait would take, then, when pressed, confessed he had taken money from the funds and could not live with the dishonor. Joseph urged that repayment would restore his honor, but Philippe fled. Joseph told Madame Descoings and Agathe; they were horrified and devastated.
That day Philippe calmly submitted accounts, inventing a safekeeping story. He then went to a gambling house—winning at first, then losing everything. Returning home, the family’s concern briefly comforted him. “Promise not to kill yourself, and all shall be forgotten,” his mother pleaded. Philippe feigned gratitude and affection; for a moment he felt relieved.
The next day Agathe seemed older and more anxious. Madame Descoings proposed consulting Desroches, who advised Agathe to sign a transfer of funds and petition for Philippe’s reinstatement; he promised Philippe would soon regain his rank, easing some fear. Yet Agathe’s faith was shaken.
Philippe resumed a life of leisure—gambling, drinking, treating women with contempt. Joseph, concentrating on painting, gained recognition and Madame Descoings’s support, who pinned hopes on his future. Noticing financial discrepancies, Joseph suspected Philippe and confided in Madame Descoings; they planned to catch him. When Joseph discovered Philippe had indeed taken money from him, he felt betrayed; despite anger, he struggled to confront his brother. Madame Descoings urged sympathy, but Joseph remained wary of Philippe’s hold over the family’s future.
Despite careful nightly gambling, Philippe was often “cleaned out.” Needing his usual ten francs, he began stealing from family—taking from his brother and scavenging whatever Madame Descoings or Agathe left about. After dreaming that Philippe had robbed her, Agathe begged him to ask for money instead of stealing; within two weeks her savings were gone. She took worsted work for twenty sous a day, spent sleepless nights waiting for Philippe, and watched his drinking and mood swings worsen.
When Madame Descoings suggested new clothes, Philippe replied that his mother had no money and that he’d mortgaged his pension. He admitted borrowing from Giroudeau as a “debt of honor.” Agathe excused him; Joseph criticized his neglect of duty and appearance. Philippe sank further into debauchery and gambling, losing the steadiness and looks he once had.
Philippe asked Joseph for money to pay a tobacconist; Joseph refused. Philippe then guessed where Madame hid her savings and, expecting she’d be out, broke into her room. He found twenty napoleons hidden in the mattress and took them to the gambling house. At first he won substantial sums—once amassing over 150,000 francs in a night—but greed drove him on and he lost everything. He awoke the next morning in despair, knowing he had ruined his family.
Agathe and Madame Descoings waited for Philippe at Christmas dinner; when he did not come they worried. Madame had pinned hopes on a lottery stake; finding her hidden money gone, she fainted. The concierge confirmed Philippe had been in the apartment. Confronted, Philippe dismissed accusations, claiming he’d taken what was owed him. A fight between Joseph and Philippe erupted, and Madame Descoings suffered a stroke amid the chaos. Joseph later offered her money, but she clung to lottery hope; when the winning numbers were announced she’d missed her chance and died shortly after.
Recovering from illness, Philippe was told by Agathe that their finances had collapsed and urged to rejoin the army. Indifferent and feeling abandoned, he accepted the money she reluctantly offered and left to seek the company of those he called true friends. Agathe, heartbroken, prayed for the son she felt she had lost.
By February 1822 Madame Bridau had been moved into the attic above the kitchen of the apartment Philippe once occupied. Joseph set out to make her comfortable: he refurnished the garret and took Agathe daily to a table d’hôte costing nearly a hundred francs a month. Alarmed by expense and urged by her confessor, Agathe accepted a seven-hundred-franc-a-year post at the lottery office, working long hours.
She adapted to routine—preparing Joseph’s breakfasts, mending his clothes, keeping the studio tidy—and their bond deepened through these small acts. Joseph visited Philippe at a newspaper; Philippe invited him to dine and to sit for a portrait. Later, thinking a Rubens copy an original, Philippe sold it cheaply; discovering the theft devastated Joseph and broke Agathe’s heart.
Two months on, an old officer told Agathe Philippe was ill and needed money. She gave what she could, then learned Philippe had been implicated in a conspiracy and arrested; she fainted. Desroches advised securing the family inheritance and gathering evidence of undue influence; he planned to visit Issoudun at the holidays to help. Agathe’s letter to her godmother met only discouragement. Agathe and Joseph set off for Issoudun, leaving Philippe to face the consequences and Agathe to confront their deep estrangement.
Issoudun, noted by Caesar and Rigord, yielded a fifth-century basilica—likely unique in France—rebuilt from stones of a Roman temple to Isis. Its name, from “Isis” with “dun” denoting a fortified religious site, points to Celtic origins.
In the Middle Ages Issoudun was powerful: Richard Coeur-de-lion built a tower over the basilica, and later the town sheltered Routiers and Cottereaux mercenaries sent by Henry II against his son Richard. After an 1830 insurrection, some eight thousand vine-growers burned tax offices and resisted government control.
In the fourteenth century the town counted 16–17 thousand inhabitants, prospering on the wool trade and producing blankets and gloves; by Louis XIV it showed some elegance. Today about twelve thousand live there, including suburban vine-growers. The local economy—especially wine—suffers from outdated practices: growers resist modernization and make poor wine, limiting prosperity.
This stagnation has social causes: opposition to a Paris–Toulouse road, the disappearance of a competitive nobility, a complacent bourgeoisie, administration dominated by intermarried families, and a weak clergy. Against that backdrop youth formed the “Knights of Idleness,” led by Maxence Gilet—rumored an illegitimate son turned soldier—whose disruptive, creative pranks, sheltered by Père and La Cognette’s tavern, express local frustration.
As Joseph and Madame Bridau traveled from Orléans to Issoudun, the Knights of Idleness played a prank. An old Spaniard, a former prisoner, left his empty cart at the foot of the Issoudun tower. Maxence had it hauled up the embankment; within an hour the youths had disassembled the cart, carried the pieces aloft, reassembled it, then, hungry and thirsty, returned to Mère Cognette’s to await the Spaniard’s surprise.
The Knights’ amusements varied; they did not prank every night. The twenty youths hunted, harvested grapes and skated in winter. Maxence, thirty-four and soldierly, was their idol. François Hochon and Baruch Borniche, Madame Hochon’s grandsons, treated him like kin and relied on him for money and adventures their grandfather would not permit.
One wine-filled evening François and Baruch warned Max that Madame Bridau, her son, and other relatives were arriving and that two rooms were being prepared for them. Max masked unease with bluster. When someone joked that Rouget’s sister would inherit and undo Max’s harvest, he snapped, defending Flore Brazier—Rouget’s servant-mistress, disparaged by society—and forbade contemptuous nicknames. His friends learned to tread carefully on the subject.
For the first time Max spoke openly about Flore. He admitted rumors that he spent on the household because of her and that she aimed for Rouget’s property. François suggested he might marry Flore once Rouget died; Max conceded the thought but insisted he would not resort to murder or poison, only “fooleries” within the Order’s limits. He declared he loved Flore even more than Mademoiselle Fichet, Issoudun’s richest heiress, courted by Goddet. The Knights cheered; Max toasted them and ordered watchfulness—“be in the market early” and signal when the old Spaniard came for his cart—while assuring he would handle direct threats. At half-past three they slipped away; Max returned to Place Saint-Jean, concealing the worry he had learned to hide during his time in Cabrera.
Rouget’s estate, producing forty thousand francs a year, anchored Max’s claim on Flore and the household. But legitimate heirs unsettled him: Rouget’s savings—accumulated over seventeen years and said to be kept with various notaries (seven hundred and fifty thousand francs was mentioned)—were in the doctor’s name; even with a will those funds might be secured elsewhere. “That fool of a girl never mentioned her sister and nephews in all these seven years!” Max muttered, resolving to rid himself of these relatives and then secure the property. Exhausted, he used a pass-key to enter Père Rouget’s house and went to bed, promising to think clearly in the morning.
The narrative then traces how Flore Brazier—later called the “Rabouilleuse”—became Jean-Jacques Rouget’s quasi-mistress. As Doctor Rouget aged he tried to impose order on his ineffectual son, who yielded to the first strong personality. In September 1799 the doctor found a captivating little girl by a brook in a tattered petticoat. She claimed to be from Vatan and to be catching crabs for her uncle Brazier, who joked about republican liberty.
Struck by her looks, the doctor took her in, promising wages and a better life. Flore’s condition improved—fed, clothed, given gifts to encourage study—but her peasant habits made her dislike books and the doctor stopped her schooling. Town gossip hinted at impropriety, yet for five years no scandal arose; Rouget merely enjoyed educating her. Born in 1787 amid Revolutionary upheaval, Flore developed pragmatic self-interest rather than strict virtue. As Rouget neared death, his notary urged provisions for her; his refusal to leave her anything revealed bitterness and set the stage for the inheritance struggle now threatening Max’s position and Flore’s hopes.
Jean-Jacques Rouget did not mourn his father’s death, though Flore Brazier did. The old doctor had made his son miserable after Jean-Jacques came of age, yet had provided Flore with the country comforts she enjoyed. At the funeral, when Fanchette asked Flore what would become of her now that Monsieur was dead, Jean-Jacques showed animation for the first time.
When his father died Jean-Jacques was thirty-seven; Flore was seventeen, still beautiful though already growing plump like other countrywomen. Timid, submissive, and solitary, Jean-Jacques remained awkward despite his father’s late attempts to teach him greed and find a daughter-in-law to secure the estate.
Secretly in love with Flore, Jean-Jacques took comfort in the instruction his father had given her, hoping her upbringing would make her easier to win. Flore, unaware of his feelings, waited for a declaration that never came. After three weeks of uncertainty she woke one night to find him crouched outside her door; seeing this as proof of affection, she began to view him less repulsively. Their tentative conversations—about work and honesty—revealed his growing but inarticulate love.
Ten months later Jean-Jacques brightened and improved his appearance; the town noticed. Fanchette, the disapproving cook, left; Flore anticipated this, learned to cook, and took pride in managing the house, surpassing her predecessor. Over the next nine years she gained control of Jean-Jacques, treating him with a blend of familiarity and respect that left him dependent and childlike.
By 1815 Flore was at her peak beauty; in 1816 she fell for Maxence Gilet, a penniless officer. She pressed Jean-Jacques about his wealth and empty rooms and persuaded him to invite Max. Jean-Jacques found in Max the conversation and companionship he lacked, while Flore neglected household duties, hired a new cook, refined her appearance to impress Max, bought him a horse, and took a servant. The household shifted: Flore and Max became central, and Jean-Jacques receded.
When Madame Bridau returned to claim her threatened inheritance, Jean-Jacques Rouget had become semi‑vegetative. After Max provided money, Flore improved Rouget’s care, feeding him from Vedie’s kitchen; he grew weaker and utterly submissive, sustained only by devotion to Flore.
Max’s modest apartment—stair matting to conceal late returns—masked his nocturnal affairs from Rouget. One morning Flore found Max asleep; he warned Rouget’s family might come to evict them. Flore wanted confrontation; Max counseled caution. Baruch then announced Fario’s cart missing. Max led a search to the tower embankment, found the cart atop, and theatrically hurled it down, humiliating Fario and fueling gossip. François warned the Bridaus would arrive that night. Max resolved they should seem non‑hostile and let the Hochons carry out any eviction. Furious, Flore punished Rouget by withdrawing affection; Vedie urged him to placate her. Rouget denied inviting kin and blamed Madame Hochon. Max advised him to welcome relatives outwardly, preserve his will, and enlist the Hochons against the Bridaus.
Across the way the frugal Hochons prepared to receive goddaughter Agathe; granddaughter Adolphine secretly hoped to win Joseph Bridau. The town split between cautious bourgeoisie and Max’s loyal “Knights of Idleness,” showing how fragile reputations and a few calculated acts could decide the inheritance conflict.
Agathe and Joseph arrived at the Messageries-Royales in Issoudun at three. Though tired, Madame Bridau felt revived by familiar sights. News spread fast; Madame Hochon embraced Agathe like a lost daughter. Agathe met the reserved Monsieur Hochon and the household: the young men Baruch and François, and shy Adolphine, who noticed Joseph. Monsieur Hochon mentioned Joseph’s recent hospital stay and hunger; the youths, struck by his looks, sent Baruch off to spread gossip that portrayed Joseph as a brigand and reflected the town’s scorn for artists. “This is my son, the painter; my good Joseph,” Agathe said proudly. Joseph’s blunt remark about resembling his father was misread by Madame Hochon as affection for Agathe.
Installed in modest attic rooms, Joseph contrasted his Parisian artistic life with provincial drabness. Dinner exposed the Hochons’ frugality—plain bouilli, hard eggs and meager accompaniments—but Joseph tried to adapt. Agathe learned her childhood home had been sold to the Borniches; Madame Hochon prepared for an influx of callers. In the salon, the women exchanged hardships and advice, repeatedly interrupted by visitors eager to meet the Parisians. Joseph kept to himself, studying callers with a critical eye that made them uneasy; later the women resumed their heartfelt talk.
Elsewhere the Knights of Idleness plotted to drive the Bridaus away: Max proposed releasing rats to ruin a disliked merchant, Fario, sparing the Hochons. Next morning Joseph glimpsed his brother Jean-Jacques Rouget—unrecognizable and disheveled—accompanied by the conspicuous Flore Brazier. Joseph admired Flore; Agathe feared her influence. A letter inviting Joseph to dine arrived, met by the Hochons’ mixed feelings. Monsieur Hochon warned Joseph to watch and speak plainly about his nephews. With rival schemes and simmering tensions, a fraught reunion loomed.
At four o’clock Joseph went from the Rouget to the Hochon house, where lunch was set and he greeted his uncle Rouget, Flore, and Maxence Gilet. After light banter he admired Rouget’s house and paintings, which Rouget said came from the Descoings and had been acquired during the Revolution. Ecstatic, Joseph and Max inspected more upstairs. Flore urged Rouget to give the pictures to Joseph, arguing Rouget didn’t care for them.
Rouget offered the paintings—without frames—and Flore proposed paying four thousand francs so Joseph could make copies. Overwhelmed, Joseph accepted. The news spread through Issoudun and raised Flore’s standing. Dinner was splendid; Rouget drank heavily and was carried to bed. Max and Flore agreed it was better to befriend the Bridaus than antagonize them and planned to send the pictures to Joseph the next day.
Next morning the paintings were propped against Rouget’s wall. Hochon, fearing the Bridaus’ influence and warning Joseph had been duped, urged Joseph’s mother Agathe to cultivate Flore and visit Rouget; she was warmly received while Rouget remained ill. Flore pretended humility, hinted Rouget should marry her, and insisted he would remember her in his will.
Meanwhile the Knights of Idleness released rats and mice into Fario’s grain store, causing havoc. The Bridaus’ hold on Rouget grew: Flore claimed he was too weak for walks and needed a carriage; they bought a shabby vehicle that attracted attention. Rouget signed a power of attorney giving Max control over his investments while Flore handled local transactions. Fario, finding his store devastated, suspected Max and began gathering evidence.
Convinced the paintings were far more than a copying job, Joseph sent them to Desroches in Paris for appraisal. Agathe warned him to keep their value quiet, but word leaked. Letters from Desroches and Schinner advised keeping Agathe in town, warned about Max, and suggested Rouget might already have a will—making the Bridaus’ hopes dependent on a struggle between Flore and the Church. Disturbed, Joseph decided to return to Paris to rely on his talent and later return the pictures. The Hochons suspected Max and Flore had known the paintings’ value and manipulated Joseph; Agathe defended her son, while Hochon warned that returning the paintings could forfeit any chance at inheritance.
Between midnight and one the Knights of Idleness fed the town’s dogs; their expedition lasted until three.
