After supper at Cognette’s, Max, turning from rue l’Avenier into Grande rue at half-past four, was stabbed by Fario, who had lurked in a recess. Fario fled toward Vilatte, wiped the knife on his handkerchief, washed it in the Rivière forcée, and returned to his lodgings at Saint-Paterne, where his watchman later found him in bed.
Max fell, crying out and alarming Lousteau-Prangin and young Goddet, who ran up the street calling for help; the townspeople, used to false alarms, stayed in bed. Max had fainted. He recognized Fario but, on regaining consciousness and seeing the wound was not fatal, decided to exploit the incident: “I think I recognized that cursed painter!” Lousteau-Prangin hurried to his father, the judge. Max was carried home by Cognette, Goddet and others. Goddet, suspicious of Max’s words, asked Gritte if Joseph Bridau was in bed; she said Joseph had left at half-past four, restless all night. The revelation shocked the group; they found Max in a bloody shirt.
Joseph had spent the night anxious about how people would view him, fearing they might think he had robbed his uncle. He longed to flee to Paris and confront Max; at dawn he walked to the poplar avenue toward Tivoli to steady his nerves.
After Goddet examined the wound and found it serious but not fatal, news spread and a crowd gathered at the place Saint-Jean. Max whispered to Flore that they could use the incident to rid themselves of the Parisians: pretend he was dying and have Joseph arrested. Flore, feigning tears, spread the story that Joseph had attacked Max out of jealousy over paintings. Incited by her, the crowd demanded Joseph’s capture; the mob grew hostile.
Monsieur Hochon, warned by Gritte, locked himself in, fearing for his safety and wealth. Agathe woke, learned of the accusations, and fainted at the sight of the gendarmes. The lieutenant reported a large crowd demanding justice.
Joseph, returning home, was nearly seized by the mob. Gendarmes intervened and, despite his protests of innocence, took him to the Palais for safety. There Joseph calmly insisted on his innocence and gave an alibi, recounting his morning’s movements. Magistrates kept him in custody while they verified his claims. Joseph wrote to his mother, reassuring her he would soon be free.
Max, recovering, recalled the attacker had worn list shoes while Joseph had left in boots. He wrote to the magistrate urging Joseph’s release. Witnesses then confirmed the alibi and Joseph was freed. He returned to his mother’s relief; the ordeal strengthened their bond.
The townspeople celebrated the Parisians’ departure; Max and Flore were pleased. Joseph and Agathe returned to Paris; Joseph’s spirits improved, while Agathe fretted about her son Philippe, sentenced to five years’ surveillance and exiled (later transferred to Issoudun). Determined to restore the family’s fortunes and confront Max and the Rabouilleuse, Philippe prepared to return to Issoudun and, after a brief dinner, departed to face the challenge.
On All-Souls’ Day Philippe Bridau returned to Issoudun and registered at the commissary, unsettling a town already uneasy over Maxence Gilet, a banished officer linked to a painter wrongly accused in the affair. Gilet had converted all of Père Rouget’s mortgages into cash, alarming locals. Monsieur Hochon, after consulting notary Héron—who thought Rouget’s heirs should be grateful Gilet hadn’t profited more—decided to protect the Bridaus, long resentful of Gilet and fearful of losing property.
Gilet and Flore Brazier expected to manipulate Rouget but were taken aback by Philippe’s blunt pride: he refused to beg and appeared in tattered clothes, impressing Flore while Gilet perceived a threat and avoided confrontation. Philippe then ingratiated himself with the Hochons, candid about living on little, and asked Monsieur Hochon for the names of officers hostile to Gilet—Mignonnet and Carpentier—which he promptly befriended. He carefully entered the local military circle, dining with them, taking fencing lessons and practicing shooting to craft a respectable image while concealing his aims.
His inquiries brought him to Fario, who revealed the Knights of Idleness and their schemes. Suspecting that Gilet and Flore planned to secure Rouget’s income and flee, Philippe encouraged Rouget to take walks to loosen Flore’s influence and warned Hochon that Gilet sought a power of attorney to access Rouget’s funds; they agreed to watch Rouget’s movements.
When Gilet proposed going to Vatan to secure the money and marry, Flore—fearful of Philippe’s influence—reluctantly consented. Philippe’s determination to protect his family and restore honor hardened. Walking with Rouget along the boulevard Baron, he contemplated the looming confrontation and bluntly told Rouget that Flore loved Maxence and wanted him out of the way so she could marry—insisting he could make her obedient again, even by killing Maxence, if Rouget refused to sign a power of attorney before December third that would allow them to sell Rouget’s assets and flee. Rouget recoiled.
Philippe instructed Mignonnet and Carpentier to arrange banquet seating to force a public confrontation with Gilet, though they warned of Gilet’s dueling skill. Meanwhile Flore briefly left with Vedie—sending Kouski into tears—prompting Rouget’s frantic search. Gilet, urged by Captain Potel to face Bridau, instead focused on transferring assets to Flore. Philippe planned to use Baruch to obtain the power of attorney while distracting Gilet; he encouraged Rouget to draft a letter threatening to revoke his will unless Flore returned to force her back. Town gossip swelled. When Flore returned, Philippe asserted control and warned her; by morning the town speculated on the duel, and Philippe, confident in his banquet scheme, believed it would decide both Rouget’s fortune and his own.
Around four the next afternoon officers of the old army gathered at the place du Marché in Issoudun for a coronation banquet, awaiting Colonel Philippe Bridau. Conversation turned to Max’s recent dismissal from old Rouget’s house; only Potel and Renard defended him. When Max arrived he was coldly received, a sharp contrast to Philippe’s warm welcome, hardening Max’s resolve to confront him.
At dinner Philippe toasted the Grand Army; Mignonnet toasted those who had tried to restore the Emperor’s son. Max, whose reputation carried weight, rose with an ironic wish to meet again next year. Tension mounted as Philippe and Max exchanged barbs: Philippe remained composed while Max struggled for control. The duel was set for the next morning with sabres, Goddet, a former army surgeon, to attend.
That night Monsieur and Madame Hochon urged Philippe to pray; Madame Hochon offered a relic, which he declined. The next morning both men appeared composed, but Max felt intimidated by Philippe’s superior stance. The duel began: Max was quickly disarmed, Philippe paused to let him recover his weapon, and when they resumed they exchanged fierce blows. Philippe was wounded but delivered a fatal stroke. Max died, ending his ambitions and the Order of Idleness, much to the town’s relief.
Flore Brazier, devastated, fell ill with fever and inflammation. Philippe, recovering from his wounds, wrote letters outlining plans to secure his inheritance and manage Flore’s situation, noting her illness prevented her from fleeing and intending to use her to his advantage. By February 1823, pressed by others and Philippe’s claim that marriage would secure her future, Flore accepted a match he arranged. The contract granted her a substantial dower and a life annuity. Agathe, Philippe’s mother, rejoiced at his apparent reform, though family lawyer Desroches warned Philippe’s cunning would likely keep Rouget’s property from Joseph and Agathe. Once married, Philippe asserted control: he demanded a power of attorney from Rouget, used Flore to manage her uncle’s affairs while watching her closely, and—backed by local endorsements and letters to the minister of war—was reinstated as lieutenant‑colonel.
Philippe Bridau returned to Paris with his aunt and the ailing Rouget, whom he escorted to the Treasury to claim his uncle’s income. Rouget and the Rabouilleuse were swallowed by Parisian extravagance; after a lavish supper with the dancer Lolotte, Rouget overindulged and died.
Augustine Guillaume's cousin married very early at 16, to a skinny man exactly twice her age with the same hair color and height, and moved to Albi with him immediately afterward. 
The last time Augustine and Élodie-Rose saw each other was at Élodie-Rose’s wedding. 
Élodie-Rose became single again very shortly after and had to continue her life in a town far from her family. She endured a life full of hardship and pain. 
Madame Rouget prospered while Philippe kept Mariette on guard. In October 1823 he settled the Issoudun estate, securing 1,600,000 francs (excluding paintings held by Monsieur Hochon) and deposited the funds with Mongenod and Sons. He married, protected his wife’s fortune, bought her an apartment, and secretly acquired and renovated a grand hôtel on rue de Clichy.
With Charles X’s rise and by informing on past conspiracies, Philippe was promoted lieutenant-colonel in the Duc de Maufrigneuse’s regiment and ennobled Comte de Brambourg. Hungry for further honors—an aide-de-camp post to the Dauphin and a general’s rank—he severed old ties and plotted to marry Mademoiselle Amélie de Soulanges to secure his place.
His brother Joseph, a struggling artist, endured poverty and criticism while their mother Agathe supported him from a lottery office. Philippe’s refusal to aid their needy mother hastened her death; Joseph buried her alone. Finding solace in friends, Joseph and Bixiou later arranged care for Flore and schemed to expose Philippe. Philippe’s fortunes collapsed in the July 1830 Revolution; Joseph inherited the estate and, achieving success in art, became Comte de Brambourg while remaining humble and mindful of the family’s tragedy.

######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
DEDICATION
To Madame la Duchesse de Castries.
CHAPTER I
The commercial traveller is a modern force, shifting enterprise into intellectual influence: tying local power to markets, equalizing goods, and exporting Parisian ideas. Self-taught, he reads people, gauges solvency, discerns vice and virtue. Persuasive and adaptable, he often succeeds where ambassadors fail, negotiating deals in taverns and importing innovations. Gaudissart is this type: an acrobat of commerce who extracts provincial wealth with a torrent of phrases. Disarming in a striped shirt, he made his fortune selling hats, winning vendors and society alike. Ever festive yet businesslike, he tailors pitches to each audience. After 1830 made ideas marketable, writers and speculators thrived; Gaudissart adapted, selling life insurance and the profitable Children's Journal.
CHAPTER II
In April, after a week of Saint-Simonism, he set out despite offers to return to hats, joking with Jenny the florist about furnishing her room from his profits as he stopped in Blois.
CHAPTER III
In Vouvray townspeople’s wit and hostility thwarted him. Monsieur Vernier mocked travellers and sent him to Margaritis, a madman obsessed with wine and newspapers; Margaritis’s absurd answers and the amused crowd undermined every pitch.
CHAPTERS IV–V
Insulted, Gaudissart demanded satisfaction; Vernier refused and a duel loomed until the innkeeper arranged a breakfast reconciliation. They patched things up and Gaudissart left for Paris, reminded that eloquence does not always overcome discernment.

######### THIS IS THE END OF A STORY. #########
################################################
To Monsieur le Comte Ferdinand de Gramont.
MY DEAR FERDINAND,—If these lines endure in literature, they will be a small token of gratitude for your efforts. You, Hozier, Cherin, the King-at-Arms of these Studies of Life, have enriched the “Human Comedy” with your heraldic designs. Your manual of French Heraldry, showing deep knowledge of the feudal spirit and learned symbolism, has greatly influenced me.
Your old friend,  
DE BALZAC.
On the skirts of Le Berry lies Sancerre, a town perched on a hill above the Loire flats, set on fertile alluvium though some tracts suffer drifting sand. Its distance from the river let the nearby port of Saint-Thibault prosper, shipping wine and goods; by this story’s opening suspension bridges at Cosne and Saint-Thibault have replaced ferries—improvements dating from the upheaval of 1830 when the House of Orleans funded local works.
Sancerre’s steep, narrow streets within the Great Ramparts form its principal ways. Wine is the chief industry, producing vintages worthy of Burgundy and supplying Paris; lower villages like Fontenoy and Saint-Satur recall their vineyard beauty. Once militarily important, the town declined as trade moved to the Loire’s right bank. Its population of about 3,500 is now eclipsed by Cosne’s 6,000, a reversal of fifty years, yet Sancerre retains historic charm, broad views, pure air, and a civic, watchful community, predominantly Calvinist without Puritan severity.
Notable natives include Horace Bianchon in medicine and Etienne Lousteau in journalism. Politically, a few large landowners dominate municipal life and skew elections. A local plot to break their hold collapsed through vanity and jealousy; the proposed remedy is to unite behind one candidate for the next election—one of Sancerre’s two Paris representatives—a progressive provincial scheme championed by the borough’s Superior Woman, whose interests and past explain her ambitions.
Around 1836 Sancerre revealed a locally notable figure—the Superior Woman—made visible by Parisian influences like Etienne Lousteau, Bianchon, and George Sand’s sentimental “Sandism,” which brought previously hidden superior women into view.
The Superior Woman of Sancerre lived at La Baudraye, a town-and-country house near Saint-Satur belonging to a diminished branch of the Milaud family. The family’s fortunes shifted over generations: a Milaud sheriff converted at the Revocation of the Edict of Nantes and was rewarded under Louis XIV, later suffered reprisals, and under Louis XV secured a military commission whose son died in battle, leaving a child later favored by Louis XVI. In 1800 an ex–farmer-general returned from exile and bought La Baudraye. His only son, Jean Athanase Polydore Milaud de la Baudraye, was frail and devotedly raised by his noble mother; on his father’s death he inherited the fief, some money, and a collection of autographs. He lived frugally, enlarged the vineyards (1802–1815), hesitated to press Parisian debts during the Restoration, tried and failed to monetize the autograph collection, then returned home.
In 1823, at forty-three, Polydore sought an heir and chose Dinah Piedefer: beautiful, educated, from a once-Calvinist family now poor, who converted to Catholicism to improve her prospects. The Archbishop of Bourges saw promise in her, but families feared her beauty and intellect. Polydore’s bluntness offended ministers when he sought ecclesiastical support; nevertheless they married, provoking local speculation.
Polydore’s Paris dealings—pressing debtors, forgiving debts in exchange for a tax-collector post, briefly holding office, securing lucrative appointments, rising to Royal Commissioner, and buying Anzy in 1827—funded the household. Dinah transformed La Baudraye—gardens, interiors, refined company—while keeping her husband’s financial maneuvers private. Her beauty, intellect, Paris correspondence, and demand for conversation set her apart: admired by local men, resented and isolated by provincial women. Madame Piedefer rejoiced, and Polydore proudly had his Superior Woman—an example of how exceptional qualities can inspire both admiration and resentment, ultimately isolating those who exceed local norms.
By the end of 1825 Dinah de la Baudraye faced gossip that she socialized only with men and became a target of criticism. In a rash reply to a false friend she declared, “I would rather have my bowl empty than with anything in it!” The remark backfired; childless after five years of marriage, she invited further ridicule.
Monsieur de la Baudraye was a frail, eccentric man who rarely contradicted Dinah and allowed her to dominate conversation. Admired for beauty and intelligence, she attracted gentlemen from nearby towns, relishing attention and delivering prepared speeches drawn from wide reading. Her charm created a protective aura that shielded her from much censure; Monsieur often excused himself from her long expositions to walk or attend to business.
Dinah championed the romantic arts, collected antiques, and filled their home with Renaissance treasures. Her husband tacitly supported these pursuits but focused chiefly on his land. Over the years her notable collection impressed visitors and provided material for talks on art and history. She tried to found a literary circle in Sancerre to elevate local culture, but it degenerated into social gatherings—an evident disappointment. Admirers, including magistrates and a priest, were often diverted from intellectual aims; one young man who yawned during a lecture fell from her favor, revealing her strict expectations for cultural devotion.
When Dinah turned twenty in 1826 suitors still courted her. Sheltered by her mother and the visible presence of admirers, she kept slander at bay while Monsieur used the marriage to secure favors. Local women speculated, and a slow domestic drama—fed by the era’s appetite for gossip—shaped her seemingly virtuous early married life.
Though Monsieur appeared generous at first—letting her use five acres and financing house improvements—his apparent liberality proved an illusion. After a trip to Paris he imposed a strict monthly allowance, paid a yearly sum to her mother, and warned of uncertain winegrower income. Dinah’s attempts to provoke jealousy produced only indifference; he remained steady and unyielding.
Frustration grew as she felt trapped in La Baudraye and Sancerre, longing for a more ambitious life. She briefly hoped for change when he bought the estate of Anzy and applied for a patent of entail, but the short reconciliation exposed deeper contrasts: Dinah’s energy and potential clashed with her husband’s lack of ambition and rigid routine. Over six years she became increasingly provincial, losing individuality and spirit—wardrobe reduced, sewing her own clothes—and a meeting with her former schoolfriend Anna, now a sophisticated Parisian, left her despondent.
She turned to poetry; her "Paquita la Sevillane" drew provincial attention and revealed talent but provoked anxiety about exposure. After the Revolution of 1830, George Sand’s fame encouraged towns to celebrate local female talent. Monsieur de Clagny published a collection by "Jan Diaz," a supposedly young dead poet—two hundred copies were printed. It emerged that Jan Diaz was a pseudonym for Madame de la Baudraye. Many copies sold in the departments where the poet had lived; those sent to Paris were largely lost in editorial offices. Dinah quickly became celebrated and was cast by some provincials as a rival to George Sand. Local, often polarized praise elevated work that might otherwise have been overlooked.
Dinah longed for recognition to fill unmet ambitions. The late Abbé Duret had warned her about her husband’s character; she resisted until she discerned his incapacity for love and vengefulness. As her reputation grew, domestic life felt ever more constraining and public sympathy intensified her despair. After a confrontation she vowed to stop writing and withdrew into desolation at Sancerre.
She had hoped to support Monsieur de Clagny’s political ambitions, but his electoral defeat left her disillusioned. At the château d’Anzy she contemplated her future and sought contact with influential figures. She admired Lousteau, a charming journalist, and tried to attract him and Bianchon to Sancerre; their visits and autographs became tangible links to the literary world and the recognition she craved. Dinah’s story reveals the tension between ambition and domesticity and a woman’s struggle for identity and fulfillment in a restrictive provincial environment.
At five the next morning the Chateau d’Anzy stirred for a day's sport arranged by the young Monsieur de la Baudraye, a scheme by Parisians to reclaim local land. Gatien Boirouge asked Monsieur Gravier why the Public Prosecutor, Monsieur de Clagny, was absent; Gravier gave court duty, but Gatien suspected Clagny spent time with Madame de la Baudraye (Dinah). Lousteau and Bianchon teased Gatien’s provincial anxieties, treating the matter as vanity and gossip.
At a copse Gravier left the men with a keeper while Bianchon and Lousteau lingered. Gatien fretted that Gravier might already be dining with Dinah and Clagny and that, if together, they would watch for opportunites. He feared Clagny’s persuasive eloquence and Dinah’s Parisian ambitions; Lousteau and Bianchon dismissed such intrigues. Lousteau proposed a prank: at dinner the Parisians would tell lurid tales of betrayed wives and vengeful husbands to watch Dinah and Clagny for reactions. Gatien favored entrapment, recalling a Parisian editor’s wife who frightened her husband with stories of lovers boiled alive. They resolved to be diplomatic by day and provocative by night.
At dinner Lousteau excelled. He produced an album of lachrymose verses purportedly by himself—an elegy that won Dinah’s sympathy. Clagny, seeing her expression, recoiled into backgammon. Dinah, stitching for her husband’s charity box, replied to Lousteau’s mockery with gentle retorts; wry jests about adultery and morality followed. The Public Prosecutor attacked Parisian writers’ treatment of illicit love; Lousteau defended literature with classical references, provoking Clagny.
Conversation widened into paradoxes on justice and innovation: history and stern courts seemed to condemn pioneers—Socrates, Jesus—and institutions persecute what they cannot assimilate. Lousteau teased that religion and state are fragile before new ideas; Clagny called this blasphemy. Gravier half-jokingly said he’d side with a betrayed husband if he were a writer; Lousteau mused on the sublimity of trust versus jealousy. Dinah’s quiet sympathy for victims tortured into crime suggested human justice poorly judges private tragedies.
Lousteau then told a Nodier tale about a Chevalier who feigned escape from a jealous commandant—harmless and bemusing—eliciting no reaction from Clagny or Dinah. Bianchon followed with the Spanish La Grande Bretèche—of a grandee walled into a cupboard to starve by a jealous husband—stunning the guests. Gravier added a Spanish adventure of illicit deliveries, betrayals, and a murdered surgeon-major. The cumulative gruesomeness chilled the table; Clagny reminded them the Gazette des Tribunaux often reported horrors rivaling romance.
The Parisians watched Dinah and Clagny closely, betting that one guilty of passion would react. After dinner the bachelors conferred: Gravier briefed them on probing Dinah’s virtue; Lousteau warned that serenity can mask cunning. Gravier suggested a country trick—pressing hairs into wax on the doors of Madame de la Baudraye and the Public Prosecutor to detect nocturnal visits. That night the doctor, Lousteau, the tax receiver, and Gatien crept barefoot to press hairs into wax seals and agreed to examine them at five.
At dawn the seals were unbroken—same wax, same hairs. Lousteau declared they had chased a will-o’-the-wisp and admitted the matter was more complex. Puzzled, Gravier and Gatien faced an unexpected result. Lousteau privately told Bianchon that if Dinah were truly virtuous she deserved the effort; his mockery softened into admiration and he sought Dinah in the garden.
Their conversation was partly contrived: she had asked him to study provincial life; he charged that Gatien had misrepresented him as adoring her and that her silence at dinner had been an unguarded sign. He confessed engineering the evening’s stories to rouse emotion and test both her and Clagny, and said her innocence seemed proved—if she had yielded to Clagny he would lose respect for her. He professed a taste for a certain perfection and disdain for cold, taciturn men. Dinah replied that Lousteau sought pleasure but not love, and that true love often comes late; she showed sympathy for his vulnerability.
Lousteau’s candor delighted Dinah. He and Bianchon spent the day entertaining her with anecdotes and wit—an intellectual feast she savored—while Clagny, Gravier, and Gatien noted her warmer bearing. By sundown sixteen people had gathered at Anzy; Dinah, proud hostess, prepared the drawing-room. Lousteau found a tattered romance used as packing—“Olympia, or Roman Revenge”—and turned its brigands, a Duchess’s glove, and Cardinal Borborigano into comic spectacle. Bianchon joined, amusing the assembled grandees and young women. The evening showcased Parisian cleverness in the provinces, while the mystery of the sealed doors remained unresolved for the men who had come to spy on hearts and reputations.
For three days Dinah’s imagination was stirred by her Parisian visitors. Lousteau watched her, eager to captivate; Bianchon, blunt and professorial, prodded her. Lousteau bantered and sat close while Bianchon mocked the local men and interrogated the unhappy circumstances of her life, putting Dinah on the defensive.
Crossing the Loire, Bianchon told her frankly she had shut herself against love but now needed it; Dinah, startled, asked if he prescribed love. He warned that her habits would make her unattractive. Lousteau half‑jokingly defended him as offering sound counsel. At Cosne, when a crowd gathered by an old carriage, Dinah insisted they disembark and walked quickly with Bianchon along the Loire. There Bianchon suggested Lousteau loved her but was frivolous and hard to keep; he advised making a friend of him, since Lousteau could bring beauty and happiness. Under Bianchon’s scrutiny Dinah felt vulnerable and unsure how she might compete with Lousteau’s past lovers.
Their coachman announced Gatien’s return. Bianchon blessed them as they entered town. Lousteau immediately took Dinah’s hand and declared his love; she resisted, asking whether she should give herself to a man who treated her as a passing pleasure. Gatien’s arrival complicated matters. Lousteau whispered that she should feign resistance to preserve his honor and, in a reckless instant, crushed her muslin dress, leaving her disordered and humiliated. Gatien, oblivious, rode up while Lousteau tried to distract him; Dinah sent Gatien to fetch Bianchon’s papers, and he complied.
Back at La Baudraye, Madame Piedefer appeared anxious; hearing Gatien’s horse, Dinah fled to her room. Outside, Lousteau and Gatien confronted one another—Gatien accused trickery, Lousteau shrugged it off as Dinah’s mockery. Dinah changed into a velvet gown; when she rejoined them, conversation skirted Bianchon while she remained silent, weighed down by recent events.
Intent on winning her, Lousteau declared he would stay in Sancerre to be near her and swore he had renounced Parisian vices. On the road back to Anzy his charm began to dissolve Dinah’s reserve; she laughed, a sign her feelings were shifting. After a stolen intimacy in the gardens and a pledge exchanged before his return to Paris, Dinah accompanied him to Cosne, openly flouting propriety. At La Baudraye she praised him for showing what true love felt like. Her three admirers, including Monsieur de Clagny, were left to reckon with their feelings; Dinah took Clagny as a confidant, lavishing affection while he remained devoted despite her new happiness.
In Paris, however, Lousteau soon forgot the happy days at the Chateau d’Anzy. He lived by his pen in an age when writing—after the rise of the bourgeoisie—had become near servitude: constant production or the appearance of it. He wrote daily for a newspaper and several literary magazines without artistic pretensions; deprived of advancement, he had become a journalist. Still, his life was not unpleasant. He mixed with blue‑stockings, aspiring actresses, publishers, and authors, keeping theatre boxes and a social life that required little beyond paying rent.
Financially he remained constrained. Lousteau rented well‑furnished ground‑floor rooms in the Rue des Martyrs for twelve hundred francs; despite earning seven to eight hundred francs a month from writing and reviews, he spent heavily on cabs, meals, and gifts for lovers and was perpetually broke. He joked with friends about marriage and romance. He had a mistress, Madame Schontz, proud to have a writer as her lover; she lived nearby and enjoyed teasing friends. This bohemian existence would later affect Dinah: he could laugh about his entanglements, including her, whom he had almost forgotten.
When Bixiou asked about Sancerre, Lousteau recounted three timid provincial men who’d failed to pursue a local beauty. He received a long, affectionate letter from Dinah, which he read and burned, dismissing her feelings; another arrived a week later and he discarded it unread. Madame Schontz noticed the scented paper, opened hampers Dinah had sent—game and flowers—and proposed a dinner. Lousteau sent a clever but insincere letter he thought would charm Dinah; by December her letters lay unread in a drawer.
Then Madame Schontz told him of a marriage opportunity: a young woman’s previous lover had died, and her father, a notary, sought a respectable man to marry his daughter quickly, restoring her position with a substantial dowry. Hesitant at first, Lousteau agreed to meet them. He flattered the notary, recalled his own father, and presented himself as ambitious. At his first dinner he noticed Félicie, the shy grateful daughter; Madame Cardot regarded him as a man of promise. Buoyed by this reception, Lousteau felt a new purpose—stability, respectability, and the financial security the marriage promised.
Madame Cardot, having borrowed an illustrated Gil Blas to inspect prints, unexpectedly took her daughter Félicie to Lousteau’s bachelor rooms to judge a prospective son‑in‑law. Madame Camusot, uneasy about the match from her husband the Councillor’s reports, had already mistrusted Lousteau. On a January day in 1837 they hired a cab; the porter’s daughter handed over the key after Madame Cardot slipped her a gold coin.
Around noon Lousteau, returning from breakfast, was startled when Dinah, his former lover, arrived by coach and revealed she was a mother. Overwhelmed, he tried to dismiss her, but she insisted on staying. The child, Pamela, sensing the awkwardness, announced that his mother‑in‑law was inside; Lousteau discovered Madame Cardot and Félicie hidden. Dinah professed her love; Lousteau, fearing the arranged marriage he faced, began to imagine using her to escape it.
Madame Cardot openly disapproved and left with Félicie, while Dinah, trusting Lousteau’s reassurances and high‑minded air, remained. She admired the apparent elegance of his rooms, not their poverty. Pamela had borrowed dinnerware from Madame Schontz, who learned of Lousteau’s predicament and warned him Cardot would not pursue the match. Lousteau, however, focused on his intimacy with Dinah and the chance to avoid a wealthy marriage.
When his friend Bixiou challenged him—arguing provincial women often failed to adapt to Paris and that marriage should secure legitimacy—Lousteau dismissed him. Later Madame Schontz told him Cardot had arranged Félicie’s marriage to another, extinguishing his respectable prospects. Having lost a stable future, Lousteau grew more entangled with Dinah; their domestic life, sustained by his entertainments, slipped into the provincial romance he once mocked, leaving both trapped in dependency and illusion.
That Paris winter did for Dinah what autumn in Sancerre had before. Etienne (Lousteau) eager to introduce her to Parisian life, took her to the theater, fashionable dressmakers, and society, stripping away her provincial modesty. Dinah adapted quickly—immersing herself in the city’s culture and proving intelligent and well read, able to hold her own among sophisticated women. Lousteau came to regard her as both muse and partner, even as he privately entertained deceits when his fancy waned.
A newspaper item about Monsieur le Baron de Clagny’s promotion recalled Sancerre to Dinah and prompted teasing. Their passion remained intense, but Lousteau’s motives were increasingly self‑interested. When Dinah became pregnant she withdrew from public life; at a premiere she was humiliated when Lousteau abandoned her and Clagny warned the liaison threatened her reputation.
After the child’s birth Dinah devoted herself to motherhood and the household, pawning jewelry to help. Lousteau failed in letters, accumulated debts, grew indifferent, and sought solace elsewhere. A second child increased Dinah’s burdens. The turning point came when her estranged husband, Monsieur de la Baudraye, reappeared seeking power of attorney over an inheritance. Clagny—now Attorney‑General—helped Dinah secure an allowance in exchange for cooperation and protected her interests. Confronted with Lousteau’s betrayal and lack of ambition, Dinah resolved to reclaim her identity and seek a future based on mutual respect and commitment rather than mere passion.
From that day Etienne lived in luxury and Dinah could stand among Paris’s best-dressed women. Lousteau, however, played the ruined lover in public—blaming Madame de la Baudraye and feigning helplessness to exploit Dinah’s devotion—while remaining unfaithful. Monsieur de Clagny, disturbed by Dinah’s disgrace, warned her of betrayal; she answered, “I know it.” When he asked if she still loved Lousteau, she cried, “I would lose my soul for you!”—leaving him speechless and moved.
Slowly Dinah perceived Lousteau’s true nature. She described him to her mother as an idle poet, pleasure-seeking and impossible to hate because she felt responsible and believed she alone supported him. Her mother deplored the hell Dinah endured; Dinah replied, “I will be a mother to him!” Accepting humiliations to keep him, she compromised her dignity, hoping by emulating Madame de Pompadour to win him back. His theatrical devotion eroded her self-respect, yet she clung to the warped consolation of being his plaything.
She rehearsed dramatic separations and even contemplated suicide in despair, but every word or gesture from Lousteau pulled her back. Influenced by the novel Adolphe, she vowed not to display pain in reproaches or tears.
Meanwhile Monsieur de la Baudraye schemed to restore his fortune by manipulating Dinah’s inheritance—underreporting its value, pocketing the difference, and investing in land—planning to defer obligations to Dinah while pursuing status: a noble life in Sancerre, a title, and higher standing.
By early 1842 Dinah felt reduced to Lousteau’s convenience, sacrificing herself for his comfort. She dressed in mourning as penitence; her mother sought help. Clagny and Madame Piedefer urged her to live apart; Clagny negotiated, assuring support for a respectable return to society.
Unbeknownst to Dinah, Monsieur de la Baudraye had invested in a grand Paris residence to polish his image and pursue a peerage. He invited her to live with him in Paris and promised financial support for their children’s education.
Now a Countess, Dinah bore the weight of title and expectation, preparing for social prominence that contrasted with earlier struggles. In May 1842 she paid off her debts and invited Lousteau to dinner—an event marking a decisive change. He arrived struck by her transformation. Dinah announced their separation: she still cared but demanded a calm parting. Lousteau accused her of ruining his life; she refused to be blamed. Their last exchange mixed sorrow and defiance. Dinah left both liberated and heartbroken.
In her new life she made her home a model of comfort, spending beyond her husband’s expectations. Charming and fashionable, she missed past passion and struggled to bond with other women. Clagny introduced her to influential figures, yet learning Lousteau lived with another provoked conflicting emotions and pushed her deeper into society. In March 1843 her appointment to collect charity donations restored her public image. Summers in Sancerre found her a doting mother and proud Countess, still torn between love and ambition—an unresolved tension that defines her journey.

################################################
Across France there were many Chevaliers de Valois; this tale centers on the one in Alençon. Though sharing the name, these chevaliers were mostly strangers to one another and had accepted the Bourbons after the Valois produced no male heir. The Alençon chevalier was an elderly, tall, thin gentleman whose Parisian youth and romantic adventures were interrupted by the Revolution.
Well regarded among the local aristocracy, he was noted for his manners, wit, and anecdotes from Louis XV’s reign. A social fixture, his charm and discretion made him a favorite. At fifty-eight he insisted he was fifty, keeping a youthful air. He had a prominent nose and a pale complexion that flushed on one side—he blamed a “hot liver” and a voracious appetite. Though seemingly frail, he remained vigorous and kept his person and worn clothes carefully maintained, upholding aristocratic standards. Modest in means, he rented a room above Madame Lardot’s laundry and lived on a small annuity. Beloved for card-playing and conversation, he mingled across classes, yet curiosity surrounded his private life, including rumors of a secret marriage to a woman named Césarine.
One spring morning Suzanne, a young laundress at Madame Lardot’s, interrupted him with news. Ambitious and clever, she aspired to leave Alençon for Paris. The chevalier, struck by her charm, encouraged her in a paternal tone and advised her to court Monsieur du Bousquier, a wealthy bachelor who had shown interest.
Du Bousquier, however, had a dubious reputation: once prosperous, his service as a purveyor during the Revolution had tarnished him. Back in Alençon, he hoped to marry Mademoiselle Cormon, a rich old maid, to restore his fortunes. His brusque opportunism contrasted with the chevalier’s elegance; their rivalry over Cormon, along with Suzanne’s ambitions, set in motion a delicate dance of desire and social expectation.
Madame Granson, a widow on a modest pension with a small property, struggled to support her son Athanase, a pale, introspective twenty-three-year-old clerk. Athanase secretly loved his cousin Cormon, believing her wealthy; what began as practical hope became genuine affection. Suzanne felt an immediate bond with Athanase and understood his struggles, yet her ambition pushed her toward deception. After a brief meeting with Madame Granson, Suzanne left town with money and new clothes, bound for Paris, while Athanase remained in Alençon, preparing a dinner with Mademoiselle Cormon and weighed down by unfulfilled dreams.
In many second-rank French prefectures a local salon gathered influential—but not top-tier—residents. Hosted by prominent locals, it included lesser nobility, clergy, and magistrates who took part in events while avoiding the highest circles and communicating formally. The salon shaped local affairs: members valued practicality over luxury, focused on land and community matters, resisted Parisian fashions, and used gossip as judgment. Young women rarely appeared except to seek social validation.
In Alençon the circle centered on Mademoiselle Rose-Marie-Victoire Cormon, an unmarried woman whose fortune was coveted by relatives and two aging bachelors. Living with her uncle, a former grand-vicar, she descended from a family long in service to the nobility and, through advantageous marriages, had acquired a semblance of aristocracy despite middle-class origins. Her house, dating from Henri IV, occupied a prime site and was kept with meticulous care: a neat courtyard, flowerbed, solid architecture, and period decor. Reception rooms were arranged for social life—a portrait-hung salon for cards and local talk, and a dining room of provincial charm. Her circle numbered about 150, mostly mature provincials who rarely traveled.
Wednesdays were notable: dinners attracted larger assemblies where modestly dressed women spoke familiarly about gossip, land disputes, and judgments of aristocratic behavior—topics reflecting communal concerns and values.
Despite wealth and standing, Mademoiselle Cormon remained single. She had hoped to marry a nobleman, but the upheavals of 1788–1815 left few suitable matches; many were too old, too young, or otherwise unsuitable. Guarding her fortune, she set subtle tests for suitors. As she aged her longing for love intensified, especially for Athanase Granson, exposing her innocence. Pious and virtuous, she tightened under social expectation and became a local byword; returning soldiers briefly raised hopes, but none met her standards. Her yearning for companionship and motherhood turned to despair, revealing the limits placed on women by rigid class conventions.
The household completed the picture: the Abbé de Sponde and servants Jacquelin, Josette, and Mariette ensured comfort. Jacquelin, short and stout with a ruddy face, had served twenty-two years and cared for everything from table service to the family mare Pénélope. He loved Josette, a thirty-year-old maid whom Mademoiselle would dismiss if she married; the couple quietly saved, hoping Mademoiselle’s marriage would free them to wed. Mariette, cook of fifteen years, prepared local dishes. Routine held while Mademoiselle grew harder to please, obsessing over linens, Pénélope’s meals, minor ailments, and household minutiae.
Each season she visited her Prébaudet estate to inspect apple trees and mark the harvest with a farewell dinner that became an Alençon event. Town gossip about suitors—notably M. du Bousquier, whom she had rejected—swirled. At the dinner she tried to engage but often lacked wit; when she clumsily defended du Bousquier against rumors involving Suzanne, a remark unexpectedly won laughter and admiration, leaving her to ponder her place amid love, ambition, and social expectation in Alençon.
Mademoiselle Cormon and Josette went to Prebaudet, unaware an event would hasten her marriage. The morning after, Jacquelin brought a letter from her uncle, the Abbé de Sponde: Monsieur de Troisville, his friend's grandson, sought hospitality. Alarmed she returned to town hopeful of marriage.
Gossip swirled as Josette and Jacquelin ran the house and Cormon prepared for the viscount, sacrificing liqueurs to impress. When Monsieur de Troisville arrived she felt attracted when he praised her home. The mood shifted when the Chevalier de Valois and other guests came and the viscount revealed he was married—Cormon fainted and Du Bousquier carried her to her room.
As gossip grew and both Du Bousquier and the Chevalier sought her favor, she accepted Du Bousquier’s proposal on condition of a sham public engagement; he agreed, leaving her triumphant yet uncertain.
The news of Mademoiselle Cormon’s marriage devastated Athanase Granson, though he concealed his pain. He first learned of it at Chief Justice du Ronceret’s house while his mother played cards. Having long clung to hope and schooled in Imperial fatalism, he sank into indifference; his mother noticed his pallor but assumed he was merely preoccupied with work.
After the announcement, Athanase wandered the Sarthe countryside, finding bitter solace in its neglected beauty. He withdrew from politics and society and became little noticed by townspeople, who did not grasp the depth of his suffering. His mother fretted, imagining he might be planning some grand project, unaware of the true cause.
Suzanne, stirred by romantic tales, returned to Alençon determined to help Athanase financially, though they never met. The town buzzed with gossip about Cormon’s match, with conflicting reports about its legitimacy. When the marriage contract was signed and the wedding held, ominous signs—like a priest accidentally reading from the De Profundis—set an uneasy tone.
The union lacked celebration; the couple retreated to Prébaudet. Du Bousquier began modernizing the estate, alarming locals who feared losing provincial charm. Consumed by grief, Athanase ultimately killed himself, leaving a letter his mother misread as evidence of newfound independence rather than despair.
His suicide shocked Alençon. Madame Granson mourned privately, bearing a grief others barely understood. The Chevalier de Valois disclosed Athanase’s unrequited love for Mademoiselle Cormon, deepening the town’s sense of tragedy. To avoid scandal, Madame Granson arranged a discreet burial, the quiet ceremony reflecting the community’s wish to contain the episode.
Over time Madame du Bousquier’s marriage became gossip and a cautionary tale: outwardly advantageous but childless and tyrannical, it left her powerless and unfulfilled. The Chevalier’s death and political change closed an era; Suzanne honored the past with a small memento. Madame du Bousquier’s life thus reflects the complexities of love, duty, and constrained choice.

DEDICATION
To Baron Von Hammer-Purgstall, Member of the Aulic Council, Author of the History of the Ottoman Empire.
Dear Baron, your encouragement of my History of French Manners in the Nineteenth Century led me to associate your name with my work. As a representative of studious Germany, your approval gives weight to my efforts. Your devotion to research sustained me in writing about modern civilization. Accept this token of respect from your sincere admirer and friend.
DE BALZAC.
THE COLLECTION OF ANTIQUITIES
In a small French town stood the Hôtel d’Esgrignon, home of the old Marquis Charles Marie Victor Ange Carol. The name matched a family proud of ancient Northman lineage and stubborn provincial dignity—marriages without dowries, refusal of titles, local reverence despite neglect by court and state.
The Revolution threatened them. The Marquis avoided emigration by hiding; family lands were sold. Mlle. d’Esgrignon, with the steward Chesnel, saved some property. After the Terror the Marquis returned to a ruined castle; the old hôtel was demolished. Chesnel used the family’s last funds to buy a modest new dwelling, wryly renamed the Hôtel d’Esgrignon.
Around 1800 émigrés returned, including the Baron de Nouastre and his daughter, whom the Marquis married; she died in childbirth, leaving a son. Grief compounded their reduced circumstances. A local contractor’s proposal to marry Mlle. d’Esgrignon offended the siblings; Chesnel was reproached for negotiating. Chesnel was more than a servant—confidant, manager, protector—faithful to a declining house. Politically nobles split between Bonapartists and monarchists; the d’Esgrignons remained royalist. By 1804 returns had not restored fortunes. The Marquis became a local emblem of old nobility, presiding over a salon mockingly called the “Collection of Antiquities.” By 1822 the Restoration brought little relief: finances strained, life modest, yet they clung to ideals and a lingering legacy.
The Marquis neglected estate management, and most believed the d’Esgrignons could not regain their former glory. Many hoped Victurnien, the handsome heir, would marry into nobility to restore prestige, but after forty years the family lacked the connections, money, and stability to reclaim status. Only Chesnel, the notary, recognized this reality and urged adaptation; the Marquis preferred to trust fate or divine intervention.
Victurnien, adored and indulged by his father and aunt, had never faced opposition. Spoiled by the Chevalier’s romantic tales of past grandeur and constant flattery, he became selfish and reckless, seeing himself above common people. His escapades alienated townspeople and led to legal troubles Chesnel discreetly managed. Encouraged by companions who enjoyed his company, his arrogance hardened; Du Croisier, a rival, resolved to exploit these weaknesses to hasten the family’s ruin.
Vanity drove Victurnien into gambling and frivolity, sustained by Chesnel’s financial sacrifices. Sent to Paris to restore the name, he found the court indifferent and instead adopted an extravagant lifestyle. His looks opened doors, and he became infatuated with the Duchesse de Maufrigneuse, blind to her debts and convinced their passion was pure. As pleasures mounted, so did debts; friends egged his spending while Chesnel repeatedly propped him up. This clash between pretension and dire finances foreshadowed downfall, illustrating the dangers of vanity and the illusion of inherited status.
By the end of 1823 the Kellers had advanced Victurnien 200,000 francs without Chesnel or Mlle. Armande knowing. Victurnien had also taken 2,000 crowns from Chesnel to conceal his sources and wrote flattering, deceitful letters home; his father and aunt remained unaware. Only Du Croisier knew, delighting not only in ruining the d’Esgrignons but in dishonoring them as debts mounted.
Du Croisier first targeted Chesnel, who lived modestly on Rue du Bercail. He presented a bill for 227,000 francs, demanding payment or threatening exposure. Overwhelmed, Chesnel saw he could raise money only by mortgaging his lands. Du Croisier seized the chance, bought the property within two days, and left Chesnel with only his practice and house, consumed by worry for Victurnien.
Chesnel dreaded telling Mlle. Armande, who had just received a charming letter from Victurnien about travels in Italy with the Duchesse and remained enchanted. His news shattered her reverie: all was lost, and Victurnien risked dishonor. She resolved to go to Paris—pretending it was for medical reasons—to confront the Duchesse; Chesnel gave her his last bag of louis.
Du Croisier then moved to cut Victurnien off, arranging for the Kellers to demand security before advancing more funds. Pressed and briefly contemplating suicide, Victurnien instead plotted to flee with the Duchesse and forged a bill of exchange; when she agreed and he presented a bill for 300,000 francs, the Kellers advanced the sum unknowingly. Du Croisier, seeing the deception and its effect, realized his scheme was succeeding.
Victurnien gave the money to Mme. de Maufrigneuse, who hid it and persuaded him to return to the Opera. He nearly confessed to Chesnel but, dazzled by the Duchess’s look, stayed at her request. The next day, when he planned to flee, she resolved to face the consequences; torn between admiration and despair, he recognized the depth of his love. After a quarrel Diane’s coldness left him distraught; his plea for forgiveness met indifference, worsening his anguish and clouding his judgment.
When a warrant was issued, Josephin urged him to go to the Hotel du Bon la Fontaine where Mlle. Armande waited. Reeling, Victurnien and his aunt discussed hiding and slipping into Chesnel’s house—a plan that proved a trap. Chesnel, preparing to sell his practice to M. Lepressoir (who hoped to profit from Victurnien’s misfortune), was devastated. He considered taking the blame for the forgery and went to confront Victurnien’s creditor, du Croisier, to seek a settlement.
Du Croisier, relishing his ascendancy over the d’Esgrignon family, treated the affair as an opportunity to assert dominance and settle aristocratic scores. At his salon news of Victurnien’s arrest spread and guests debated the impending trial. Chesnel tried to influence the examining magistrate, M. Camusot, by appealing to his ambitions and leaned on Mme. de Maufrigneuse—who, once she grasped Victurnien’s love and the stakes, felt compelled to help.
Negotiations with du Croisier were fraught: he demanded money, political favors, and social recognition—terms Chesnel could scarcely meet. Desperate, Chesnel offered a large sum and even contemplated perjury, but du Croisier’s pride resisted compromise. The legal struggle came to symbolize a broader clash between aristocracy and bourgeoisie, where loyalty, love, honor, and ambition would decide more than one fate.
Meanwhile Blondet, more devoted to gardening than his bench, neglected career prospects amid a troubled marriage; he favored his illegitimate son Émile in Paris while grooming his legitimate son Joseph for the law. Ambitious younger lawyers outshone the elders. Under pressure from the Tribunal’s president and his wife, a politically astute prosecutor was drawn into a scheme against the d’Esgrignons. The Duchess and Chesnel funded efforts to free Victurnien; the trial ultimately exonerated him and exposed du Croisier’s plots. Though socially scrutinized, the d’Esgrignons preserved their honor; Victurnien’s future remained uncertain, highlighting shifting loyalties and ambitions in a changing France.
